# SON OF COSMOS: JUMPCHAIN

## CHAPTER 1: APRIL 1989

*A Tenchi Muyo! × Sailor Moon Crossover*

---


### Day 1

#### Scene 1.A: The Awakening

Scene: 1 — The Awakening
Date/Time: April 3, 1989, 2:47 PM – ~3:37 PM
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
VERIFICATION COMPLETE — SCENE APPROVED
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════


The sky was blue.

That was the first thing. Blue, interrupted by white — clouds, his mind supplied, before he'd decided to think. Cumulus. Fair weather clouds. The kind that meant no rain.

He was cataloging. He noticed himself doing it and couldn't stop. The blue was deep, almost cobalt where the clouds didn't reach. The white was pure, edges well-defined. The light suggested mid-afternoon, sun past zenith, probably forty-five degrees above the horizon based on the brightness in his peripheral vision.

He was lying on his back. That came next. Something prickly-soft beneath him — grass, his hands confirmed without his permission, fingers curling into it. Cool earth under that. The smell of green things and damp soil and something sweet he couldn't name.

A bird called.

Three notes. Clear as struck crystal. Rising, clipping short, falling away. *Ho-hokekyo.* He didn't know the bird. Had never heard that particular call. But something in him — something older than the part that cataloged cloud types — recognized it anyway.

*Spring,* his body said. *You're alive, and it's spring.*

The thought should have been reassuring. Instead it sent ice through his chest, because the last thing he remembered was dying.


The cascade failure. The containment breach. Maria's face through the observation window, her mouth open in a scream he couldn't hear over the alarms. The light — not light, *energy* — expanding outward from the focal point, and in that final fraction of a second, the absolute certainty that there was no time to run, no time to shield, no time for anything at all.

He had died.

He remembered dying.

And now he was lying in a field, looking at clouds, listening to a bird he didn't recognize, and his heart was beating steady and strong in a chest that didn't hurt at all.

Sheldon sat up.

The motion came easier than it should have. No catch in his lower back — the familiar twinge that had been his companion for fifteen years, absent. His hands pressed into the grass to push himself upright, and they moved without the morning stiffness in his knuckles that he'd learned to expect. He looked at them.

Wrong. They were wrong.

The shape was his. The length of the fingers, the particular curve of the thumb, the way the tendons moved under skin — all recognizably *his*. But the skin was too smooth. Too young. And the scar on his right thumb — the paring knife, Grandmother's kitchen, eight years old and learning to peel apples — was gone.

He turned his hand over. Traced the place where the scar should be. Smooth. Unmarked. As if it had never happened.

*As if he had never happened.*

The bird called again. *Ho-hokekyo.* Somewhere to his left, hidden in trees he was only now noticing. A treeline, thirty or forty meters away. Green leaves and — 

Pink.

A tree covered in pale pink blossoms, so thick they nearly obscured the branches. Petals drifting down like snow, carpeting the dark earth beneath. Cherry blossoms. *Sakura.* The word surfaced from somewhere, from the years in Kyoto that suddenly felt very far away.

Cherry blossoms meant spring. Early April, in most of Japan. Peak bloom.

He was in Japan.

The realization settled into him with strange weight. The vegetation was right. The quality of the light. The bird call — Japanese, probably, though he'd never studied ornithology. And when he looked beyond the cherry tree to the horizon, he saw low mountains with the particular silhouette of the Japanese countryside, the kind of view he'd seen a hundred times during his postdoc years.

But his postdoc had been in 2010. And he had died in 2025. In Alaska. In a particle physics laboratory that was probably a smoking crater now.

*Think,* he told himself. *You're a scientist. Observe. Catalog. Hypothesize.*

He looked down at himself.

Gray slacks. Single pleat, no cuffs. Well-fitted. White button-down shirt, slightly rumpled from lying in grass but clean. Black leather belt. The clothes were not his — he'd been wearing a lab coat over a Stanford t-shirt when—

He stopped that thought. Filed it away. Kept cataloging.

He patted his pockets. Left trouser: coins, jingling. Right trouser: nothing. Back right: a wallet, he realized, pulling it free. Brown leather. Worn at the edges. Not his wallet — his had been newer, bought at a Target in Berkeley when the old one finally fell apart.

He opened it.

Cash. A lot of it — Japanese yen, he recognized the Fukuzawa Yukichi portrait on the ten-thousand note. Three of those. More in smaller denominations. A coin pouch built into the wallet, heavy with change.

A card in a plastic sleeve. He pulled it out.

An ID. His face — *his* face, unmistakably — in a small photograph. But younger. The face he remembered from his thirties, not the one he'd seen in the mirror the morning he died. Text in Japanese, which he read with surprising ease — the characters sharp and clear in his mind, the grammar parsing itself without the hesitation he'd grown used to in the years since Kyoto. His Japanese shouldn't be this fluent. It had been fifteen years. But the words resolved themselves anyway, and he added it to his growing list.

外国人登録証明書. Alien Registration Certificate.

Name: 月野 シェルドン. *Tsukino Sheldon.*

Nationality: アメリカ合衆国. United States of America.

Date of Birth: 1959年3月15日. March 15, 1959.

Status of Residence: 永住者. Permanent Resident.

Date of Registration: 1987年4月.

He stared at the card for a long time.

His name was not Tsukino. His birthday was not March 15, 1959 — it was November 3, 1978. He had never held Japanese permanent residency. And he had certainly not registered anywhere in April of 1987, because in April of 1987 he had been nine years old and living in Marietta, Georgia, still two years away from losing his father to an industrial accident.

And yet.

The face in the photograph was his face. The card looked genuine — proper materials, official stamps, the particular texture of government-issued documents. If this was a forgery, it was flawless.

His hands were shaking. He noticed that with the same detached observation he'd been applying to everything else. Tremor in the fingers. Elevated heart rate — though still steady, still stronger than he remembered it being. Symptoms of shock, probably. Reasonable, under the circumstances.

He kept going through the wallet.

More documents. A folded paper — *koseki*, family registry excerpt, he recognized the format from his Kyoto years. It showed a family he'd never heard of. Tsukino Takeo, deceased 1978. Tsukino Sachiko, deceased 1985. And Tsukino Sheldon, born 1959, status: living.

A photograph, wallet-sized. An elderly woman in kimono. A middle-aged man in work clothes. A woman in a simple dress. A young man who looked... 

He held the photo closer.

The young man in the photograph looked like him. Or like a version of him. The resemblance was unmistakable — same bone structure, same set of the eyes. But he had never taken this photograph. Had never worn those clothes. Had never stood in front of that house with those people.

Had never had a family called Tsukino.

A letter, folded. He didn't read it yet. Two bankbooks — one for a regional bank, one for postal savings. Both in the name Tsukino Sheldon. Both showing balances that added up to more money than he'd ever had in savings at any point in his previous life.

*Previous life.*

The phrase echoed in his mind. He let it sit there. Didn't examine it too closely. Not yet.

He put the wallet back together. Returned it to his pocket. Stood up.

His body moved like a well-maintained machine. No aches. No stiffness. No familiar complaints. His vision was sharp — sharper than it had been since he was twelve years old and got his first pair of glasses. He could see individual leaves on the cherry tree. Could count the petals falling.

He wasn't wearing glasses. He should have been wearing glasses.

Sheldon looked around.

The field stretched maybe sixty meters long, fifty wide. Fallow — grass and wildflowers growing where crops had once been planted, the ghost of paddy ridges still visible under the vegetation. A low stone wall to the south, partially collapsed. The treeline to the north, with the cherry tree standing slightly apart like a sentinel. To the west, the land rose toward forested hills. To the east, it descended toward more fields, and beyond them — far off — hints of buildings. A village.

No people. No sounds of machinery. No aircraft overhead. Just wind in the grass, birds in the trees, and his own breathing.

He looked at the ID card again. The address field showed characters he could read: Okayama Prefecture. A town name. A specific location.

But reading an address and knowing where it was were two different things. He had no map. No familiarity with this area. And whoever or whatever had placed him here had apparently not seen fit to include a GPS — not that such a thing existed, if the registration date on his ID was any indication.

*1987. The card says I registered in 1987.*

He looked at the sun. At the vegetation. At the style of the clothes he was wearing.

*What year is it?*

The question opened a pit in his stomach. He filed it with the others. Kept moving.

He needed to find people. People could tell him where he was, what year it was, how to reach the address on his ID. People meant water, food, shelter — Grandmother's voice, suddenly clear in his mind: *First you survive. Then you figure out the rest.*

He looked south, toward the stone wall and, beyond it, the suggestion of more fields descending toward lower ground. East, where he'd seen the distant buildings. West, where the land rose into forest.

Roads were usually in valleys, not on hillsides. Villages were built where the land was flat, where water gathered, where farming was easiest. If he walked downhill — south and east — he'd eventually find a road. And roads led to people.

He started walking.

The grass whispered against his slacks as he moved. His shoes — black oxfords, comfortable, broken-in like he'd been wearing them for years — made soft sounds on the uneven ground. A butterfly crossed his path, yellow and erratic, and he watched it until it disappeared into the treeline.

At the stone wall, he paused. Looked back at the field where he'd awakened. The grass was still pressed flat where he'd lain. In an hour, maybe two, it would spring back. There would be no evidence he'd ever been there.

The cherry tree shed another handful of petals. They drifted down like pink snow, settling on the dark earth.

He turned away and climbed over the wall.


The next field was similar to the first — fallow, overgrown, the bones of old rice paddies visible under the wild grass. He crossed it, then another. The land sloped gently downward, confirming his guess about the terrain.

After fifteen minutes of walking, he saw the road.

It appeared as a gray line cutting through the green — pavement, cracked and weathered by years of use. Barely wide enough for a single car. No markings, no signs visible from this distance. But it was a road. It went somewhere.

Sheldon quickened his pace, ignoring the mild burn in his calves that was the first hint his body had any limits at all. His throat was dry. His stomach, now that he was paying attention, felt hollow. He didn't know when this body had last eaten or drunk, but apparently it needed to do both soon.

He reached the road and stood at its edge. Pavement warm under his feet, sun-baked, holding the afternoon heat. It curved away in both directions — west, back toward the mountains; east, toward lower ground and, presumably, civilization.

East, then. Toward the valley. Toward the village he'd glimpsed from the field.

He started walking.

The road was quiet. No traffic, no engine sounds, no noise but his own footsteps and the wind. Power lines ran along one side — wooden poles, ceramic insulators, the kind of rural infrastructure that hadn't changed much since the mid-century. Confirmation, if he needed it, that he wasn't in a city.

His shadow stretched out in front of him, long in the afternoon light. He watched it move and tried not to think about the fact that he shouldn't have a shadow at all. Shouldn't have a body to cast one.

*Who?* The question surfaced again. *Who could do this? Who would do this?*

No answer. He kept walking.

A sound behind him — engine noise, growing closer. He stepped to the side of the narrow road and turned.

A small white truck appeared around the curve. A kei truck, his mind supplied — the compact utility vehicles that were everywhere in rural Japan. It was slowing down.

The truck pulled alongside him and stopped. The driver's window was already down. An older man peered out — sixties, maybe, weathered face, work clothes, a cap with some agricultural company logo. His eyes took in Sheldon's appearance with frank curiosity.

"You lost?" the man asked in Japanese. The Okayama dialect, Sheldon noted — longer vowels, softer consonants than the Tokyo standard he'd learned.

"I'm looking for—" Sheldon pulled out his ID card, showed the address. "Can you tell me how to get here?"

The man squinted at the card. His eyebrows rose. "Tsukino? Near Masaki Shrine?" He looked at Sheldon again, reassessing. "You family?"

"Yes," Sheldon said. The lie came easily. It was on the documents, after all.

The man nodded slowly. "That's a ways from here. You'd need to take the bus from the stop up ahead." He jerked his thumb eastward. "I can give you a ride that far, if you want. Heading that direction anyway."

"Thank you," Sheldon said. "I would appreciate that."

The man reached over and pushed open the passenger door. Sheldon climbed in.

The cab smelled of fertilizer and tobacco and something else — warm vinyl, maybe, baked by years of sun. An omamori charm hung from the rearview mirror, faded red and gold. The radio was silent, the dashboard simple and worn.

"Yamamoto," the man said, not quite looking at him. "Yamamoto Takeshi."

"Tsukino Sheldon." The name still felt strange in his mouth. But the man nodded as if it were the most natural thing in the world.

The truck pulled back onto the road, and Sheldon watched the fields slide past, and tried to remember how to breathe.


**SCENE 1 — END**

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
VERIFICATION COMPLETE — PROCEED TO SCENE
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════


The bus stop was nothing more than a wooden bench under a tin roof, set back from the road on a patch of gravel. A schedule was posted on a metal pole — white paper behind plastic, the kind that yellowed in the sun. Sheldon studied it.

His eyes found the characters without effort. Line 47. Kamogata-Yama Line. A list of stops in neat columns, departure times running down the side. The stop he was at — 原田, Harada — was highlighted with a small red dot. The schedule showed the next bus at 4:15.

He checked his watch.

He wasn't wearing a watch.

The realization stopped him cold for a moment. He'd worn a watch since graduate school — a cheap Casio digital that had survived two decades of lab work. His wrist was bare now. Smooth skin where the tan line should have been.

He looked at the sun instead. Descending toward the western hills, maybe three hours from setting. If the next bus was at 4:15, and he'd been walking for roughly thirty minutes before Yamamoto picked him up, and the drive had been perhaps ten minutes... 

Close to four o'clock. Maybe a bit before.

He had time.

Sheldon sat on the bench. The wood was warm from the afternoon sun, weathered smooth by years of use. He could smell dust and greenery and, faintly, the exhaust residue of buses that had come and gone.

The road was quiet. A car passed — small, white, moving with the unhurried pace of rural traffic — and then nothing. Birds called from the fields. The same three-note song he'd heard in the field, *ho-hokekyo*, more distant now but still audible. 

He pulled out his wallet again.

The letter was still there, folded. He hadn't read it yet. Some part of him had been putting it off, as if the wallet's contents were a puzzle he needed to solve in order — ID first, then family registry, then photograph, then finally the words someone had written to him. Or written to whoever he was supposed to be.

He unfolded it.


The paper was thin, slightly yellowed. Handwritten in neat, careful characters — a woman's hand, he thought, though he couldn't say why. The date at the top read 1985年8月. August 1985.

*Sheldon,*

*By the time you read this, I will be gone. The doctors say weeks, maybe days. I'm writing while I still can.*

*I have left you everything — the house, the land, the savings. It isn't much, but it's yours. You are the last of the Tsukinos now. I'm sorry to leave you with that burden.*

*Your father's grave is in the village cemetery, next to your grandmother's. I couldn't bring myself to be buried there. I've asked to have my ashes scattered at sea, off the coast where your father and I walked when we were young. Forgive me for not being with him. Forgive me for leaving you alone.*

*Go home, Sheldon. Go back to Japan. The house is waiting for you. The land is waiting. Your uncle Kiyoshi will help you — he's a good man, even if we weren't close.*

*I love you. I have always loved you. You were the best thing that ever happened to me.*

*Your mother,*
*Sachiko*


Sheldon read the letter twice.

Then he folded it carefully and put it back in his wallet, and he sat very still on the bench while the world continued around him, heedless.

A mother who had died in 1985. A father who had died in 1978. An uncle named Kiyoshi. A house waiting for him in a village near a shrine called Masaki.

None of it was real. He knew that. He had been born in 1978, not 1959. His mother's name had been Dorothy, not Sachiko. His father had been a machinist in Marietta, not a farmer in Okayama.

But someone had built this life for him. Someone had created documents, planted memories, written letters in a dead woman's hand. Someone had given him a past he'd never lived, complete with grief he'd never earned.

*Why?*

The question was becoming familiar. He filed it with the others.

A sound intruded — engine noise, growing louder. He looked up.

A bus was approaching from the east. Small, white with blue trim, the kanji for "Shimoden" painted on the side. It slowed as it neared the stop, air brakes hissing, and came to rest precisely at the edge of the gravel.

The door folded open.


The driver was a man in his fifties, uniformed in the practical navy blue of transit workers. He glanced at Sheldon with professional neutrality — a quick assessment, nothing more.

"Jinja-mae?" Sheldon asked, confirming the destination. The words came out smooth, unaccented. He'd meant to ask more — how far, how long, whether this was the right bus — but the simple question was all that emerged.

The driver nodded. "Last stop."

Sheldon climbed aboard. The fare box was mounted beside the driver's seat, a mechanical device with a coin slot and a small digital display showing ¥230. He fed in coins — two hundreds, a fifty — and heard them clatter into the collection chamber.

The driver handed him a small paper stub. A receipt, or a ticket, or simply proof that he'd paid. Sheldon took it and moved down the aisle.

The bus was nearly empty. Two elderly women sat in the front seats, heads bent together in quiet conversation. A teenager in a school uniform sprawled in the back row, head against the window, eyes closed. No one looked at Sheldon as he chose a seat in the middle, on the right side, where he could watch the road ahead.

The door closed. The bus pulled away.


The landscape unfolded through the window — rice paddies giving way to forest, forest opening onto more paddies, the road winding north along the contour of the hills. Sheldon watched it pass and tried to think.

He was in Japan. In Okayama Prefecture, based on the documents. The year was 1989 — or close to it — if the registration date on his ID meant anything. April, based on the cherry blossoms. A Monday, maybe, given how empty the roads were.

He had money, documents, a destination. He had a house waiting for him, apparently, and an uncle who might recognize him. He had a history that wasn't his and a body that felt like a stranger's.

And he had died.

That part kept circling back. The containment breach. The light that wasn't light. The moment of ending, absolute and total. He remembered it clearly — more clearly than he wanted to. The memory wasn't fading or blurring the way dreams did. It sat in his mind like a stone, solid and undeniable.

He had died. And now he was on a bus in rural Japan, watching power lines tick past, holding a ticket stub he'd paid for with coins from a wallet he'd never owned.

The bus stopped. One of the elderly women stood, gathered a shopping bag, and shuffled toward the door. The driver called out the stop name — "Ōike" — and waited while she descended.

Four more stops, Sheldon thought. Eighteen minutes, maybe. Or less, with no one else getting off.

He looked at his hands. Still wrong. Still too young. The knuckles flexed smoothly when he made a fist, no ache, no stiffness. His grandmother had had arthritis by sixty. His father had complained of joint pain before he died. Sheldon had felt the first twinges in his own hands at forty-three, the inheritance coming due.

These hands had no inheritance. No history. No scars.

*Whose hands are these?*

The bus rumbled on.


The light was changing. Still bright, but softer now, the angle lowering as afternoon tilted toward evening. Shadows stretched longer across the paddies. The hills to the west were backlit, their edges glowing.

"Nakayama," the driver announced. No one moved. The bus paused, waited, continued.

The road climbed. Not steeply, but noticeably — the engine working harder, the view out the window tilting upward toward the tree line. They were leaving the valley floor, ascending toward the mountains.

Sheldon's throat was dry. He realized he hadn't had water since... since waking up. However long ago that was. An hour? More? His body was still functioning well — no headache, no dizziness — but the thirst was real, building.

*First you survive,* Grandmother's voice said. *Then you figure out the rest.*

Water, food, shelter. In that order. The shrine would have water, probably. Or he'd find the house. Either way, he'd manage.

"Sugitani."

The student in the back stirred, lifted his head, looked around with the dazed expression of someone surfacing from sleep. He didn't get up.

Two more stops.

The forest was thicker now, pressing close to the road. Cedar and cypress, dark green walls broken by the occasional splash of pink — wild cherry trees, blooming in the understory. The air through the bus's vents smelled different up here. Cooler. Cleaner. Mountain air.

"Miya-no-shita."

The second elderly woman rose, slower than the first. She nodded to the driver as she passed, murmured something polite. The door opened, closed. The bus pulled away.

One more stop.

Sheldon realized his heart was beating faster. Not fear, exactly. Anticipation. Or something like it. He was about to arrive somewhere — a place that his documents said was home, that a dead woman's letter said was waiting for him. He didn't know what he'd find there. Didn't know if the house existed, if the uncle was real, if any of the carefully constructed past would hold up to scrutiny.

But he was about to find out.

The bus rounded a curve. The forest opened. And there, ahead, Sheldon saw it:

A torii gate, vermillion red against the green, standing at the edge of the road. Beyond it, a gravel path leading into the trees. And beyond that, visible through the branches, the unmistakable shape of stone steps rising up the hillside.

"Jinja-mae," the driver announced. "Last stop."

Sheldon stood.


The air outside was noticeably cooler than it had been at the lower stop. Mountain air, thin and clean, carrying the scent of cedar and something else — incense, maybe, drifting down from above.

The bus pulled away behind him, its engine fading into the distance. Sheldon stood at the edge of the gravel and looked at the torii gate.

It was old. The paint was faded in places, weathered to a soft pink-red, but the structure itself was solid — massive wooden pillars sunk into stone bases, a curved crossbeam overhead. Characters were carved into a wooden plaque hanging from the beam:

柾木神社

*Masaki Jinja. Masaki Shrine.*

The farmer had been right. This was where the Tsukinos lived — near the shrine, he'd said. Sheldon still didn't know exactly where the house was, but this was the right area. This was where he was supposed to be.

He looked past the gate.

The stone steps began perhaps fifty meters ahead, rising into the forest. From here, he could only see the first dozen or so before they curved out of sight. But he could tell there were many more. The path climbed steeply, disappearing into green shadows.

Sheldon checked the sun. Still an hour or two of good light. Enough time to climb, to see what was at the top.

He walked toward the gate.


**SCENE 2 — END**

#### Scene 1.C: The 320 Steps

Scene: 3 — The 320 Steps
Date/Time: April 3, 1989, 4:35 PM – ~4:50 PM
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

ACTIVE LAWS FOR THIS SCENE:

☑ Law 3 (Pre-Scene Checkpoint): 8-point check
  - Abilities: Phase I — enhanced human, no supernatural powers
  - Physical: Thirst moderate (~2 hours without water), hunger mild, good stamina
  - Possessions: Wallet (all contents), ticket stub from bus
  - Location: Base of shrine steps → climbing → shrine grounds
  - Time: 4:35 PM start climb → ~4:50 PM arrival at top
  - Weather: Late afternoon, ~17°C (cooling), golden light, shadows lengthening
  - Knowledge: Knows shrine name, knows Tsukinos live nearby, has read letter. Does NOT know who lives at shrine.
  - Relationships: None active. About to meet Katsuhito and Tenchi.

☑ Law 8 (Period Authenticity): 1989 Japan
  - Tech check: Stone steps, wooden shrine buildings, traditional architecture ✓
  - Price check: N/A this scene
  - Culture check: Shrine etiquette (torii bow, approach behavior) — Sheldon has Kyoto experience ✓

☑ Law 9 (POV Discipline): Forbidden words

  - Post-scene audit: WILL RUN ✓
  - CRITICAL: Sheldon sees Funaho as "a large tree" — does NOT know it's special

  - Physical: 320 steps is significant exertion — should handle it WELL (restored stamina), note surprise

  - Back/knees: No pain climbing — note absence of expected discomfort

  - The shrine layout per OVA: torii, steps, main grounds, priest residence, lake beyond
  - Katsuhito's appearance: Elderly priest, ~80 apparent age, traditional robes

  - Scene ends: ~4:50 PM (15 minutes for 320 steps at moderate pace)

- Scene covers: Climb begins 4:35 PM, arrives top ~4:50 PM
- Match: YES ✓

POTENTIAL ERROR PATTERNS IDENTIFIED:

VERIFICATION COMPLETE — PROCEED TO SCENE

Sheldon paused at the torii gate.

The vermillion paint was faded but still vivid against the green of the forest. Up close, he could see the weathering — cracks in the wood, places where the color had worn to gray, moss climbing the stone bases. Old. Decades old, at least. Maybe centuries.

He knew the etiquette. The years in Kyoto had taught him that much. You bowed before passing through. You walked to the side, not the center — the center was for the kami. You approached with respect, regardless of what you believed.

Sheldon bowed. A small bow, the kind you gave to a stranger's house. Then he stepped through, keeping to the left, and walked toward the steps.


The path was gravel, well-maintained, crunching softly under his shoes. It led through a gap in the trees to where the stone steps began — a stairway climbing straight up the hillside, disappearing into the green.

He stopped at the base and looked up.

The steps were old. Worn smooth in the center where generations of feet had climbed. Some were cracked, others slightly tilted, but the overall structure was solid. Stone risers, maybe thirty centimeters each. He couldn't see the top from here — the stairway curved, or the forest blocked the view, or it simply went on too long.

*How many?*

He started climbing.


The first fifty steps were easy. His legs moved without complaint, his breathing stayed even. The late afternoon light filtered through the trees, golden and warm, casting long shadows across the stones.

Sheldon counted as he climbed. It was something to do with his mind while his body worked. Fifty-one. Fifty-two. Fifty-three.

At a hundred steps, he paused. Not because he needed to — his breathing was still steady, his legs still strong — but because he expected to need to. A hundred steps was significant. A hundred steps should have left him winded, should have set his knees complaining, should have reminded him that he was forty-seven years old and spent most of his time sitting in front of computers.

None of that happened.

He stood on the hundredth step and felt... fine. Good, even. His heart rate was elevated but not racing. His muscles were warm but not burning. His back — his treacherous back, the one that had forced him to buy a standing desk and see a chiropractor twice a month — was silent.

*Wrong,* some part of him whispered. *This is wrong.*

He kept climbing.


At two hundred steps, the trees began to thin. More light reached the stairway, and Sheldon could see further ahead — the steps continuing upward, curving slightly to the right, a flash of color at the top that might have been a building.

He was breathing harder now. Not gasping, not struggling, but definitely working. His thighs felt the effort. Sweat was beginning to prickle at his hairline.

*Good,* he thought. *Normal. This is what climbing two hundred steps should feel like.*

Except it wasn't. Not really. He remembered climbing the steps at Kiyomizu-dera during his Kyoto years, remembered how his lungs had burned and his calves had screamed by the halfway point. He'd been thirty then — younger than this body appeared to be — and in reasonable shape. It had still nearly killed him.

This was... manageable. Challenging, but manageable. As if his body had been tuned for efficiency, optimized for exactly this kind of sustained effort.

He filed the observation with the others and kept climbing.


Two hundred fifty. Two hundred seventy. Two hundred ninety.

The shrine was visible now. He could see the edge of a roof above the tree line, the distinctive curve of Japanese temple architecture. Another torii, smaller than the first, marked the top of the stairs. Stone lanterns flanked the final approach.

Three hundred.

Three hundred ten.

Three hundred fifteen.

Sheldon emerged from the stairway onto level ground, and the Masaki Shrine spread out before him.


It was beautiful.

The main building sat at the center of a cleared space, its wooden walls weathered to a soft gray, its roof tiles dark with age. Smaller structures flanked it — a worship hall, a bell tower, what might have been a storehouse. Stone paths connected them, lined with carefully raked gravel. Moss grew on the older stones, green and soft in the fading light.

And behind it all, dominating the grounds, stood a tree.

Sheldon had seen large trees before. The redwoods in California. The chestnuts in Georgia. But this was different — not taller, necessarily, but *older*. The trunk was massive, easily ten meters in diameter, its bark deeply furrowed. Branches spread outward like arms, casting shade over half the shrine grounds. The leaves were thick and dark, rustling softly in the evening breeze.

*Old,* he thought. *Very old. Hundreds of years, maybe more.*

It was just a tree. A big tree, ancient and impressive, but just a tree. Sheldon didn't know why he kept looking at it, why his eyes kept drifting back to its trunk, its branches, the way the light seemed to gather in its crown.

He pulled his gaze away. Looked around the grounds instead.

The shrine appeared empty. No visitors, no priests, no movement except the wind in the leaves. The shadows were lengthening quickly now — sunset was perhaps an hour away, and the mountains to the west were already blocking the direct sun.

Sheldon stood at the edge of the grounds and wondered what to do next.

He could explore. Look for the priest, ask about the Tsukino family, find out where the house was. That was the logical approach — gather information, establish his cover story, proceed with the plan.

Except he didn't have a plan. He had documents and money and a body that didn't belong to him, and he had a letter from a dead woman telling him to come home. He had a destination but no idea what to do once he arrived.

His throat was very dry. His stomach had moved past hunger into a hollow ache. He'd been awake for — how long? Two hours? Three? It felt like a lifetime.

*First you survive,* Grandmother's voice reminded him. *Then you figure out the rest.*

Water. He needed water.

Sheldon looked around the grounds again, searching for a well, a fountain, anything. Shrines usually had a temizu-ya, a water basin for purification. If he could find that—

"Excuse me."

The voice came from behind him. Sheldon turned.

An old man stood at the edge of the worship hall, watching him. He wore the white robe and dark hakama of a Shinto priest, and his face was lined with the kind of deep wrinkles that spoke of decades in the sun. His hair was gray, pulled back. His posture was straight despite his apparent age — eighty, maybe older.

But his eyes.

His eyes were sharp. Alert. They took in Sheldon with a single glance, assessing, and something in them flickered — recognition? Curiosity? Sheldon couldn't tell.

"You look tired," the priest said. His voice was calm, unhurried. "Would you like some water?"

Sheldon's throat tightened. Whether from thirst or something else, he couldn't say.

"Yes," he managed. "Please."

The old man nodded and turned toward one of the smaller buildings. "Come, then. I'll make tea."

And because he had no better plan, and because his body was demanding water with increasing urgency, and because there was something in the old priest's calm that felt almost like safety, Sheldon followed.


**SCENE 3 — END**

#### Scene 1.D: Tea With The Priest

- Scene 3 ended with Katsuhito offering water and saying "Come, then. I'll make tea."
- Scene 4 picks up immediately following him

BIBLE DETAILS FOR THIS SCENE:
- Katsuhito's appearance: Late 60s-70s (illusion), gray ponytail, round glasses, gray working clothes
- Katsuhito's assessment: Notes Sheldon's "wrong" energy signature, Lambda resonance
- Tenchi: 4 years old, playing with wooden blocks, curious about stranger
- Tenchi's appearance: ~100cm, bowl-cut black hair, yellow t-shirt, navy shorts, scrape on left knee

POTENTIAL ERROR PATTERNS:
- ⚠️ Making Katsuhito too mystical/knowing in dialogue (he's subtle, not obvious)
- ⚠️ Making Tenchi too articulate (he's 4 — simple sentences, many questions)
- ⚠️ Sheldon perceiving Katsuhito's assessment (Law 22 violation)

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
VERIFICATION COMPLETE — PROCEED TO SCENE
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════


The priest's residence was set back from the main shrine building, connected by a covered walkway. Traditional construction — dark wood, sliding doors, a narrow engawa running along the front. A pair of small white sneakers sat neatly beside the entrance, child-sized, with velcro straps.

The old man slid open the door and gestured inside. "Please."

Sheldon removed his shoes — muscle memory from Kyoto, the automatic action of entering a Japanese home — and stepped up onto the wooden floor. The interior was dim and cool, lit by the afternoon light filtering through paper screens. He caught the scent of tatami and something else, older, the accumulated years of a building that had stood for generations.

"This way." The priest led him through a short hallway into a room facing the courtyard. A low table sat at the center, surrounded by cushions. Through the open shoji, Sheldon could see the shrine grounds — the main building, the raked gravel, and beyond it all, the massive tree.

His eyes found it again without meaning to. Just a tree. He looked away.

"Please, sit." The priest indicated a cushion. "I'll prepare tea."

"Thank you." Sheldon's voice came out rough, his throat tight. He lowered himself onto the cushion, folding his legs beneath him in an approximation of seiza. His knees protested — 

No. They didn't.

He waited for the ache, the stiffness that came with sitting on hard floors, the familiar complaint of joints that had been complaining since his late thirties. It didn't come. His body folded into position with an ease that felt borrowed, as if he'd been practicing this posture his whole life.

*Wrong. Still wrong. Add it to the list.*


The priest moved around a small kitchen area visible through a doorway — the sound of water, the click of a gas stove, the quiet domesticity of tea preparation. Sheldon sat and tried to organize his thoughts.

He was in a shrine. He had climbed 320 steps — he'd counted — to reach it. An elderly priest had invited him in for tea. This was Japan, probably 1989, probably April. He had documents that said he was someone named Tsukino Sheldon, and a letter from a dead mother, and an uncle named Kiyoshi.

None of it made sense. All of it was happening anyway.

The priest returned carrying a wooden tray. On it sat a ceramic teapot, two cups, and a small plate of what looked like rice crackers. He set it on the table and lowered himself onto the opposite cushion with the practiced ease of someone who had been sitting on floors for decades.

"You climbed all 320 steps," the priest observed, pouring tea. Steam rose from the cups. "Most visitors take the road around the back."

"There's a road?"

The priest's lips twitched — not quite a smile, but something close. "There is. But you found the stairs." He slid a cup across the table. "Drink. You look like you need it."

Sheldon took the cup. The ceramic was warm against his palms, the heat seeping into his hands. He lifted it and drank.

The tea was hot and bitter and perfect. It hit his throat like water in a desert, spreading warmth through his chest. He hadn't realized how desperately thirsty he'd been until the relief came. He drained the cup in three long swallows and then sat there, embarrassed, holding the empty vessel.

The priest poured him another without comment.

"Thank you," Sheldon said. "I — thank you."

"You're welcome." The priest picked up his own cup, cradling it in both hands. "I am Masaki Katsuhito. I tend this shrine." A pause. "You are?"

"Tsukino." The name still felt strange on his tongue, a costume he hadn't learned to wear. "Tsukino Sheldon."

"Tsukino." Katsuhito's eyebrows rose slightly. "The family near here?"

"I don't know. Maybe. I'm trying to find them." Sheldon reached for the second cup, forced himself to drink more slowly. The tea was green, grassy, with a slight astringency that cut through the dust in his mouth. "My documents say I'm from this area. There's supposed to be a house. An uncle."

"Kiyoshi?"

Sheldon looked up sharply. "You know him?"

"I know of him. He moved to Osaka some years ago." Katsuhito sipped his tea, watching Sheldon over the rim. His eyes were calm, assessing, but not unkind. "The Tsukino house is about ten minutes' walk from here, down the mountain. It's been empty since Sachiko-san passed."

*Sachiko.* The name from the letter. The woman who had signed herself *Your mother*.

"You knew her?"

"Not well. She kept to herself in her last years. Illness." Katsuhito set down his cup. "You're her son?"

The question should have been simple. It wasn't.

"I..." Sheldon hesitated. *I don't know* was the honest answer, but it was also insane. He had documents. He had a koseki showing his birth, his parents' marriage, his grandmother's death. He had a letter in a dead woman's handwriting.

"Yes," he said finally. "I'm her son."

Katsuhito nodded slowly, as if the answer had told him something. What, Sheldon couldn't guess.


A sound from outside — footsteps on wood, quick and light. The shoji slid open and a small figure appeared in the doorway.

"Grandfather, who—"

The child stopped. Stared.

Sheldon stared back.

The boy was perhaps four years old, small and compact, with black hair cut in a simple bowl shape that stuck up slightly in the back. He wore a yellow t-shirt with some cartoon character Sheldon didn't recognize and navy blue shorts. His feet were bare, and there was a scrape on his left knee, the kind children collected by the dozen.

His eyes were wide. Dark brown, round with childhood, fixed on Sheldon's face with undisguised curiosity.

"Grandfather," the boy said, not looking away from Sheldon, "his face is different."

"Tenchi." Katsuhito's voice was gentle but firm. "That's not polite."

"But it is." The boy — Tenchi — took a step into the room, head tilted. "Why is it different?"

Sheldon felt something loosen in his chest. Children were honest. Children didn't dissemble or play social games they didn't understand. This boy was looking at him and seeing something unusual, and he was saying so, and there was nothing mean in it. Just curiosity.

"I'm from America," Sheldon said. "People there look different."

"America." Tenchi tested the word. "That's far away."

"Very far."

"Why did you come here?"

It was a good question. Sheldon didn't have an answer — not one that made sense, not one he could explain to a four-year-old, not one he could explain to himself.

"I'm looking for my family's house," he said. "Your grandfather is helping me."

Tenchi considered this. Then, apparently satisfied, he padded across the tatami and sat down next to Katsuhito, folding his legs with the unselfconscious ease of childhood. He reached for a rice cracker and began eating it, still watching Sheldon.

"Tenchi, this is Tsukino Sheldon-san," Katsuhito said. "He'll be staying in the area."

"Sheldon-san," Tenchi repeated carefully. "That's a hard name."

"You can call me Sheldon. Just Sheldon."

"Sheldon." The boy nodded, as if filing this away. "I'm Tenchi. I'm four." He held up four fingers to demonstrate. "I'll be five soon."

"Tenchi," Katsuhito said mildly, "let Sheldon-san finish his tea."

"He can talk and drink," Tenchi said reasonably. "I can do both at the same time. Watch." He took another bite of cracker and said, through a full mouth, "See?"

"Tenchi."

The boy subsided, but his eyes stayed on Sheldon, bright with questions held temporarily in reserve.


Sheldon drank his tea and ate two rice crackers that Katsuhito pressed on him, and slowly, piece by piece, the world began to feel slightly more real. His stomach stopped aching. His throat stopped burning. The late afternoon light slanted golden through the shoji, warming the tatami, and for a few minutes he allowed himself to simply exist in this room with this old priest and this curious child, suspended between the impossible past and the unknown future.

Katsuhito asked questions — where he'd studied, how long he'd been in Japan, what work he'd done. Sheldon answered as truthfully as he could, which meant half-truths and evasions, fragments of a life that had been Sheldon Carter's rather than Sheldon Tsukino's. Physics. Kyoto. Research.

If Katsuhito noticed the gaps, he didn't press.

"The house has been empty for four years," the priest said eventually. "Kiyoshi hired someone to check on it when he left, but I don't know what state it's in now. The village might have information."

"I'll find it tomorrow." The sun was getting lower. Sheldon could feel exhaustion pulling at him, the accumulated weight of a day that had started with dying. "Is there an inn nearby? Somewhere I could stay tonight?"

Katsuhito was quiet for a moment. His eyes moved to Tenchi, who had given up on staying still and was now examining Sheldon's shoes with great interest, then back to Sheldon.

"There's a room here," he said. "For guests. It's simple, but you're welcome to it."

Sheldon opened his mouth to refuse — politeness, automatic, the instinct to not impose. But he was tired. So tired. And there was something about this place, this shrine, this quiet room with its view of the ancient tree, that made him want to stay.

*Careful,* some part of him whispered. *You don't know these people. You don't know anything.*

But he was tired. And he had nowhere else to go.

"Thank you," Sheldon said. "I'd be grateful."

Katsuhito nodded, and something in his expression shifted — satisfaction, perhaps, or something more complicated.

"Then it's settled." He rose smoothly, joints apparently unbothered by the hard floor. "I'll show you the room. Tenchi, help me prepare dinner."

"Can Sheldon-san eat with us?"

"If he'd like."

"Do you like rice?" Tenchi asked Sheldon directly. "Grandfather makes good rice. And pickles. I don't like the pickles but he says I have to eat them anyway."

"I like rice," Sheldon said.

"Good." Tenchi nodded seriously. "That's good."

And Sheldon, despite everything — despite the impossibility of his situation, despite the terror lurking at the edges of his mind, despite not knowing who he was or how he'd gotten here or what any of it meant — found himself almost smiling.


**SCENE 4 — END**

- Knowledge: Learned Katsuhito's name; learned about Tsukino house (empty, 10 min walk); learned uncle Kiyoshi moved to Osaka; will stay at shrine tonight

#### Scene 1.E: Dinner And Darkness

## Part 1: Dinner

The kitchen was small and practical — a gas stove, a rice cooker with a dented lid, a refrigerator that hummed in the corner. Katsuhito moved through it with the economy of long habit, setting water to boil, pulling vegetables from a basket, sliding fish from paper wrapping onto a cutting board.

"Can I help?" Sheldon asked.

Katsuhito glanced at him. "You're a guest."

"I'd like to help."

A pause. Then: "The daikon needs washing. Sink is there."

Sheldon washed the daikon. It was a simple task — cold water, scrubbing away the dirt, the pale root emerging clean beneath his hands. His grandmother had grown daikon in her garden in Georgia, a small rebellion of Japanese vegetables among the collards and tomatoes. He hadn't thought about that in years.

Tenchi appeared at his elbow, standing on tiptoes to see what he was doing. "That's a big one."

"It is."

"Grandfather makes it into soup. Or pickles." The boy made a face. "I don't like pickles."

"You said that."

"I'm saying it again so you remember."

Sheldon found himself almost smiling. Again. That kept happening around this child — the corners of his mouth wanting to lift despite everything, despite the impossibility of his situation, despite the terror that lurked at the edges of his mind. Tenchi was four years old and earnest and had no idea that the stranger in his kitchen had died that morning and didn't understand why he was alive.

*Keep it together. You can fall apart later.*


Dinner was rice, miso soup, grilled mackerel, and pickled vegetables. They sat at the low table in the main room, the shoji open to the evening air. The sun had dropped behind the mountains, leaving the sky streaked with orange and purple. Somewhere outside, a bird was calling — not the uguisu, something else, a longer melody that rose and fell in the gathering dusk.

Katsuhito said a brief blessing. Tenchi echoed it, hands pressed together, eyes squeezed shut with exaggerated concentration. Then they ate.

The fish was good. Simple, salted, charred at the edges. The rice was perfect — each grain distinct, slightly sticky, the way Sheldon's grandmother had taught him to make it. The miso was rich with dashi, warming his stomach, spreading comfort through his tired body.

He ate more than he meant to. Three bowls of rice. Two servings of fish. Everything except the pickles, which Tenchi watched him refuse with visible satisfaction.

"See?" the boy said to Katsuhito. "Sheldon doesn't like them either."

"Sheldon-*san*," Katsuhito corrected mildly. "And guests are polite about food."

"I'm not a guest."

"Then you can eat your pickles."

Tenchi scowled but ate his pickles, one reluctant piece at a time, watching Sheldon as if expecting solidarity. Sheldon kept his expression neutral and took another bite of fish.


After dinner, Katsuhito cleared the dishes while Tenchi showed Sheldon his wooden blocks. They were old, hand-carved, the edges worn smooth from years of small hands. Tenchi had built a structure that was either a castle or a shrine — he couldn't decide which — and explained its features in exhaustive detail.

"This is where the king lives. Or the priest. And this is the tower for watching bad guys. And this is where they keep the food so the king doesn't get hungry."

"That's important," Sheldon agreed. "Kings need food."

"Everyone needs food." Tenchi stacked another block on top, wobbling the whole structure. "Do you have a castle?"

"No."

"A house?"

The question caught Sheldon off guard. Did he? His documents said there was a house — the Tsukino house, ten minutes down the mountain, empty for four years. But that wasn't *his* house. His house had been a two-bedroom apartment in Pasadena, cluttered with physics journals and take-out containers, the place where he'd lived alone since the divorce.

"I might," he said finally. "I'm not sure yet."

"How can you not be sure if you have a house?"

"It's complicated."

Tenchi considered this. "Grown-up things are always complicated," he said, with the weary wisdom of a four-year-old. "That's what grandfather says."

"Your grandfather is right."


At seven-thirty, Katsuhito announced that it was time for Tenchi's bath. The boy protested — he wanted to show Sheldon more blocks, he wasn't tired, he had important things to say — but Katsuhito was immovable, guiding him toward the bath with gentle firmness.

"Say goodnight to Sheldon-san."

Tenchi turned at the doorway. "Will you be here tomorrow?"

Sheldon looked at Katsuhito, who gave a small nod.

"Yes," Sheldon said. "I'll be here."

"Good." Tenchi smiled — a sudden, bright expression that transformed his whole face. "I'll show you the big tree tomorrow. It's really, really big. Grandfather says it's older than everything."

"I'd like that."

"Goodnight, Sheldon."

"Goodnight, Tenchi."

The boy disappeared into the hallway, and Sheldon was alone.


## Part 2: The Guest Room

The room Katsuhito showed him was small, spare, and clean. A futon had been laid out on the tatami, topped with a thick comforter. A low table sat against one wall, bearing a ceramic lamp that cast warm light across the ceiling. A window looked out onto the shrine grounds, the glass dark now, reflecting the lamp's glow.

"The bathroom is at the end of the hall," Katsuhito said. "There should be a clean towel. If you need anything..." He paused. "I'm an early riser. Breakfast is at six-thirty."

"Thank you." The words felt inadequate. "For everything. The tea, the dinner, the room. You didn't have to—"

"You looked like you needed it."

It wasn't a question, and it wasn't an invitation to explain. Just a statement of fact, delivered with the same calm that seemed to be Katsuhito's default mode. The old priest had watched Sheldon all evening — not obviously, not intrusively, but consistently, those sharp eyes taking in details Sheldon couldn't guess at.

"Sleep well," Katsuhito said. "Tomorrow will be a long day."

Then he was gone, the shoji sliding shut behind him, and Sheldon was alone.


## Part 3: Darkness

He didn't turn off the lamp.

He sat on the futon, back against the wall, legs stretched out before him, and stared at nothing. The room was quiet. The shrine was quiet. Somewhere in the distance, he could hear Katsuhito's voice, muffled — probably putting Tenchi to bed, reading a story, doing the ordinary things that ordinary grandparents did with ordinary children.

Sheldon was not ordinary. He never had been. But this — *this* — was something else entirely.

He had died.

He let the thought sit there, heavy and undeniable. He had been in the containment lab. The field had collapsed. The light had come. And he had ended — not gradually, not with time to prepare, but instantly, completely, between one heartbeat and the nothing that followed.

He remembered it. That was the worst part. He remembered dying, and he remembered what came after: the darkness, the absence, the void that wasn't sleep because sleep implied waking. He had been *gone*. Utterly, irrevocably gone.

And then he'd woken up in a field in Japan with cherry blossoms in his hair and a body that wasn't his.


The wallet sat on the table beside the lamp. Sheldon reached for it, opened it, spread the contents across the futon.

The ID card with his face and a stranger's history. The koseki showing a family tree he'd never climbed. The bankbooks proving access to money he'd never earned. The photograph — he hadn't looked at it closely before. He looked now.

Three people stood in front of a modest house, mountains visible behind them. A man in work clothes, unsmiling but not unkind. A woman in a floral dress, her hair pulled back, her expression gentle. Between them, a boy — maybe six or seven, thin and serious, looking at the camera with a directness that was almost challenging.

Was that him? Was that supposed to be him?

The boy in the photograph was Japanese. Both parents were Japanese. Sheldon was Black — had been Black his entire life, born in Atlanta to a Black father and a Japanese mother, growing up in the strange borderland between two communities that didn't always know what to make of him.

But this photograph showed a family that didn't include him. Couldn't include him. The boy's face was too different, his features too—

Wait.

Sheldon looked at the photograph again. Then he looked at the ID card. Then he held both up to the light.

The man on the ID card was Black. Light-skinned, mixed, but unmistakably Black. The bone structure, the nose, the hair texture — all of it screamed the same heritage Sheldon had carried his whole life.

But the boy in the photograph...

*What the hell?*


He stared at the two images for a long time, trying to make them fit. They didn't. They couldn't. A Japanese family couldn't produce a Black son. The genetics didn't work that way. Whatever had created his documents had made a mistake — an obvious, glaring mistake that anyone who looked would notice.

Unless they weren't meant to be looked at together. Unless the photograph was from *before*, and the ID was from *after*, and something had changed between them.

*Changed you,* a voice whispered. *Changed everything.*

Sheldon put the documents away. His hands were shaking.


He didn't sleep. Not really.

He lay on the futon with the lamp still on, watching shadows on the ceiling, his mind running in circles. The containment breach. The light. The field. The body. The documents. The photograph. The boy who wasn't him. The man who was.

None of it added up. None of it made sense. Someone had gone to extraordinary lengths to build him a life — a past, a family, a place in this world — and they'd done it imperfectly, leaving seams that showed if you looked hard enough.

*Why?*

The question had been following him all day. It was no closer to an answer now than it had been in the field.

Outside, the night was deep and quiet. No cars, no sirens, no hum of traffic. Just the wind in the trees and the distant sound of water — a spring, maybe, or a stream — and occasionally a bird calling out across the darkness.

His grandmother would have called this peaceful. She had grown up in a village not so different from this one, before the war, before America, before everything changed. She had told him stories about nights like this — the silence so complete you could hear your own heartbeat, the stars so bright they cast shadows.

*You would have understood,* he thought. *You always understood the strange things. The things that didn't fit.*

His grandmother had believed in spirits. In ancestors who watched over the living. In the idea that death wasn't an ending but a doorway.

Sheldon had never believed. He was a physicist. He dealt in equations and evidence, in things that could be measured and proven. Spirits and doorways and ancestors — those were stories, comforting fictions to soften the hard truth of entropy and dissolution.

But he had died. And he was here. And there was no equation that explained it.


At some point, exhaustion won.

Not sleep, exactly — his mind kept churning, processing, refusing to quiet — but something close to it. A drifting state where thoughts became tangled and the boundaries between memory and imagination blurred.

He dreamed of light. Not the harsh light of the containment breach, but something softer — silver, like moonlight, like starlight, like something that had no name. It wrapped around him, warm and patient, and a voice that wasn't a voice said:

*You're safe now.*

He woke to darkness, the lamp having finally burned out, and for a moment he didn't know where he was. Then it came back — the shrine, the guest room, the impossible day — and he lay there in the dark, heart pounding, until the gray light of dawn began to seep through the window.

Day two.

He had survived day one. He didn't know what day two would bring, but he had survived.

For now, that would have to be enough.


**SCENE 5 — END**

## Part 1: Waking

Gray light. Bird calls. The creak of old wood somewhere in the house.

Sheldon opened his eyes to an unfamiliar ceiling — dark beams, plastered white between them, shadows gathered in the corners. For a long moment he didn't move, his mind struggling to bridge the gap between sleep and waking, between the silver light of his dreams and the pale reality of dawn.

*Japan. Shrine. Guest room.*

It came back in pieces. The field. The bus. The 320 steps. Katsuhito and Tenchi. Dinner. The photograph.

The photograph.

He sat up. His body moved easily — no stiffness, no aching joints, none of the morning complaints that had been his constant companions for the last decade. That wrongness again, that persistent reminder that this body wasn't his, that he was wearing borrowed flesh like borrowed clothes.

The lamp had burned out sometime in the night. Sheldon's wallet sat on the low table where he'd left it, the documents still inside, the photograph with its impossible family tucked between his ID and his cash.

He didn't reach for it. Not yet. Some problems were too large for dawn.


The bathroom was at the end of the hall, as Katsuhito had said. Sheldon used the toilet, splashed water on his face, studied himself in the small mirror above the sink.

The face looking back was still wrong. Still young. Still his and not his. The eyes were the same — he recognized his own eyes, his grandmother's eyes, the shape and color unchanged despite everything else. But the skin was smoother, the jaw firmer, the gray that had crept into his temples gone as if it had never been.

*Thirty,* he thought. *I look thirty.*

He had been thirty once, a lifetime ago. He remembered it vaguely — the post-doc years at Berkeley, the failed marriage already crumbling, the work that had consumed everything else. He hadn't liked being thirty. Too young to be taken seriously, too old to be forgiven for his failures.

But this thirty was different. This was a body without history, without scars, without the accumulated damage of years. A fresh start in the most literal sense possible.

*Who did this to me? Why?*

The questions had no answers. He dried his face and went to find breakfast.


## Part 2: Breakfast

The kitchen was already occupied. Katsuhito stood at the stove, stirring something in a pot. The smell of miso filled the small space, rich and familiar, mixing with the sharper scent of grilling fish.

"Good morning," Katsuhito said without turning. "Did you sleep?"

"Some." It wasn't quite a lie.

"Tea is ready. Rice will be a few minutes."

Sheldon poured himself tea from the pot on the table. The ceramic was warm, the liquid bitter and bracing. He drank and felt the fog in his head begin to lift.

Footsteps in the hallway — quick, light, the unmistakable rhythm of a child. Tenchi appeared in the doorway, already dressed in what Sheldon recognized as a school uniform: white shirt, navy shorts, a yellow cap clutched in one hand.

"You're still here!" The boy's face lit up. "Grandfather said you might leave."

"Did he?"

"He said sometimes people leave in the night. But you didn't."

"I didn't."

Tenchi nodded seriously, as if this confirmed something important. Then he climbed onto a cushion at the table and began arranging his chopsticks with exaggerated care.

"I have school today," he announced. "Kindergarten. The bus comes at eight-thirty. I know all my letters. Do you want to hear them?"

"After breakfast," Katsuhito said, setting plates on the table. "Eat first."

Breakfast was rice, miso soup, a small grilled fish, and pickled vegetables. Tenchi ate quickly, stealing glances at Sheldon between bites, clearly bursting with questions he'd been told to hold. Katsuhito ate slowly, methodically, his attention seemingly on his food but his awareness — Sheldon could feel it — extending to everything in the room.

"After Tenchi catches his bus," Katsuhito said, "I thought we might walk to the Tsukino house. If you're ready."

Sheldon's chopsticks paused over his rice. The house. His supposed inheritance. The place where a family he'd never known had lived and died.

"Yes," he said. "I'd like that."


## Part 3: The Bus

At eight-twenty, Tenchi was vibrating.

"The bus is coming," he said for the fourth time, pressing his face against the window. "I can't see it yet but I know it's coming."

"Then you should go down to meet it," Katsuhito said.

"Can Sheldon watch?"

Sheldon looked at Katsuhito, who nodded slightly.

"I'll watch from here," Sheldon said.

They walked out to the engawa — the wooden veranda that wrapped around the residence. From here, Sheldon could see the shrine grounds spreading out before him: the main building, the raked gravel, the massive tree that dominated everything. In the morning light, the tree looked different — more solid somehow, more present, its leaves catching the sun and holding it.

He made himself look away.

A path led down from the shrine grounds, disappearing into the trees — a shortcut, Sheldon guessed, steeper than the main stairs but faster for small legs that knew the way. Tenchi pulled on his yellow cap, adjusted his backpack, and turned to face them.

"I'll be back at two-forty-five," he said. "That's when the bus brings us home. Will you still be here?"

"I'll be here," Sheldon said.

"Promise?"

The word caught in Sheldon's throat. He had made very few promises in his life, and he'd broken most of them. But this was a four-year-old asking if the stranger who'd appeared in his life would still exist when he got home from school.

"Promise," Sheldon said.

Tenchi smiled — that sudden, transforming expression — and then he was gone, darting down the path with the heedless speed of childhood, his yellow cap bobbing until the trees swallowed him.


## Part 4: The Walk

"He likes you," Katsuhito observed.

They were standing at the edge of the shrine grounds, looking down the path Tenchi had taken. In the distance, Sheldon could hear the sound of an engine — the kindergarten bus, probably, making its rounds through the mountain roads.

"He doesn't know me."

"Children often see more clearly than adults." Katsuhito turned toward a small shed near the residence. "The house is about fifteen minutes' walk. Do you need a walking stick?"

Sheldon almost said no — his body felt fine, strong, capable of any walk — but something in Katsuhito's tone suggested this was a test. Or perhaps just practical wisdom from someone who knew the paths.

"If you have one to spare."

Katsuhito retrieved two walking sticks from the shed, both well-worn, both clearly handmade. He offered one to Sheldon and kept the other, and together they set off down a different path than the one Tenchi had taken — this one curving around the back of the shrine, descending through a forest of cedar and cypress.

The morning was cool but warming. Sunlight filtered through the canopy in shafts and patches, and birds called from the branches overhead. Sheldon recognized the uguisu — that three-note song, *ho-hokekyo*, that had followed him since the field. It felt almost like a companion now, a thread of continuity in a world that had lost all other anchors.

"You speak Japanese very well," Katsuhito said after a few minutes of walking. "Where did you study?"

"Kyoto. Twelve years ago." *In another life. In another body.* "I was there for graduate research."

"And then?"

"California. Work. Life." The words came out flat, inadequate. How could he explain the years that followed — the career, the divorce, the slow narrowing of his world until it contained nothing but equations and empty apartments?

"But you came back."

"Apparently."

Katsuhito glanced at him — a quick, assessing look that revealed nothing. "Family calls us home sometimes. Even when we don't expect it."

*Family.* The word felt strange. Sheldon had family — had *had* family. His grandmother, dead these fifteen years. His parents, gone even longer. A brother somewhere in Chicago who he hadn't spoken to since their mother's funeral. Those were real. Those had happened.

But Sachiko Tsukino, who had died in 1985, who had written him a letter full of love and grief, who had left him a house and land and a place in this village — she wasn't family. She couldn't be. He had never met her, never known her, never existed in the same world she'd inhabited.

And yet her letter was in his wallet, and her house was ahead of him, and her son — supposedly — was walking through a forest in Japan trying to understand why.


## Part 5: The House

The Tsukino house sat at the bottom of a small valley, surrounded by what had once been cultivated land. Now the fields were overgrown, knee-high with grass and weeds, the irrigation channels choked with debris. A path led from the forest to the house, barely visible under the vegetation.

The house itself was small — two rooms, maybe three, traditional construction with a tiled roof and wooden walls. The engawa sagged visibly on the left side, several boards missing or broken. Tiles had slipped from the roof, leaving gaps that would let in rain. The shoji screens were torn, the paper hanging in strips.

But it was still standing. Still whole, in its way. A house that had been waiting.

Katsuhito stopped at the edge of the property. "I'll wait here. Take your time."

Sheldon walked forward alone.

The garden had been allowed to run wild. What might have been vegetable plots were now tangles of volunteer growth — something that might be daikon, gone to seed; tomato plants sprawling unsupported; what looked like the remains of a cucumber trellis, collapsed and overgrown. Near the house, a small shed had partially caved in, its roof timbers visible through the gap.

He climbed the three steps to the engawa, testing each board before putting his weight on it. The wood creaked but held. The front door was locked — a simple wooden lock, the kind that could be opened with any flat implement — but he didn't try it. He walked around the engawa instead, peering through the gaps in the shoji, trying to see inside.

Tatami floors, covered in dust. A low table, still in place. A scroll on the wall, faded beyond reading. In one room, a futon had been left out — four years ago, perhaps, when Sachiko had last slept here. Or longer, if someone had come to close up the house after she died.

*Home,* something whispered. *This could be home.*

He didn't know if the voice was his own or something else. It didn't matter. The thought was true regardless of its source.


## Part 6: The Decision

He found Katsuhito where he'd left him, waiting at the forest's edge with the patience of someone who had all the time in the world.

"The house needs work," Sheldon said.

"Yes."

"The roof needs retiling. The engawa needs rebuilding. The screens need replacing." He was listing them out, organizing the chaos into manageable pieces, the way he'd always organized problems. "The garden is overgrown but the soil looks good. The structure itself seems sound."

"You know something about construction?"

"No." *Not in this life.* "But I could learn."

Katsuhito studied him for a long moment. Those sharp eyes, seeing things Sheldon couldn't guess at.

"It will cost money," the old priest said. "Labor, materials, tools."

"I have some money." The bankbooks in his wallet. Eight hundred thousand yen, give or take. Not a fortune, but not nothing either.

"It will take time."

"I have time."

"You'll need help. The village has carpenters, craftsmen. They'll want to know who you are."

Sheldon thought about that. Who was he? A dead physicist in a borrowed body? A son who had never been born? A stranger with documents that said he belonged here even though he knew he didn't?

"I'm Tsukino Sheldon," he said finally. "Sachiko's son. I've come home."

The words felt like a choice. Like something settling into place, a door closing behind him even as another opened ahead.

Katsuhito nodded slowly. "Then we should talk to Yamada-san. He does roofing. And Suzuki at the lumber yard — he'll give you fair prices if I vouch for you." A pause. "It won't be easy. Foreigners aren't common here. There will be questions."

"I know."

"And you're staying anyway."

It wasn't a question, but Sheldon answered it as if it were.

"Yes. I'm staying."

The walk back to the shrine was quieter than the walk down. The forest pressed close around them, green and alive, and the birds sang in the branches, and the morning sun climbed higher through the trees. Sheldon's mind was already working on lists — materials needed, skills to acquire, people to meet, a house to make habitable.

He had a place now. A purpose. It wasn't much — just a decaying house in a rural village, a mystery he couldn't solve, a life that wasn't his. But it was more than he'd had yesterday.

*First you survive,* Grandmother's voice said, warm and approving. *Then you build.*

He was going to build.


**SCENE 6 — END**

**Word Count:** ~2,400 words

**Time Elapsed (Story):** ~4.5 hours (5:30 AM to ~10:00 AM)

**Sheldon's Current State:**
- Physical: Sleep-deprived but functional; body handled walk easily
- Emotional: Resolved; made a decision; has a purpose now
- Knowledge: Has seen house condition; knows approximate repair costs; committed to staying

**Key Beat:**
- **THE DECISION:** Sheldon commits to staying and rebuilding the Tsukino house. This is the first major choice he makes in his new life.

**Foreshadowing Planted:**
- "Children often see more clearly" — Tenchi perceives something adults don't
- Uguisu as companion/continuity thread
- "Home" voice — his own? Or something else?
- Katsuhito vouching for Sheldon with villagers — building network

- Vibrating with held questions (established pattern)

**Next Scene:** Day 2 continues — work begins (dishes, sweeping, Tenchi returns)

#### Scene 2.B: Work

- Tenchi: Would be overjoyed Sheldon kept promise; want to help/be near him
