## Part 1: Dishes

Back at the shrine, Sheldon looked for something to do.

The kitchen had dishes from breakfast — bowls, chopsticks, the pot that had held miso soup. Katsuhito was somewhere else, doing whatever it was priests did in the mid-morning, and Sheldon stood in the small kitchen feeling useless.

He hated feeling useless.

The sink was a deep ceramic basin with a hand pump mounted on one side. Sheldon tried it — stiff resistance, then nothing. He tried again, putting more weight into it. A dribble of water. Third time, a proper stream.

*Three pulls,* he noted. *Primes on the third pull.*

He washed the dishes. It wasn't complicated — rinse, scrub with the brush he found in a dish beside the sink, rinse again, set to dry on the wooden rack. The water was cold, drawn from a well somewhere below the kitchen. The soap was a rough bar that smelled of lye and something floral.

His grandmother had washed dishes this way. Not with a pump — she'd had running water by the time he knew her — but with the same economy of motion, the same quiet attention to the task. She'd taught him that dishes weren't just dishes. They were respect for the food that had been served, for the hands that had prepared it, for the home that held them all.

*You're never too important for honest work,* she'd said. *The moment you think you are, you've lost something you can't get back.*

He finished the dishes and dried his hands on a rough towel hanging by the stove. The kitchen was clean now, or cleaner than it had been. A small thing. But it was something.


## Part 2: The Courtyard

He found Katsuhito sweeping the courtyard.

The old priest moved with the same unhurried rhythm Sheldon had noticed at breakfast — steady, methodical, each stroke of the bamboo broom pushing gravel into neat lines. The pattern that emerged was intentional, Sheldon realized. Not just cleaning but arranging, creating order from randomness.

"I finished the dishes," Sheldon said.

Katsuhito didn't stop sweeping. "Thank you."

"Is there something else I can do?"

Now the priest paused, looking at Sheldon with those sharp, assessing eyes. "You don't have to work. You're a guest."

"I'd rather work."

A long moment. Something shifted in Katsuhito's expression — not approval exactly, but something adjacent to it. He gestured toward the shed where he'd gotten the walking sticks that morning.

"There's another broom. The courtyard is large."


The courtyard was large.

Sheldon swept. The broom was similar to Katsuhito's — bamboo handle, soft bristles, lighter than it looked. The motion came back to him without thought: long strokes, let the bristles do the work, keep the line even. His grandmother again. The church parking lot on Saturday mornings, preparing for Sunday services, the old women sweeping and gossiping while he trailed behind with his child-sized broom.

*Physical work is prayer,* she'd told him. *When your hands are busy, your soul can rest.*

He hadn't believed that then. He'd been a teenager, impatient with anything that couldn't be solved by thinking harder. But now, decades later — or was it decades? time had become strange — he understood what she meant. There was something meditative in the repetition, something settling. His mind, which had been racing since he woke up in the field, began to slow.

Sweep. Step. Sweep. Step.

The gravel made satisfying sounds under the bristles. The sun climbed higher, warming his back. His shoulders found a rhythm that didn't tire, his legs carried him across the courtyard without complaint. The body he was wearing — this young, restored, borrowed body — was built for this kind of work. It responded to exertion with efficiency rather than protest.

He swept for two hours. When he stopped, the courtyard was clean, the gravel raked into the same careful pattern Katsuhito had begun, and his shirt was damp with sweat but his muscles felt loose rather than sore.

Katsuhito was watching him from the engawa.

"You've done this before," the priest said.

"My grandmother. Church grounds." Sheldon leaned the broom against his shoulder. "Saturday mornings."

"She taught you well."

It wasn't a question, and Sheldon didn't treat it as one. He simply nodded and waited.

"Tea," Katsuhito said. "Then we'll see about the garden."


## Part 3: Tea

They sat on the engawa, legs hanging over the edge, cups of green tea warming their hands. The courtyard spread out before them, freshly swept, the gravel catching the early afternoon light. Beyond it, the main shrine building stood quiet and solid, and beyond that—

The tree.

Sheldon's eyes found it without meaning to. They kept doing that — drifting toward the massive trunk, the spreading branches, the dark leaves that seemed to hold the light rather than reflect it. It was just a tree. He knew it was just a tree. But his attention kept returning to it, the way a compass needle returns to north.

"The tree is old," Katsuhito said, following his gaze.

"How old?"

"Very." The priest sipped his tea. "It was here when my ancestors built the shrine. It will be here when my descendants tend it."

*Ancestors. Descendants.* The words carried weight Sheldon couldn't parse. He thought of Tenchi — the boy who called this man grandfather, who would someday inherit this shrine, this tree, this quiet life among the mountains. A line stretching backward and forward through time, anchored by this place.

Sheldon had no such line. His family had been scattered by history — war, migration, the slow attrition of generations that didn't reproduce. His brother had no children. His ex-wife had remarried someone else. His parents' line ended with him.

Or had ended with him. Before.

"You're thinking," Katsuhito observed.

"I think too much. It's a habit."

"Habits can be changed."

"Can they?"

The priest didn't answer. He finished his tea, set the cup aside, and rose with the fluid motion of someone half his apparent age.

"The garden needs weeding. Tomorrow, if you're staying, we can talk about what comes next."


## Part 4: The Garden

The garden behind the residence was smaller than the courtyard but more complex. Vegetable plots in raised beds, separated by narrow paths. A tool shed. A compost pile. The remnants of last year's crops — withered tomato stakes, tangled bean frames — mixed with the new growth of spring weeds.

Katsuhito handed him a pair of worn gloves and a hand trowel.

"Pull the weeds, not the vegetables. If you're not sure, ask."

Then he left. Back to the shrine, to whatever duties occupied a priest's afternoon, leaving Sheldon alone with the soil and the sun and the steady work of clearing.

It was harder than sweeping. The weeds had roots, some of them deep, and the ground was packed from winter. Sheldon knelt in the dirt and dug, pulled, shook loose soil from roots, added the weeds to a growing pile. His knees didn't complain about the kneeling. His back didn't seize when he bent and straightened. The body just... worked.

*Wrong,* the familiar voice whispered. *Still wrong.*

But useful. Whatever had been done to him, however impossible this body was, it was letting him do things his old body couldn't have managed. Two hours of sweeping followed by an afternoon of weeding — his forty-seven-year-old self would have been crippled by now. This version of him just kept going.

He found a rhythm. Pull, shake, pile. Pull, shake, pile. The sun tracked across the sky, shadows shifting. Birds called in the trees. Occasionally he heard sounds from the shrine — Katsuhito's footsteps, the distant ring of a bell, the creak of old wood — but mostly there was just the garden and the work and the slow, meditative emptiness of physical labor.

His grandmother would have approved.


## Part 5: Return

"You're still here!"

The voice came from behind him. Sheldon turned, hands dirty, knees grass-stained, and found Tenchi standing at the edge of the garden, yellow cap askew, backpack hanging half-off one shoulder, face split in a grin.

"I'm still here."

"You promised and you stayed!" The boy's delight was so complete, so unguarded, that Sheldon felt something shift in his chest. "Grandfather said maybe you'd be here and maybe you wouldn't and I said you promised and promises are important and you DID stay!"

"I did."

Tenchi dropped his backpack on the path and came closer, examining the garden with the serious attention of a four-year-old.

"You're pulling weeds."

"Yes."

"I can help. I know which ones are weeds. That one's a weed." He pointed at something green. "And that one. But that one's daikon. You can't pull that."

"Thank you. I wasn't sure."

Tenchi nodded gravely, then sat down on the path, apparently planning to supervise. His uniform was dusty — evidence of a day's worth of kindergarten activities — and there was something sticky on his cheek, but his eyes were bright and fixed on Sheldon with a focus that felt almost physical.

"My teacher asked where I'm from," Tenchi said. "I said here. She said everyone's from somewhere else before here. Is that true?"

"Sometimes." Sheldon pulled another weed, added it to the pile. "People move around. They come from one place and go to another."

"Where did you come from?"

*California. Atlanta. Berkeley. A containment lab in Pasadena. A life that doesn't exist anymore.*

"America," he said. "A long way away."

"Why did you leave?"

"I..." He paused, trowel in hand, unsure how to answer. Why had he left? He hadn't left. He'd died. He'd been brought here against his will — or without his consent, at least. Nothing about his presence in Japan was a choice he'd made.

Except staying. That was a choice. He'd made it this morning, standing in front of the house that wasn't his, looking at a life that had been built for him by someone or something he didn't understand.

"I came to find my family," he said finally. It was true enough. "My mother's family. They lived here, before."

"But they're gone now?"

"Yes. They're gone now."

Tenchi considered this, his small face serious. "Grandfather says people who go away are still with us. Just different. Like they're far away but also here." He touched his chest, over his heart. "In here."

Sheldon's throat tightened. "Your grandfather is wise."

"He's old." Tenchi said it matter-of-factly. "Old people know things. Do you know things? You're not as old as grandfather but you're older than me."

"I know some things."

"Like what?"

*Like the quantum properties of confined electromagnetic fields. Like the taste of failure at 3 AM in an empty lab. Like what it feels like to die.*

"I know how to pull weeds," Sheldon said. "Do you want to help?"

Tenchi's face lit up. "Yes!"

He scrambled over to kneel beside Sheldon, small hands reaching for the nearest green thing. Sheldon caught his wrist gently.

"That one's daikon. Remember?"

"Oh." Tenchi squinted at it. "They look the same."

"Look at the leaves. See how this one has smooth edges?" Sheldon pointed. "That's the daikon. Weeds usually have jagged edges, or fuzzy stems, or different colors."

"Smooth is good. Jagged is bad."

"For now, yes."

Tenchi nodded seriously and reached for a weed — a real one this time, with jagged leaves and a fuzzy stem. He pulled. It came up easily, roots and all.

"I did it!"

"You did. Put it on the pile."

They worked together in the afternoon sun — the man with no past and the boy with too much future, pulling weeds side by side, not talking much, not needing to. The shadows grew longer. The pile of weeds grew higher. And somewhere in the process, without either of them noticing, something began to take root.


**SCENE 7 — END**

**Word Count:** ~2,200 words

**Sheldon's Current State:**
- Physical: Tired but satisfied; body handled full day of labor easily
- Emotional: More grounded; found purpose in work; connection with Tenchi deepening
- Knowledge: Learning shrine routines; learning garden layout

- Leaves Sheldon alone to work — trust gesture

- Overjoyed at kept promise
- Wants to help/supervise
- Deep question: "Why did you leave?"
- Shares grandfather's wisdom about people who go away
- Works alongside Sheldon — bonding through shared task

- Grandmother's voice continues as guide

**POST-SCENE AUDIT:**

#### Scene 2.C: Questions

The weed pile had grown impressive — knee-high, spilling across the path, evidence of an afternoon's labor. Tenchi had tired eventually, his four-year-old energy cycling from manic enthusiasm to sudden stillness, and now he sat on the edge of a raised bed, dirty hands in his lap, watching Sheldon finish the last row.

"Sheldon?"

"Mm?"

"Are you tired?"

Sheldon straightened, pressing a hand to his lower back out of habit. There was no pain — there hadn't been pain all day — but the gesture was automatic, a remnant of a body he no longer wore.

"A little," he said. "Good tired."

"What's good tired?"

"The kind you get from doing something useful." He pulled the last weed, shook the dirt from its roots, added it to the pile. "When you work hard and then you stop, and your body says *thank you for stopping* but also *that was worth doing*."

Tenchi considered this with the gravity of a philosopher. "I get tired from running. Is that good tired?"

"If you were running somewhere important."

"I was running to see if you were still here."

Sheldon's chest did something complicated. "Then yes. Good tired."


## Part 2: Washing Up

There was a spigot at the side of the residence, fed by the same well that supplied the kitchen. Katsuhito had left a bar of soap and two rough towels on a wooden bench beside it.

"Wash your hands and face," Sheldon told Tenchi. "Dinner soon."

"I know. Grandfather's making curry." The boy's face lit up. "Curry is my favorite. Do you like curry?"

"I do."

"Good. Grandfather makes it from scratch. He says the store kind is cheating."

Sheldon turned the spigot, let cold water run over his hands. The dirt of the afternoon sluiced away, brown rivulets disappearing into the drain. His hands emerged clean — still wrong, still too young, but clean.

Tenchi splashed beside him, more water on his shirt than on his hands, making sounds of exaggerated effort. Sheldon watched him and felt something unfamiliar settling in his chest. Not quite warmth. Not quite peace. Something quieter than either.

*This is real,* he thought. *Whatever else is happening, this moment is real.*


## Part 3: Curry

The kitchen smelled extraordinary.

Katsuhito stood at the stove, stirring a large pot with unhurried attention. The curry sauce was dark and rich, studded with chunks of potato and carrot and what looked like pork. A rice cooker steamed on the counter. The whole room was warm and fragrant and *alive* in a way that made Sheldon's stomach clench with sudden, desperate hunger.

"Smells good," he said.

Katsuhito glanced over his shoulder. "Sit. It's almost ready."

Sheldon sat. Tenchi climbed onto the cushion beside him, still damp from washing, practically vibrating with anticipation.

"Grandfather's curry is the best," the boy announced. "Better than anyone's. Even better than the curry at school."

"High praise."

"School curry is very good. Grandfather's is better."

Katsuhito served without ceremony — large bowls of rice, the curry ladled over top, a small dish of pickled vegetables on the side. The portions were generous, the presentation simple, the smell overwhelming.

Sheldon took his first bite.

The curry was... 

He stopped. Chewed. Swallowed.

It was *good*. Remarkably good. The sauce was complex — sweet and savory and slightly spicy, with a depth that suggested hours of simmering. The pork was tender, the vegetables perfectly cooked, the rice the ideal texture to absorb the sauce without becoming mush.

But more than that, it was *familiar*. Not in the sense that he'd tasted this specific curry before, but in a deeper way, a way that bypassed thought and went straight to something older. His grandmother had made curry sometimes — her version, adapted from her mother's recipe, adjusted for American ingredients. It hadn't tasted like this. But it had *felt* like this. Like someone had taken time and care to make something nourishing, something meant to fill more than just a stomach.

"You stopped," Tenchi observed.

Sheldon realized he was sitting with his spoon halfway to his mouth, staring at nothing.

"It's very good," he said. "I was... appreciating it."

Katsuhito's eyes flickered with something that might have been amusement. "Eat. There's more."

Sheldon ate. Two bowls. Would have eaten a third if Tenchi hadn't gotten there first.


## Part 4: Questions

After dinner, Katsuhito cleared the dishes. Tenchi remained at the table, watching Sheldon with the particular intensity of a child who has been waiting for something.

"Sheldon?"

"Yes?"

"Can I ask you things now?"

Sheldon looked at Katsuhito, who was doing something at the sink and very deliberately not turning around.

"What kind of things?"

"Questions. I've been saving them."

"How many have you saved?"

Tenchi held up both hands, fingers spread. Then frowned, folded down two fingers, reconsidered, put one back up. "This many. Maybe more."

Nine questions. Maybe more.

"All right," Sheldon said. "Ask."


"Why is your face different?"

"I told you. I'm from America. People there—"

"No, I mean *different* different. Your eyes are like mine but your skin isn't and your hair is curly and grandfather's hair isn't curly and neither is mine." Tenchi reached up to touch his own straight black hair, as if confirming. "Did you get it from your mother? Grandfather says I got my hair from my mother."

Sheldon's throat tightened. "My mother was Japanese. Like yours. My father was Black — that's why my skin is darker."

"Black?"

"It's... a word for people whose ancestors came from Africa. A long time ago."

"Africa." Tenchi tested the word. "Is that far?"

"Very far."

"Farther than America?"

"About the same, depending on where you start."

Tenchi absorbed this, nodding seriously. Then: "Is your mother here too?"

And there it was. The question Sheldon had known was coming, had been dreading since he first read the letter in his wallet.

"No," he said. "My mother died."

Tenchi's face changed. Not shock — he was too young for the full weight of that word — but recognition. Something he understood.

"My mother died too," he said quietly.


The silence stretched. Katsuhito had stopped moving at the sink.

"I know," Sheldon said, though he hadn't known, not really. The documents had mentioned Tenchi lived with his grandfather, that his father worked in the city. He'd assumed divorce, distance, the ordinary fractures of modern families. Not this.

"I don't remember her," Tenchi continued. "Grandfather shows me pictures but I don't remember her face. Just..." He touched his chest again, the same gesture from the garden. "Here. I remember here."

"That's good. That's important."

"Do you remember your mother?"

*No,* Sheldon thought. *I never met her. She never existed. She's a story someone wrote to give me a place in this world.*

But that wasn't what he said.

"I remember her voice," he heard himself say. "She used to sing to me when I was small. And I remember her hands — she had strong hands from working. And I remember the way she smelled, like green tea and something sweet."

Where were these words coming from? He didn't know. They rose up from somewhere inside him, false and true at the same time, a fiction that felt more real than memory.

"That's nice," Tenchi said. "Remembering."

"Yes."

"Does it hurt? When you remember?"

Sheldon looked at this small boy, this four-year-old who had lost his mother and didn't fully understand what that meant, who was asking questions no child should have to ask.

"Sometimes," he said honestly. "But it's a good hurt. Like good tired."

Tenchi nodded as if this made perfect sense.


## Part 5: More Questions

"Do you have a father?"

"He died too. A long time ago."

"Do you have a grandfather?"

"No. Not anymore."

"Do you have *anyone*?"

The question hit harder than Sheldon expected. Do you have anyone? He thought of his brother in Chicago, unreachable across decades and dimensions. His ex-wife, remarried, moved on. The colleagues who would have noticed his absence from the lab, eventually, when the experiments stopped running and the papers stopped appearing.

No one. He had no one.

"I have an uncle," he said. "In Osaka. I haven't seen him in a long time."

"You should see him. Uncles are family."

"You're right. Maybe I will."

Tenchi considered his next question carefully. Sheldon could see him selecting from his saved collection, weighing options.

"Why did you come here? To the shrine?"

"I was looking for my family's house. Your grandfather helped me find it."

"The empty house down the mountain?"

"Yes."

"Are you going to live there?"

"I'm going to try. It needs a lot of work first."

"I could help." Tenchi's eyes brightened. "I helped with the weeds. I could help with the house too."

"You'd have to ask your grandfather."

Both of them looked at Katsuhito, who had finished at the sink and was leaning against the counter, watching them with an expression Sheldon couldn't read.

"We'll see," the old priest said.


## Part 6: Bath and Bed

Tenchi's bath time came at seven-thirty, non-negotiable, even when there were questions left unasked. He protested — he always protested, Sheldon was learning — but Katsuhito was immovable.

"Say goodnight to Sheldon-san."

"Goodnight, Sheldon." Tenchi paused at the door. "Will you be here tomorrow?"

"I'll be here."

"Promise?"

"Promise."

The boy smiled, that transforming expression, and disappeared toward the bathroom. A moment later, Sheldon heard water running.

Katsuhito lowered himself onto the cushion across from Sheldon, moving with that peculiar grace that seemed to belong to someone much younger than he appeared.

"He likes you," the priest said. It was the same observation from this morning, but it landed differently now, weighted with everything the day had built.

"He's a good kid."

"He is." A pause. "He lost his mother when he was two. He doesn't remember her, not really. But he feels the absence."

"I noticed."

"You handled it well. The questions about your mother." Katsuhito's eyes were steady, unreadable. "I wasn't sure how you would."

Sheldon didn't know how to respond to that. Had he handled it well? He'd lied — told a story about a woman who had never existed, painted memories that weren't his. But the emotion behind the lies had been real. The ache had been real.

"He needed to hear that remembering is okay," Sheldon said finally. "Even when it hurts."

"Yes. He did."

Silence stretched between them, comfortable rather than awkward. Outside, the evening had deepened into true dark. Stars were appearing through the window, bright and clear, undimmed by city lights.

"The bath will be free after Tenchi," Katsuhito said. "You worked hard today. You should rest."

"Thank you." For the bath. For the work. For letting me be here. For not asking questions I can't answer.

Katsuhito nodded and rose, and Sheldon was alone with the stars and the quiet and the strange, impossible life he was building.


## Part 7: Night

The bath was hot and deep and perfect.

Sheldon lowered himself into the water and felt his muscles unknot, tension he hadn't known he was carrying dissolving in the heat. The bathroom was small and traditional — wooden tub, tiled floor, a small window letting in the night air. Steam rose around him, fogging the edges of his vision.

He thought about the day. The house. The decision. The sweeping and the weeding and the small boy who asked too many questions. The curry that tasted like his grandmother's love translated into a language he didn't know he remembered.

He thought about the lies he'd told. My mother used to sing to me. I remember her hands.

Were they lies? He had a mother — Dorothy Carter, née Tanaka, who had sung to him, who had strong hands from years of nursing work. She had died when he was twenty-three, breast cancer, quick and brutal. He remembered her voice. He remembered her hands.

He'd just... transplanted those memories. Given them to a woman named Sachiko, who had also died, who had also left a son behind. The feelings were real. Only the names had changed.

Was that a lie? Or was it a kind of truth?

He didn't know. He was too tired to know.


The guest room was dark and quiet. Sheldon lay on the futon, clean and warm, exhaustion pulling at him.

His second day. His first full day in this impossible life.

He had swept a courtyard. Weeded a garden. Eaten curry. Answered a child's questions about death and loss and family. Made promises he intended to keep.

It wasn't much. It was everything.

*Tomorrow,* he thought. *Tomorrow I'll figure out more. Tomorrow I'll visit the house again, start making plans. Tomorrow I'll...*

Sleep took him mid-thought, deep and heavy, pulling him down into darkness.

He dreamed of his mother — both of them, somehow, Dorothy and Sachiko, their faces blending, their voices harmonizing on a song he couldn't quite hear. They were proud of him, he understood. They were glad he was somewhere safe.

He slept until morning.


**SCENE 8 — END**

**Word Count:** ~2,400 words

**Time Elapsed (Story):** ~5.5 hours (3:30 PM to ~9:00 PM)

**Day 2 Complete:** 5:30 AM to ~9:00 PM (~15.5 hours)

**Sheldon's Current State:**
- Physical: Clean, rested, recovered from day's labor
- Emotional: Deepened connection with Tenchi; navigated difficult conversation; found truth in lies
- Knowledge: Learned Tenchi's mother is dead (Kiyone Masaki, though he doesn't know her name)

**Seeds Planted:**

 Both lost mothers; Tenchi "feels absence"

 "Maybe I will" visit him

- Sheldon: Transplants real memories (Dorothy) into fictional mother (Sachiko)
- Katsuhito: "You handled it well" — Quiet approval, trust building
- Dream: Both mothers proud of him — Integration beginning

**Law 10 (Emotional Realism) Check:**

- Connection is EARNED through shared work, shared loss, honest emotion within false framework
- Nothing forced; characters driving the scene


**POST-SCENE AUDIT:**

#### Scene 3.A: Third Morning

Sheldon woke before dawn.

Not with the gasping disorientation of the first morning, not with the lingering confusion of the second. This time his eyes simply opened, and he knew where he was. The ceiling beams of the guest room had become familiar over two nights. The weight of the futon, the particular quality of mountain silence, the faint smell of cedar and old incense—these had stopped being strange and started being simply *where he was*.

He lay still for a moment, listening. Birds were beginning their dawn chorus, but tentatively, as if they weren't quite sure the sun would keep its promise. The shrine was quiet. Katsuhito would be awake soon if he wasn't already—the old priest kept farmer's hours, Sheldon had noticed. Tenchi would sleep until someone woke him, small body curled tight as a fist under his blankets.

*Day three.*

The thought arrived without drama. He was three days into... whatever this was. This life. This second chance. This existence as Tsukino Sheldon, a thirty-year-old man with money in his wallet and a childhood he couldn't remember and parents who had died loving him.

The grief that rose was familiar now, worn smooth by two days of handling it. Not his grief—not exactly. The grief for Dorothy and James Carter, for the life that had ended in that moment of white light, that remained sharp and specific. But layered over it was the borrowed grief for Sachiko and Takeo Tsukino, parents he'd never met but whose love he carried in a letter folded carefully in his wallet. Two sets of parents. Two orphanhoods. The mathematics of loss didn't add up to double—it was something stranger, grief that echoed against itself.

*Get up*, his grandmother's voice said. Not unkindly. *Lying in bed never fixed anything.*

He sat up, muscles responding easily. That still surprised him—the absence of the morning stiffness he'd grown accustomed to at forty-seven. His back didn't creak. His knees didn't protest. When he stretched his arms overhead, his shoulders moved through their full range without the familiar catch on the left side where he'd pulled something years ago.

*Eighteen years ago*, he corrected himself. *In a body that no longer exists.*

The thought was getting easier to hold. Not comfortable—he doubted it would ever be comfortable—but manageable. He was not the same man who had died. He was something new, built from the template of that man but modified in ways he was still discovering.

He folded his futon with practiced movements. His mother—Dorothy—had been particular about bed-making. *A made bed is a day that's started right*, she used to say. The muscle memory served him even now, even when the bed was a Japanese futon and the context was a mountain shrine in rural Japan.

The morning was cool when he slid open the shoji and stepped onto the engawa. The sky was lightening in the east, painting the mountains in shades of grey and pale rose. He could see his breath in small clouds. The air smelled of damp earth and green things growing—spring asserting itself despite the lingering chill.

His eyes found the tree.

He didn't mean to look for it. The great tree by the shrine office simply... caught his attention, the way a compass needle catches north. It stood massive and dark against the lightening sky, branches spreading in a pattern that seemed almost designed, too perfect to be natural but too organic to be artificial. Morning light was just beginning to touch its highest branches.

Sheldon looked away, frowning slightly. It was just a tree. An old one, impressive certainly, but nothing more than that. He'd noticed it several times now—kept noticing it—and he didn't know why. Some aesthetic appreciation for ancient things, perhaps. The scientist in him would have said it was pattern recognition, his brain flagging the unusual specimen for attention.

He went to use the bathroom, washed his face with cold water that made him gasp, and returned to find his clothes where he'd folded them the night before. The same clothes he'd woken in three days ago—slacks and a white shirt that had been cleaned and dried, though they were beginning to show the wear of being his only outfit.

*Shopping*, he thought. *At some point, I need to buy clothes.*

The thought felt absurdly normal. He was a man displaced in time and space, identity uncertain, past shrouded in mystery, and he was thinking about shopping for socks. The absurdity made him smile despite himself.

He dressed and made his way quietly toward the main residence. The shrine grounds were grey and silver in the predawn light, stone lanterns casting no shadows yet, the paths still damp with dew. He passed the main hall, passed the offering box, passed the smaller outbuilding he'd helped clean two days ago.

The kitchen light was already on.

Katsuhito stood at the counter, back to the door, doing something that involved rhythmic sounds—a knife on a cutting board, Sheldon realized. The old priest was chopping vegetables with the unhurried precision of long practice.

"Good morning," Sheldon said from the doorway.

Katsuhito didn't startle. "Good morning, Tsukino-san. You're up early."

"The birds woke me." It wasn't quite true, but it was close enough.

"Ah. The bush warblers. They've been excited lately. Mating season." Katsuhito continued his rhythmic chopping. "There's tea in the pot, if you'd like."

Sheldon entered the kitchen, moving around Katsuhito to find a cup. The layout was becoming familiar—cups in this cabinet, tea things on that shelf, the pot kept warm on a low flame. He poured for himself, then hesitated.

"May I pour you a cup as well?"

"Thank you." Katsuhito's hands never stopped their work. Daikon radish, Sheldon saw now, being reduced to precise cubes. "Set it there. I'll get to it."

Sheldon poured a second cup and set it on the counter where Katsuhito had indicated, then found a spot out of the way to stand with his own tea. The kitchen was small but efficient, designed for one person to work comfortably. Morning light was beginning to filter through the window now, warming the room degree by degree.

He watched Katsuhito work. There was something meditative about it—the steady pace, the economy of motion. The old priest had clearly been doing this for decades. His knife moved in patterns worn smooth by repetition, each cut exactly the same as the last.

"Miso soup?" Sheldon guessed.

"And rice. Pickles. Eggs." Katsuhito scraped the cubed daikon into a bowl. "A simple breakfast, but it sustains."

Sheldon found himself studying the ingredients laid out on the counter. Eggs, yes—probably from the shrine's chickens, which he'd heard but not yet seen. Green onions. The rice cooker sat in the corner, already doing its work, the small light indicating the cycle was in progress.

His mother had cooked. Dorothy Carter had made Sunday dinners that could feed an army, raised-in-the-church Southern cooking that filled the house with smells of collard greens and cornbread and slow-simmered meats. He'd learned the basics from her, enough to survive as a bachelor academic—eggs, simple pastas, the occasional roasted chicken when he felt ambitious.

But watching Katsuhito work, he became aware of how much he didn't know. Japanese cooking was a different vocabulary entirely. The ingredients were familiar enough—vegetables, protein, rice—but the grammar was foreign. The way things were cut, the sequence of preparation, the subtle dance of timing that produced a meal rather than a collection of dishes.

"Is there something I can help with?" The question emerged before he'd fully formed the thought.

Katsuhito paused in his chopping. Turned slightly to regard Sheldon with those eyes that seemed to see more than they should. "Can you cook?"

"I can... manage. Simple things." Sheldon felt suddenly self-conscious. "My mother taught me the basics. American cooking, though. Southern. I don't know—" He gestured vaguely at the ingredients on the counter. "I don't know this."

"This," Katsuhito said, turning back to his cutting board, "can be learned."

It was neither an invitation nor a refusal. Just a statement of fact. Sheldon sipped his tea and watched as Katsuhito moved on to slicing green onions, the rings falling from his knife in paper-thin circles.

"The eggs are not complicated," Katsuhito said after a moment. "Tamagoyaki—the rolled omelet. Tenchi's favorite. If you wish to try."

Sheldon's hands wanted to say yes before his brain caught up. He set down his tea cup. "Show me?"

What followed was a lesson in three acts.

First: the eggs. Crack them into a bowl—easy enough. Add a small splash of dashi, the fish stock that was apparently the foundation of all things. A little sugar, a little soy sauce. Beat them smooth, but not too much, not until they foam.

Second: the pan. Small, rectangular, specifically designed for this one task. Heat it, oil it lightly, wait until a drop of egg mixture sizzled immediately but didn't burn.

Third: the technique. Pour a thin layer. Let it set. Roll it toward you using chopsticks. Push the rolled portion to the far end of the pan. Add more egg mixture. Let it set. Roll again. Layer upon layer, building the omelet through repetition rather than all at once.

Sheldon's first attempt was... educational.

"Too much heat," Katsuhito observed as brown patches appeared where golden was desired. "The pan runs hot at the edges."

His second attempt was better. Not good—the roll was lumpy, the layers uneven—but edible. It would not win any awards, but it was recognizably tamagoyaki.

"Again," Katsuhito said, and there was something almost like approval in his voice. "Tomorrow."

By the time Tenchi stumbled into the kitchen, still half-asleep and listing to one side like a small ship in rough water, there was breakfast on the table. Rice, miso soup with daikon and green onion, pickled vegetables, and two tamagoyaki—one perfect golden log, one somewhat irregular companion.

"Sheldon-san made the eggs," Katsuhito announced, settling onto his cushion.

Tenchi blinked at the table, then at Sheldon, then at the eggs. His face underwent a complicated journey from sleepy confusion to cautious interest.

"Which ones?" the boy asked.

"The ugly one," Sheldon said, straight-faced.

Tenchi considered this. Reached out and, with the decisive certainty of childhood, selected the irregular omelet for his own plate.

"I like ugly eggs," he said. "They taste like adventure."

Sheldon felt something shift in his chest. Something small and warm, taking root.

"Then tomorrow," he said, "I'll make sure to make them even uglier."

Tenchi's smile was the brightest thing in the room.


- Tenchi prefers the "adventure" of imperfect food

**Foreshadowing Maintained:**
- Funaho draw continues (eyes find the tree, dismissed as aesthetic appreciation)
- Body restoration noted (no morning stiffness, full range of motion)
- Supreme Chef seed watered (interest in learning, hands wanting to cook)

**Character Development:**
- Sheldon: Actively seeking ways to contribute; cooking as connection
- Katsuhito: Teaching without fanfare; "can be learned" = invitation through understatement
- Tenchi: "Ugly eggs taste like adventure" — childlike wisdom, choosing Sheldon's work

**Relationship Progress:**
- Sheldon → Katsuhito: Moving from guest to student (cooking lesson)
- Sheldon → Tenchi: Deepening bond through food, humor, presence

**Seeds Planted:**
- "Again. Tomorrow." — establishes cooking will become routine
- Shopping needed for clothes — mundane needs grounding the extraordinary
- Kitchen becomes a space for connection

---

#### Scene 3.B: The Village

"You'll need to go into the village eventually."

Katsuhito said it casually, not looking up from the accounts ledger he was reviewing at the low table. Sheldon had been helping Tenchi feed the chickens—a task that mostly involved the boy chasing the birds while Sheldon scattered grain in their actual direction—and had returned to find the old priest in a contemplative mood.

"I was thinking the same thing." Sheldon looked down at himself. The white shirt was holding up, but barely. The slacks had a grass stain on one knee from yesterday's garden work that hadn't quite come out. "I need clothes. Supplies. I can't keep borrowing your soap."

"The village has what you need. Small shops, but sufficient." Katsuhito turned a page. "Take the path down from the shrine—not the main steps. There's a forest trail that joins the road near the bridge. Faster, and less climbing on the return."

Sheldon filed this information away. "Is there a particular shop you'd recommend?"

"Tanaka's, for clothing. Tell them you're staying at the shrine. They'll understand." A pause. "The general store is Mori's. The woman who runs it—Mori Fumiko—she talks. Be prepared."

There was something almost like humor in Katsuhito's voice. Sheldon found himself smiling. "Warning received."

"Tenchi." Katsuhito raised his voice slightly. The boy appeared in the doorway with suspicious speed, suggesting he'd been hovering just out of sight. "Would you like to show Tsukino-san the way to the village?"

Tenchi's face underwent a rapid transformation from caught-eavesdropping guilt to barely contained excitement. "Can I? Really?"

"If Tsukino-san doesn't mind the company."

Sheldon looked at the boy—four years old, nearly five, vibrating with the prospect of an outing. "I'd appreciate the guide," he said. "I might get lost otherwise."

This was, of course, ridiculous. The path to the village couldn't be that complicated. But Tenchi didn't need to know that, and the way his whole small body lit up at being given a responsibility was worth the small fiction.

"I know all the shortcuts!" Tenchi announced. "And I can show you where the really big fish live in the river!"

"Fish viewing will have to wait for another day," Katsuhito said mildly. "Shopping first. Perhaps Tsukino-san will buy you a treat if you're helpful."

Tenchi's eyes went very wide. He looked at Sheldon with an expression of profound hope.

"We'll see," Sheldon said, and that seemed to be enough.


The forest path was exactly as Katsuhito had described—a narrow track through cedar and cypress that wound down the mountainside at a gentler grade than the shrine's 320 steps. Tenchi led the way with the confidence of long familiarity, occasionally pointing out landmarks with the gravity of a seasoned guide.

"That's the rock that looks like a frog," he announced, indicating a moss-covered boulder that, with generous imagination, might have resembled an amphibian. "And that tree has a hole where an owl lives. I've never seen the owl, but Grandfather says it's there."

"Maybe it's shy," Sheldon offered.

"Maybe." Tenchi considered this. "I would be shy too, if I lived in a tree and people kept looking at my house."

The logic was unassailable.

The path emerged onto a paved road after perhaps twenty minutes of walking. The river Katsuhito had mentioned ran alongside it, wide and shallow at this point, sunlight glinting off the water. Tenchi gave it a longing look but kept walking, responsibility outweighing temptation.

The village announced itself gradually—first a few houses, then a small shrine (not Masaki, a simpler affair with a single torii gate), then the beginnings of a commercial street. It was not large. A single main road with perhaps two dozen shops, houses clustered behind them, everything scaled to human measure. No buildings over two stories. Bicycles parked outside the grocer's. An old woman sweeping the sidewalk in front of what appeared to be a pharmacy.

Sheldon's last experience with small-town Japan had been thirty years ago in another life—a semester abroad that had taken him through villages not unlike this one. The details had blurred with time, but the *feeling* came back now: the particular quiet of a Japanese town mid-morning, the careful arrangement of everything, the sense that each building knew its place and purpose.

"Tanaka's is there," Tenchi said, pointing to a shop with clothing displayed in the window. "And Mori's is there." He pointed across the street to a larger establishment with a sign advertising everything from rice to farm tools. "Grandfather says Mori-san talks too much, but she gives me candy sometimes."

"That seems like a fair trade."

They went to Tanaka's first.

The shop was dim and cool, racks of clothing arranged by type and size. A bell chimed as they entered, and a moment later, a man emerged from a back room—middle-aged, neat in a way that suggested he took his profession seriously, dark hair silvering at the temples.

"Ah, Tenchi-kun! What brings you down from the mountain?" His eyes moved to Sheldon, curious but not unfriendly. "And you've brought a guest."

"This is Tsukino-san," Tenchi announced with evident pride. "He's staying at the shrine. He needs clothes because he only has one outfit and it's getting dirty."

The bluntness of childhood. Sheldon felt heat rise in his face, but Tanaka merely smiled.

"Traveling light, I see. Well, we can help with that." He was already assessing Sheldon with a professional eye—height, shoulder width, the length of his arms. "Western or Japanese style?"

"Both, perhaps? I'll need work clothes. Something practical."

What followed was an efficient transaction. Tanaka produced options with minimal fuss—two pairs of durable trousers, three shirts in muted colors, undergarments, socks. When Sheldon mentioned he'd be doing repairs on an old house, Tanaka added a set of work clothes to the pile: heavy cotton pants and a jacket that would stand up to rough use.

"The Tsukino house," Tanaka said as he folded the items. It wasn't a question. "Word travels. You're the nephew, then? Kiyoshi-san's family?"

"His nephew, yes." The lie—or the partial truth, or whatever it was—came easier each time. "I'm here to see about the property."

"Good. It's been sitting too long." Tanaka's hands moved with practiced efficiency, wrapping the clothing in brown paper. "A house needs people in it, or it forgets what it's for. Fifteen thousand yen for everything."

Sheldon paid from the cash in his wallet, watching the bills disappear into Tanaka's register with something like relief. The transaction felt normal. Ordinary. A man buying clothes in a village shop. No mystery to it at all.

"Mori-san will have anything else you need," Tanaka said as he handed over the wrapped package. "Soap, cleaning supplies, whatever. Tell her I sent you—she'll only talk your ear off for twenty minutes instead of thirty."

"That's what Grandfather said too," Tenchi confided. "About the talking."

Tanaka's smile widened. "The priest is a wise man."


Mori's General Store was everything Tanaka's shop was not—cluttered, overflowing, chaotic in a way that somehow still made sense. Shelves lined every wall, stacked with goods that ranged from kitchen supplies to farming equipment to imported snacks in bright packaging. The smell was complex: soap and incense and dried fish and something sweet that Sheldon couldn't identify.

The woman behind the counter matched her store: small, energetic, with sharp eyes that missed nothing and a mouth that apparently never stopped moving.

"Tenchi-kun! And—oh! You must be the man staying at the shrine. The nephew! Kiyoshi-san's boy, yes? Though you're not a boy anymore, are you? How old are you? You look young. Good skin. Do you moisturize? The air up by the shrine is very dry, you know, especially in winter, but it's spring now so it should be fine. What do you need? Soap? I have soap. Seven kinds. The lavender is popular with the women, but men like the hinoki cypress, very traditional. Are you traditional?"

Sheldon opened his mouth to answer, but Mori Fumiko was already moving, pulling items from shelves with an efficiency that belied her apparent scatter.

"Toothpaste, you'll need toothpaste. And a brush. We have the new ones from Osaka, very modern. Do you want modern or traditional? The traditional ones are bamboo, very nice, very authentic. Cleaning supplies? The house has been empty four years—four years! Such a shame. Sachiko-san was a lovely woman. You have her eyes, did you know that? No, you wouldn't know that, you never met her, isn't that sad? Family should be together, but everyone goes to the cities now. Not you, though! You came back. That's good. That's very good."

She paused for breath—the first pause since he'd walked in—and Sheldon seized the opportunity.

"Soap, please. The hinoki. A toothbrush—traditional is fine. And whatever cleaning supplies you'd recommend for a house that's been closed up."

Mori beamed at him as though he'd passed a test. "Smart man. Listen, take advice, don't argue. Your uncle is the same way—stubborn, but he listens eventually. I have just the thing. Sachiko-san always bought this brand—" She produced a bucket and a selection of bottles with the air of a magician revealing her best trick. "Floors, windows, bathroom. Everything you need. And I'll give you a discount because you're new and because the priest is a good man and because Tenchi-kun is my favorite customer, yes you are."

She reached under the counter and produced a small paper bag. Candy. She pressed it into Tenchi's hands with conspiratorial solemnity.

"For being a good guide," she said. "Don't tell your grandfather I'm spoiling you."

"I won't," Tenchi said, which was almost certainly a lie.

The total came to two thousand yen, which seemed reasonable given the quantity of supplies. Sheldon paid, accepted the paper bags Mori pressed upon him, and managed to escape the shop only after promising to return when he needed more supplies—which Mori assured him would be very soon, because a house that had been empty four years would need more cleaning than he expected, mark her words.

Outside, the midday sun was warm. Tenchi was already investigating his candy with the focused intensity of a researcher examining rare specimens.

"She's nice," the boy concluded, selecting a small wrapped sweet. "She talks a lot, but she always gives me candy."

"A reasonable assessment." Sheldon shifted the packages in his arms, finding a comfortable arrangement. His purchases formed a satisfying weight—tangible evidence of belonging, of staying, of building a life here. "Should we head back? Or is there something else you wanted to show me?"

Tenchi considered, unwrapping his candy with careful attention. "The river?"

"The river," Sheldon agreed.

They found a spot on the bank where the water ran clear and shallow, and Tenchi pointed out the rocks where fish supposedly gathered. Sheldon couldn't see any fish, but Tenchi assured him they were there, and that seemed true enough.

The candy was melon-flavored. Tenchi offered to share. Sheldon accepted.

Sitting on the riverbank with a four-year-old, eating melon candy and watching for fish that may or may not have existed, Sheldon felt something settle in his chest. Something that had been clenched began to loosen.

*This is real*, he thought. *Whatever else is true—however I got here, whoever I was—this is real. This boy, this candy, this river, this sun.*

The walk back up the mountain took longer than the walk down, burdened as he was with packages. But Tenchi kept up a steady commentary on the trees, the rocks, the birds they passed, and the time went quickly.

By the time they reached the shrine, it was early afternoon. Sheldon's arms ached pleasantly from the weight of his purchases. His feet were tired. He was, he realized with something like wonder, happy.

Not the complicated happiness of achievement or the sharp happiness of surprise. Just the simple, steady happiness of a day spent well in good company.

He'd buy groceries tomorrow, he decided. And he'd cook dinner.


- Forest path (shrine to village): Cedar/cypress, ~20 min walk, passes "frog rock" and owl-tree
- Village main street: ~24 shops, small shrine with single torii, river nearby
- Tanaka's Clothing Shop: Dim, cool, organized by type/size
- Mori's General Store: Cluttered, overflowing, complex smells (soap, incense, dried fish)
- Riverbank: Where Tenchi shows fish rocks

**Financial Tracking:**
- Starting funds: ~¥847,000
- Tanaka's purchase: -¥15,000 (clothes, work clothes)
- Mori's purchase: -¥2,000 (cleaning supplies, toiletries)
- Remaining: ~¥830,000

**Items Acquired:**
- 2 pairs trousers
- 3 shirts (muted colors)
- Undergarments, socks
- Work clothes set (heavy cotton)
- Hinoki soap
- Bamboo toothbrush
- Cleaning supplies (floors, windows, bathroom)

**Character Development:**
- Sheldon: Finding happiness in simple things; planning to cook dinner (Supreme Chef building)
- Tenchi: Pride in being guide; "ugly eggs" connection continues through candy sharing
- Village integration begun: Tanaka and Mori both know about him now

**Seeds Planted:**
- "Sachiko-san was a lovely woman. You have her eyes." — Mori's observation (identity thread)
- Sheldon planning to buy groceries and cook dinner (Supreme Chef escalation)
- Uncle Kiyoshi known in village (future thread)
- House repairs anticipated by villagers ("been sitting too long")

---

#### Scene 3.C: The Afternoon

The shrine was quiet when they returned.

Katsuhito was not in the main residence, though the low table still held his ledger and pen, set aside as if he'd only stepped out for a moment. Tenchi immediately claimed a cushion and began extracting his candy from the paper bag with the focused deliberation of a surgeon, sorting the sweets by color and type into small piles on the tatami.

Sheldon set his packages down by the wall and stretched, working the stiffness from his shoulders. The walk uphill had been longer than the walk down, the weight of his purchases making itself felt in muscles that, while restored, were not yet hardened to this kind of labor.

"I'm going to put these away," he said to Tenchi, who nodded without looking up from his inventory.

The guest room was as he'd left it—futon folded, his few possessions arranged neatly in the corner. The room felt different now, though. Less borrowed. He'd slept here two nights. He'd been given tea here, been welcomed here. The space was beginning to hold his shape.

He unwrapped his packages on the floor, laying out the new clothes in neat stacks. Two pairs of trousers—one charcoal, one brown. Three shirts in muted colors that Tanaka had selected without consultation but that Sheldon found himself approving: a soft grey, a pale blue, a cream that would hide dirt poorly but felt good against his skin when he tried it on. The work clothes were heavier, practical—the kind of thing you wore when you expected to get dirty.

He changed into one of the new shirts, tucking it into his old slacks. The fabric was crisp, unfamiliar. He caught himself smoothing it unnecessarily.

*Shopping*, he thought again. *I went shopping. Like a person who lives here.*

The cleaning supplies went into a corner. He'd need to visit the Tsukino house soon—tomorrow, perhaps—to assess what needed to be done. The thought of it stirred something complicated in his chest. A house that had belonged to parents he'd never met, waiting empty for a son who had never existed until three days ago.

*Or who had existed, and who had been somewhere else for two years.*

The ID said he'd arrived in 1987. The house had been empty since 1985. Where had he been for those two years? What had he been doing? The gap in his memory was a shape he could feel but not see, like a room he knew existed but couldn't find the door to.

He pushed the thought aside. There would be time for mysteries later. Right now, there was an afternoon to fill.


He found Katsuhito in the garden.

The old priest was kneeling in the vegetable beds, hands working steadily through the soil around a row of young plants. He didn't look up as Sheldon approached, but there was a subtle shift in his posture—acknowledgment without interruption.

"The village trip went well?" Katsuhito asked.

"Very." Sheldon crouched at the edge of the bed, careful not to disturb anything. "Tanaka was helpful. Mori-san was... thorough."

"Ah. She talked your ear off."

"Both ears. And possibly my nose."

Katsuhito's hands paused. When he looked up, there was something that might have been amusement around his eyes. "She means well. She remembers everyone who's ever passed through the village, and she believes everyone should know everything she remembers."

"She mentioned my—" Sheldon caught himself. "She mentioned Sachiko. Said I have her eyes."

Katsuhito returned to his work, but Sheldon caught the slight tension in his shoulders. "Mori-san knew the Tsukino family. Most of the village did. They were..." He seemed to search for the right word. "Present. Sachiko-san in particular. She had opinions, and she shared them."

"That sounds like someone I would have—" *Loved.* The word stuck in his throat. "Respected."

"She was respected. And loved." Katsuhito pulled a weed with more force than seemed necessary. "The village mourned when she passed."

Sheldon watched the old priest's hands move through the soil. There were questions he wanted to ask—about Sachiko, about Takeo, about the house and the family and the life that had ended before he'd arrived to inherit its remnants. But something in Katsuhito's silence suggested that this was not the moment.

"May I help?" he asked instead.

Katsuhito gestured to a patch of ground beside him. "The weeds don't pull themselves."


They worked in companionable silence for nearly an hour.

The garden was larger than Sheldon had realized, extending behind the residence in terraced beds that climbed the slope in neat rectangles. Vegetables occupied most of the space—daikon, carrots, onions, leafy greens he couldn't identify—but there were herbs too, clustered near the kitchen door where they could be harvested easily. He recognized some from his mother's garden in Georgia: basil, something that looked like rosemary. Others were new to him, their scents unfamiliar but not unpleasant.

The soil was good. He'd taken a geology elective in undergrad, learned enough to recognize healthy loam when he saw it. Dark, rich, full of organic matter. Someone—Katsuhito, presumably—had been tending this garden for years, building the soil season by season.

His hands remembered weeding. Dorothy had kept a vegetable garden behind the house in Atlanta, and summers had meant hours of work in the heat, pulling out the endless creeping grasses and broadleaves that tried to swallow her tomatoes. He'd complained at the time, a teenager convinced that manual labor was beneath a future scientist. Now the muscle memory was a comfort, something his body knew how to do without consultation.

Tenchi appeared around two o'clock, candy consumption apparently complete, and inserted himself into the work with the easy confidence of a child who'd done this before. His small hands were surprisingly efficient, finding weeds that Sheldon had missed and extracting them with practiced tugs.

"This one's not a weed," Tenchi informed him, pointing to a plant Sheldon had been about to pull. "That's shiso. Grandfather uses it for pickling."

"Ah." Sheldon withdrew his hand. "Thank you. I would have murdered an innocent herb."

"Grandfather says plants don't have feelings. But I think they might." Tenchi patted the shiso leaves gently. "I always say sorry when I pick them."

"That seems wise."

They worked their way down the row together, Tenchi providing commentary on each plant they encountered. The boy's knowledge was impressive for his age—he knew which vegetables were ready to harvest and which needed more time, which plants liked sun and which preferred shade, which bugs were helpful and which were pests.

"Grandmother taught me," Tenchi said when Sheldon commented on his expertise. "Before she went away."

Sheldon's hands stilled. "Your grandmother?"

"Grandfather's wife. She died when I was little. I don't remember her very well." Tenchi pulled a weed, frowning at the dirt under his fingernails. "But I remember she had a garden. And she used to sing while she worked."

"Do you remember the songs?"

Tenchi hummed a few notes—something minor-key and meandering, more impression than melody. "I don't remember the words. Just the sound."

Sheldon felt something tighten in his chest. This boy, not yet five, had already lost two women who'd loved him. His mother, dead. His grandmother, dead. The world was greedy with its takings.

"My grandmother used to sing too," he said. "Gospel music, mostly. Songs about light and hope and being carried home."

"What's gospel?"

"Church music. Music about God and faith and..." He searched for words a four-year-old might understand. "Music about believing that things will be okay, even when they're hard."

Tenchi considered this with the gravity only a child could muster. "Did your grandmother go away too?"

"Yes. A long time ago."

"Do you miss her?"

The question was simple, direct. Children asked such things because they didn't know yet that some questions had complicated answers.

"Every day," Sheldon said. "But I also remember her. And that helps."

Tenchi nodded, apparently satisfied. He returned to his weeding with renewed focus, small hands moving efficiently through the soil.


By three-thirty, the afternoon light had shifted to gold, and Sheldon's back was beginning to protest. They had cleared four beds of weeds and turned the soil in two more. Katsuhito had disappeared at some point—back to his ledgers, Sheldon assumed—leaving Sheldon and Tenchi to finish the work.

"I'm hungry," Tenchi announced, sitting back on his heels.

"It's too early for dinner." Sheldon stretched, feeling his spine pop in several places. "But we could have a snack."

"What kind of snack?"

The question sparked something in Sheldon's mind. The kitchen. The ingredients he'd seen that morning—rice, vegetables, the remains of yesterday's curry. And the cleaning supplies he'd bought, waiting in his room.

"I have an idea," he said. "But I need to check something first."


The kitchen was empty when they entered, afternoon light slanting through the window in long rectangles across the floor. Sheldon surveyed the space with new eyes—not as a guest looking for tea, but as someone assessing possibilities.

The pantry was better stocked than he'd expected. Rice, of course—multiple varieties, he noted, stored in large covered containers. Dried goods: beans, noodles, various things in packages he couldn't read. Fresh vegetables, some from the garden. Eggs from the chickens. Small containers of sauces and seasonings, arranged with the same careful organization he'd seen in everything Katsuhito touched.

*What do I know how to make?*

The question turned over in his mind. He knew Southern cooking—comfort food, his mother's recipes, the kind of thing that required ingredients not easily found in rural Japan. He knew bachelor cooking—pasta, scrambled eggs, sandwiches. He knew how to follow a recipe, but he'd never been adventurous.

But this morning. The tamagoyaki. His hands had wanted to learn. And watching Katsuhito's measured movements, watching the rice cooker cycle through its stages, he'd felt something stir. A curiosity. A pull toward understanding.

"Can you make onigiri?" Tenchi asked, appearing at his elbow.

"Rice balls?" Sheldon had eaten them, once, at a Japanese restaurant in Chicago. Simple triangles of rice, sometimes wrapped in seaweed. "I've never tried. Do you know how?"

Tenchi nodded eagerly. "Grandmother showed me! You cook the rice, and then you wet your hands, and then you squeeze it." He mimed the motion, small hands pressing together. "And you can put things inside. Like umeboshi. Or salmon."

"Do we have salmon?"

"No. But we have umeboshi!" Tenchi pointed to a jar on a shelf—small, pickled plums that Sheldon had noticed but not investigated. "They're sour but good."

*Rice balls with pickled plum.* It was simple. It was manageable. It was something he could learn.

"Show me," Sheldon said.

What followed was an education.

Tenchi's knowledge was fragmentary but enthusiastic. He knew the basics—wet your hands to prevent sticking, use salt on your palms, cup the rice and press gently but firmly—but the finer points escaped him. His demonstration produced a rice ball that was more sphere than triangle, lopsided and leaking grains.

"It's supposed to look nicer," he admitted, frowning at his creation. "Grandmother's were perfect."

"Let me try."

Sheldon wet his hands, salted them, and took a portion of the rice. It was warm from the cooker, slightly sticky, pleasant against his skin. He cupped it in his left palm and began to press, rotating the ball slowly, trying to form the triangular shape he remembered.

His first attempt was worse than Tenchi's—a misshapen lump that fell apart when he tried to set it down.

His second was better. Not good, but better. The rice held together, formed something approximately triangular if he squinted.

His third...

Something shifted.

He couldn't explain it afterward, couldn't point to the exact moment when his hands seemed to understand what his mind didn't. But on the third attempt, the rice responded differently. The pressure felt right. The rotation felt natural. The shape emerged not through force but through some kind of negotiation between his intention and the material.

The onigiri that formed was perfect.

Not adequate. Not passable. *Perfect.* Crisp edges, smooth surfaces, the kind of thing you'd see in a shop window. He stared at it, sitting in his palm like a small white monument.

"Wow," Tenchi breathed.

Sheldon made another one. And another. Each emerged from his hands with the same unlikely precision, as if his fingers had been doing this for years. He pressed a cavity into one, added an umeboshi, closed the rice around it and shaped it smooth. Wrapped it in a strip of nori seaweed that he found in a packet near the rice.

"You're really good at this," Tenchi said.

"I..." Sheldon looked at the row of onigiri on the cutting board. Five of them, identical, professional-quality. "I don't know how I'm doing this."

But his hands did. His hands knew exactly what they were doing.


They ate their snack on the engawa, watching the afternoon fade toward evening.

The onigiri were good. Better than good. The rice had a texture that Sheldon didn't remember achieving, a slight chewiness that gave way to softness. The umeboshi inside provided a bright counterpoint, salty and sour against the neutral grain.

Tenchi ate two before remembering to pace himself, then ate a third anyway.

"These are as good as grandmother's," he said, and there was wonder in his voice.

Sheldon didn't know what to say. He'd never made onigiri before today. He'd never even formed rice in his hands before this morning's failed tamagoyaki. And yet somehow, on his third attempt, he'd produced something that a four-year-old compared favorably to his dead grandmother's cooking.

*Something is happening to me.*

The thought was neither alarming nor comforting. It was simply true. Something was different. Something was changing. Whether it was something that had always been there, waiting to emerge, or something new—something given—he couldn't say.

His eyes drifted across the shrine grounds, and he found the tree again. The massive trunk, the spreading branches, the quality of presence that seemed to fill the air around it.

*Just a tree*, he told himself.

But his hands still tingled with the memory of rice taking shape beneath them, responding to pressure he hadn't known he could apply.

*Just a tree. Just rice. Just a coincidence.*

He ate another onigiri and watched the shadows lengthen across the stones.


- Hands "know what they're doing" before mind catches up
- Tenchi compares to grandmother's quality

**Early Period Manifestation (Years 1-12):**
- Manifests as exceptional human skill, not visible magic
- Internal experience: hands responding to intention
- No supernatural visual effects
- Could be explained as "natural talent" to observers

**Garden Details Expanded:**
- Terraced beds climbing slope
- Vegetables: daikon, carrots, onions, leafy greens
- Herbs near kitchen door: basil, rosemary-like, shiso
- Soil quality: dark, rich loam (years of cultivation)
- Katsuhito has tended for years

**Character Development:**
- Sheldon: Experiencing unexplained skill emergence; processing
- Tenchi: Teaching role reversed (showed onigiri basics); connection to dead grandmother through food
- Katsuhito's wife: Referenced — "went away," Tenchi remembers her songs, garden

**Memory Gap Acknowledgment:**
- ID says arrived 1987
- House empty since 1985  
- Two-year gap explicitly noted as unremembered
- "A room he knew existed but couldn't find the door to"

**Seeds Planted:**
- Gospel music / grandmother's songs — cultural identity thread
- Katsuhito's wife — backstory hook
- Shiso plant — specific herb for future use
- Onigiri as Tenchi connection point

**Foreshadowing Maintained:**
- Funaho draw: Eyes find tree again at scene end
- Supreme Chef: Now actively manifesting
- Body knowledge vs. mind knowledge — restoration theme

---

#### Scene 3.D: Late Afternoon

The shadows had grown long across the shrine grounds by the time Tenchi finished his third onigiri.

The boy sat cross-legged on the engawa, small legs tucked beneath him, watching the afternoon fade with the particular intensity children brought to simple moments. He'd stopped talking somewhere in the middle of his second rice ball, settling into a companionable quiet that Sheldon found surprisingly comfortable.

"Grandfather will wonder where we are," Tenchi said eventually.

"He probably knows." Sheldon had seen Katsuhito glance their way earlier, a brief acknowledgment from across the courtyard before disappearing into the main hall. The old priest seemed to have a sense for where everyone was at any given moment—not intrusive, just... aware.

"He always knows." Tenchi picked a grain of rice from his sleeve and ate it. "Mother used to say he had eyes in the back of his head. But I looked once, and he doesn't. Just regular hair."

The casual mention of his mother made Sheldon's chest tighten. This was the first time Tenchi had mentioned her directly—not his grandmother, but his mother. The woman who had died and left this small boy motherless.

"My mother used to say the same thing about my grandmother," Sheldon said carefully. "I think it's something mothers say."

"Did your grandmother have eyes in the back of her head?"

"No. But she always seemed to know when I was doing something I shouldn't be doing."

Tenchi nodded, accepting this as universal truth. "Grandfather is like that too." He stood, brushing rice from his lap with the unselfconscious thoroughness of childhood. "I should wash my hands before dinner. Grandfather says dirty hands at the table are rude."

"Good manners," Sheldon agreed.

He watched the boy pad inside, small feet quiet on the wooden floor, and found himself alone on the engawa with the dregs of afternoon.

The tree caught his attention again.

It stood in the slanting golden light, its massive trunk throwing a shadow that stretched nearly to the steps. Something about it in this light—the way the bark seemed to hold warmth, the slight movement of branches in a breeze he couldn't feel—made it hard to look away.

*Beautiful*, he thought. *Old. Patient.*

He blinked and turned away. Enough staring at trees.


The kitchen was empty when he entered, but signs of preparation had begun. A pot sat on the counter, waiting. Vegetables had been set out—daikon, carrots, what looked like burdock root. A piece of fish wrapped in paper rested near the sink.

Someone had started dinner, then been called away.

Sheldon stood in the doorway, uncertain. This wasn't his kitchen. He'd been a guest here for three days. But his hands remembered the feel of rice between his palms, the unexpected rightness of the onigiri taking shape, and something in him wanted to continue.

*Itadakimasu*, he thought. Not for eating—for beginning. For receiving.

He washed his hands at the sink, cold water shocking against skin still warm from the afternoon sun. Dried them on the cloth hanging nearby. Surveyed the ingredients again.

The fish was mackerel. He could tell by the silver-blue skin, the particular pattern of stripes. His mother had never cooked mackerel—too strong for her taste, she'd said—but he'd eaten it at that Japanese restaurant in Chicago, grilled with salt and served with grated radish.

*Yakizakana*. The word surfaced from somewhere, clear and certain. Grilled fish.

He didn't know when he'd learned that word. It felt like something he'd always known.

The vegetables were for simmered dishes—he could see that much. Daikon and carrots cut into chunks, slow-cooked in broth until tender. The burdock root would need to be cleaned, scraped, its earthy bitterness balanced with sweetness and soy.

*Nimono*. Simmered things.

His hands wanted to move. He let them.


The fish went first into a bath of salt, drawing moisture from the skin to ensure crispness when grilled. While it sat, he turned to the vegetables.

The daikon came apart under his knife in thick rounds, then quarters. His cuts were clean, even—not the awkward hackwork he would have expected from himself. The knife felt comfortable in his grip, the angle of the blade natural against the cutting board.

Carrots followed. He cut them at an angle, the way he'd seen—where had he seen it? In a cookbook? On television?—the bias cut that allowed more surface area to absorb flavor.

The burdock root was different. He scraped it with the back of the knife, removing the thin outer layer without losing the flesh beneath. Then shaved it into ribbons that curled as they fell, dropping them immediately into cold water to prevent browning.

He worked without thinking. Or rather, he worked while thinking about other things—Tenchi's casual mention of his mother, the way the afternoon light had caught the tree, the feel of rice between his palms—and his hands continued their task as if they belonged to someone else. Someone who knew what they were doing.

*This isn't normal.*

The thought surfaced and he let it float there, neither accepting nor rejecting it. Something was different. Something had changed, or was changing, or had always been this way and he was only now noticing. The onigiri had been the first clear sign, but now, watching his hands move through preparation steps he'd never learned, he couldn't pretend it was coincidence.

He set a pot of water to boil for the rice. Measured out enough for three, then added a bit more—he was hungrier than usual after the day's work, and he suspected Katsuhito and Tenchi were as well. The rice went into the cooker with practiced motions, water added by feel rather than measurement, and he set it going before he could question how he knew the right proportions.

The fish came out of its salt bath and went under the grill.

The vegetables went into a pot with dashi—the stock he found already made in a container in the refrigerator, clear and golden—along with soy sauce, mirin, and a pinch of sugar. He brought it to a simmer and lowered the heat, letting time do the work.

He was cleaning the burdock ribbons, preparing to add them to the pot, when Katsuhito appeared in the doorway.


The old priest said nothing for a long moment.

He stood at the threshold, watching Sheldon work with an expression that might have been curiosity or might have been something else entirely. His eyes moved from the fish under the grill to the simmering pot to Sheldon's hands, still holding the dripping burdock.

"I was going to make dinner," Katsuhito said finally.

"I—" Sheldon stopped. What could he say? *I started cooking and couldn't stop?* *My hands know things I don't remember learning?* "I hope you don't mind. I wanted to help."

"Help." Katsuhito entered the kitchen, moving to check the fish. He lifted the edge with chopsticks, examining the underside. "This is well-seasoned."

"I salted it first. To draw out moisture."

"I know the technique." There was something careful in Katsuhito's voice. "Where did you learn it?"

Sheldon's hands paused over the burdock. "I don't know," he admitted. "I've never cooked fish before. Not like this."

The silence stretched between them, filled with the sound of simmering broth and the faint hiss of fish fat dripping onto coals.

"The onigiri," Katsuhito said. "Tenchi showed me. He said you made them."

"We made them together. He taught me how—or tried to. My first two attempts were..." Sheldon searched for the right word. "Educational."

"And the third?"

"Different."

Katsuhito nodded slowly, as if this confirmed something he'd suspected. "Different," he repeated. "Yes. Tenchi said they were perfect. He said they were as good as his grandmother's." A pause. "My wife made onigiri for forty years. She was very skilled."

The weight of that statement settled over Sheldon like a blanket. He'd been compared to a woman who had forty years of practice, and he'd met that standard on his third attempt.

"I don't understand it either," he said quietly. "I don't know how I'm doing this."

"No." Katsuhito's eyes met his, and for a moment Sheldon felt seen in a way that was almost uncomfortable—not invasive, but penetrating, as if the old priest was reading something written beneath his skin. "But you are doing it. That is what matters."

He moved past Sheldon to check the simmered vegetables, lifting the lid and inhaling the steam with an approving nod. "This will be ready soon. The fish needs another few minutes." He set the lid back down. "I will set the table. You will finish here."

It wasn't a question. It was permission—or perhaps acknowledgment. Sheldon wasn't sure which.

"Thank you," he said.

Katsuhito paused at the doorway. "My wife would have liked you," he said, not looking back. "She always said cooking was listening. Listening to the ingredients, to the heat, to the moment." A slight inclination of his head. "You listen well, Tsukino-san."

Then he was gone, and Sheldon was alone with the fish and the vegetables and the strange certainty that something fundamental was shifting beneath his feet.


Dinner was served at six-thirty.

The low table was set with three places, each with its own arrangement of dishes—rice in individual bowls, miso soup in lacquered containers, the grilled mackerel on rectangular plates, the simmered vegetables in small bowls. Everything in its proper place, every flavor distinct, the meal laid out with the care of a ritual.

Sheldon had done this. He'd made this food, arranged these plates, created this moment. And he still didn't fully understand how.

They sat in seiza—Sheldon's knees protesting only faintly, another small surprise—and Katsuhito led them in the evening prayer. Then the word that began all meals:

"Itadakimasu."

Tenchi attacked his food with the single-minded focus of a child who had spent the afternoon weeding a garden and eating only rice balls for sustenance. The mackerel disappeared in alarming quantities. The vegetables were consumed without complaint, which Sheldon suspected was unusual based on Katsuhito's slightly raised eyebrow.

The old priest ate more slowly, savoring each bite with deliberate attention. He said nothing about the food, but his silence spoke its own language—approval, perhaps even surprise, communicated through the thoroughness with which he cleaned each dish.

Sheldon ate his own portion with a strange detachment. The food was good. Better than good—the mackerel was perfectly crisp on the outside, moist within, the salt bringing out its rich flavor rather than overwhelming it. The vegetables had absorbed the broth beautifully, each bite offering a depth of flavor that seemed impossible given how simply he'd prepared them.

*I made this.*

The thought kept circling back, insistent and bewildering.

*I made this, and I don't know how.*

"More rice?" Katsuhito asked, and Sheldon realized his bowl was empty.

"Please."

The old priest served him with the same economical movements he brought to everything. "You will cook again tomorrow?"

It was phrased as a question, but something in his tone made it clear what answer he expected.

"If you'd like me to," Sheldon said.

"I would like that very much."

Tenchi looked up from his third helping of mackerel. "Can you make curry? I like curry."

"I..." Sheldon thought of the curry he'd eaten two nights ago, the complexity of its spice blend, the way the flavors had layered and built. Could he make that? He didn't know. But his hands tingled with something that might have been anticipation. "I can try."

"Tomorrow?" The hope in Tenchi's voice was painfully earnest.

"Tomorrow," Sheldon agreed, and watched the boy's face light up like sunrise.

Across the table, Katsuhito said nothing. But the faint smile at the corner of his mouth said enough.


- Katsuhito noticed and acknowledged: "You listen well"
- Comparison to Katsuhito's wife (40 years practice) now explicit

**Early Period Presentation (Years 1-12):**
- Manifests as exceptional skill, not visible supernatural effect
- "Hands know things I don't remember learning"
- Sheldon aware something is "different" but cannot explain
- Katsuhito suspects but does not confront

**Character Development:**
- Sheldon: Processing strange abilities; accepted cooking role at shrine
- Katsuhito: Acknowledged Sheldon's skill; offered permission to continue; revealed wife detail
- Tenchi: First direct mention of mother (casual); requested curry for tomorrow
