- Made onigiri for 40 years

- Her philosophy: "Cooking was listening"

- "Mother used to say he had eyes in the back of his head"
- Casual mention, not elaborated

**Seeds Planted:**
- Curry promised for tomorrow (Day 4)
- "Something fundamental was shifting beneath his feet"
- Katsuhito's observation/assessment deepening

**Foreshadowing Maintained:**
- Funaho draw: Eyes found tree again in golden light; "beautiful, old, patient"
- Supreme Chef: Now fully blooming through dinner preparation
- Katsuhito's awareness: He knows something is unusual

#### Scene 3.E: Evening

The dishes were a simple matter.

Sheldon had offered to wash them, and Katsuhito had accepted with a nod that suggested this was expected—not demanded, but hoped for. The kitchen was quiet except for the sound of running water and the clink of ceramic against ceramic. Outside the small window over the sink, the sky had deepened from gold to purple to the first true darkness of night.

Tenchi had been excused to prepare for his bath. Sheldon could hear him somewhere in the house, small feet padding on wooden floors, the occasional thump of something being set down or picked up. The sounds of a child's evening routine, unremarkable and somehow precious.

"You have done well today."

Katsuhito's voice came from behind him. The old priest had returned to the kitchen doorway, arms folded into his sleeves in that characteristic posture of his.

"The food, you mean?" Sheldon scrubbed at a rice bowl, though it was already clean.

"The food. The garden. The village." A pause. "Being present."

Sheldon wasn't sure how to respond to that. Being present seemed like the least one could do—simply existing where you were, paying attention. But something in Katsuhito's tone suggested it was not, in fact, the least. That presence was its own form of effort.

"Tenchi will want to say goodnight to you," Katsuhito continued. "After his bath. He asked."

"Of course."

"He doesn't ask everyone." The old priest's eyes were steady on Sheldon's back. "He's particular about who he trusts. Like his mother was."

Another mention of Tenchi's mother—twice in one day now, after none in the previous two. Sheldon filed this away, uncertain what it meant but sensing it was significant.

"I'm honored," he said, and meant it.


The bath at the Masaki shrine was traditional.

Sheldon had used it once before, on his second night, but in that exhausted haze he'd barely registered the details. Now, waiting his turn while Katsuhito bathed Tenchi, he had time to observe the space properly.

The bathroom was separated from the changing area by a sliding door. The changing area held baskets for clothing, a low shelf with folded towels, a mirror that had seen better decades. Everything was clean but worn, surfaces polished by years of use.

He could hear water splashing on the other side of the door, and Tenchi's voice, high and bright, telling some story about the chickens and what they'd done that morning. Katsuhito's responses were low murmurs, their words indistinct but their tone patient. Grandfather and grandson, sharing a ritual that was older than either of them.

*Father to bathe with young children*, some part of Sheldon's mind supplied. He didn't know where that knowledge came from—cultural osmosis, perhaps, from his semester abroad thirty years ago. Or from something else entirely, something he couldn't name.

The splashing stopped. Voices moved, and then the door slid open.

Tenchi emerged wrapped in a towel that was slightly too large for him, his hair wet and sticking up in several directions at once. His cheeks were flushed from the heat, his eyes bright with that particular alertness that came just before the crash of exhaustion.

"Sheldon-san! I'm clean now!"

"I can see that." Sheldon crouched to meet his eyes. "You look like a very clean lobster."

Tenchi giggled, then tried to look offended, then gave up and giggled again. "Lobsters are red. I'm pink."

"A very clean pink lobster, then."

"Grandfather says I have to go to bed soon." The brightness in Tenchi's eyes flickered with something like disappointment. "But I wanted to say goodnight."

"Then say goodnight."

Tenchi considered this with the gravity of a child making a Very Important Decision. Then he stepped forward and wrapped his small arms around Sheldon's neck, pressing his damp face against Sheldon's shoulder.

"Goodnight, Sheldon-san."

The embrace was brief—children's hugs always were, burning hot and quickly released. But Sheldon felt it echo through him long after Tenchi had pulled away, padding off toward his room with Katsuhito following behind.

*Day three*, he thought. *Three days, and this child is hugging me goodnight.*

It should have felt too fast. It should have felt presumptuous, or overwhelming, or strange. Instead it felt like the most natural thing in the world—a small boy reaching for comfort, and a man offering it without condition.


Katsuhito returned a few minutes later, while Sheldon was still standing in the changing area, processing.

"The bath is yours," the old priest said. "Take your time. The water is still hot."

"Thank you."

Sheldon hesitated, then asked the question that had been forming in his mind. "Does he—does Tenchi do that often? The hugging?"

Katsuhito's expression shifted, something moving beneath the surface that Sheldon couldn't read. "He did. Before his mother died. Then he stopped." A pause, weighted with things unsaid. "Tonight was the first time in almost two years."

The words landed like stones in still water.

"I didn't know," Sheldon said quietly.

"How could you?" Katsuhito moved toward the door, then stopped. "He has chosen to trust you, Tsukino-san. That is not a small thing. Please do not make him regret it."

It wasn't a threat. It was a request—perhaps even a plea, wrapped in the careful dignity of an old man who had already lost too much.

"I won't," Sheldon said.

Katsuhito nodded once, and then he was gone.


The bath was scalding.

Sheldon washed first, as was proper—sitting on the low wooden stool, using the small basin to pour water over himself, scrubbing with soap that smelled of hinoki cypress. The ritual was both foreign and familiar, his hands knowing the movements even as his mind registered their strangeness.

Then the tub itself. Deep enough to sit with water to his shoulders, hot enough to make his skin flush and his muscles unknot. He lowered himself in slowly, breath catching at the temperature, then settling as his body adjusted.

The heat seeped into him, finding the places where tension had gathered—his shoulders, his lower back, the subtle ache in his hands from a day of work. He closed his eyes and let himself float in the sensation.

*Day three.*

The thought kept circling back, a refrain he couldn't escape. Three days ago he had woken in a field with cherry blossoms in his hair and no memory of how he'd arrived. Three days ago he had been a stranger, lost and confused, carrying identification that said he was someone he didn't remember being.

Now he was here. In a bath. In a shrine. In a life that was beginning to take shape around him.

He had made dinner tonight. He had made onigiri that a child compared to his dead grandmother's. He had found himself wielding a knife with skills he'd never learned, cooking fish with techniques that emerged from nowhere.

*Something is happening to me.*

The thought was less frightening than it had been that afternoon. The hot water softened everything, including fear. Whatever was changing in him—whatever had been given or awakened or unlocked—it had let him feed a family tonight. It had let him contribute. It had let him belong.

*Maybe that's enough*, he thought. *Maybe understanding can wait. Maybe the doing is what matters.*

He stayed in the bath until the water began to cool, then dried himself with a towel that was soft from many washings and returned to the changing area. His new clothes were waiting—the grey shirt he'd bought that morning, fresh undergarments, the comfort of fabric that was his own.


The guest room was quiet when he returned to it.

He could hear the shrine settling around him—the small creaks and sighs of an old building adjusting to the night, the distant sound of wind in trees, the absolute silence of a mountain without traffic or crowds. The futon had been laid out for him, probably by Katsuhito while he bathed.

He sat on the edge of the futon, not yet ready to sleep.

The day played back through his mind in fragments. Waking before dawn. The tamagoyaki lesson, his first imperfect attempts. The walk to the village, Tenchi's running commentary on frog rocks and owl trees. Mori-san's torrent of words, Tanaka's quiet efficiency. The garden, the weeding, Tenchi saving the shiso from accidental murder.

And then: the onigiri. The strange certainty of his hands. The dinner that emerged from ingredients he'd never cooked, techniques he'd never learned.

*You listen well, Tsukino-san.*

Katsuhito's words returned to him, and with them the memory of his wife's philosophy. *Cooking was listening. Listening to the ingredients, to the heat, to the moment.*

Maybe that was what was happening. Maybe whatever had been done to him—whatever had restored his body, given him new documents, placed him in this life—had also made him better at listening. To ingredients. To children. To himself.

Or maybe he was looking for patterns in chaos, the scientist's reflex to explain even the inexplicable.

He lay back on the futon, staring at the ceiling beams that had become familiar over three nights. Tomorrow he would make curry, as he'd promised. Tomorrow the skills would either come or they wouldn't, and he would learn something either way.

But tonight—tonight he had fed a family. He had made a child laugh. He had received a hug that apparently hadn't been given in two years.

*Something is taking root*, he thought, and this time the words felt less like observation and more like prayer.

Sleep came slowly, and when it came, it was deep and dreamless and warm.


- Katsuhito: Revealed significance of Tenchi's gesture; asked Sheldon not to betray trust
- Sheldon: Processing changes; finding peace with uncertainty

**Bath Details Established:**
- Traditional ofuro setup: changing area separate from bath
- Wash first (stool, basin), then soak
- Katsuhito bathes Tenchi (grandfather-grandson ritual)
- Water very hot; shared by household
- Hinoki (cypress) soap

**Emotional Beats:**
- Tenchi's hug = breakthrough moment
- "First time in almost two years" = since Kiyone's death (1987/88)
- Katsuhito's request: "Please do not make him regret it"
- Sheldon's acceptance of uncertainty: "Maybe the doing is what matters"

**Tenchi's Mother Timeline Clarification:**
- Died "almost two years" before April 1989
- Places death around mid-1987
- Tenchi was ~2.5-3 years old when she died

**Foreshadowing Maintained:**
- "Something is taking root" — recurring theme
- Processing continues but fear diminishing
- Acceptance emerging

**Day 3 Complete.**

---

#### Scene 4.A: Fourth Morning

Sheldon woke before dawn again.

It was becoming a pattern, this early rising—his body attuned to rhythms he didn't remember learning, surfacing from sleep with the first grey light as if responding to some internal signal. Four days now, and each morning had found him awake before the sun cleared the mountains.

He lay still for a moment, cataloguing sensations. The futon beneath him, firm and familiar. The cool air of early morning on his face. The distant sound of birdsong, thin and clear in the pre-dawn quiet.

No pain. No stiffness. No lingering ache from yesterday's garden work.

*Four days*, he thought. *Four days since I woke up in a field with cherry blossoms in my hair.*

The thought felt different this morning. Less urgent. Less frightening. He'd slept deeply, dreaming of nothing he could remember, and woken to a body that felt more his own than it had the day before. Whatever was happening to him—whatever had been given or awakened or restored—it seemed to be settling in. Taking root.

*Something is taking root.*

The phrase returned to him, and he let it sit there without examination. He was learning, slowly, to let things be without needing to understand them.


The kitchen was empty when he entered, dawn light just beginning to color the windows.

Katsuhito was not yet awake, or at least not yet emerged from his quarters. The shrine held that particular silence of early morning—deep and complete, broken only by the creak of old wood and the occasional rustle of wind in trees.

Sheldon moved through the space quietly, orienting himself. The rice cooker. The stove. The cutting board, worn smooth by years of use. The knife block with its array of blades, each designed for a specific purpose.

Today he would make curry.

The thought sat in his mind like a small, warm coal. He'd promised Tenchi. He'd promised Katsuhito too, in a way—agreed to cook again, accepted the role that was forming around him. Now he had to deliver.

*Can I actually do this?*

Yesterday's dinner had emerged from somewhere he couldn't name, techniques surfacing without conscious thought. But curry was different. Curry was complex—spices and timing and the slow building of flavor. He'd eaten Japanese curry before, in Chicago and Atlanta and once at a conference in San Francisco, but he'd never made it.

His hands didn't tingle with anticipation this time. They felt ordinary. Human. Perhaps yesterday had been an anomaly. Perhaps today the strange competence would desert him, and he'd be left fumbling through a recipe he didn't know.

Only one way to find out.


He started with inventory.

The pantry yielded its secrets slowly. Rice, of course—multiple varieties, more than enough for several days. Vegetables: potatoes, carrots, onions. These were the standards, the foundation. He'd seen curry made with these. He knew they belonged.

In a cabinet above the stove, he found what he was looking for.

The box was yellow and orange, decorated with text he couldn't fully read but recognized nonetheless. *House Vermont Curry*, the English letters proclaimed beneath the Japanese characters. Medium-Hot. The picture showed a plate of rice covered in rich brown sauce, steam rising in appetizing curls.

Curry roux. Pre-made, packaged, designed for exactly this purpose.

He turned the box over, examining the instructions. Pictures showed the process: sauté vegetables, add water, simmer, add roux blocks. Simple. Foolproof. The kind of thing anyone could do.

But something in him resisted.

*That's not cooking*, a voice said—not quite his own, or not quite the voice he remembered being his. *That's assembly. That's following instructions. That's not listening.*

He set the box aside and began to look deeper.


The spice cabinet was smaller than he expected, tucked in a corner near the stove. Most Japanese cooking, he was coming to understand, relied on a different palette than Western cuisine—soy and mirin and dashi rather than cumin and coriander.

But curry had been imported. Curry had been adapted. And somewhere in this kitchen, someone had gathered what was needed.

He found it on the top shelf: a small tin, dented and worn, with faded text. *S&B Curry Powder*. When he opened it, the scent hit him like a wave—earthy, warm, complex. Turmeric and cumin and fenugreek and things he couldn't name, blended into something that smelled like memory.

His hands began to move.

Butter from the cold storage. Flour from the pantry. He set them on the counter alongside the curry powder and stared at them, waiting for the knowledge to come.

It came.

*Roux*, the voice said. *You make a roux. Butter and flour, cooked until brown. Then the spices. Then the liquid. You build it layer by layer, listening to each stage before moving to the next.*

He didn't know how he knew this. He'd never made roux from scratch, never combined these particular ingredients in these particular proportions. But his hands were already reaching for a pan, already measuring butter by eye, already heating the element beneath.

The butter melted, foaming gently. He added flour, stirring with a wooden spoon that felt natural in his grip. The mixture thickened, turned paste-like, began to brown as he continued to stir.

*Patience*, the voice reminded him. *Don't rush. Let it develop. Let it tell you when it's ready.*

The kitchen filled with the scent of toasting flour, nutty and warm. He stirred, watching the color deepen from pale gold to amber to something approaching mahogany. His arm should have been tiring—the constant motion, the careful attention—but it felt effortless. Automatic. Right.

When the roux reached the color he somehow knew it needed to be, he added the curry powder. The spices bloomed in the hot fat, releasing aromas that layered over each other in waves: the earthiness of turmeric, the warmth of cumin, something floral he couldn't identify. He stirred, incorporating, letting the flavors marry.

Then he removed the pan from heat and poured the mixture into a shallow dish to cool and set.

Homemade curry roux. Done in twenty minutes. Without a recipe.

*What am I becoming?*


Katsuhito found him an hour later, standing at the stove with vegetables simmering in a pot.

The old priest stopped in the doorway, much as he had the evening before, and surveyed the scene. Onions browning in a pan. Potatoes and carrots cut into precise chunks, waiting their turn. The homemade roux sitting in its dish, firm now and fragrant. Chicken pieces—found in the cold storage, wrapped in paper—marinating in something Sheldon had improvised from soy sauce and ginger.

"You are making curry," Katsuhito said.

"I promised Tenchi."

"So you did." The old priest moved into the kitchen, examining the roux with an expression Sheldon couldn't read. "You made this yourself."

It wasn't a question. Sheldon nodded anyway.

"My wife used to make roux from scratch." Katsuhito's voice was quiet, almost distant. "She said the packaged kind was convenient, but soulless. She said cooking was about putting yourself into the food."

"She sounds like a wise woman."

"She was." Katsuhito looked up from the roux, meeting Sheldon's eyes. "Where did you learn this, Tsukino-san?"

The question hung between them, heavy with implications neither of them spoke aloud.

"I don't know," Sheldon admitted. "I've never made curry before. I've never made roux. I just..." He searched for words that wouldn't sound insane. "I just knew what to do. My hands knew."

Katsuhito was silent for a long moment. Then he nodded, slowly, as if confirming something to himself.

"There are many kinds of knowledge," he said. "Not all of them come from books or teachers. Some are older. Some are given." He moved toward the door, then paused. "Tenchi will be awake soon. He will want to know about the curry."

"It will be ready by dinner."

"I have no doubt." The old priest's eyes held something that might have been wonder, or might have been wariness. "No doubt at all."


Tenchi arrived in the kitchen like a small hurricane, still rubbing sleep from his eyes but already asking questions.

"Is it curry? Are you making curry? Grandfather said you were making curry!"

"I'm making curry," Sheldon confirmed. "But it won't be ready until dinner. Curry needs time."

"Why does it need time?"

"Because the flavors need to get to know each other." Sheldon found himself crouching to meet the boy's eyes. "Like people. When you first meet someone, you don't know everything about them. You have to spend time together, learn how you fit. Curry is the same. The vegetables and the spices and the sauce all need time to understand each other."

Tenchi considered this with his characteristic gravity. "So the curry is making friends?"

"Something like that."

"Okay." He seemed satisfied by this explanation. "Can I help?"

Sheldon looked at the kitchen—the pot simmering on the stove, the chicken waiting to be added, the roux that would need to be incorporated later. There were things Tenchi could do. Things that would make him feel included, part of the process.

"Can you wash the rice?" he asked. "For tonight's dinner. Four cups, in the rice cooker bowl."

Tenchi's face lit up. "I can do that! Grandmother taught me!"

He scrambled to the counter, dragging a step stool with him, and began the process with more enthusiasm than precision. Water splashed. Rice grains escaped. But he was careful, determined, focused on the task with the intensity only children could bring to something they considered important.

Sheldon watched him and felt that warm thing in his chest again—the one that had been growing since he'd arrived. The one that felt like roots spreading, like foundations being laid, like something permanent being built from temporary moments.

*Four days*, he thought. *Four days, and I'm teaching a child to wash rice while curry simmers on the stove.*

The sun had risen fully now, filling the kitchen with morning light. Through the window, he could see the shrine grounds, the great tree standing patient in its courtyard, the steps leading down toward the village he was beginning to know.

He turned back to the curry and continued to cook.


- Rejected pre-packaged roux ("That's not cooking. That's assembly.")
- Process: butter + flour (browned to mahogany) + curry powder
- Internal voice guiding: "Let it tell you when it's ready"
- Completed in ~20 minutes

- "My hands knew" — recurring motif
- No visible supernatural effect
- Katsuhito's observation: "Some [knowledge] is given"

- Believed packaged roux was "convenient but soulless"
- Philosophy: "Cooking was about putting yourself into the food"
- Confirmed as wise, thoughtful, skilled cook

- Asked directly: "Where did you learn this?"
- Sheldon's honest answer: "I don't know"
- Katsuhito's philosophy: "Some [knowledge] are older. Some are given."
- Did not press further; accepted mystery

- Excited about curry (enthusiasm carried from previous day)
- Given task: washing rice
- "Grandmother taught me!" — connection to deceased grandmother continues
- Working alongside Sheldon = bonding

#### Scene 4.B: The Tsukino House

"You should see it."

Katsuhito's voice was matter-of-fact as they finished breakfast—leftover rice, miso soup, the simple fare of a household between elaborate meals. The curry simmered on the stove, filling the kitchen with promises for dinner. Tenchi had been dispatched to feed the chickens, his protests fading into the morning air.

"The house?" Sheldon set down his chopsticks. "I was thinking the same thing."

"It's yours, according to your documents." The old priest's eyes were steady. "You should know what you've inherited."

*What I've inherited.* The words sat strangely in his mind. He'd inherited nothing—he'd been given a life, a history, documents that claimed he belonged to people he'd never met. But the house was real. The house existed regardless of how his connection to it had been created.

"Do you know where it is?"

"Down the mountain. Three hundred meters, perhaps less." Katsuhito gestured vaguely toward the east. "Past the second torii, down the stone path that forks left. You'll see it from the road—the dark house with the overgrown garden."

"Has anyone been inside recently?"

"Not in years. Villagers check on it occasionally—make sure the roof hasn't collapsed, no vagrants have moved in. But the family moved to Osaka long ago." A pause. "Your family, I suppose."

The correction hung between them, neither quite accurate and neither quite wrong.


The path down the mountain was different from the one to the village.

Narrower, less traveled, the stones worn smooth in different patterns. Trees pressed closer here, their branches meeting overhead to form a green tunnel that filtered the morning light into shifting coins of gold. Sheldon walked slowly, observing, letting his feet find their own way over the uneven ground.

He passed the second torii—smaller than the first, weathered red fading to pink at the edges—and found the fork Katsuhito had mentioned. Left led downhill toward a cluster of rooftops barely visible through the trees. Right continued along the ridge toward destinations unknown.

Left.

The path opened after fifty meters, trees giving way to a clearing where a house sat in patient silence.

Sheldon stopped.

*The dark house with the overgrown garden.* Katsuhito's description was accurate but insufficient. The house wasn't just dark—it was *quiet*, the kind of quiet that spoke of years without voices, without footsteps, without the small daily sounds that made a dwelling into a home.

It was a minka, a traditional farmhouse, single-story with dark wooden walls and a roof of traditional tiles. The architecture was modest but solid—the work of people who had built for permanence, not show. The engawa wrapped around two visible sides, its boards weathered to silver-grey.

And the garden. Oh, the garden.

What had once been cultivated had become wild. Bushes that might have been ornamental had grown into shapeless masses. Weeds rose knee-high through what Sheldon guessed had been a vegetable plot. A small maple tree—probably decorative, probably carefully pruned for decades—had sent up shoots in all directions, its disciplined shape lost to years of freedom.

But beneath the neglect, he could see the bones of something lovely. Stone paths, barely visible under growth. A small pond, choked with algae but still holding water. The suggestion of beds, borders, intentions.

*A house waiting for someone to return.*

He walked toward the front entrance.


The door was unlocked.

This surprised him until he remembered where he was—rural Japan, 1989, a village where everyone knew everyone and crime was something that happened in cities. The door slid open with a rasp of wood on wood, old and stiff but functional.

The smell hit him first: dust, age, the particular mustiness of enclosed spaces. Not rot—the house was dry, structurally sound—but the accumulated stillness of years without air circulation. He left the door open behind him, letting daylight and fresh breeze begin the process of awakening.

The genkan was small, traditional—a step down from the outside, then a step up to the raised floor. He removed his shoes and placed them neatly beside a rack that held nothing, a ghost of domestic organization.

Beyond the genkan, the main room opened wide and dim.

Dust cloths covered the furniture—white shapes in the darkness, like things sleeping under snow. He could make out the suggestion of a low table, floor cushions, a tansu cabinet against the far wall. The fusuma—sliding paper doors—stood closed, dividing the space into mysteries.

Sheldon moved through the room slowly, pulling back cloths as he went.

The table was old, solid, the kind that had hosted countless meals and would host countless more if given the chance. The wood beneath the dust was good—dark and polished, showing only superficial wear. The cushions had seen better days—fabric faded, stuffing compressed—but they were intact.

The tansu revealed itself as beautiful when uncovered, its wood grain rich even under accumulated grime. The drawers opened smoothly, empty except for the faint scent of cedar.

He opened the fusuma to the first small room.

A bedroom, or what had been one. Futon storage built into the wall, empty now. A low dressing table with a mirror clouded by age. On the wall, a scroll painting—mountains in mist, the brushwork confident and spare.

The second small room was similar in size but different in function. A desk against one wall, Western-style, with a chair tucked beneath. Bookshelves, mostly empty but showing the ghosts of spines in the dust patterns. A single book remained, fallen behind the lowest shelf—he retrieved it and found a Japanese-English dictionary, its pages yellowed but intact.

*Someone studied languages here.*

The kitchen was at the back—smaller than the Masaki shrine's, equipped for a family of two or three. A kamado stove, traditional, cold for years. A sink with taps that, when he tried them, produced nothing but a hollow groan from disconnected pipes. Shelves held dishes—simple ceramics, a few chipped but most sound. Pots and pans hung from hooks, dusty but functional.

Through the kitchen window, he could see the back garden—more overgrowth, what might have been a vegetable patch now consumed by grass and weeds. A shed stood at the property's edge, door hanging askew.

He didn't go outside yet. There was more to see indoors.


The engawa was where the house began to speak.

He slid open the exterior fusuma and stepped onto the veranda, feeling the boards give slightly under his weight. Katsuhito had been right—soft in places, the wood beginning to rot where rain had reached it. But the structure was sound. The joists beneath held firm. This was surface damage, repairable with time and resources.

He walked the length of the engawa, testing each section, cataloguing problems as he went. Three boards needed replacement on the south side. A post had begun to lean at the corner—not dangerous yet, but requiring attention. The roof above showed gaps where tiles had shifted or fallen, letting in water that had done its slow damage.

From the engawa, he could see the garden properly. The morning light was stronger now, revealing details hidden in the dimness of approach. The maple tree was actually quite lovely, its wild growth creating a canopy of shade over what had been a seating area—a stone bench, barely visible, moss-covered and patient. The pond reflected sky through its algae veil.

*This could be beautiful again.*

The thought emerged unbidden, and with it came something else—a sense of possibility, of potential, of work that could be done and results that could be achieved. He didn't know this house. He had no memories of running through these rooms as a child, no images of a mother cooking in that kitchen or a father reading at that desk. The photograph in his wallet showed people he'd never met, and the letter spoke of connections he couldn't feel.

But the house was real. The garden was real. The damage was real, and therefore the repair was possible.

*This could be home.*


He spent two hours making an assessment.

The roof was the most urgent issue. Missing tiles needed replacement, and some of the underlying structure had water damage. He climbed onto a storage chest to peer into the crawl space beneath the eaves and found rot in two support beams—not critical yet, but escalating. Yamada-san's estimate of ¥200,000-250,000 for roofing alone seemed accurate, perhaps optimistic.

The engawa repairs he could do himself, given time and materials. Lumber for replacement boards, some basic tools, the willingness to work. Call it ¥50,000 in materials if he did the labor.

Utilities were a separate consideration. Reconnecting water would require the town office, inspections, fees. Electric the same. He estimated ¥30,000-50,000 for each, plus whatever work the internal systems needed after years of dormancy.

The garden was labor, not money—hours of cutting, clearing, coaxing. He could do that himself, over time.

Interior cleaning the same. Soap and water and elbow grease. The dust would yield to effort.

Furniture was usable. Dishes were usable. The bones were good.

Total immediate needs: ¥300,000-400,000, assuming Yamada handled the roof and he did everything else himself.

He had ¥830,000.

*This is possible.*


He found the photograph album in the tansu's bottom drawer, hidden beneath a layer of rice paper.

He hadn't been looking for it. He'd been checking the drawer mechanism, testing for damage, and his fingers had touched the edge of something that wasn't supposed to be there. He pulled it out and sat on the floor with it in his lap, morning light slanting through the open fusuma.

The album was old, its cover fabric faded from deep blue to something closer to grey. The binding was loose but held. Inside, the pages were thick paper, photographs attached with corner mounts that had yellowed with age.

The first page showed a couple he didn't recognize—a man in traditional clothing, a woman in kimono, both young, both serious-faced in the way of formal photographs. The date written beneath: 1925.

*Grandparents?*

He turned pages. The couple aged gradually, photograph by photograph. A child appeared—a boy, serious like his parents. Then another. The family grew, changed, moved through time preserved in silver and paper.

He found Takeo.

The name was written beneath the photograph in careful characters. Takeo at five, Takeo at twelve, Takeo in his twenties wearing work clothes and something approaching a smile. The resemblance was there—not strong, but present. The shape of the jaw, perhaps. The set of the shoulders.

*My father. According to the documents.*

He felt nothing.

This was what disturbed him most—not the strangeness of finding himself in possession of a dead man's photographs, but the complete absence of connection. He should feel something. Grief, perhaps, for a father he'd never known. Curiosity about the life captured in these images. Recognition of features that might echo in his own face.

Instead, there was only observation. A man lived here. He had a family. They are gone. This remains.

Sheldon closed the album and returned it to its place beneath the rice paper.


He was still sitting on the floor when the knock came.

"Hello?" A woman's voice, uncertain. "Is someone there?"

He rose, brushing dust from his trousers, and went to the genkan. A woman stood outside—middle-aged, neat clothing, the kind of carefully arranged appearance that suggested effort. She had a basket over one arm.

"I'm sorry to intrude." Her eyes widened slightly as she took him in—the foreign features, the height, the unexpected presence in an empty house. "I saw the door open. I thought perhaps..."

"I'm Tsukino Sheldon." He bowed, the gesture becoming more natural with each repetition. "I'm the owner. Or—the family's representative, at least."

"Tsukino?" Her expression shifted through several emotions—surprise, recognition, something that might have been relief. "You're the one Mori-san mentioned. The one staying at the shrine."

News traveled fast in small villages. Sheldon wasn't surprised.

"I came to assess the house," he said. "See what condition it's in."

"It's been empty for years." The woman seemed to relax slightly, accepting his presence if not entirely comfortable with it. "We've tried to keep an eye on it—the neighborhood, I mean. Make sure nothing terrible happens."

"I appreciate that. The house is... it needs work, but it's not beyond saving."

"Will you—" She hesitated, then pushed forward. "Will you be moving in?"

The question hung in the morning air. Sheldon looked past her at the overgrown garden, the patient house, the life waiting to be reclaimed.

"Yes," he said. "I think I will."

#### Scene 4.C: The Afternoon

The neighbor's name was Tanaka Michiko.

She was, it turned out, the wife of Tanaka-san from the general store—the man who had sold Sheldon clothes two days ago with efficient precision and no wasted words. His wife was the opposite: warm, effusive, the kind of person who filled silences before they had a chance to form.

"My husband mentioned you," she said, following Sheldon back through the house as he closed the exterior fusuma and prepared to leave. "Said you bought work clothes. Good quality ones. I told him, 'That's the kind of man who plans to stay.' And here you are!"

"Here I am," Sheldon agreed.

"The house needs work, of course." She peered around him at the dusty interior. "But the bones are good. The Tsukinos built well, back in the day. My mother-in-law remembers when the family was still here—said they were quiet people, kept to themselves, but always helped when there was trouble."

She paused, perhaps realizing she was speaking of his alleged family to him. "I'm sorry. You probably know all this already."

"Actually, I don't." Sheldon slid the door closed behind them, sealing the house back into its patient silence. "I was raised in America. My mother talked about Japan, but not... not in detail. I'm learning as I go."

"Oh." Michiko's expression softened with something like sympathy. "Well. We're glad you've come back, then. Houses shouldn't sit empty. They get sad."

*Houses get sad.* It was a strange way to put it, anthropomorphizing a structure of wood and tile. But standing here, looking at the overgrown garden and the weathered walls, Sheldon thought he understood what she meant. The house *did* feel sad—not ruined, not abandoned, but waiting. Hoping. Ready to welcome whoever would give it life again.

"I'll be back," he told the house as much as Michiko. "Soon."


The walk back to the shrine felt shorter than the walk down.

Perhaps it was the slope—uphill now instead of down—or perhaps it was the lightness in his chest, the sense of purpose that came with having made a decision. He would move into the Tsukino house. He would repair it, clean it, restore it to something worthy of being called home. He had the money. He had the time. And apparently, he had skills he hadn't known he possessed.

The second torii appeared through the trees, and then the main grounds of the shrine opened before him—the courtyard, the buildings, the great tree standing patient in its place.

His eyes found the tree again.

*Beautiful*, he thought. *Old. Patient.* The same words that came to him every time he looked at it, as if no others were adequate. He didn't know why this particular tree drew his attention so consistently. There were other trees on the grounds, other ancient things worthy of admiration. But this one called to him in a way he couldn't name.

He walked past it without stopping. There would be time for contemplation later. Right now, there was curry to check on and a child who was probably wondering where he'd gone.


Tenchi found him before he reached the kitchen.

The boy came barreling around the corner of the main building, momentum carrying him several steps past Sheldon before he could stop and reverse course. "You're back! Grandfather said you went to the old house. Was it scary? Was there ghosts? Was it really old?"

"Very old," Sheldon confirmed. "But no ghosts. Just dust."

"I don't like dust." Tenchi wrinkled his nose. "It makes me sneeze."

"It makes everyone sneeze. That's what dust does."

"Why?"

"Because—" Sheldon paused, caught off guard by the simplicity of the question and the complexity of its answer. Because dust particles irritate the nasal passages, triggering a reflexive expulsion of air designed to clear the respiratory system of foreign material. That was the scientific answer, the one his physicist's mind supplied automatically.

But Tenchi was four, almost five. He didn't need cellular biology.

"Because your nose doesn't like things that don't belong there," Sheldon said instead. "It sneezes to push them out. Like when you blow a dandelion—" He mimed the action. "Your nose does that with dust."

"Oh." Tenchi considered this. "My nose is very smart."

"It is. All noses are."

"Even Grandfather's nose?"

"Especially Grandfather's nose. His nose has been sneezing for a very long time. It's had lots of practice."

Tenchi giggled at the image of his grandfather's nose practicing anything, and Sheldon felt that warm thing in his chest again—the one that seemed to grow every time he made this child laugh.


The curry had reduced beautifully in his absence.

He checked the pot, lifting the lid to release steam fragrant with spices and long-simmered vegetables. The homemade roux had dissolved completely, thickening the liquid into something rich and complex. The chicken, added mid-morning, had cooked through and begun to fall apart at the edges.

It needed rice. It needed bowls and spoons and hungry people to appreciate it. But first, it needed a few more hours—time for the flavors to deepen, for the sauce to concentrate, for everything to become more than the sum of its parts.

*Just like curry at home*, he thought, and then stopped.

Home. He'd thought of this kitchen—this shrine—as a point of reference for "home." Not the Tsukino house he'd just claimed, not the apartment in Atlanta he'd lived in for decades, not any of the other places he'd passed through in his life. This kitchen, here, where he'd been a guest for four days.

Was that strange? Was that too fast?

He decided it didn't matter. The feeling was what it was. He would let it be without needing to understand it.


The afternoon passed in small tasks.

He helped Katsuhito repair a section of fence that had come loose over the winter, holding boards in place while the old priest hammered with sure, economical strokes. The work was physical but not demanding—the kind of labor that let the mind wander while the hands stayed busy.

"I visited the house," Sheldon said during a pause between boards. "The Tsukino house."

"I assumed you would." Katsuhito selected another nail from the can at his feet. "What did you think?"

"It needs work. The roof especially. But it's... salvageable."

"Salvageable." The priest positioned the nail, tapped it once to set, then drove it home with three clean strikes. "That's an interesting word for an inheritance."

"It's an accurate word. The house has been empty for years. It's not in condition to live in without repairs."

"And you intend to make those repairs?"

"Yes."

Katsuhito set down the hammer and looked at Sheldon directly—the first time in the conversation he'd done so. "You've been here four days, Tsukino-san. In that time, you've cooked for my family, worked in my garden, walked to the village, and now you're planning to restore a house. Most people who find themselves in an unfamiliar place would still be... adjusting."

"I'm not most people."

"No." The old priest's eyes were unreadable. "You are not."

He picked up the hammer again and returned to work without further comment. Sheldon held the next board and wondered what exactly Katsuhito saw when he looked at him—what conclusions he was drawing, what questions he was choosing not to ask.


Tenchi helped set the table for dinner.

He carried bowls to the low table with exaggerated care, placing each one precisely where Katsuhito indicated it should go. Sheldon watched from the kitchen doorway, curry ladle in hand, and marveled at the boy's determination. Each bowl was a mission. Each placement was an achievement.

"Is the curry ready?" Tenchi asked for the sixth time in an hour.

"It's ready."

"Is it *really* ready?"

"Would I lie to you?"

Tenchi considered this. "No," he decided. "You don't lie. Your face is too honest."

Sheldon wasn't sure whether to be flattered or concerned by this assessment of his transparency. He settled for ladling curry into bowls while Katsuhito served rice, and soon the table was complete—three places, three bowls of curry, three portions of rice, steam rising in the evening light.

The first bite told him everything he needed to know.

The curry was *right*. Not just edible, not just acceptable—right. The homemade roux had given it depth that the packaged kind could never achieve. The long simmer had married the flavors into something greater than their individual parts. The spices sang in harmony rather than competing for attention.

He hadn't made curry before. He'd never learned, never practiced, never even watched someone else do it. And yet here it was: a dish worthy of praise, emerging from hands that moved with knowledge they hadn't earned.

Tenchi was already on his second helping.

"This is *good*," the boy announced between bites. "This is the best curry ever."

"You've had curry before?" Sheldon asked.

"Father makes it sometimes. From the box." Tenchi wrinkled his nose. "It's okay. But this is *better*."

Katsuhito ate in silence, as was his way. But his bowl was emptying steadily, and when he finished, he placed his chopsticks across the rim with the particular care of someone acknowledging quality.

"You said you hadn't made curry before," he said.

"I hadn't."

"And yet."

"And yet."

They sat in silence for a moment, the weight of unexplained competence hanging between them. Then Katsuhito rose to begin clearing dishes, and the moment passed.


After dinner, Sheldon found himself on the engawa again.

The evening had settled into that particular quality of stillness that came between the activity of day and the rest of night. Somewhere inside, Katsuhito was putting Tenchi to bed—the sound of small protests and patient responses drifting through the walls.

The great tree stood before him in the darkness, its shape barely visible against the stars. He couldn't see it clearly, but he could feel it somehow—a presence, patient and ancient, watching over the shrine as it had for centuries.

*I'm going to move into the house*, he thought. *I'm going to leave this place.*

The thought brought unexpected sadness. Four days, and he'd begun to feel like he belonged here. Four days, and the shrine had become something like home.

But the Tsukino house was his. By right of documents, by decision of will. He would restore it. He would live in it. He would make it into a home.

And the shrine would still be here. Three hundred meters up the mountain, a path through the trees, the second torii marking the boundary between worlds. He would visit. He would continue to cook, perhaps, if Katsuhito would allow it. He would watch Tenchi grow.

*This isn't an ending*, he told himself. *It's a beginning.*

The tree seemed to agree, though he couldn't have said how he knew.

#### Scene 4.D: Evening

"Grandfather says I have to go to bed."

"Then you should go to bed."

"But I'm not tired." The protest was undercut by a yawn that seemed to consume his entire face. "I wanted to ask you something."

Sheldon shifted on the engawa, making room beside him. The boy settled into the space with the boneless ease of children, his shoulder pressing against Sheldon's arm.

"What did you want to ask?"

"Are you going to leave?"

The question landed in the quiet like a stone in water. Sheldon looked down at Tenchi's face—upturned, serious, the expression of a child who had learned too young that people could leave and not come back.

"I'm going to move into a house nearby," he said carefully. "The old Tsukino house, down the mountain. But it's not far. I'll still visit."

"How far?"

"Three hundred meters. Maybe a little less."

Tenchi considered this, his brow furrowed with the effort of conceptualization. "Is that far?"

"It's about as far as the walk to the village torii. You could run there in two or three minutes."

"Oh." The furrow smoothed slightly. "That's not very far."

"No. It's not."

They sat in silence for a moment, watching the darkness deepen over the courtyard. The great tree was a shape against the stars now, its branches barely visible.

"Will you still make curry?"

"If Grandfather lets me use the kitchen."

"He will. He likes your cooking." Tenchi yawned again. "I like your cooking too."

"I'm glad."

"And I like you." The words came out matter-of-fact, the simple declaration of a child who had not yet learned to guard his heart. "I hope you stay forever."

Sheldon's chest tightened around something warm and sharp. "I can't promise forever. But I can promise I'll be here tomorrow. And the day after that. And the day after that. As far ahead as I can see."

Tenchi seemed to find this acceptable. He leaned more heavily against Sheldon's arm, his eyes beginning to drift closed despite his earlier protests.

"Okay," he murmured. "That's okay."


Katsuhito appeared a few minutes later to collect his grandson.

He didn't comment on finding them together on the engawa, Tenchi half-asleep against Sheldon's side. He simply lifted the boy with the ease of long practice and nodded once toward Sheldon.

"The kitchen is yours, if you wish to wash the dishes."

"I was planning to."

"I thought you might." The old priest paused at the door. "Tenchi asked you about leaving."

"He did."

"And your answer?"

"That I would be nearby. That I would visit. That I can't promise forever, but I can promise tomorrow."

Something shifted in Katsuhito's expression—not quite a smile, but something warmer than his usual reserve. "That is a wise answer. Children need honesty more than comfort. Comfort built on lies breaks when they need it most."

He disappeared into the house with Tenchi, and Sheldon was left alone on the engawa with the weight of that approval settling over him like a blanket.


The dishes were a familiar ritual now.

He stood at the sink with hot water and soap, scrubbing the remnants of curry from bowls and pot, watching the brown sauce swirl down the drain. The kitchen was quiet except for the sounds of his work—water running, ceramic clinking, the soft scrape of the brush against metal.

Five days ago, he hadn't existed.

The thought came unbidden, startling in its clarity. Five days ago—or rather, five days before his awakening in the field—there had been no Tsukino Sheldon. No man with this face, this body, this collection of documents claiming identity. He had been... elsewhere. Someone else. A physicist dying in a laboratory accident, the last thing he'd known the flash of white light and the certainty that everything was ending.

And then: cherry blossoms. A field. A wallet full of someone else's life.

*Except it's my life now*, he thought, setting a clean bowl in the drying rack. *However it happened. Whoever arranged it. This is who I am.*

The acceptance felt deeper tonight than it had before. Not resignation—he wasn't surrendering to circumstance—but integration. The man he'd been and the man he was becoming were beginning to merge, their edges blurring into something new.

He dried his hands and went to draw the bath.


The hot water embraced him like forgiveness.

He sat in the ofuro with water to his shoulders, steam rising around him, muscles unknotting in the heat. The day's labor released its hold—the fence repair, the walk up and down the mountain, the tension of standing in a house that belonged to strangers who shared his name.

*Not strangers*, he corrected himself. *Family.*

But they were strangers. The photograph album had proved that. He'd looked at Takeo's face and felt nothing—no recognition, no grief, no sense of connection. The documents said these people were his parents, his grandmother, his uncle. The documents were, in their way, accurate. But documents couldn't create feeling.

*Neither can absence*, he thought. *You can't miss what you never had.*

He'd never had parents, in this life. Never had a childhood spent in that house, learning to love those faces. His history had been created whole, inserted into reality like a key into a lock, functional but not earned.

The Masaki family was different.

He'd earned nothing there either—hadn't known them before four days ago, hadn't built relationship through years of shared experience. But something was growing nonetheless. Tenchi's hugs, Katsuhito's measured trust, the way the shrine was beginning to feel like somewhere he belonged. These connections were being built in real time, moment by moment, choice by choice.

*Maybe that's what matters*, he thought. *Not how you came to be somewhere, but what you do once you're there.*

The water had cooled slightly. He stayed a few minutes longer, letting the heat finish its work, then rose and dried himself and went to prepare for sleep.


The guest room waited for him, futon already laid out.

Katsuhito had done it again—prepared the sleeping space while Sheldon bathed, a small kindness offered without comment or expectation of thanks. The blanket was turned down. The pillow was positioned precisely. Everything was exactly as it should be.

Sheldon lay down and stared at the ceiling beams that had become familiar over four nights.

*Tomorrow*, he thought. *Tomorrow I'll talk to Yamada-san about the roof. Tomorrow I'll start making plans, creating timelines, turning the decision into reality.*

But tonight—tonight he could rest.

The sounds of the shrine wrapped around him: the distant murmur of wind in trees, the creak of old wood settling, the absolute quiet of a mountain without cities. Somewhere in the house, Katsuhito was doing whatever old priests did in the evening hours—reading, perhaps, or meditating, or simply being.

Somewhere closer, Tenchi was sleeping the deep sleep of childhood, dreaming whatever children dream.

And here, in this room that was beginning to feel like his, Sheldon let himself drift toward unconsciousness.

*Four days*, was his last coherent thought. *Four days, and I've found a family. Found a purpose. Found something worth building toward.*

The sleep that took him was deep and warm and full of nothing at all.


- Asked if Sheldon is leaving
- Learned about house move (~300m away)
- Comparison: "as far as walk to village torii"
- Declaration: "I like you. I hope you stay forever."
- Sheldon's promise: "I can't promise forever. But I can promise tomorrow."

**Katsuhito's Approval:**
- Observed Tenchi/Sheldon moment without comment
- Asked about Sheldon's answer re: leaving
- Response: "That is a wise answer. Children need honesty more than comfort."
- Continued laying out futon — small kindness maintained

**Sheldon's Internal Processing:**
- "Five days ago, he hadn't existed" — grasping magnitude
- Acceptance deepening: "integration" rather than "resignation"
- Differentiated created history (Tsukino family) vs. earned connection (Masaki family)
- Key insight: "Not how you came to be somewhere, but what you do once you're there"

**Emotional State (End of Day 4):**
- Peaceful
- Purposeful
- Integrated (past/present merging)
- Looking forward to tomorrow

- Morning: Curry prep, roux from scratch
- Mid-morning: Tsukino house assessment
- Afternoon: Return, fence repair, processing

---

#### Scene 5.A: Fifth Morning

The fifth morning arrived with rain.

Sheldon woke to the sound of it against the roof—a gentle drumming that had become background noise at some point in the night, easing him through dreams he couldn't remember. He lay in the futon for a moment, listening, cataloguing the differences this weather brought to the shrine's usual soundscape.

The birds were quieter. The wood creaked in different places, swelling with moisture. Somewhere in the house, he could hear the faint drip of water—not inside, but close, perhaps from an eave that channeled rain in a particular pattern.

*Good day for roofing arrangements*, he thought, and almost laughed at the irony.

He rose, dressed, and went to begin the morning.


Breakfast was simple: leftover rice, pickled vegetables, miso soup that Katsuhito had somehow assembled while Sheldon was still waking. The old priest moved through the kitchen with the efficiency of long practice, each motion purposeful, nothing wasted.

"I'm going to find Yamada-san today," Sheldon said over his bowl. "About the roof."

Katsuhito nodded. "He lives on the east side of the village. Past Ito-san's store, left at the shrine, third house with the blue tiles." A pause. "He doesn't work in rain. You'll find him home."

"Should I bring anything? As introduction?"

"Your business will be introduction enough. Yamada-san cares about work, not ceremony." Katsuhito set down his chopsticks. "He is a man of few words and many opinions. Don't be offended if he tests you before accepting the job."

"Tests me how?"

"He'll want to see the house himself. He'll want to know you understand what needs doing, and why. He doesn't work for people who don't respect the craft."

Sheldon absorbed this. It reminded him, distantly, of advisors he'd worked with in his previous life—academics who cared more about intellectual rigor than social niceties, who tested students with questions before committing to mentor them.

"I'll try not to disappoint him."

"I don't think you will." Katsuhito's eyes held that measuring quality Sheldon had come to recognize. "You understand work. He'll see that."


The rain had softened to a mist by the time he descended the mountain.

Sheldon took his time on the steps, each one slick with water that made the stone gleam like polished glass. The forest pressed close on either side, greener than it had been yesterday, drinking in the moisture with visible enthusiasm. Droplets hung from every leaf, trembling in the faint breeze before falling to join their fellows on the ground below.

Three hundred twenty steps. He'd counted them his first day, exhausted and uncertain, and now they felt almost routine—a transition between worlds rather than an obstacle to be overcome.

The village appeared through the mist as a collection of shapes: rooftops first, then walls, then the familiar layout of the main street. Few people were about in this weather—an elderly woman with an umbrella, hurrying toward the general store; a cat watching the rain from beneath a wagon; the distant sound of someone hammering, muffled by moisture.

Sheldon found Ito-san's store first, its windows glowing with warm light against the grey morning. He nodded to the old man through the glass and received a wave in return—Haruki's wife must have mentioned the encounter yesterday, because the gesture carried the ease of established acquaintance rather than cautious curiosity.

Left at the shrine. A small one, not like the Masaki grounds, just a weathered torii and a stone marker and a collection box that probably saw more visitors during festivals than ordinary days. Third house with the blue tiles.

The house was modest, well-maintained, with a workshop attached to the side that was larger than the living quarters. The roof tiles were indeed blue—distinctive, probably custom work, the kind of thing a roofer might do to advertise his skill.

Sheldon knocked on the workshop door.


Yamada Kenji was sixty-three years old, built like a barrel, and had eyes that seemed to take measurements of everything they saw.

"Tsukino, is it." Not a question. Word traveled fast in small villages. "Katsuhito-sensei's guest. The one with the old house."

"Yes."

"Come in, then. Close the door—the damp is bad for the tools."

The workshop was a revelation: organized chaos that was actually precise order, every tool hanging in its designated place, every material sorted by type and size. Stacks of tiles lined one wall—traditional clay, various colors, some clearly salvaged and restored. Wood samples occupied another section. A workbench dominated the center of the space, currently hosting what looked like a half-completed repair on a wooden beam.

"Sit." Yamada gestured to a stool near the workbench. "Tea?"

"Please."

The roofer moved to a small kettle in the corner, his movements economical but not rushed. He poured two cups, handed one to Sheldon, and settled onto his own stool with the air of a man preparing for serious conversation.

"The Tsukino house," he said. "I've seen it. Drove by six months ago, noticed the tiles." He took a sip of tea. "You want me to fix the roof."

"I want you to assess it first. Tell me what needs to be done and what it will cost. Then I'll decide."

Something shifted in Yamada's expression—not quite approval, but perhaps the absence of disapproval. "You've looked at it yourself."

"Yesterday. I counted missing tiles, checked for water damage, looked at the support beams in the crawl space." Sheldon listed his findings methodically: the gaps in the tile coverage, the rot in two support beams, the evidence of water intrusion along the south-facing eave. "I estimated between two hundred and two hundred fifty thousand yen for materials and labor, but I wanted a professional opinion."

Yamada listened without interruption. When Sheldon finished, he set down his cup and studied his guest with those measuring eyes.

"You're not a builder."

"No."

"But you know how to look at a building."

"I know how to observe. How to identify patterns. How to recognize when something isn't working the way it should."

"What did you do? Before you came here?"

The question could have been intrusive. From Yamada, it felt like professional curiosity—one craftsman asking another about their trade.

"I was a scientist. A physicist. I studied theoretical models of how the universe works." Sheldon paused, considering how to translate this for a man who built things with his hands. "I looked at problems and tried to understand their underlying structure. What caused them. How they connected to other problems."

"Hmm." Yamada rose from his stool and went to a cabinet, retrieving a rolled paper that he spread across the workbench. It was a diagram—cross-section of a traditional roof, every beam and joint labeled in careful handwriting.

"This is how a roof works," he said. "The tiles keep out water. The underlayment guides water that gets through. The beams carry weight. The joists distribute load. Every piece depends on every other piece." He traced connections with a calloused finger. "You can replace a tile here, shore up a beam there. But if you don't understand how they fit together, you fix one problem and create three more."

"Like a system," Sheldon said. "Change one variable and the effects propagate."

"Yes." Yamada met his eyes. "Exactly like that."

They spent the next hour discussing the house. Yamada asked questions—specific, technical, probing—and listened to the answers with the attention of someone who was genuinely interested rather than simply being polite. By the end, Sheldon had learned more about traditional Japanese roofing than he'd ever expected to know, and Yamada had formed whatever opinion he needed to form.

"I'll come look at the house tomorrow," the roofer said finally. "If the rain clears. We'll walk the roof together, and I'll give you a real estimate."

"Thank you."

"Don't thank me yet." Yamada's mouth twitched—not quite a smile, but something close. "I haven't agreed to do the work. I've agreed to look. Looking is free."

"Fair enough."

"You can find your own way out." The roofer turned back to his workbench, picking up a tool that looked like a specialized chisel. "Tell Katsuhito-sensei his roof beam is ready when he wants it."


The rain had stopped entirely by the time Sheldon emerged from the workshop.

The sky remained grey, but lighter now—the kind of overcast that might break into sun by afternoon or might persist through evening, undecided about its intentions. Water still dripped from eaves and pooled in the shallow places of the road, but the steady fall had ceased.

Sheldon walked through the village slowly, taking in details he'd missed on previous visits. A bakery he hadn't noticed before, its window displaying bread in shapes he didn't recognize. A small hardware store, doors open to let in the fresh post-rain air. Children in school uniforms, emerging from houses with umbrellas they wouldn't need, heading toward wherever children went on Friday mornings.

*This is my village now*, he thought. *Or it will be.*

The thought felt strange but not unwelcome. He was building something here—not just a house, but a life. Connections and competencies and the slow accumulation of belonging that came from showing up, day after day, and doing the work.

He stopped at Ito-san's store on the way back, buying eggs and green onions and a small bottle of vinegar he thought might be useful. Haruki chatted at him enthusiastically while Fumiko observed from behind the counter, her sharp eyes taking note of his purchases and probably drawing conclusions about what he intended to cook.

"You're settling in well," Haruki said as he made change. "Mori-san says you've been helping at the shrine. Yamada-san will be glad to have the work—he's been complaining about being bored."

"He didn't seem bored to me."

"Ha! Yamada-san always seems busy. But he likes a real project. The Tsukino house—that's a real project." Haruki handed over the bag. "Good luck with it."

"Thank you."

The walk back up the mountain was long but pleasant, the air washed clean by rain, the forest gleaming with moisture and the particular green that came after storms. Sheldon carried his purchases and his thoughts and the quiet satisfaction of a morning well spent.

Tomorrow, Yamada would look at the roof.

Tomorrow, the work would begin in earnest.

But today—today was for cooking, and contemplation, and the small rituals of belonging that were becoming, slowly but surely, his own.


- "You fix one problem and create three more" if you don't understand the system
- Respects craft; wants clients who understand what they're paying for
- "Looking is free" — will assess before committing

- Sheldon demonstrated observational competence
- Sheldon compared systems: roof as interconnected system, like physics
- Yamada responded positively to "systems thinking" approach

#### Scene 5.B: The Afternoon

The shrine was quiet when Sheldon returned, the particular silence of a place whose inhabitants had departed on their own errands.

Katsuhito had left a note on the kitchen table: *Gone to town. Back before dinner. Rice in cooker.* The handwriting was neat, each character precisely formed, the kind of penmanship that came from decades of practice.

Sheldon set down his purchases and stood for a moment in the empty kitchen, listening to the house settle around him. Outside, the post-rain world dripped and sparkled, sunlight beginning to break through the clouds and set everything gleaming.

He had several hours to himself. Tenchi wouldn't return from kindergarten until mid-afternoon. Katsuhito was in town. The shrine waited, patient as always, for whatever he chose to do with the time.

*Cook*, the voice suggested—the one that wasn't quite his own, or wasn't quite the voice he remembered. *You have ingredients. You have time. Cook.*

He looked at the eggs, the green onions, the vinegar. Something was forming in his mind—not a plan exactly, but a shape. A possibility.

His hands began to move.


Tamagoyaki again, but different this time.

The basic technique had come to him on Day 3, imperfect at first, then improving with each attempt. Now he wanted to try a variation—something that used the vinegar, that incorporated the green onions, that pushed beyond the familiar into new territory.

*Pickled eggs*, the voice murmured. *Not Japanese. American South. But the technique translates.*

He boiled six eggs until they were just set, the yolks still slightly soft in their centers. While they cooled, he sliced the green onions and combined them with the vinegar, sugar he found in the pantry, and a splash of soy sauce for depth. A quick pickling liquid, improvised from what was available.

The peeled eggs went into a glass jar with the liquid and the green onions. He sealed it and set it aside to marinate.

*That will need time*, the voice said. *Hours, at least. But they'll be good.*

He trusted it. He was learning to trust it.


With the eggs marinating, he turned to the kitchen itself.

It needed cleaning—not because it was dirty, exactly, but because it could be cleaner. The kind of deep cleaning that went beyond daily maintenance, that addressed the accumulated film of months and years. Sheldon found a bucket, filled it with hot water and the cleaning soap Katsuhito kept under the sink, and began.

He started with the surfaces—counters, cabinets, the area around the stove where oil had splattered and been wiped away without being fully removed. The work was meditative, repetitive, the kind of labor that occupied the hands and freed the mind.

*Houses need care*, he thought, scrubbing at a stubborn spot near the rice cooker. *Not just repair, but care. Attention to the small things that accumulate into larger things.*

The Tsukino house would need this too—not just structural repair but cleaning, organizing, making habitable a space that had been empty too long. Yamada could fix the roof, could shore up the beams, but someone would need to address the dust, the cobwebs, the staleness of rooms that hadn't held life in years.

*I can do that*, he realized. *The physical work, the patient attention. I can give the house what it needs.*

The kitchen gleamed by the time he finished, surfaces reflecting the afternoon light that had finally broken through the clouds. He emptied the bucket, rinsed the cloth, and stepped back to admire his work.

It was small. It was domestic. It mattered.


Tenchi's return was announced by the sound of small feet on stone.

Sheldon was on the engawa when the bus arrived, sitting with a cup of tea he'd made himself, watching the shrine grounds dry in the emerging sun. The steps echoed with descending footsteps, and then a small voice called out—

"Sheldon-san! You're still here!"

The same greeting as yesterday, and the day before. The same joy, the same relief, as if each return was a small miracle that might not repeat.

"I'm still here," Sheldon confirmed. "How was school?"

Tenchi appeared at the top of the steps, cheeks flushed from the climb, backpack dangling from one shoulder. He dropped it on the engawa and settled beside Sheldon with the boneless ease of childhood.

"We learned about frogs," he announced. "Sensei showed us pictures. Did you know frogs start as dots?"

"Eggs," Sheldon said. "They start as eggs, and then they become tadpoles, and then they become frogs."

"Tadpoles are the wiggly ones with tails?"

"Yes."

"I want to see real tadpoles." Tenchi's eyes went wide with sudden inspiration. "Can we go look for them? There's a pond near the old tree. Grandfather says there are frogs there sometimes."

The old tree. The great tree in the courtyard, the one Sheldon's eyes always found without quite meaning to. He hadn't known there was a pond nearby—another detail of the shrine grounds he'd yet to explore.

"We can look," he said. "After you have a snack."

"Okay!" Tenchi scrambled up, already heading for the kitchen. "What's for snack?"

Sheldon thought of the eggs marinating in their jar—not ready yet, but interesting. "Onigiri," he said. "With pickled plum, if there's any left."

"There's always pickled plum. Grandmother made a lot."

Another mention of the grandmother—Katsuhito's wife, dead now but still present in preserved foods and careful mentions. Sheldon followed Tenchi into the kitchen, wondering how many years of pickled plum remained, and whether anyone would ever make more.


The pond was small and hidden.

It sat in a hollow behind the great tree, screened from view by a natural arrangement of rocks and bushes that suggested deliberate design—a garden feature, perhaps, from decades or centuries past. The water was clear despite the rain, fed by some underground source that kept it fresh.

Tenchi crouched at the edge, peering into the depths with the focused intensity only children could sustain. "I don't see any tadpoles."

"It might be too early in the season. Or too late in the day." Sheldon crouched beside him, studying the water. Small insects skated across the surface. Green things grew at the edges—water plants he couldn't identify. "Frogs are more active at dawn and dusk."

"Can we come back at dawn?"

"Maybe. If you wake up early enough."

"I can wake up early!" Tenchi's protest was undermined by a yawn that seemed to surprise him as much as anyone. "I'm not tired."

"Of course not."

They stayed by the pond for a while longer, watching for movement, pointing out things that caught their attention—a dragonfly, a leaf floating on the surface, the way the light filtered through the overhanging branches. The great tree rose behind them, its presence felt even when Sheldon wasn't looking at it.

*Beautiful*, he thought. *Old. Patient.*

The same words, every time. As if no others were adequate.


Katsuhito returned as the sun was beginning to lower.

He found Sheldon in the kitchen, preparing dinner—rice was already cooking, vegetables were sliced for miso soup, and the pickled eggs had been retrieved from their jar and cut into careful quarters.

"You cleaned," the old priest observed, looking around at the gleaming surfaces.

"I had time."

"Hmm." Katsuhito examined the egg quarters with interest. "What are these?"

"Something my grandmother used to make." The words came easily now—the blending of Dorothy's recipes with the created history of Sachiko, the truth that was also a lie. "Eggs pickled in vinegar and green onion. They're good with rice."

Katsuhito picked up a quarter and tasted it, chewing thoughtfully. His expression gave nothing away.

"Different," he said finally. "But good."

"Thank you."

"Tenchi will want to try them."

"I saved him some. They're his favorite color." Sheldon gestured to the jar, where the remaining eggs had taken on a pale greenish tint from the onion.

"Green is not his favorite color. That would be red."

"Then they're his new favorite color."

Something that might have been amusement crossed Katsuhito's face. "We'll see."


Dinner was a small celebration of the ordinary.

Tenchi did, in fact, declare the green eggs his new favorite, eating three quarters before being reminded that other foods existed. The miso soup was good—Sheldon's confidence with Japanese cooking growing with each attempt—and the rice was perfect, as always.

"Yamada-san will come tomorrow," Sheldon said over tea. "To look at the house."

Katsuhito nodded. "He mentioned you'd visited. Said you understood what needed doing."

"I hope so."

"Yamada-san doesn't say things he doesn't mean." The old priest set down his cup. "He also said I should tell you the beam is ready. He'll bring it when he comes for the inspection."

"The beam for the shrine?"

"For the engawa. One of the joists has been weakening. I asked him to prepare a replacement three months ago." Katsuhito's eyes held that measuring quality again. "He's been busy. But he's making time now."

The implication was clear: Yamada was expediting Sheldon's roof inspection because he'd been impressed by their meeting. The craftsman didn't waste time on people he didn't respect.

"I'll try to be useful while he's here," Sheldon said.

"I'm sure you will."


The evening settled into familiar patterns.

Tenchi demanded stories, which Katsuhito provided with the practiced ease of a grandfather who had told these tales many times before. Sheldon listened from the kitchen, washing dishes, the priest's low voice drifting through the house like incense.

The story was about a crane—a woman who transformed into a bird to repay a kindness, weaving feathers into cloth of impossible beauty. Sheldon recognized the tale from somewhere, though he couldn't have said where. Perhaps Dorothy had told it, or perhaps he'd read it in a book. Perhaps it was simply one of those stories that existed everywhere, in every culture, wearing different clothes but carrying the same heart.

When the dishes were done, he joined them in the main room. Tenchi had migrated into his grandfather's lap, eyes heavy but fighting sleep with the determination of the very young.

"Sheldon-san," the boy murmured. "Are you going to be here tomorrow?"

"Yes."

"And the day after?"

"Yes."

"And the day after that?"

"As far as I can see."

Tenchi's eyes drifted closed, satisfied. Katsuhito caught Sheldon's gaze over the boy's head, and something passed between them—not words, but understanding. Acknowledgment of what was being built, day by day, promise by promise.

*Five days*, Sheldon thought. *Five days, and this is becoming home.*

He didn't fight the thought anymore. He let it be.

#### Scene 5.C: Evening

"He never makes it to the end," Katsuhito murmured, more to himself than to Sheldon. "Twenty years I've been telling this story, and no one has ever stayed awake for the ending."

"What happens at the end?"

"She flies away. The husband breaks his promise and opens the door to watch her weave, and she has no choice but to leave." The old priest shifted Tenchi's weight, preparing to stand. "All the old stories end with someone leaving. The Japanese have never been comfortable with permanence."

Sheldon thought about that as Katsuhito carried Tenchi toward the bedrooms. Leaving and staying. Promises kept and broken. The crane who couldn't remain once she'd been seen for what she truly was.

*Would I fly away*, he wondered, *if someone opened the door?*

He didn't have wings. He didn't have anywhere to fly to. Whatever he was now—whatever he'd been made into—this was his only life. There was no going back to Atlanta, to the lab, to the man he'd been before the white light and the cherry blossoms.

*Maybe that's the difference*, he thought. *She had somewhere to return to. I don't.*


The dishes from dinner were few, easily handled.

Sheldon washed them by the light of the kitchen lamp, the window above the sink showing only darkness and the faint reflection of his own face. He looked tired—not exhausted, but worn in the way that came from a full day of doing things that mattered.

Tomorrow, Yamada would come to inspect the house. Tomorrow, numbers would be assigned to problems, and problems would begin their transformation into solutions. Tomorrow, the abstract idea of "restoration" would become concrete, measurable, actionable.

*One step at a time*, he told himself. *That's how you build anything. One step, and then the next.*

He dried the last bowl and set it in its place.


The bath was hot and quiet.

He soaked in the ofuro, water to his shoulders, steam rising around him in the lamplight. The day replayed behind his closed eyes: Yamada's workshop, the smell of cedar and clay; the pond behind the great tree, Tenchi's earnest search for tadpoles; Katsuhito's story, the crane weaving beauty from her own body.

*Five days.*

The count had become a kind of ritual, a way of marking progress through territory that had no other landmarks. Five days since waking in the field. Five days of learning names, learning routines, learning the shape of a life he hadn't chosen but was beginning to claim.

The water cooled slowly. He stayed until it was merely warm, then rose and dried himself and went to prepare for sleep.


The guest room had become familiar.

The ceiling beams, the paper screens, the particular quality of darkness that gathered in the corners—all of it registered now as *known* rather than *strange*. He'd slept here five nights. His body had learned the futon's contours, the way the pillow compressed, the sounds that meant safety versus the sounds that meant attention.

*This is mine now*, he thought, lying down. *This room, this routine, this place in the world.*

Not forever. The Tsukino house waited down the mountain, empty and patient, ready to become his in a different way. But for now—for these last weeks or months of transition—this room was home.

He closed his eyes and listened to the shrine settle around him.

Somewhere in the house, Katsuhito was doing whatever old priests did in the final hours before sleep. Somewhere closer, Tenchi was dreaming whatever children dreamed—frogs, perhaps, or cranes, or the promise that someone would still be there tomorrow.

*I'll be there*, Sheldon thought. *Tomorrow and the day after. As far as I can see.*

Sleep came gently, carrying him into darkness without dreams.


- "All the old stories end with someone leaving"
- Katsuhito: "The Japanese have never been comfortable with permanence"

**Sheldon's Reflection:**
- Compared self to crane
- Key difference: "She had somewhere to return to. I don't."
- Atlanta, lab, previous life = closed doors
- This life = only option

- Bath
- Guest room
- Sleep by ~9:30 PM

- Afternoon: Pickled eggs; kitchen cleaning; pond exploration
- Evening: Dinner; Crane Wife story; bath; sleep
