#### Scene 6.A: Sixth Morning

The sixth morning arrived with clear skies and the particular stillness that followed rain.

Sheldon woke to light streaming through the paper screens, brighter than it had been yesterday, carrying the promise of a day suited for outdoor work. He lay in the futon for a moment, listening to the shrine settle around him, then rose and began his preparation.

Today was the inspection. Today, Yamada would walk the roof of the Tsukino house and turn Sheldon's amateur observations into professional assessment. Today, the abstract concept of "restoration" would acquire numbers, timelines, costs.

*Today*, he thought, *things become real.*


Breakfast was quieter than usual.

Tenchi seemed subdued, pushing rice around his bowl rather than eating with his typical enthusiasm. Katsuhito watched his grandson with an expression Sheldon couldn't quite read—not concern exactly, but attention. Awareness.

"Is something wrong?" Sheldon asked.

Tenchi looked up, then back at his rice. "Father's coming today."

The words landed in the silence with unexpected weight. Sheldon had known, abstractly, that Nobuyuki Masaki visited on weekends—Katsuhito had mentioned it that first evening, a fact filed away among dozens of others. But knowing and experiencing were different things.

"That's good," Sheldon said carefully. "You must miss him during the week."

"Yeah." Tenchi's voice was small. "He stays in my room sometimes. The one you're in."

*The guest room.* The room Sheldon had been sleeping in for six nights—Nobuyuki's childhood room, still holding his belongings, still belonging to him in ways Sheldon hadn't fully considered.

"Should I—" He hesitated. "Should I move my things? Give you and your father space?"

"No!" Tenchi's response was immediate, alarmed. "I didn't mean—I want you to stay. I was just saying."

Katsuhito set down his chopsticks. "Nobuyuki won't arrive until evening. He works half-days on Saturday." A pause. "You'll meet him then. For now, you have other business."

The dismissal was gentle but clear: deal with the house first, the family dynamics later. Sheldon nodded and finished his breakfast in silence.


Yamada arrived at the shrine precisely at eight o'clock.

He drove a small truck—white, practical, with a ladder rack on top and wooden crates visible in the bed. When he emerged from the cab, he was carrying a leather bag that clinked with tools and a clipboard thick with papers.

"Tsukino." The greeting was a nod, nothing more.

"Yamada-san. Thank you for coming."

"Brought the beam for Katsuhito-sensei." Yamada gestured to the truck bed, where a heavy timber lay among the crates. "We'll unload it after. House first."

They walked down the mountain together, following the path Sheldon had learned by now—past the second torii, left at the fork, through the green tunnel of overhanging branches. Yamada walked in silence, his eyes moving constantly, noting things Sheldon couldn't identify. Assessing.

The Tsukino house emerged from the trees as it had before: quiet, patient, waiting. In the morning light, the overgrown garden looked less melancholy than it had on his first visit—more wild than neglected, full of potential rather than loss.

Yamada stopped at the edge of the property and studied the building with the intensity of a doctor examining a patient.

"Hmm," he said. It was not an encouraging sound.


The inspection took three hours.

Yamada moved through the house systematically, starting with the exterior and working inward. He climbed onto the roof using his own ladder, walking the tiles with the confidence of a man who had spent decades doing exactly this. Sheldon watched from below, cataloguing the places where Yamada paused, crouched, took notes.

Inside, the roofer examined the crawl space Sheldon had found, confirming the rot in the support beams and identifying additional damage Sheldon had missed. He tested the engawa boards with his boot heel, marked the soft ones with chalk, checked the posts and joists beneath.

Sheldon followed him, answering questions when asked, staying silent otherwise. This was Yamada's domain, and Sheldon was here to learn.

"You were right about the two beams," Yamada said finally, emerging from the crawl space and dusting off his knees. "But there's a third—small section, easy to miss. And the underlayment along the south eave needs replacement, not just the tiles."

He led Sheldon outside and pointed upward, tracing lines Sheldon couldn't see from the ground.

"Water gets in here—" A gap in the tiles. "Runs along here—" A channel in the structure. "And collects here—" The corner where the damage was worst. "Been doing it for years. Previous owners probably patched it, but patches don't last."

"What would you recommend?"

Yamada was silent for a long moment, still looking at the roof. Then he turned to Sheldon with an expression that was almost—but not quite—approving.

"Full roof work. Replace the damaged tiles—about forty of them. New underlayment for the south and east sections. Three beam repairs. Flashing around the chimney." He pulled out his clipboard and began writing. "Two weeks of work, maybe three. Depends on weather."

"And the cost?"

The number Yamada named was higher than Sheldon's estimate—not by much, but enough to notice. ¥280,000 for materials and labor, paid in installments as work progressed.

"That includes my time," Yamada added. "I don't usually do labor estimates, but you seem like someone who understands work. Wouldn't feel right padding it."

Sheldon did the math in his head. After the roof, he would still have over ¥500,000—enough for the other repairs, utilities, living expenses. It was tight but manageable.

"When can you start?"

Yamada's eyebrows rose slightly—surprise at the quick decision, perhaps. "Monday. If the weather holds."

"Then Monday it is."

They shook hands. Yamada's grip was firm, calloused, the handshake of a man who meant what he said and expected the same in return.


They walked back up the mountain in companionable silence.

The shrine appeared through the trees, and with it the sound of voices—Tenchi's high and bright, and another that Sheldon didn't recognize. Deeper, adult, male.

*Nobuyuki*, he thought. *Earlier than expected.*

The courtyard came into view, and there he was: a man in his thirties or forties, dressed in the kind of casual clothing that suggested a professional relaxing on his day off. He was crouched near the great tree, listening to something Tenchi was explaining with animated gestures.

He looked up as Sheldon and Yamada approached, and for a moment their eyes met.

Sheldon saw: curiosity, wariness, the protective assessment of a father encountering a stranger in his child's life. Dark hair, glasses, the faint lines around the eyes that came from smiling often or squinting at detailed work. The architect. The weekend father. The man whose childhood room Sheldon had been sleeping in.

Nobuyuki saw: a tall Black man in work clothes, walking alongside the village roofer, arriving at his family's shrine with the ease of someone who belonged there.

The moment stretched.

Then Yamada broke the tension with a grunt and a nod. "Nobuyuki-san. Brought your father's beam."

"Yamada-san." Nobuyuki stood, and the protective assessment flickered into something more neutral—recognition of a familiar face, the social reflex of greeting before evaluating. "Father mentioned it was ready."

"This is Tsukino Sheldon," Yamada said, gesturing. "The one fixing up the old Tsukino place."

Nobuyuki turned back to Sheldon. "So I've heard. Tenchi hasn't stopped talking about you since I arrived."

"I hope good things."

"Very good things. He says you make better curry than I do." A hint of a smile, rueful and genuine. "He's probably right. I've never been much of a cook."

The tension eased, not entirely gone but manageable. Nobuyuki offered his hand, and Sheldon shook it—another firm grip, another handshake between men who didn't know yet what they would be to each other.

"Welcome," Nobuyuki said. "I understand you've been helping around the shrine."

"Trying to. Your father has been very generous."

"He has his reasons." Something flickered in Nobuyuki's expression—knowledge, or suspicion, or simply the awareness of a son who knew his father kept secrets. "We should talk later. After dinner, perhaps."

"I'd like that."

Yamada cleared his throat. "Beam needs unloading. Nobuyuki-san, you mind helping?"

The moment passed. Work resumed. But Sheldon felt the weight of what had just happened—the introduction, the assessment, the beginning of something that would need to be navigated carefully in the days ahead.

Tenchi appeared at his side as the men moved toward the truck.

"That's my father," the boy said, with a mixture of pride and something like anxiety. "He works very hard."

"I can tell."

"He likes you." Tenchi's confidence was absolute, based on evidence invisible to adults. "I can tell too."

Sheldon hoped the boy was right.


- "Been doing it for years. Previous owners probably patched it."

#### Scene 6.B: The Afternoon

The beam was heavier than it looked.

Sheldon helped Yamada and Nobuyuki wrestle it from the truck bed, the timber solid in his hands, smelling of fresh-cut cedar. They carried it to the side of the main building and laid it on supports Katsuhito had prepared, the old priest directing placement with quiet precision.

"Good wood," Nobuyuki said, running a hand along the grain. "Yamada-san, where did you source it?"

"Tanaka Jiro's cousin. Has a stand of cedar up near Niimi. Knows how to dry it proper." Yamada was already climbing back into his truck. "I'll be back Monday. Tsukino, have the house open by eight."

"I'll be there."

The truck departed, leaving the four of them in the suddenly quiet courtyard—Sheldon, Nobuyuki, Katsuhito, and Tenchi, who had been watching the whole operation from a safe distance with the fascination of a child who loved watching adults do physical work.

"Well," Nobuyuki said, brushing sawdust from his hands. "That's done."

"Thank you for your help," Sheldon offered.

"It's my father's shrine." A slight smile, not unfriendly. "Least I can do."

Tenchi broke from his observation post and ran to his father's side, tugging at his sleeve. "Father, Sheldon-san made pickled eggs yesterday. Green ones. They're really good. Can we have them for lunch?"

Nobuyuki looked at Sheldon with something that might have been amusement. "Green eggs?"

"The green onion gives them color. They're—" Sheldon hesitated. "They're something my grandmother used to make."

"American style?"

"A hybrid, I suppose."

Nobuyuki's expression was unreadable for a moment. Then he nodded. "I'd like to try them."


Lunch was an exercise in careful navigation.

Sheldon prepared it—the remaining pickled eggs, rice, miso soup, pickled vegetables from Katsuhito's stores. Nothing elaborate, but solid. The kind of meal that said *competent* without saying *showing off*.

They ate at the low table in the main room, Tenchi positioned strategically between his father and his grandfather, stealing glances at both adults and at Sheldon in turn. The boy was vibrating with barely contained energy, the kind that came from having all his people in one place and not knowing how to make them fit together.

"Father says you're from America," Nobuyuki said between bites. "But you speak Japanese very well."

"My mother taught me." The familiar story, growing smoother with each telling. "She wanted me to know my heritage."

"And your father?"

"American. They divorced when I was young."

Nobuyuki nodded, accepting this without pressing further. He was good at reading social cues, Sheldon noticed—knew when to ask and when to let things be. An architect's precision applied to human interaction.

"The eggs are good," Nobuyuki added. "Different from what I'm used to, but good."

"Thank you."

Tenchi beamed, as if the compliment had been directed at him personally.


The afternoon passed in domestic rhythms.

Nobuyuki spent time with Tenchi—walking the shrine grounds, examining the great tree together, answering the endless questions that children asked when reunited with absent parents. Sheldon kept his distance, occupying himself with small tasks around the residence, giving father and son space to reconnect.

But he watched, when he could. The way Nobuyuki crouched to Tenchi's level. The way his hand rested on the boy's shoulder. The careful attention of a man who knew his time was limited and wanted to make it count.

*He loves his son*, Sheldon thought. *Genuinely, deeply, despite the distance.*

It was both reassuring and complicated. Reassuring because Tenchi deserved that love, needed it in ways that went beyond what a temporary guest could provide. Complicated because Sheldon's presence changed the equation—added another adult to a system that had found its balance, disrupted patterns that had been working well enough.

*I'm not trying to replace you*, he thought, watching Nobuyuki lift Tenchi onto his shoulders. *I'm just... here.*

Whether that was enough, or too much, remained to be seen.


Dinner was Sheldon's domain.

He'd claimed the kitchen mid-afternoon, waving off Katsuhito's half-hearted offer to help. This was something he could do—something concrete to contribute, a way of demonstrating value that didn't require explaining mysteries he couldn't explain.

The menu came to him without conscious planning: grilled fish (fresh from the village, purchased that morning by Katsuhito), rice (always rice), vegetables sautéed with garlic and a touch of soy sauce, clear soup with tofu and green onion. Simple but careful. Each element given attention.

*Cook for the table you have*, the voice suggested. *Four people, four different needs. Feed them all.*

He found himself adjusting portions, considering preferences he'd observed over six days. Tenchi liked things slightly sweet; Katsuhito preferred subtle flavors; Nobuyuki was an unknown, but architects appreciated balance and proportion.

The meal came together in the quiet concentration that cooking always brought him now—hands moving, mind settling, the world narrowing to the immediate tasks of heat and timing and taste.


They ate by lamplight.

The sun had set during preparation, and Katsuhito had lit the traditional lamps rather than switching on the electric lights. The effect was warm, intimate, the kind of lighting that softened edges and encouraged conversation.

"This is excellent," Nobuyuki said, and there was genuine surprise in his voice. "Tenchi wasn't exaggerating."

"He rarely does," Katsuhito observed mildly. "About food, at least."

"I exaggerate about other things," Tenchi said, with the self-aware honesty of a child who'd been caught in small fibs before.

"Like what?"

"Like how far I can throw rocks. And how many stars I can count."

"Those seem like important things to exaggerate about," Nobuyuki said, his smile genuine now, the careful assessment fading into something warmer.

Sheldon ate and listened, contributing when addressed but mostly observing. The family had its own language—references to shared memories, shorthand born from years of knowing each other. He was an outsider to that language, could only catch the edges of meaning without understanding the full vocabulary.

But he was learning. Day by day, meal by meal, he was learning.


After dinner, Nobuyuki found him on the engawa.

Tenchi had been put to bed—reluctantly, with promises of a full day together tomorrow—and Katsuhito had retreated to whatever evening rituals occupied him. The shrine was quiet in that particular way of evening, full of sounds that were really just varieties of silence.

"May I?" Nobuyuki gestured to the space beside Sheldon.

"Of course."

They sat together, looking out at the darkened courtyard, the great tree a shape against the stars. Two men who didn't know each other, sharing space in the way that strangers sometimes did when circumstances threw them together.

"My father likes you," Nobuyuki said finally. "That's not nothing."

"I've tried to be useful."

"I can see that." A pause. "He's not an easy man to impress. Or to earn. But he's let you into his kitchen, his home, his grandson's life. That means something."

"I'm grateful for his generosity."

"Gratitude is one thing." Nobuyuki's voice was careful, measured. "Understanding is another. Do you understand what you've walked into?"

Sheldon considered the question. He could answer truthfully—*no, I don't understand anything, I woke up in a field six days ago with someone else's memories and a body that isn't mine*—but that truth wouldn't serve anyone.

"I understand that Tenchi needs people who will stay," he said instead. "I understand that your father is carrying burdens I can't see. And I understand that I'm a stranger who's been given more trust than I've earned."

Nobuyuki was silent for a long moment.

"That's... more than I expected."

"I'm good at observing. It's what I do."

"Did. What you did. You said you were a scientist."

"In another life." The words came out before Sheldon could stop them, truer than he'd intended. "Now I'm just someone trying to figure out where I belong."

"And you think you belong here?"

The question hung between them, heavy with implications. Here, in this village. Here, in this family. Here, in the room that had once been Nobuyuki's, caring for the son Nobuyuki couldn't raise himself.

"I think I could," Sheldon said carefully. "If I'm allowed."

Another long silence. Then Nobuyuki exhaled, something in his posture relaxing.

"Tenchi hasn't attached to anyone since his mother died. Not like this. He talks about you like you've been here forever." A pause. "That scares me a little. But it also... it also gives me hope."

"I won't hurt him."

"I don't think you will. I think if you were going to, my father would have seen it and stopped it." Nobuyuki turned to look at Sheldon directly, glasses reflecting the faint starlight. "He sees things, my father. More than he says. If he's accepted you, there's a reason."

*There's a reason.* Sheldon thought of Katsuhito's measuring eyes, the questions asked and unasked, the careful observation that had been constant since the first day. If the old priest had reasons for accepting a stranger into his home, Sheldon didn't know them—but he was beginning to suspect they went beyond simple hospitality.

"I should go check on Tenchi," Nobuyuki said, rising. "He doesn't always stay asleep when he knows I'm here."

"Good night, Nobuyuki-san."

"Good night, Tsukino-san." A pause at the door. "Thank you. For the meal. And the honesty."

He disappeared into the house, leaving Sheldon alone with the night and the tree and the questions that wouldn't resolve themselves no matter how many times he asked them.

*If he's accepted you, there's a reason.*

Sheldon looked at the great tree, its branches barely visible against the stars, and wondered what that reason might be.


- "Tenchi needs people who will stay"
- "Your father is carrying burdens I can't see"
- "I'm a stranger given more trust than I've earned"
- "I won't hurt him"
- "I think I could [belong here]. If I'm allowed."

#### Scene 6.C: Evening

The night settled around the shrine like a familiar blanket.

Sheldon remained on the engawa after Nobuyuki went inside, watching the darkness deepen over the courtyard. The great tree stood patient against the stars, its branches barely visible, its presence felt rather than seen.

*If he's accepted you, there's a reason.*

The words circled in his mind, seeking purchase. Nobuyuki had meant Katsuhito—meant that the old priest's approval wasn't given lightly, that it implied something about Sheldon that Sheldon himself didn't understand. But the words applied more broadly than that.

If he was here at all, there was a reason.

Someone—something—had pulled him from death and placed him in this body, in this life, in this village at the foot of a mountain shrine. Had given him skills he'd never learned and removed limitations he'd carried for decades. Had made him *new* in ways he still couldn't fully catalogue.

Why?

The question had no answer. It had had no answer for six days, and he suspected it would have no answer for six years, or sixty. Some mysteries didn't resolve themselves on convenient timelines. Some mysteries were meant to be lived rather than solved.

*Maybe that's enough*, he thought. *Maybe living it is the answer.*


Inside, the house had grown quiet.

He found the main room empty, the dinner dishes already washed—Katsuhito's work, done without comment while Sheldon had been talking with Nobuyuki. The old priest had retreated to his own quarters, and the sounds from Tenchi's room suggested that Nobuyuki had indeed found his son awake and was reading him back to sleep.

The guest room—Nobuyuki's childhood room, now Sheldon's temporary space—waited at the end of the hall. He entered quietly, closed the door behind him, and stood for a moment in the darkness.

*This was his room*, he thought. *His childhood. His memories.*

And now it held Sheldon's futon, Sheldon's few possessions, the accumulated weight of six nights' occupation. The scroll painting on the wall had been Nobuyuki's choice; the wooden chest had held Nobuyuki's belongings. Layers of history, one family's past becoming another stranger's present.

Tomorrow, Nobuyuki would be here all day—a full Sunday with his son, the kind of unhurried time that weekday schedules rarely allowed. And Monday, the roof repair would begin, and the house down the mountain would start its transformation from abandoned shell to livable home.

*Soon*, Sheldon thought, *I'll have my own space. My own walls. My own room that doesn't belong to someone else's memories.*

But for now, this room was home. This room, with its borrowed past and its uncertain future, was where he belonged.

He changed into sleeping clothes, laid out the futon, and settled into the familiar contours of rest.


Sleep didn't come immediately.

He lay in the darkness, listening to the shrine's nighttime sounds—the settling of wood, the distant call of an owl, the faint murmur of voices from Tenchi's room where Nobuyuki was still talking his son toward dreams. The sounds were becoming known to him now, catalogued and categorized, part of the mental map he was building of this place.

*Six days.*

Tomorrow would be seven. A week since he'd woken in the field, a week since the cherry blossoms and the white truck and the man who'd offered a ride to a stranger. A week of learning names, learning routines, learning the shape of a life he was still discovering.

He thought about the roof inspection, the formal numbers Yamada had provided. ¥280,000 to repair what time had damaged. Two to three weeks of work, if weather cooperated. The old Tsukino house would become new again—not new in the way Sheldon's body was new, sudden and complete, but new in the slower way of human effort. Board by board. Tile by tile. Day by day.

*That's how you build anything*, he thought. *Slowly. Carefully. One piece at a time.*

He was building something here. Not just a house, but a life. Not just repairs, but relationships. Not just restoration, but belonging.

The sleep that finally came was deep and dreamless, carrying him through the night toward whatever tomorrow would bring.


- Afternoon: Father-son time; Sheldon kept distance
- Evening: Family dinner; Nobuyuki-Sheldon conversation
- Night: Reflection; "slowly, carefully, one piece at a time"

#### Scene 7.A: Seventh Day

The seventh day belonged to Nobuyuki.

Sheldon understood this from the moment he woke—understood it in the way the shrine felt different, the energy shifted toward a configuration that didn't include him at its center. Father and son needed time. Father and son needed space. Sheldon's role today was to be present without being intrusive, useful without being indispensable.

He rose early, before the household stirred, and went to the kitchen.


Breakfast was waiting when the family emerged.

Simple fare—rice, miso soup, grilled fish, pickled vegetables—but prepared with care and set out in portions that suggested whoever had made it understood the day's priorities. Sheldon had eaten already, his bowl washed and put away, his presence reduced to the evidence of his labor.

"You didn't have to do this," Nobuyuki said, surveying the table.

"I wanted to. It's the least I can do." Sheldon was already pulling on his work jacket. "I'll be at the house today. Preparing for tomorrow."

Katsuhito caught his eye across the kitchen. Something passed between them—understanding, or perhaps approval. The old priest nodded once, almost imperceptibly.

"We'll save you dinner," Nobuyuki said.

"Thank you."

Sheldon left before Tenchi could protest, slipping out the door and down the mountain path while the sun was still low in the sky. Behind him, he could hear the boy's voice rising in question—*Where's Sheldon-san going?*—and Nobuyuki's patient response.

*He'll be back*, Sheldon thought. *He'll learn that I always come back.*


The Tsukino house was cold with morning.

He opened the doors and shutters, letting light and air flood through rooms that had been sealed since his last visit. Dust motes danced in the sunbeams. The photograph album waited in its drawer, undisturbed. The Japanese-English dictionary still sat on the desk in the study, a relic of someone else's learning.

*Tomorrow*, he thought. *Tomorrow, Yamada begins.*

But there was work he could do today. Work that didn't require a professional's expertise, only willingness and time.

He started with the garden.


The overgrowth yielded to effort.

Sheldon worked through the morning, pulling weeds that had claimed the vegetable patch, clearing paths that had disappeared under years of neglect. His hands—these new hands, strong and capable—found the rhythm of the work quickly, moving from task to task with an efficiency that would have been impossible in his old body.

By noon, the outline of the garden was visible again. Stone paths emerged from beneath their green shroud. The pond's edges became distinct, algae retreating under his methodical attention. The maple tree still needed pruning, but its base was clear now, the stone bench beneath it brushed clean of moss.

He sat on that bench and ate the onigiri he'd packed, looking at what he'd accomplished.

*Not much*, by some standards. A few hours of labor, a small section of neglected ground. But it was *his* labor, his ground, his progress toward something he could call home.

The afternoon stretched ahead, full of possibility.


He was cleaning the kitchen when the sound reached him.

Footsteps on the overgrown path—small ones, quick, accompanied by larger ones moving more slowly. Sheldon set down his rag and went to the front door.

Tenchi burst into view first, face flushed with exertion and excitement. "Sheldon-san! We came to find you!"

Behind him, Nobuyuki appeared at a more measured pace. He was carrying a basket, and his expression held something Sheldon couldn't quite read—amusement, perhaps, or resignation.

"He insisted," Nobuyuki said. "Wouldn't take no for an answer. Kept saying you'd be hungry."

"I'm fine. I brought food."

"I told him that." Nobuyuki set the basket down on the engawa. "He didn't believe me."

Tenchi had already darted past Sheldon into the house, his curiosity overwhelming any sense of propriety. "Is this your house? It's old! There's dust everywhere! Can I explore?"

"Carefully," Sheldon said. "Some of the floors are soft."

The boy vanished into the interior, his footsteps echoing through empty rooms. Sheldon and Nobuyuki stood in the doorway, listening to the sounds of childhood discovery.

"He missed you," Nobuyuki said quietly. "All morning. Kept looking toward the path, asking when you'd be back."

"I wanted to give you time together."

"I know. And I appreciate it." Nobuyuki's gaze swept over the visible garden work, the cleared paths, the progress Sheldon had made. "You've been busy."

"There's a lot to do."

"There always is." A pause. "My father says you're the hardest worker he's seen in years. Coming from him, that's significant."

*Katsuhito said that.* Sheldon filed the information away, uncertain what to do with it. "I just... I need something to do. Something to build. Otherwise I start thinking too much."

"About what?"

The question was gentle, not prying. An architect's instinct for understanding structures—including human ones.

"About how I got here. About why." Sheldon watched Tenchi's shadow pass a window. "About whether I deserve any of this."

"Do any of us deserve what we get?" Nobuyuki's voice was soft with something that might have been recognition. "I didn't deserve Kiyone. I didn't deserve Tenchi. But I got them anyway, and I try to be worthy of the gift."

*Kiyone.* Tenchi's mother. The woman who had died, leaving a husband in the city and a son at the shrine and a hole in a family that was still trying to heal.

"I'm sorry for your loss," Sheldon said.

"It was years ago. But thank you." Nobuyuki straightened. "We should eat. Tenchi will have worked up an appetite by now."


They ate on the engawa, the three of them, with the clearing garden spread before them.

The basket held onigiri shaped by Nobuyuki's inexpert hands—lopsided but earnest—and pickled vegetables and fruit that had clearly been packed by Katsuhito. Tenchi ate with the enthusiasm of a child who had burned energy exploring, chattering between bites about what he'd found in the house.

"There's a room with books! And a room with a big mirror! And a room that's just empty, but it has a nice window!"

"The study, the bedroom, and the spare room," Sheldon said. "I'll need to decide what to do with each of them."

"You could make the empty one a playroom," Tenchi suggested. "For when I visit."

The assumption—*when I visit*, not *if*—landed in Sheldon's chest like a small warm stone.

"Maybe I could."

Nobuyuki said nothing, but his expression suggested he'd heard what his son had assumed, and didn't disapprove.


They walked back to the shrine together as the afternoon aged.

Tenchi ran ahead, then circled back, then ran ahead again—the perpetual motion of childhood, burning energy without apparent source. Nobuyuki and Sheldon walked at an adult pace, letting the boy orbit around them like a small, enthusiastic planet.

"Tomorrow, the roof work starts," Sheldon said.

"Father mentioned. Yamada-san is good. He's done work on the shrine before."

"That's what I heard."

"The house will be livable soon." Nobuyuki's voice was carefully neutral. "You'll be moving out of the residence."

"Yes. Eventually."

"Tenchi will miss you."

"I'll visit. Every day, if I'm allowed."

"You'll be allowed." Nobuyuki glanced at him. "Whatever my father sees in you, he's not wrong. I've learned to trust his judgment, even when I don't understand it."

*What my father sees in you.* Another reference to Katsuhito's perception, to the things the old priest noticed that others didn't. Sheldon wondered, not for the first time, exactly what Katsuhito had seen—and what it meant.

"Thank you," he said. "For bringing Tenchi. For... for accepting this."

"I'm not sure I have a choice." Nobuyuki's smile was rueful. "My son chose you. My father chose you. I'm just trying to keep up."

They emerged from the forest onto the shrine grounds, the great tree rising before them, Tenchi already racing toward the main building with news of their return.

*One week*, Sheldon thought. *One week, and somehow I've been chosen.*

He still didn't know by whom. Or why. But maybe, as he'd thought last night, living the answer was enough.


- Sheldon gave Nobuyuki-Tenchi space
- Made breakfast before leaving
- Worked at Tsukino house alone (garden clearing)
- Nobuyuki-Tenchi visited mid-afternoon
- Walked back together; dinner at shrine

**Tsukino House Progress:**
- Garden weeds pulled
- Vegetable patch outlined
- Stone paths cleared
- Pond edges visible
- Stone bench under maple cleaned
- Kitchen cleaning begun

**Nobuyuki Insights:**

 Meaning -------
 Tenchi's attachment confirmed  "Hardest worker he's seen in years" 
 Trust in Katsuhito's judgment  "My son chose you. My father chose you." 

**Tenchi's Assumption:**
- "When I visit" (not "if")
- Suggested playroom in spare room
- Natural expectation of ongoing relationship

**Kiyone Mentioned:**
- Nobuyuki: "I didn't deserve Kiyone. I didn't deserve Tenchi."
- First direct mention of Tenchi's mother by father
- Loss acknowledged but not dwelt upon

**One-Week Milestone:**
- Day 7 = first full week since awakening
- "I've been chosen" — Sheldon's realization
- Living the answer, not solving the mystery


## Audit Compliance

- Time flow: 6:30 AM - 7:00 PM (full day, condensed) ✓
- Day 7: COMPLETE ✓

---

#### Scene 8.A: The Nightmare

The eighth day began with hammering.

Sheldon was at the Tsukino house by seven-thirty, unlocking the door for Yamada and helping unload materials from the roofer's truck. Tiles, underlayment, lumber for beam repairs—the components of restoration stacked neatly in the overgrown garden while Yamada surveyed the roof with professional intensity.

"Weather looks good through Thursday," Yamada said, more words at once than he usually offered. "We start with the south side. That's where the worst damage is."

"What do you need from me?"

"Stay out of my way. Bring tea at ten and two. Ask questions if you don't understand something." Yamada was already climbing his ladder. "Learning is free. Stupidity costs money."

Sheldon filed this philosophy away and retreated to the garden, where the morning's work waited. The clearing he'd begun on Sunday needed continuation—weeds that had returned despite his efforts, paths that wanted further definition, the maple tree that still required pruning.

He worked through the morning, pausing only to bring Yamada tea as instructed. The sound of the roofer's labor formed a constant backdrop—tiles being pried loose, wood being tested, the occasional grunt of discovery or disapproval. Progress, measured in small sounds and systematic effort.

By noon, the south side of the roof was stripped to its bones.


## Part II: The Return

He walked back to the shrine in the early afternoon, leaving Yamada to continue alone.

The path had become familiar now—eight days of walking it in various directions, at various times, until his feet knew the way without conscious thought. Past the fork, past the second torii, up the green tunnel of overhanging branches. The shrine emerged through the trees like something waiting to be found.

Tenchi wasn't at the bus stop yet—too early, still another hour before kindergarten released—but Katsuhito was on the engawa, working on something with his hands. As Sheldon approached, he saw it was a small wooden figure, being shaped with careful knife strokes.

"How does the work proceed?" Katsuhito asked without looking up.

"Yamada stripped the south side this morning. He found more damage than expected in the second support beam, but says it's manageable."

"Yamada-san always finds more damage than expected. It's his way of preparing clients for the bill." The old priest set down his carving. "Did you learn anything?"

"That I know nothing about roofing." Sheldon settled onto the engawa beside him. "But I'm learning."

"That's all anyone can do."

They sat in companionable silence, the afternoon settling around them. The great tree cast its shadow across the courtyard, and Sheldon's eyes found it again—that automatic draw, like gravity, like instinct.

"The boy missed you yesterday," Katsuhito said quietly. "While you were at the house."

"I know. He came to find me."

"With his father." A pause. "Nobuyuki told me about your conversation. He said you were honest with him."

"I tried to be."

"Honesty is rarer than people think. Especially about things we don't understand." Katsuhito picked up his carving again, the knife resuming its patient work. "Nobuyuki left this morning, before Tenchi woke. The boy was... quiet at breakfast. More than usual."

Sheldon thought about that. Tenchi, waking to find his father gone without goodbye. The familiar pattern of weekend visits ending in Monday-morning absences. The weight of loving someone who couldn't stay.

"I'll be here when he gets home," Sheldon said.

"I thought you might."


## Part III: The Evening

Tenchi's return was subdued.

He came up the steps with his backpack dragging, none of the usual energy in his movements. When he saw Sheldon on the engawa, his face flickered—hope, relief, something that might have been desperation—before settling into a careful neutrality that no four-year-old should have mastered.

"Hi," he said.

"Hi. How was school?"

"Fine." The word was flat, dismissive. "We did painting."

"What did you paint?"

"A house." Tenchi sat down on the engawa, leaving space between himself and Sheldon. More space than usual. "It wasn't very good."

"I'd like to see it."

"I threw it away."

The silence that followed was heavy with things unsaid. Sheldon waited, letting Tenchi work through whatever he was working through, not pushing but not retreating either. Present. Available. There.

"Father left," Tenchi said finally.

"I know."

"He didn't say goodbye."

"He didn't want to wake you."

"He never wants to wake me." The words came out bitter, older than Tenchi's years. "He just leaves and then he's gone and I don't see him until Saturday and Saturday is forever away."

Sheldon didn't have an answer to that. No words that could bridge the gap between a child's need and a father's obligations, between love that wanted to stay and life that demanded departure.

"I'm here," he said instead. "I'm not going anywhere."

Tenchi looked at him—really looked, searching for something in Sheldon's face. Whatever he found must have been enough, because after a long moment he shifted closer, closing the gap he'd created.

"Okay," he said. "Okay."


Dinner was quiet.

Sheldon made comfort food—rice porridge with umeboshi, the kind of soft, warm meal that asked nothing of the person eating it. Tenchi ate mechanically, spoon moving from bowl to mouth without enthusiasm. Katsuhito watched them both with the patient attention of someone who had seen this pattern before.

"Bath time soon," the old priest said as the meal wound down. "Would you like me to read you a story tonight, Tenchi?"

"No." The word was small but firm. "I want to go to bed early."

"Are you feeling unwell?"

"I'm just tired."

He wasn't tired. Sheldon could see it in the set of his shoulders, the tightness around his eyes. He was bracing for something—the dark, the dreams, the long hours until morning.

"I'll wash the dishes," Sheldon offered. "And then I'll be in my room. If you need anything."

Tenchi nodded, not meeting his eyes.


## Part IV: The Night

Sleep didn't come easily.

Sheldon lay in the guest room—Nobuyuki's childhood room, the room that held so many layers of this family's history—and listened to the shrine settle into nighttime silence. The wind in the trees. The occasional creak of old wood. The distant call of a night bird.

He'd been asleep for perhaps two hours when the sound woke him.

A cry. High, thin, terrified—the sound of a child in the grip of something that couldn't be reasoned with or bargained away. It came from Tenchi's room, through the thin walls that separated their spaces.

Sheldon was on his feet before he fully registered moving.


The hallway was dark, lit only by the faint glow of Tenchi's nightlight spilling from beneath his door. Sheldon moved toward it without hesitation, the floor cold against his bare feet, the sound of crying growing clearer with each step.

He knocked once, softly. "Tenchi?"

No answer except sobs—the breathless, gulping kind that meant panic rather than simple distress.

Sheldon opened the door.

Tenchi was tangled in his blankets, thrashing against imaginary restraints, face wet with tears. His eyes were open but unseeing, fixed on something in the nightmare realm that Sheldon couldn't reach.

"Mama," the boy was saying. "Mama, don't go. Mama, please—"

Sheldon crossed the room in two steps and knelt beside the futon.

"Tenchi." He kept his voice calm, steady. "Tenchi, wake up. You're dreaming. It's just a dream."

The boy's eyes didn't focus. His hands reached out, grasping at air, at absence, at the ghost of someone who had left too soon and too permanently.

"Mama—"

"Tenchi." Sheldon reached out and touched his shoulder—gently, carefully, the way you touched something fragile. "I'm here. You're safe. Wake up, Tenchi. Wake up."

The touch seemed to break through where words hadn't. Tenchi's eyes blinked, focused, found Sheldon's face in the dim light of the nightlight. For a moment he stared, not comprehending—and then recognition flooded in, and with it fresh tears.

"Sheldon-san—"

"I'm here."

The boy threw himself forward, small arms wrapping around Sheldon's neck with desperate strength. He was trembling, his whole body shaking with the aftershocks of terror. Sheldon caught him, held him, pulled him close.

"I'm here," he said again. "I've got you. You're safe."

"I was dreaming—" Tenchi's voice was muffled against Sheldon's shoulder. "I was dreaming about—about—"

"I know. I know."

"She went away. In the dream she went away and I couldn't—I couldn't make her stay—"

"I know."

They stayed like that, Sheldon holding Tenchi on the floor of his small room, the nightlight casting everything in soft gold. The boy's trembling slowly eased, his grip slowly loosened, his breathing slowly steadied. But he didn't let go.

"Will you stay?" The question was barely a whisper. "Until I fall asleep?"

"Yes."

"Do you promise?"

"I promise."


## Part V: The Vigil

Sheldon settled against the wall, Tenchi curled in his lap.

The boy was small—so small, lighter than Sheldon had expected, all sharp angles and soft skin and the particular warmth of childhood. His head rested against Sheldon's chest, rising and falling with each breath. His hands clutched the fabric of Sheldon's sleeping yukata with the grip of someone afraid to let go.

The room was quiet except for breathing.

Sheldon watched the shadows move on the ceiling as the hours passed. He didn't sleep—wouldn't sleep, not with this small life depending on him to be present, to be real, to be the anchor that held against the darkness. His back ached from the awkward position. His legs fell asleep and woke again with pins and needles. None of it mattered.

*This is what it means*, he thought. *To be chosen. To be needed. To be here.*

Dorothy had held him like this once, when he was small and scared and the world seemed too big to face. He remembered the warmth of her, the steadiness, the absolute certainty that nothing could hurt him as long as she was there. He hadn't thought about that memory in years—decades, maybe. But it rose now, unbidden, called up by the weight of the child in his arms.

*This is what you gave me*, he thought. *And now I give it forward.*

Tenchi murmured something in his sleep, too soft to understand. His grip tightened momentarily, then relaxed. He was dreaming again—but not the nightmare, not the terror. Something gentler. Something safe.


The door opened silently around two in the morning.

Katsuhito stood in the hallway, silhouetted by moonlight from somewhere deeper in the house. He looked at the scene before him—Sheldon against the wall, Tenchi sleeping in his lap, the nightlight burning steady—and said nothing.

For a long moment they simply regarded each other, two men who had been strangers eight days ago, bound now by circumstance and choice and the child who connected them.

Then Katsuhito nodded, once, and withdrew. The door closed softly behind him.

Sheldon stayed until dawn.


- Sheldon remembered Dorothy holding him when he was scared
- "This is what you gave me. And now I give it forward."
- Comfort Cooking seed may have activated (rice porridge)

**Yamada Work Progress:**
- South side of roof stripped
- More damage found in second beam (manageable)
- Work continues through week (weather permitting)

#### Scene 9.A: The Morning After

Sheldon watched it brighten by degrees, his back against the wall, his legs numb, his arms still curved around the small weight that had barely moved in hours. Tenchi's breathing was slow and even now—real sleep, the kind that came after exhaustion, not the fitful half-rest of nightmares.

He didn't want to move. Moving would break the spell, would return them both to the world of schedules and obligations and the ordinary business of Tuesday mornings. But the light was growing, and soon the household would stir, and Tenchi had school.

"Tenchi." His voice came out rough from hours of silence. "Time to wake up."

The boy shifted, murmured something unintelligible, burrowed closer. Then, slowly, awareness returned—the stiffening of someone realizing where they were and who they were with, the careful opening of eyes.

"Sheldon-san?"

"Good morning."

Tenchi sat up, looking around his room as if seeing it for the first time. His face was puffy from sleep and tears, his hair a disaster of tangles and pillow-pressed patterns. But his eyes, when they found Sheldon's face, were clear.

"You stayed."

"I promised."

Something shifted in the boy's expression—not surprise exactly, but a kind of settling. The confirmation of something hoped for, now proven true.

"My back hurts," Tenchi said, with the sudden subject-changing honesty of childhood. "And I'm hungry."

"Then let's get you fed."


The kitchen was already occupied.

Katsuhito stood at the stove, stirring something in a pot, the smell of miso and rice filling the small space. He looked up as Sheldon and Tenchi appeared in the doorway—Sheldon moving stiffly, Tenchi trailing behind with a grip on his sleeve.

"Breakfast is almost ready," the old priest said. His eyes moved from Sheldon's face to Tenchi's, cataloguing what he saw. "Sit. Both of you."

They sat. Tenchi kept his hold on Sheldon's sleeve even at the table, as if afraid that releasing it would break some fragile connection. Sheldon didn't pull away.

Katsuhito served them—rice, soup, pickled vegetables, a small grilled fish for each. Then he sat across from them and began to eat in silence.

"Grandfather." Tenchi's voice was small. "I had a bad dream last night."

"I know."

"Sheldon-san stayed with me."

"I know that too."

The boy looked between them, trying to read the adult communication happening above his head. "Are you angry?"

"Why would I be angry?"

"Because I was loud. Because I woke everyone up."

Katsuhito set down his chopsticks. "Tenchi. Look at me."

The boy met his grandfather's eyes with visible effort.

"Dreams come whether we want them to or not. They are not something to be ashamed of, and they are not something to apologize for." The old priest's voice was gentle but firm. "When bad dreams come, the only thing that matters is that someone is there to help you through them. Sheldon-san was there. That is good."

Tenchi's grip on Sheldon's sleeve tightened.

"But," Katsuhito continued, "you have school today. And Sheldon-san has work at the house. So we must eat, and get ready, and continue with the day. Life does not stop for dreams, even bad ones. Understood?"

"Understood."

"Good. Finish your breakfast."


The bus came at 8:27, as it always did.

Sheldon walked Tenchi to the top of the shrine steps, watching the boy descend with his backpack bouncing against his small frame. At the bottom, Tenchi turned and waved—a bigger wave than usual, more emphatic, holding until Sheldon waved back.

Then he was gone, swallowed by the yellow bus and the road and the ordinary rhythm of kindergarten Tuesdays.

Sheldon stood at the top of the steps for a long moment, looking down at the empty road. His body ached in ways he hadn't known it could ache—the accumulated cost of hours in one position, the debt of a sleepless night. But beneath the ache was something else. Something that felt like purpose.

*I kept my promise*, he thought. *That's what matters.*


Katsuhito found him on the engawa, drinking tea with hands that trembled slightly from exhaustion.

"You should rest," the old priest said, settling beside him.

"I should go to the house. Yamada is waiting."

"Yamada can wait. The roof will still be there tomorrow." Katsuhito poured himself tea from the pot Sheldon had prepared. "You've been awake since yesterday morning. The body has limits, even young ones."

*Young.* The word caught Sheldon's attention, as it always did. This body was young—thirty, according to the documents, but feeling younger still, full of energy and capability that his previous form had never possessed. Even exhausted, even aching, it functioned better than his old body had at its best.

"I'll rest after I check on the progress," he said. "Yamada said to bring tea at ten."

"I'll bring the tea. You'll sleep."

It wasn't a request. Sheldon recognized the tone—the same one Katsuhito used with Tenchi when negotiation was not an option.

"All right," he said. "A few hours."

"Good." Katsuhito sipped his tea. "What you did last night—staying with him—that was not a small thing."

"It felt like the only thing."

"Perhaps. But many people, faced with a crying child in the dark, choose differently. They call for someone else. They offer comfort from a distance. They do what is required and no more." The old priest's eyes found the great tree, as they often did. "You stayed until dawn. You held him while he slept. That is not 'the only thing.' That is everything."

Sheldon didn't know how to respond to that. The words sat between them, heavy with meaning he wasn't sure he understood.

"He chose you," Katsuhito continued. "Not me. Not his father. You. A stranger who arrived eight days ago." A pause. "Children see things clearly. They know who can be trusted with their fear."

"I'm not sure I deserve that trust."

"None of us are sure. But the trust is given anyway, and we must decide what to do with it." Katsuhito finished his tea and stood. "Sleep now. The house will wait. The boy will come home this afternoon, and he will need to see you rested and present. That is what matters."

He left Sheldon alone on the engawa, watching the morning light strengthen over the courtyard.


Sleep, when it came, was dreamless.

Sheldon lay down in the guest room—Nobuyuki's room, his room, the room where he'd woken to a child's cry just hours before—and let the exhaustion pull him under. No thoughts, no questions, no attempts to understand what was happening to him or why. Just darkness, and rest, and the slow repair of a body that had given everything it had.

He woke in the early afternoon to the sound of the wind in the trees and the distant rhythm of Yamada's hammer.

The house was waiting. The work was waiting. Life was continuing, as Katsuhito had said it would.

Sheldon rose, washed his face, and went to meet it.


- "You stayed" — confirmation of hope

**Katsuhito's Orders:**

- Sheldon must rest (not negotiable)
- Katsuhito will bring tea to Yamada
- "The boy will need to see you rested and present"

**Physical Cost:**

- No sleep all night
- Hands trembling from exhaustion
- Body aching in new ways
- Still functional (restored body advantage)

- Woke early afternoon
- Yamada's hammer audible (work continuing)

#### Scene 9.B: The Afternoon

The house smelled like progress.

Sheldon arrived to find Yamada on the roof, methodically laying new underlayment where the old had rotted away. The roofer acknowledged his arrival with a grunt and a gesture toward the tea thermos Katsuhito had left on the engawa.

"Your priest brought the tea. Said you were sleeping."

"I was. I'm sorry I wasn't here this morning."

"Nothing to be sorry for." Yamada adjusted a section of material, his movements precise and unhurried. "A man who doesn't sleep is a man who makes mistakes. Mistakes cost money."

Sheldon poured himself tea and sat on the engawa, watching the work. The south side was taking shape now—the skeleton of the roof visible where tiles had been removed, new bones being added where old ones had failed. It was slow work, careful work, the kind that couldn't be rushed without consequences.

"The second beam is worse than I thought," Yamada said. "But not as bad as it could be. Another year, maybe two, and it would have gone. You're lucky you came when you did."

*Lucky.* The word sat strangely in Sheldon's mind. He wasn't sure luck had anything to do with how he'd arrived, or when, or why. But the timing—that, at least, seemed fortunate.

"How much longer for the south side?"

"Three more days, if weather holds. Then we start on the east."

Three days. Then more days. Then, eventually, a house that could be lived in. A home that was his own.

Sheldon finished his tea and went to the garden.


The work was good.

There was something meditative about pulling weeds, about clearing paths, about the slow transformation of neglected ground into something tended and cared for. Sheldon's hands found the rhythm easily now, moving without conscious direction, responding to the needs of the garden as if they'd always known how.

He worked until the shadows began to lengthen, until the sound of Yamada's hammer fell silent and the roofer descended for the day.

"Same time tomorrow," Yamada said, loading tools into his truck. "The beam work will take longer. Might need an extra pair of hands for the heavy lifting."

"I'll be here."

Yamada nodded and drove away, leaving Sheldon alone with the house and the garden and the approaching evening.

Time to go back.


The shrine was quiet when he arrived.

Too quiet—the particular silence of a place holding its breath, waiting for something. Sheldon climbed the steps with growing unease, his body still tired from the sleepless night but alert now, tuned to frequencies he couldn't name.

He found them on the engawa.

Katsuhito sat in his usual spot, carving something small from a block of wood. And there, pressed against the old priest's side with a book open in his lap, was Tenchi—home from school, changed out of his uniform, watching the path with eyes that tracked Sheldon's approach like radar.

The boy's face transformed when he saw Sheldon. Relief, joy, something desperate and glad all at once.

"Sheldon-san!"

He was up and running before Sheldon had fully reached the engawa, small body colliding with Sheldon's legs in a hug that had more force than a four-year-old should have been capable of.

"You came back," Tenchi said, the words muffled against Sheldon's work clothes. "You came back."

"I always come back." Sheldon crouched to meet the boy's eyes. "Remember? As far as I can see."

Tenchi's face was serious, searching Sheldon's expression for something—truth, maybe, or the evidence of promises kept. Whatever he found seemed to satisfy him, because after a long moment he nodded.

"Okay," he said. "Okay."


Dinner was different.

Not in what was served—rice, miso soup, grilled fish, the standard rotation of shrine meals—but in how they ate it. Tenchi sat closer to Sheldon than usual, their shoulders almost touching. He passed dishes without being asked, anticipated needs, maintained a constant low-level awareness of where Sheldon was in the room.

Katsuhito observed without comment, his eyes moving between them with the patient attention of someone cataloguing data.

"I drew a picture today," Tenchi announced midway through the meal. "At school. Sensei said it was good."

"What did you draw?"

"A house. With a garden. And people in front of it."

"Who were the people?"

Tenchi's face went pink. "Just... people. Me and Grandfather and..." He trailed off, suddenly fascinated by his rice bowl.

"And?" Sheldon prompted gently.

"And you." The words came out in a rush. "I put you in the picture too. Is that okay?"

Something shifted in Sheldon's chest—a warmth, a settling, a recognition of significance. "That's more than okay."

Tenchi beamed, the pinkness in his cheeks transforming from embarrassment to pleasure. "Sensei asked who everyone was. I said it was my family. Was that—is that wrong?"

The question hung in the air, weighted with meaning that a four-year-old couldn't fully understand but clearly felt. *Family.* The word that Tenchi had used, unprompted, to describe the picture he'd drawn.

"It's not wrong," Sheldon said carefully. "Family is... family is the people who take care of each other. The people who stay."

"You stay."

"I stay."

Katsuhito set down his chopsticks. "Tenchi. What did Sensei say when you told her about your picture?"

"She smiled. She said it was a nice family." Tenchi's brow furrowed. "But then she looked... I don't know. Funny. Like she was thinking about something."

*A child with a grandfather, a weekend father, and a strange Black man he calls family.* Sheldon could imagine exactly what Suzuki-sensei had been thinking, the questions forming behind her professional smile.

"I'm sure she was just happy you drew something nice," Sheldon said.

"Maybe." Tenchi didn't sound convinced, but he let the subject drop, returning to his dinner with renewed focus.


Bath time came and went.

Sheldon waited in the main room while Tenchi bathed, listening to the splash of water and the boy's off-key humming. It was a different sound than it had been before the nightmare—lighter somehow, less fragile. As if something that had been wound too tight had finally been allowed to loosen.

When Tenchi emerged in his sleeping yukata, he didn't go straight to his room. Instead, he came to where Sheldon was sitting and stood there, small and clean and smelling like soap.

"Sheldon-san?"

"Yes?"

"Will you..." The boy hesitated, gathering courage. "Will you sit with me? Until I fall asleep? Like last night?"

The request was tentative, prepared for refusal. Sheldon understood—understood that asking for comfort was harder than receiving it unasked, that vulnerability was easier when it was accidental.

"Of course," he said. "Let me tell your grandfather."


Tenchi's room was different by lamplight.

Sheldon had only seen it in the dim glow of the nightlight, in the gray of dawn. Now, with the lamp burning steady, he could see the details: the small shelf of picture books, the worn stuffed animal (a rabbit, missing one ear) tucked in the corner of the futon, the drawing pinned to the wall that showed a house with a garden and three figures in front of it.

The picture from today. Tenchi must have kept it instead of throwing it away.

"That's us," Tenchi said, following Sheldon's gaze. "The tall one is you. See? I made your hair curly."

The figure in question was mostly a brown oval with spirals on top, but the intent was clear. "It's a good likeness."

"Sensei helped me with the hands. Hands are hard."

"They are."

Tenchi climbed into his futon, arranging himself with the particular fussiness of children at bedtime. The rabbit went under one arm. The blanket was pulled up to his chin. His eyes found Sheldon's face and stayed there.

"You don't have to stay all night," he said. "Just until I'm asleep. That's okay, right?"

"That's okay."

"And if I have a bad dream again—"

"I'll be here. Or I'll come. Either way, you won't be alone."

Tenchi nodded, accepting this. His eyes began to drift closed, then snapped open again.

"Sheldon-san?"

"Yes?"

"I'm glad you came to the shrine. I'm glad you're staying."

"I'm glad too, Tenchi."

The boy's eyes closed for good this time, his breathing slowly evening out into the rhythm of sleep. Sheldon sat beside the futon and watched him drift away, this small person who had somehow decided that a stranger was family, that a promise made was a promise that would be kept.

*Nine days*, he thought. *Nine days, and I have a family.*

He stayed until Tenchi was deeply asleep, then rose quietly and slipped from the room, leaving the lamp burning low against the dark.


- "A child with a grandfather, a weekend father, and a strange Black man he calls family"

#### Scene 9.C: Evening

The main room was quiet when Sheldon emerged from Tenchi's room.

Katsuhito sat at the low table, a cup of tea before him, the small wooden carving from earlier in his hands. He didn't look up as Sheldon entered, but something in his posture acknowledged the arrival.

"He's asleep," Sheldon said.

"I know. I heard him ask you to stay."

Sheldon settled across the table, accepting the tea Katsuhito poured for him. The warmth was welcome—the evening had turned cool, and the shrine's old construction didn't hold heat the way modern buildings might.

"He drew a picture today," Sheldon said. "At school. He told his teacher we were his family."

"I saw the picture. He showed me when he came home." Katsuhito set down his carving—a bird, Sheldon could see now, wings half-spread as if about to take flight. "He's never drawn me before. Not in four years of kindergarten."

The words landed with unexpected weight. Four years of pictures, and Katsuhito had never appeared in them. But now—now there was a house with three figures, pinned to a bedroom wall.

"What changed?" Sheldon asked.

"You did." The old priest's eyes met his. "Or rather—you made him believe that change was possible. That the people in his life might actually stay."

"I haven't done anything special."

"You kept a promise. You stayed through a nightmare. You came back when you said you would." Katsuhito picked up the bird again, turning it in his fingers. "For a child who has lost his mother and sees his father only two days a week, those are not small things. Those are everything."

The silence that followed was not uncomfortable. It was the silence of two people who had reached an understanding, who had moved past the need for constant words.

"The teacher may ask questions," Katsuhito said eventually. "Suzuki-sensei is young, but she cares about her students. She'll want to know who you are."

"What should I tell her?"

"The truth, as far as you know it. That you're a family friend. That you're restoring the Tsukino house. That you help care for Tenchi while his father works." A pause. "She doesn't need to know more than that. Not yet."

*Not yet.* The words implied a future where more would be known, where the careful story Sheldon had been telling would need to expand or deepen. But that future wasn't today.

"I understand."

"Good." Katsuhito stood, the bird carving still in his hand. "Get some rest. You look like you need it."

"I do." Sheldon managed a small smile. "What's the bird for?"

"Tenchi. For his birthday." The old priest examined his work with a critical eye. "Still needs finishing. Two more weeks should be enough."

Two weeks until Tenchi's birthday. April 24th. Another date to remember, another milestone in this life he was building day by day.

"Good night, Katsuhito-sensei."

"Good night, Tsukino-san."


The bath was brief and hot.

Sheldon soaked in the ofuro, letting the water work on muscles that still carried the tension of a sleepless night and a day of physical labor. His body was recovering—this new body, this young body that healed faster and hurt less than the one he remembered. But even it had limits, and those limits had been tested.

He thought about Tenchi's picture, pinned to the bedroom wall. Three figures in front of a house. A family, drawn by a child who had lost so much and was still brave enough to hope for more.

*Family is the people who take care of each other. The people who stay.*

He'd said that without thinking, the words coming from somewhere deeper than conscious thought. But they were true—true in a way that mattered. Blood made relatives. Choice made family. And Tenchi had chosen, with the clarity that only children possessed, to include Sheldon in that circle.

*I'll try to be worthy of it*, he thought. *Whatever that takes. However long it takes.*

The water cooled. He rose, dried himself, dressed in sleeping clothes, and went to bed.


Sleep came easier this time.

The exhaustion was still there, layered deep, but beneath it was something else—a settling, a grounding, the particular peace of knowing where you belonged and why you were there. Nine days since waking in the field. Nine days of learning names and faces, of building routines and relationships, of discovering what it meant to be Tsukino Sheldon in this place and time.

He dreamed of Dorothy.

Not the dream from before, with both mothers standing together. Just Dorothy this time, sitting in her kitchen in Atlanta, shelling peas into a bowl. She looked up when he entered, smiled, and said: *You're doing good, baby. You're doing real good.*

He woke to dawn light, the dream already fading, but the warmth of it remaining.


- "Still needs finishing. Two more weeks."

- Tenchi had never drawn him in 4 years of kindergarten
- The family picture is unprecedented

---

#### Scene 10.A: Tenth Morning

The tenth morning came gentle and warm.

Sheldon woke with the echo of Dorothy's voice still resonating in his chest—*you're doing good, baby*—and lay still for a moment, letting the feeling settle. Outside the paper screens, birds were beginning their morning conversation, and the light filtering through carried the particular gold of a spring day promising sunshine.

He rose, dressed, and went to the kitchen.


The routine had become familiar.

Water from the pump (three pulls for good pressure). Rice from the bin. The particular sequence of movements that turned raw ingredients into breakfast—miso paste dissolved in dashi, vegetables sliced thin, fish laid out for grilling. His hands knew the work now, moving without conscious direction, finding their rhythm in the quiet morning kitchen.

*Ten days*, he thought, stirring the soup. *Ten days of mornings like this.*

The realization brought with it a strange vertigo—not unpleasant, just disorienting. A week and a half ago, he hadn't existed. He'd been dead, or something like it, his consciousness dissolved into whatever came after. And now he was here, making breakfast in a Japanese shrine, counting days like they were coins in a jar.

*Don't think too hard*, the internal voice suggested. *Just keep moving. The thinking comes later.*

He trusted the voice. He was learning when to trust it and when to question it. In the kitchen, at least, it was always right.


Tenchi appeared at seven, dressed in his kindergarten uniform.

He looked different this morning—not dramatically, but noticeably. Something in the way he moved, in the set of his shoulders. Less fragile. More grounded. The nightmare and its aftermath had changed something in him, had shifted some internal weight from one position to another.

"Good morning, Sheldon-san."

"Good morning. Did you sleep well?"

"I had a dream about frogs." Tenchi climbed onto his cushion at the table. "They were jumping on clouds. It was funny."

"Frogs on clouds sounds like a good dream."

"It was." The boy's smile was easy, unshadowed. "Better than the other kind."

*The other kind.* They both knew what he meant. But they didn't need to name it—not this morning, not when the clouds were full of frogs and the light was golden and breakfast was ready.

Katsuhito joined them a few minutes later, moving with the unhurried grace of someone who had spent decades mastering the art of morning. He surveyed the table—rice, soup, fish, pickles—and nodded once.

"You're getting better at the fish."

"I'm learning."

"So I see."

They ate in companionable silence, the three of them, the morning settling around them like a comfortable blanket. When Tenchi finished, he carried his dishes to the kitchen without being asked, then returned to collect his backpack.

"Sheldon-san?"

"Yes?"

"Will you wave when the bus leaves? From the top of the steps?"

"I always do."

"I know." Tenchi's smile widened. "But I like asking anyway."


The bus arrived at 8:27, precise as always.

Sheldon stood at the top of the shrine steps, watching Tenchi descend. The boy moved quickly now, confident on the familiar path, pausing only once to look back and wave. Sheldon waved in return—a big wave, the kind Tenchi had given him the day before—and watched until the yellow bus swallowed the small figure and carried it away.

"You could go down with him," Katsuhito said from somewhere behind him. "Stand at the stop. See him onto the bus."

"He didn't ask for that."

"No. He asked for the wave." The old priest came to stand beside him, looking out over the valley. "Children know what they need. Tenchi needs to know you're watching. That you're there when he leaves, and there when he returns."

"The anchor."

"Yes. The anchor." Katsuhito's eyes found the tree, as they always did. "He had one, once. His mother. She would stand on the engawa every morning and wave until he was out of sight. Then she would wave when he came home. She said it made her feel connected to him, even when they were apart."

Sheldon absorbed this. Another piece of the puzzle, another fragment of the family history he was slowly learning. Kiyone—Tenchi's mother, dead now almost two years—had waved from the engawa. Had been the anchor before anchoring became impossible.

"You're giving him back something he lost," Katsuhito continued. "Not the same thing—never the same—but something similar enough to matter."

"I'm not trying to replace her."

"No one could replace her. No one should try." The old priest turned to look at him directly. "But presence isn't replacement. Comfort isn't usurpation. You can be what you are without diminishing what she was."

The words settled something that Sheldon hadn't realized was unsettled. He'd been carrying a worry, somewhere beneath conscious thought—a fear that in taking this role, he was somehow erasing Kiyone's place in Tenchi's life. But Katsuhito was right. Presence and memory could coexist. The living and the dead could share space in a child's heart.

"Thank you," he said.

"Thank me by keeping him safe." Katsuhito began walking back toward the shrine. "The house is waiting. Yamada will need his tea."


The walk down the mountain was peaceful.

The forest was fully awake now, birds calling from the canopy, insects beginning their daily hum. The path was dappled with light and shadow, the morning sun filtering through branches that were thick with new spring growth.

Sheldon moved through it without hurrying, letting his body find its own pace. Ten days of this path had made it familiar—the rocks to step around, the roots to watch for, the places where the trail narrowed and the places where it opened onto brief clearings.

*This is mine now*, he thought. *This walk. This morning. This life.*

The Tsukino house appeared through the trees, and with it the sound of Yamada's truck pulling up. Day three of roof repair. Day ten of Sheldon's new existence. Another morning of building what needed to be built.

He quickened his pace. There was work to be done.


- Said it made her feel "connected to him, even when they were apart"
- Sheldon is unconsciously replicating this

#### Scene 10.B: The Afternoon

Yamada was already on the roof when Sheldon arrived.

The roofer worked with the methodical patience of someone who understood that speed and quality rarely coexisted. Each movement was deliberate—testing a board before putting weight on it, examining a joint before driving a nail, pausing to assess before proceeding. It was craftsmanship in its purest form, and Sheldon found himself watching more than working.

"You're staring," Yamada said without looking down.

"I'm learning."

"Hmph." The sound might have been approval. "The beam work starts tomorrow. I'll need you up here for that."

"You said you needed extra hands."

"I need hands that follow directions. Can you follow directions?"

"Yes."

"Good. Ten o'clock. Don't be late, and don't wear anything you care about keeping clean."

Sheldon nodded, though Yamada couldn't see it, and returned to the garden.


The work was becoming familiar.

He'd cleared most of the obvious overgrowth now—the weeds that had claimed the paths, the vines that had wrapped themselves around the fence posts, the wild grass that had swallowed the vegetable beds. What remained was more subtle work: shaping, defining, preparing the ground for what would come next.

The stone path needed resetting. Several of the flat rocks had shifted over the years, creating uneven surfaces that pooled water and encouraged moss. Sheldon found a small pry bar among the tools Katsuhito had lent him and began the slow process of lifting, leveling, and replacing.

*This is meditation*, he thought, working a stubborn stone free from its bed of packed earth. *The same as sweeping. The same as cooking. Focus the hands, and the mind follows.*

The internal voice didn't comment. It rarely did during physical work—as if it understood that the body's wisdom was sufficient for these tasks, that its guidance was needed only when the mind threatened to complicate what should be simple.

By noon, a third of the path was reset. By two o'clock, half. By three, when Sheldon finally stopped and looked up at the sky, nearly two-thirds.

His back ached. His hands were dirty. He felt better than he had in—

*In this life*, he finished. *Better than I have in this life.*

Which was only ten days, but still.


The walk back to the shrine was unhurried.

Sheldon had learned to time his return for just before Tenchi's bus arrived—late enough to have accomplished real work, early enough to be present when the boy came home. It was a balance, a rhythm, one of the many small calibrations that made a household function.

He emerged from the forest to find Katsuhito on the engawa, the bird carving in his hands, the afternoon sun casting long shadows across the courtyard. The old priest looked up as Sheldon approached.

"The path?"

"Almost done. Tomorrow I'll finish it."

"And then?"

"The pond, I think. It needs clearing. And the maple still needs pruning."

Katsuhito nodded, returning to his carving. The bird was taking shape now—Sheldon could see the curve of its breast, the suggestion of folded wings, the way the wood grain followed the line of its body. It would be beautiful when it was finished.

"Yamada wants me on the roof tomorrow," Sheldon said. "For the beam work."

"He must trust you. He doesn't usually let clients near his projects."

"He said I follow directions."

"That's high praise, from Yamada-san." The knife paused its work. "He fired his last apprentice for asking too many questions."

"I'll try to ask the right amount."

"That would be wise."


The bus arrived at 2:45.

Sheldon was at the top of the steps when it appeared around the bend in the road, yellow against the green of the forest. He watched it stop, watched the small figure emerge, watched Tenchi begin his climb with the particular energy of a child who knew someone was waiting.

The wave came halfway up—a big wave, enthusiastic, both arms if Tenchi could have managed it without dropping his backpack. Sheldon waved back, and something settled in his chest. A rightness. A completion.

"You're dirty," Tenchi announced when he reached the top, surveying Sheldon's work clothes with the critical eye of a four-year-old.

"I was working."

"On the garden?"

"On the path. The stones needed fixing."

Tenchi considered this. "Can I help next time?"

"If you want to. It's hard work."

"I like hard work." The boy straightened with exaggerated pride. "Grandfather says I'm a good helper."

"Then I'd be glad to have you."


Dinner was simple.

Rice, miso soup, grilled vegetables, the last of the pickled plum from the jar Katsuhito's wife had prepared years ago. Sheldon made a mental note to learn how to make umeboshi himself—the supply wouldn't last forever, and it was becoming clear that some things were worth preserving.

"Sensei asked about you today," Tenchi said midway through the meal.

Sheldon's chopsticks paused. "Oh?"

"She asked if you were my uncle." The boy's face was thoughtful. "I said you weren't. I said you were... I said you were Sheldon-san."

"That's accurate."

"She wanted to know more. I said you lived at the shrine and helped with things." Tenchi looked at Katsuhito, then back at Sheldon. "Was that okay to say?"

"That was fine," Katsuhito answered before Sheldon could. "Suzuki-sensei is curious because she cares about her students. It's natural to want to know about the people in a child's life."

"She said maybe she'd meet you sometime." Tenchi's eyes were bright with something—excitement, maybe, or anxiety. "Would that be okay?"

Sheldon thought about Suzuki-sensei, the young teacher he'd never met, asking questions about the stranger who had appeared in her student's drawings and conversations. It was inevitable, really. A child didn't suddenly start talking about a new family member without the adults around him taking notice.

"If she wants to meet me, I'd be happy to talk with her."

Tenchi relaxed visibly. "Good. I told her you were nice."

"That was kind of you."

"It's true." The boy returned to his rice with the matter-of-fact certainty of childhood. "You're the nicest person I know. After Grandfather."

Katsuhito's lips twitched. "I'm honored to remain in first place."

"You're old," Tenchi explained. "Old people are supposed to be nice. Sheldon-san is young, so it's more special."

Sheldon found himself laughing—a genuine laugh, the kind that came from somewhere deep and surprised him with its presence. Katsuhito's eyes crinkled with amusement, and Tenchi beamed at having made both adults happy.

*This*, Sheldon thought. *This is what I was brought here for. Whatever else happens, this moment is enough.*


The evening settled into familiar patterns.

Bath, stories, the ritual of settling Tenchi for sleep. The boy didn't ask Sheldon to stay tonight—didn't need to, perhaps, or was testing whether the offer still stood without asking. Sheldon waited in the hallway until Tenchi's breathing evened out, then returned to the main room.

Katsuhito was there, tea poured, the bird carving set aside for the night.

"He's doing better," the old priest observed.

"He is."

"The nightmares may return. They often do, for children who've lost a parent. But having someone present—someone consistent—that helps."

"I'll be here."

"I know." Katsuhito sipped his tea. "That's why I said it."

They sat in comfortable silence, two men who had found a balance without naming it. Outside, the night deepened, and somewhere in the distance an owl called its question to the dark.

"Ten days," Sheldon said finally.

"Ten days."

"It feels longer. And shorter. Both at once."

"That's how it always feels," Katsuhito said. "When you're building something that matters."
