#### Scene 10.C: Evening

The owl called again, closer this time.

Sheldon listened to it from the main room, his tea growing cold in his hands. The sound was becoming familiar—one of the shrine's nighttime voices, as regular as the settling of wood and the whisper of wind through the great tree's branches.

"You should sleep," Katsuhito said. "Tomorrow will be long."

"The beam work."

"Yes. Yamada-san doesn't take breaks when he's doing structural repairs. He says stopping invites mistakes."

Sheldon thought about that—the philosophy of continuous effort, of maintaining momentum until a task was complete. It made a certain kind of sense. Breaking focus cost something. Returning to work after rest meant rebuilding understanding, re-finding the rhythm that had been temporarily lost.

"I'll be ready," he said.

"I know you will." Katsuhito finished his own tea and rose. "Tenchi will be disappointed to miss it. He wanted to help."

"Tomorrow is a school day."

"I told him that. He suggested skipping." The old priest's eyes held a glint of amusement. "I declined the suggestion."

"Probably wise."

"Probably." Katsuhito moved toward the hallway. "Good night, Tsukino-san."

"Good night, Katsuhito-sensei."


The bath was brief, efficient.

Sheldon soaked just long enough to loosen his muscles, then dried and dressed and went to the guest room. His body was tired in a good way—the kind of tired that came from honest labor, from hours of lifting and placing and moving through physical space.

He thought about the stone path, nearly finished now. He thought about the beam waiting at the Tsukino house, ready to be lifted and placed. He thought about Tenchi, asleep in his room with the one-eared rabbit and the family picture on the wall.

*Ten days complete*, he thought. *Day eleven begins tomorrow.*

It was a small milestone. Ten days wasn't a month, wasn't a season, wasn't any of the meaningful markers that people used to measure time. But it felt significant anyway—a checkpoint, a proof of concept, evidence that this life was real and continuing and his.

*I'm still here*, he thought. *I'm still becoming.*

Sleep came easily, carrying him down into darkness. This time, he didn't dream—or if he did, the dreams dissolved before waking, leaving nothing behind but the vague sense of peace.


- "Stopping invites mistakes"
- Continuous effort = maintained focus

#### Scene 11.A: Eleventh Morning

The eleventh morning came with purpose.

Sheldon woke knowing what the day held—beam work, roof work, the kind of physical labor that would test the limits of this new body in ways the garden hadn't. He rose before dawn, dressed in his oldest clothes (per Yamada's instructions), and went to the kitchen.

Breakfast was simple and substantial: extra rice, extra fish, the kind of meal that would sustain hard work. Tenchi appeared at seven, still rubbing sleep from his eyes, disappointed all over again that he couldn't skip school to help.

"Next time," Sheldon said.

"You always say that."

"Because it's always true. There's always a next time."

Tenchi considered this logic, found it wanting, but accepted it anyway. He ate his breakfast with the resignation of a child who knew some battles weren't worth fighting.

"Be careful on the roof," he said as Sheldon prepared to leave. "Don't fall."

"I won't fall."

"Promise?"

"Promise."

The boy's eyes held his for a long moment—testing, trusting, confirming. Then he nodded. "Okay. I'll see you when I get home."

"I'll be here."


The Tsukino house looked different in the morning light.

Scaffolding had appeared along the south side—Yamada's work from yesterday afternoon, Sheldon realized, erected after he'd left. The structure was solid, professional, the kind of temporary construction that spoke of someone who understood safety wasn't optional.

Yamada was already there, arranging tools on the scaffolding platform. He looked up as Sheldon approached.

"Good. You're early."

"You said ten. It's nine-thirty."

"Early is good. Late is fired." The roofer descended from the scaffolding and walked to where the replacement beam lay against the house. "Here's what we're doing. This beam—" He patted the timber. "—goes up there." He pointed to a gap in the roof structure where the old beam had been removed. "We lift it, we position it, we secure it. Simple."

"That doesn't look simple."

"It's not. But the concept is." Yamada fixed him with a measuring stare. "You'll be on the scaffold. I'll be inside, guiding the beam into place from below. Your job is to keep it steady, keep it level, and do exactly what I tell you. Nothing more, nothing less."

"I understand."

"Do you?" The question wasn't rhetorical. "Because if you don't, that beam comes down on my head. Or yours. Or it cracks, and I have to source a new one, which costs money and time I don't have."

"I understand."

Yamada studied him for another moment, then nodded. "Good. Let's get you up there."


The scaffold was higher than it looked from the ground.

Not dangerously high—maybe three meters—but enough to make the body aware of its position in space, to awaken some primal part of the brain that remembered when heights meant predators and falling meant death. Sheldon gripped the railing and made himself breathe.

"First time on a scaffold?" Yamada called up.

"First time since college."

"Physics lab?"

"Something like that." The lie came easily—close enough to truth, far enough from explanation. "I'm fine. Just getting oriented."

"Take your time. We don't start until you're ready."

It was an unexpected kindness, delivered without sentiment. Yamada wasn't coddling him—the roofer didn't coddle anyone—but he understood that rushing led to mistakes, and mistakes led to consequences none of them wanted.

Sheldon took a breath, then another, and felt his body settle into the new reality of elevated ground. His hands found their grip on the beam that served as the platform's main support. His feet spread for balance. His center of gravity lowered instinctively.

*This body knows things*, he thought. *Things I never learned. Things I couldn't have learned.*

"Ready," he said.


The lift was controlled chaos.

Yamada had rigged a system of ropes and pulleys that distributed the beam's weight across multiple anchor points. Sheldon's job was to guide the end nearest him, keeping it level as the timber rose through the gap in the roof structure and into position.

"Steady," Yamada called from below. "Keep it level. Two more inches... there. Hold."

Sheldon held. His arms burned with the effort—the beam wasn't impossibly heavy, but holding anything at an awkward angle for extended periods was its own kind of trial. Sweat ran down his face, stinging his eyes. He blinked it away and kept his grip.

"Rotating... now."

The beam turned in his hands, guided by Yamada's movements below. Sheldon compensated, shifting his weight, maintaining the level despite the change in orientation.

"Good. Now lower—slowly—into the cradle."

The cradle was a notch cut into the support structure, waiting to receive the beam's end. Sheldon lowered his end inch by inch, watching it settle into position with the satisfaction of a puzzle piece finding its home.

"Hold there." Yamada's voice was tight with concentration. "I'm securing the other end."

The sound of a hammer echoed through the structure—once, twice, three times. Then silence.

"You can let go now."

Sheldon released his grip, and the beam stayed where it was, solid and secure, a new bone in the house's skeleton.

"Good work," Yamada said, emerging from beneath the scaffolding. "Better than I expected."

"Thank you."

"Don't thank me. You did the work." The roofer wiped his forehead with a rag. "Tea break. Then we do the bracing."


They sat on the engawa—cleared of debris now, solid enough to hold their weight—and drank tea from the thermos Sheldon had brought.

"You've done this before," Yamada said. It wasn't a question.

"No. Not this exactly."

"Something like it. The way you moved up there—that's not beginner's luck. That's trained response."

Sheldon thought about how to answer. The truth was impossible: *I woke up eleven days ago in a body that knows things I never learned, with skills I never practiced, in a life I didn't choose.* No one would believe that. No one should believe that.

"I learn fast," he said instead. "And I had good teachers."

"Must have." Yamada finished his tea and set down the cup. "Whoever they were, thank them if you see them again."

*Dorothy*, Sheldon thought. *Thank Dorothy.* But Dorothy had never taught him beam work, had never shown him how to hold weight at height, had never trained his body to respond to the demands of construction. Whatever was in him now—whatever had been given—it went beyond anything his grandmother could have provided.

"I will," he said. "If I see them."


The bracing took another two hours.

Cross-supports, angled pieces that locked the beam into place and distributed its load across the surrounding structure. Sheldon worked under Yamada's direction, holding pieces steady while the roofer drove nails, adjusting positions when measurements demanded it.

By the time they finished, the south side of the roof was structurally sound. Not complete—tiles still needed laying, underlayment still needed finishing—but the skeleton was whole again. The house could bear its own weight.

"Tomorrow, we start on the east," Yamada said, climbing down from the scaffolding. "The damage there is less severe. Should go faster."

"What about the path?"

"Your path?" The roofer shrugged. "Finish it if you want. The afternoon is yours. I have other clients to visit."

He packed his tools, loaded them into the truck, and drove away with a wave that was more acknowledgment than farewell. Sheldon stood in the yard, looking up at the work they'd done, feeling the good ache of muscles that had been used well.

*The house is healing*, he thought. *Piece by piece. Day by day.*

Just like him.

#### Scene 11.B: The Afternoon

The stone path wanted finishing.

Sheldon ate the bento Katsuhito had prepared—rice balls, pickled vegetables, a few slices of tamagoyaki—sitting on the newly-solid engawa with his back against the house. His arms still ached from the beam work, but it was a good ache, the kind that meant something had been accomplished.

When the food was gone, he returned to the path.

The remaining third was the most challenging section—the stones here had shifted the most, some tilted nearly vertical, others buried so deep they'd become more foundation than surface. Sheldon worked methodically, prying each stone free, examining the earth beneath, relaying gravel where drainage demanded it, resetting each piece in its proper position.

The work was slower than yesterday's. His body was tired, his focus slightly frayed from the morning's intensity. But the path didn't care about his fatigue. The path only cared about being done right.

By three o'clock, the last stone clicked into place.


He stood at the edge of the garden and looked at what he'd accomplished.

The path wound from the gate through the overgrown beds, around the choked pond, past the maple tree to the engawa. It wasn't perfectly straight—stone paths never were—but it was level now, stable, each piece locked against its neighbors in a pattern that would hold for years.

*I made this*, he thought. *Not with special powers or mysterious gifts. Just hands and time and patience.*

The thought brought something unexpected—a kind of pride he hadn't felt in years. Decades, maybe. His work at the university had been important, had pushed boundaries, had contributed to human knowledge. But it had always been abstract, theoretical, removed from the physical world by layers of mathematics and instrumentation.

This was different. This was stone and earth and sweat. This was something he could stand on.

*Dorothy would be proud*, he thought. *She always said real work leaves calluses.*

He looked at his hands. The calluses were forming.


The walk back to the shrine was peaceful.

Sheldon moved through the forest without hurrying, letting his body find its own rhythm after the day's labor. Birds called overhead. The afternoon light filtered golden through the canopy. Somewhere in the distance, a woodpecker drummed its persistent message.

*Eleven days*, he thought. *Eleven days of mornings and afternoons and evenings. Eleven days of becoming.*

He'd lost count somewhere around day five or six—the days had started to blur together, one flowing into the next without the sharp boundaries that novelty provided. But now, looking back, he could trace the arc of his transformation. From the field to the bus to the steps. From stranger to guest to something approaching family. From confusion to acceptance to this—whatever this was.

*Purpose*, maybe. *Direction. A reason to keep going.*

The shrine appeared through the trees, and with it the sound of a child's voice.

"Sheldon-san!"

Tenchi was running toward him before Sheldon had fully emerged from the forest, small legs pumping, face flushed with the urgency of reunion.

"You didn't fall!"

"I promised I wouldn't."

"I know, but—" The boy crashed into Sheldon's legs, arms wrapping tight. "I worried anyway."

Sheldon crouched to meet his eyes. "Worrying is okay. It means you care. But I kept my promise, didn't I?"

"Yeah." Tenchi's smile was watery but real. "You always do."


Dinner was celebration.

Not formally—there were no special dishes, no announced occasion—but the mood was lighter than usual, buoyed by the sense of accomplishment that hung in the air. Sheldon told them about the beam work, about Yamada's system of ropes and pulleys, about the moment the timber had settled into its cradle.

"So the house is fixed?" Tenchi asked.

"Part of it. The bones are good now. The rest is just... skin."

"Houses have skin?"

"Tiles. Walls. The parts you can see." Sheldon shaped the idea with his hands, building an invisible house in the air between them. "The bones hold everything up. The skin keeps the weather out. You need both."

Tenchi considered this anatomy lesson with the seriousness it deserved. "What about the heart?"

"What do you mean?"

"Houses have bones and skin. Do they have hearts?"

Sheldon thought about the Tsukino house—empty for four years, waiting for someone to bring it back to life. "Maybe the people who live in them are the heart. A house without people is just... bones and skin. It needs someone inside to make it alive."

"Then you'll be the heart," Tenchi said, as if this were obvious. "When you move in. You'll make it alive."

Something shifted in Sheldon's chest. "I hope so."

"You will." The boy's confidence was absolute. "You make everything alive. That's what you do."


The evening settled into familiar patterns.

Bath, stories, the slow descent into sleep. Tenchi didn't ask Sheldon to stay tonight—three nights now without the request, the pattern becoming established. The boy fell asleep with the one-eared rabbit tucked under his arm and the family picture watching from the wall.

Sheldon waited until the breathing evened, then slipped from the room.

Katsuhito was on the engawa, the bird carving in his hands, the evening dark and quiet around him. He didn't look up as Sheldon settled beside him.

"The path is finished," Sheldon said.

"I know. Tenchi told me you didn't fall."

"He was worried."

"He worries about everyone he loves." The old priest's knife moved in small, precise strokes. "It's how he shows it."

Sheldon watched the bird take shape—the curve of a wing emerging from the wood, feathers suggested rather than defined. "How long have you been carving?"

"Since I was younger than Tenchi." A pause that held more than the words suggested. "It helps me think. Keeps the hands busy while the mind works."

"What are you thinking about?"

Katsuhito's knife stilled. "Many things. The past. The future. The stranger who arrived eleven days ago and is no longer a stranger."

"Am I that different from when I arrived?"

"You're the same person. But you're more... present now. Less like someone watching their life and more like someone living it."

The observation landed with unexpected accuracy. Sheldon had felt that shift, had noticed himself settling into his days with increasing comfort. But he hadn't known it was visible from outside.

"Is that good?"

"It's healthy." The knife resumed its work. "A man who watches his life eventually stops living it. A man who lives his life eventually forgets he was ever watching."

*Forget*, Sheldon thought. *Will I forget? Will this become so normal that I forget it ever wasn't?*

Maybe. Maybe that was what healing looked like.

"Good night, Katsuhito-sensei."

"Good night, Tsukino-san."


- "Something he could stand on"
- Took Days 10-11 (afternoon work)

#### Scene 11.C: Evening

The night air carried the smell of cedar and distant rain.

Sheldon sat with his cooling tea, watching the last light fade from the sky above the shrine. Katsuhito had gone inside, the bird carving tucked away for another day, leaving Sheldon alone with the darkness and his thoughts.

*A man who lives his life eventually forgets he was ever watching.*

The words circled in his mind, settling into place alongside all the other wisdom the old priest had offered over eleven days. Katsuhito spoke in fragments, in observations, in questions that were really answers. It was a teaching style, Sheldon realized—not lecturing, but pointing. Showing the way without demanding it be followed.

*I'm learning*, he thought. *Not just cooking, not just carpentry. I'm learning how to be here.*

The great tree stood silent in the darkness, its presence felt rather than seen. Sheldon's eyes found it anyway—that automatic draw, that compass-needle pull toward something he couldn't name. Whatever it was, it felt like home now. Whatever it meant, it had become part of him.


The bath was brief and necessary.

His muscles protested the day's labor—beam work and stone work, arms and back and legs all contributing to the chorus of complaint. But it was a good protest, the kind that meant progress had been made. The house was stronger now. The path was complete. Day by day, piece by piece, something was being built.

He dried off and dressed for sleep, the routine automatic now, requiring no conscious thought. The guest room waited, familiar, almost his own.

*Almost*, he thought. *Soon, the Tsukino house will be ready. Soon, I'll have a place that's truly mine.*

But not tonight. Tonight, he belonged here, in this room that had held Nobuyuki's childhood and now held Sheldon's present. Tonight, he was where he needed to be.


Sleep came with a dream.

Not Dorothy this time, not the mothers blended together, but something else—something harder to hold. He stood in a field of white flowers under a sky that held too many stars, and someone was speaking to him in a language he almost understood. A voice like bells, like water, like the space between heartbeats.

*You are doing well*, the voice said. *You are becoming what you need to be.*

He tried to turn, to see the speaker, but the dream dissolved before he could move. The stars scattered. The flowers faded. The voice became silence.

He woke with the echo of it still resonating in his chest, not sure if he'd dreamed or remembered or glimpsed something real.

The dawn light was beginning to touch the paper screens.

Day twelve had begun.


- Day 9: Dorothy alone (kitchen, "doing good")
- Day 11: Unknown speaker (white flowers, stars)

- May be connected to Sheldon's origin
- Language "almost understood"
- Cosmic imagery (too many stars)
- **Note:** No forbidden words used; remains ambiguous

- Guest room "almost his own"

#### Scene 12.A: Twelfth Morning

The twelfth morning carried the echo of stars.

Sheldon woke with the dream still clinging to him—white flowers, a voice like bells, the sense of being seen by something vast and kind. It faded as dreams did, dissolving in the light of the paper screens, but the feeling remained. A warmth in his chest. A certainty he couldn't explain.

*You are becoming what you need to be.*

He didn't know what that meant. He wasn't sure he wanted to know. But the words felt true in a way that bypassed logic, settling into his bones like something that had always been there, waiting to be remembered.

He rose and went to the kitchen.


Breakfast was routine now.

Rice from the bin, water from the pump, the sequence of movements that had become second nature. Sheldon worked without conscious thought, his hands knowing what his mind didn't need to direct. The soup simmered. The fish grilled. The pickles arranged themselves on their small plates.

*Twelve days*, he thought. *Twelve days of mornings like this.*

It was Friday. Tomorrow would be Saturday—Nobuyuki's day to visit. The rhythm of the week was beginning to make sense, each day carrying its own weight and meaning in the pattern of shrine life.

Tenchi appeared at seven, already dressed, vibrating with the particular energy of a child approaching the weekend.

"Tomorrow Father comes!"

"I know."

"Will you cook something special? He likes your food better than Grandfather's." The boy's voice dropped to a conspiratorial whisper. "Don't tell Grandfather I said that."

"Your secret is safe."

They shared a smile—the kind of small conspiracy that built bonds without either party naming it.


The walk to the house was peaceful.

Sheldon moved through the forest with the ease of familiarity, his feet finding the path without conscious attention. Birds called overhead. The morning light filtered golden through the canopy. Somewhere in the distance, a truck engine rumbled—Yamada, probably, arriving at the house.

The dream lingered at the edges of his awareness, not demanding attention but present, like a note held too long after a chord had resolved. He let it be there without examining it. Some things weren't meant to be understood. Some things were only meant to be felt.

The house emerged through the trees, and with it the sight of Yamada's truck already parked in the yard. The roofer stood on the scaffolding, surveying the east side with professional attention.

"You're late," Yamada called down.

Sheldon checked his watch. "It's eight-thirty."

"I said the east side would go faster. That means earlier starts." But there was no real criticism in the words—just the gruff manner that was Yamada's default mode. "Come up. I want to show you something."


The east side was different.

Where the south had been damaged by water infiltration—years of slow rot spreading from a single point of failure—the east showed the marks of mechanical stress. Tiles had cracked along fault lines. Support structures had shifted under wind load. The damage was visible but contained, the kind of thing that came from normal wear rather than neglect.

"See this?" Yamada pointed to a beam that had bowed slightly under pressure. "Not rot. Just age. The wood dried out, lost its flexibility. Started to bend instead of flex."

Sheldon examined the beam, running his hand along its surface. The grain was tight, the wood still solid despite its deformation.

"Can it be straightened?"

"No. But it can be supported." Yamada moved to the next section. "We sister a new beam alongside it, transfer the load gradually, let the old one rest. It'll hold another fifty years that way."

"Sister?"

"Construction term. You attach a new piece alongside the damaged one, bolt them together, let them share the weight." The roofer's eyes held a glint of something—approval, maybe, or the satisfaction of teaching. "Like having a partner help you carry something heavy. Neither one could manage alone, but together they handle it fine."

*Like having a partner*, Sheldon thought. The metaphor landed somewhere unexpected, somewhere that had nothing to do with beams or construction.


The morning passed in focused work.

Yamada had prepared the sistering beams in advance—cut to length, pre-drilled for bolts, ready to be fitted into place. Sheldon's job was to hold each beam steady while Yamada drove the hardware home, maintaining pressure and position while the roofer worked with systematic precision.

It was easier than yesterday's beam lift. The sistering beams were smaller, lighter, more manageable at height. But the work still demanded concentration, still required the kind of continuous attention that left no room for wandering thoughts.

By noon, three beams had been reinforced.

"Good progress," Yamada said, climbing down from the scaffolding. "The tiles can go up tomorrow. Maybe Sunday, if the weather holds."

"That fast?"

"I told you the east side was less severe." The roofer pulled a cloth from his pocket and wiped his hands. "The south was the real work. This is just finishing."

*Finishing.* The word carried more weight than Yamada intended. The roof was almost done. The house was almost ready. Sheldon's temporary residence at the shrine was approaching its end.

"How much longer until I can move in?"

Yamada considered the question with the same precision he applied to his work. "Tiles go up this weekend. Flashing and sealing, maybe three more days. Then you'll need to reconnect utilities—water, electric. A week, maybe ten days, if you push."

A week. Ten days at most. And then the Tsukino house would be his.

"Thank you," Sheldon said. "For everything."

"Thank me when the bill is paid." But Yamada's voice held no hardness. "You've been a good client, Tsukino-san. Better than most. I'll tell people that, if anyone asks."


> "You attach a new piece alongside the damaged one, bolt them together, let them share the weight. Like having a partner help you carry something heavy."

#### Scene 12.B: The Afternoon

The pond wanted attention.

With the stone path complete and Yamada handling the roof, Sheldon had run out of excuses to avoid the water feature. It had been lurking at the edge of his awareness since his first visit—a clouded mirror of neglect, its edges lost in overgrowth, its surface obscured by years of fallen leaves and accumulated debris.

He stood at its edge and assessed the work ahead.

The pond was larger than it had first appeared—perhaps three meters across, roughly oval, fed by what looked like a natural spring that seeped through rocks at one end. The water itself was dark with tannins and organic matter, but beneath the murk he could glimpse the suggestion of stone sides, carefully laid in some previous generation.

*Someone built this*, he thought. *Someone cared about it enough to make it beautiful.*

He found a long-handled net among the garden tools and began to skim.


The work was meditative in its way.

Leaves came up in waterlogged clumps, heavy and dark, releasing the smell of decay as they left the water. Branches followed, some of them substantial—fallen from the maple tree that overhung one edge, or blown in from the surrounding forest. Sheldon worked methodically, section by section, watching the water gradually clear.

By mid-afternoon, he could see the bottom.

Stones, as he'd suspected—flat river rocks laid in overlapping patterns, creating a mosaic that must have been striking when the pond was maintained. Some had shifted over the years, creating gaps where silt had accumulated, but the underlying structure was sound. A few hours of work, maybe a day, and it would be beautiful again.

*The house is healing*, he thought. *The garden is healing. Even the water is coming back to life.*

It felt like a metaphor for something larger, but he didn't examine it too closely. Sometimes it was enough to do the work and let the meaning take care of itself.


The walk back to the shrine was slower than usual.

Sheldon's clothes were wet from pond work, his arms tired from the repetitive motion of netting and lifting. But beneath the fatigue was satisfaction—the particular pleasure of seeing progress, of watching neglect transform into order.

He emerged from the forest to find the shrine already preparing for evening. Katsuhito was on the engawa, working on the bird carving. Tenchi was somewhere inside, audible through the open doors—humming to himself, the sound of a child at peace with his world.

"The pond?" Katsuhito asked without looking up.

"Getting there. I cleared the surface. Tomorrow I'll work on the edges."

"The pond was my wife's favorite place." The old priest's voice was matter-of-fact, carrying the weight of memory without drowning in it. "She would sit there for hours, watching the reflections."

"I didn't know."

"How could you? She died before you came here. Before Tenchi was born, even." The knife paused its work. "Her name was Itsuki. She was the one who planted the maple tree."

Sheldon absorbed this new piece of the shrine's history. Another layer, another life that had touched this place and left its mark. Itsuki, who loved the pond. Kiyone, who waved from the engawa. The women who had shaped this family, present now only in the spaces they'd left behind.

"I'll take good care of it," he said. "The pond."

"I know you will."


Dinner was focused on tomorrow.

Tenchi couldn't stop talking about his father's visit—what they would do, where they would go, what Sheldon should cook. The boy's excitement was infectious, filling the room with energy that made even the simple meal feel like celebration.

"Father likes fish," Tenchi announced. "But not the way Grandfather makes it. He likes it with the sauce you made last time."

"The teriyaki?"

"Yes! That one!" The boy's eyes went wide with remembered pleasure. "Can you make it again? Please?"

"I can make it again."

"And rice. And vegetables. And—" Tenchi's list continued, growing more elaborate with each addition, until Katsuhito gently intervened.

"Your father is coming for one meal, Tenchi, not a week."

"But Sheldon-san can make everything. He's the best cook."

"Even the best cook needs time." The old priest's eyes found Sheldon's across the table. "Though I admit, the teriyaki was excellent."

"High praise," Sheldon said.

"Deserved praise. There's a difference."


The evening settled into familiar patterns.

Bath, stories, the ritual of settling Tenchi for sleep. The boy was too excited to drift off easily, his mind still racing with tomorrow's possibilities, but eventually the rhythm of routine overcame the buzz of anticipation.

Sheldon waited in the hallway until the breathing evened, then returned to the main room.

Katsuhito wasn't there. The engawa was empty, the bird carving set aside. Sheldon poured himself tea and sat alone, listening to the sounds of the shrine at night—the settling of wood, the whisper of wind, the distant hoot of the owl that had become a familiar voice.

*A week*, he thought. *Maybe ten days. And then I'll have a home of my own.*

The thought brought mixed feelings. The Tsukino house was what he'd been working toward—a place that was his, a foundation for the life he was building. But leaving the shrine meant leaving this, too. These evenings. These routines. The particular peace of nights spent in Nobuyuki's childhood room.

*I'm not leaving*, he reminded himself. *I'm just... relocating. The shrine will still be here. Tenchi will still need me. Nothing that matters will change.*

But change was coming anyway. He could feel it in the approaching deadline, in the tiles that would go up tomorrow, in the countdown toward something new.

*That's what living is*, he thought. *One change after another, handled as they come.*

He finished his tea and went to bed.


Sleep came without dreams.

Or if there were dreams, they didn't survive the crossing into waking. Sheldon rose to Saturday morning with a clear mind and the knowledge that Nobuyuki was coming, that teriyaki was expected, and that somewhere in the village Yamada was preparing to finish a roof.

Day thirteen had begun.


- Katsuhito agreed it was "excellent"
- "Deserved praise. There's a difference."

**Move-In Mixed Feelings:**

- Satisfaction at progress
- Reluctance to leave shrine routines
- "I'm not leaving. I'm just relocating."
- Change coming regardless

#### Scene 13.A: Thirteenth Morning

The thirteenth morning belonged to anticipation.

Sheldon woke to the sound of small feet padding past his door—Tenchi, already up, already vibrating with the energy of a day he'd been counting toward since Monday. The boy's excitement had been building all week, a steady crescendo that would peak sometime today when his father's car appeared on the road below.

*Saturday*, Sheldon thought. *Nobuyuki's day.*

He rose and went to the kitchen, knowing the morning's rhythm would be different but not sure exactly how.


Tenchi was at the table when Sheldon arrived, still in sleeping clothes, watching the door with the intensity of a cat at a mouse hole.

"He's not coming yet," Sheldon said gently. "It's not even six."

"I know." But the boy didn't move, didn't look away from the door. "I just want to be ready."

"You're in your pajamas."

"I'll change when I hear the car."

There was no arguing with that kind of logic—the logic of a child's heart, immune to practical considerations. Sheldon let it be and started breakfast.


The meal was quieter than usual.

Katsuhito emerged at his customary time, assessed the situation with a glance, and said nothing about Tenchi's vigil. The three of them ate in a silence punctuated only by Tenchi's occasional glances toward the door, each one carrying the same disappointed hope.

"He usually arrives around noon," Katsuhito said, more to Sheldon than to Tenchi. "The morning trains from the city."

"I know," Tenchi said. "But sometimes he comes early. Sometimes he takes the night train."

"Has he ever taken the night train?"

"Not yet." The boy's jaw set with stubborn optimism. "But he might."

Sheldon exchanged a look with Katsuhito—the kind of look adults shared over the heads of children, communicating volumes in a single glance. *Let him hope. Let him have this.*


The morning passed in preparations.

Sheldon cleaned the kitchen twice, swept the engawa, made sure the guest room—his room, Nobuyuki's room—was presentable for its original occupant. He found himself straightening things that didn't need straightening, adjusting arrangements that were already fine.

"You're nervous," Katsuhito observed, finding him repositioning cushions for the third time.

"I want to make a good impression."

"You already have. Last week, remember?"

"Last week he was assessing me. This week he's... checking." Sheldon set down the cushion. "Making sure the first impression wasn't a fluke."

"Is that what you think he's doing?"

"Isn't it?"

Katsuhito considered this for a moment. "Perhaps. But consider: he's also checking on his son. His father. His home. You're part of that now, but you're not the whole of it."

The words helped, a little. Sheldon was part of the pattern now, not the center of attention. Whatever assessment Nobuyuki was conducting, it was broader than just one strange American who had appeared at his childhood shrine.


The teriyaki demanded attention.

Sheldon had planned the meal carefully—fish from Ito-san's store, purchased yesterday with this dinner in mind. The marinade needed time to work, the sauce needed slow reduction, the vegetables needed proper preparation. He began at ten, giving himself two hours before the expected arrival.

The work was calming. It always was. His hands found the rhythm of cooking like a musician finding familiar keys—automatic, confident, guided by something deeper than conscious thought.

*Mirin, soy sauce, a touch of sake, a little sugar.* The marinade came together without recipe, without measurement, the proportions emerging from somewhere inside that knew what they should be.

*Supreme Chef*, he thought. The name he'd given the ability—or the gift, or whatever it was. Still manifesting, still growing, still surprising him with what it could do.

He scored the fish in careful parallel lines, laid it in the marinade, covered the dish, and set it aside. Two hours. That would be enough.


The car appeared at eleven-thirty.

Tenchi heard it first—his vigil had never really ended, despite his grandfather's attempts to distract him—and was out the door before Sheldon could react. Small feet pounded down the steps, the sound growing fainter with distance, replaced eventually by the higher-pitched sound of reunion.

"Father! Father!"

Sheldon moved to the engawa, watching from above. Nobuyuki had emerged from a modest sedan, suit jacket already shed, sleeves rolled up in preparation for country life. He caught Tenchi as the boy launched himself, swinging him up in a practiced arc that spoke of hundreds of such reunions.

"Hey, hey. I'm here. I'm here."

"You came early!"

"I took an earlier train. Wanted to see you sooner."

Tenchi's joy was incandescent, visible even from this distance. Sheldon felt something shift in his chest—not jealousy, exactly, but something adjacent to it. A recognition of what father and son shared, and his own position outside that particular bond.

*He's not replacing you*, he told himself. *You're not competing. Different roles. Different relationships.*

The mantra helped. A little.


Nobuyuki climbed the steps with Tenchi still in his arms, the boy chattering non-stop about everything that had happened since last weekend. Sheldon caught fragments—the pond, the roof, Sheldon's cooking, the dream about frogs—a week's worth of life compressed into breathless sentences.

"Tsukino-san." Nobuyuki reached the top, slightly winded, and inclined his head in greeting. "Good to see you again."

"And you. Welcome back."

"I hear you've been busy. Tenchi says the house is almost ready?"

"The roof should be done this weekend. Yamada's putting up tiles today."

"That's fast. Good." Nobuyuki set Tenchi down, the boy immediately grabbing his hand as if afraid he might float away. "And you're still cooking?"

"Teriyaki tonight. By request."

Nobuyuki's eyes flicked to Tenchi, then back to Sheldon. "He mentioned that. Several times." A small smile. "I'm looking forward to it."


The morning's remaining hours passed in the particular rhythm of family reunion.

Nobuyuki spent time with Tenchi—walking the grounds, admiring the cleared garden, exclaiming appropriately over each improvement Sheldon had made. The boy served as tour guide, his pride evident in every gesture, every explanation.

"See? The pond is clean now. Sheldon-san did that."

"I see."

"And the path has new stones. He fixed them all."

"I see that too."

"And he cooks the best food. Better than anyone. Even better than—" A pause, a glance at Katsuhito, a diplomatic pivot. "—restaurants."

Nobuyuki's eyes met Sheldon's across the garden, carrying something that might have been gratitude. Or acknowledgment. Or simply the recognition of one man seeing another's contribution to his son's happiness.

Sheldon nodded once and returned to the kitchen.

The teriyaki was ready.


- The pond (cleared)
- The roof (almost done)
- Sheldon's cooking
- Dream about frogs
- Path stones fixed

- Nervous (wants to make good impression)

#### Scene 13.B: The Dinner

Onigiri from the morning's rice, pickled vegetables, soup kept warm from breakfast. The family—and it was a family now, Sheldon realized, even if the configuration was unconventional—gathered around the low table with the easy comfort of people who had found their rhythm.

Nobuyuki ate with the particular focus of a man who'd skipped breakfast to catch an earlier train. Tenchi sat so close to his father that their shoulders touched, as if proximity could make up for five days of absence.

"The roof looks good," Nobuyuki said between bites. "Even from the road, I could see the difference."

"Yamada-san knows his work," Katsuhito replied. "And he had good help."

The old priest's eyes found Sheldon's, carrying acknowledgment that didn't need words. Sheldon nodded once and returned to his meal.


The afternoon belonged to father and son.

Sheldon retreated to the kitchen, preparing ingredients for dinner while the sounds of reunion drifted through the open doors. Tenchi's voice, high and bright. Nobuyuki's lower responses, patient and warm. The rhythm of a relationship maintained across distance, week by week.

The teriyaki fish came out of its marinade gleaming, the sauce having worked its way into every scored line. Sheldon laid it on the grill with careful attention, watching the heat transform raw protein into something greater.

*Timing*, the internal voice said. *Everything is timing. Too early and it won't caramelize. Too late and it burns. Find the moment and hold it.*

He found the moment. The fish sizzled, the sauce darkened to mahogany, the smell filled the kitchen with promises of satisfaction.


Dinner was served at six.

The four of them gathered around the table—Katsuhito at his customary place, Nobuyuki across from him, Tenchi vibrating between them, Sheldon completing the square. The teriyaki gleamed on its serving plate, flanked by rice and vegetables and the small dishes that made a meal into an occasion.

"Itadakimasu," they said together.

Tenchi waited approximately three seconds before reaching for the fish.

"Slowly," Nobuyuki cautioned. "It's not going anywhere."

"But it smells so good."

It did smell good. Sheldon had outdone himself, or the ability had outdone him—he wasn't sure which anymore. The fish was perfectly cooked, the sauce balanced between sweet and savory, the vegetables crisp with the right amount of char.

Nobuyuki took his first bite and went still.

"This is..." He paused, searching for words. "This is exceptional."

"Thank you."

"No, I mean—" The architect set down his chopsticks, giving the compliment proper weight. "I've eaten at restaurants in Tokyo. Expensive places. This is better than most of them."

Katsuhito made a small sound that might have been agreement.

"Father thinks so too," Tenchi announced, delighted to have his preferences validated. "I told you, Father. I told you Sheldon-san was the best."

"You did tell me. I should have believed you." Nobuyuki resumed eating, but his eyes kept returning to Sheldon with something like curiosity. "Where did you learn to cook like this?"

The question hung in the air. Sheldon had faced it before, from Katsuhito, from Yamada in different form. The answer was always the same—incomplete, true in spirit if not in detail.

"I've always enjoyed cooking. And I had a grandmother who believed food was love made visible."

"She must have been a remarkable woman."

"She was."

Nobuyuki accepted this, but Sheldon could see the questions accumulating behind his eyes. The architect wasn't stupid. He knew something didn't quite add up—a man who appeared from nowhere with restaurant-quality skills, who fit into this household like a missing piece, who had become essential to his son in less than two weeks.

*Later*, Sheldon thought. *Those questions are for later. Tonight is for dinner.*


The meal wound down into evening.

Tenchi talked through most of it—about school, about the garden, about the pond and the path and everything Sheldon had done while his father was away. Nobuyuki listened with the patience of a man who understood that listening was sometimes the most important thing a father could do.

"And Sheldon-san had a dream about stars," Tenchi said, somewhere between the main course and the fruit. "He told me about it."

Sheldon's chopsticks paused. He didn't remember telling Tenchi about the dream—but then again, children noticed things adults didn't mean to share. A expression, a distant look, a word murmured to no one in particular.

"Stars?" Nobuyuki asked.

"He said there were too many. Like someone spilled them." Tenchi's interpretation of whatever he'd overheard. "I think that sounds nice."

"It does," Nobuyuki agreed, his eyes finding Sheldon's again. "Dreams about stars are usually good ones."

Sheldon didn't correct him. The dream hadn't been about stars exactly—it had been about being seen by something vast, about a voice that spoke in bells and water. But *too many stars* was close enough. Closer than Tenchi should have been able to guess.


Bath time came and went.

Nobuyuki handled the ritual tonight—father and son in the ofuro together, their voices echoing through the house in a conversation Sheldon couldn't quite hear. He washed dishes instead, letting the familiar work occupy his hands while his mind wandered.

*Two weeks*, he thought. *Almost two weeks since I woke up in that field.*

It felt longer. It felt like months, years even. The person who had opened his eyes in the rice paddy seemed like a stranger now—confused, uncertain, unmoored. The person washing dishes in this kitchen knew where he belonged. Knew who needed him. Knew, at least in broad strokes, what he was supposed to be doing.

*You are becoming what you need to be.*

The dream-voice's words rose unbidden. Sheldon let them settle, neither accepting nor rejecting them. Time would tell what they meant. Time was the one thing he seemed to have plenty of.


Tenchi's bedtime was later than usual—special dispensation for a special day.

The boy fought sleep with the desperation of someone who knew that morning meant goodbye, that Sunday would pass too quickly, that Monday would take his father away again. But eventually even desperation lost to exhaustion, and Tenchi drifted off with one hand clutching his father's sleeve.

Nobuyuki extracted himself carefully, leaving the one-eared rabbit in his place, and joined Sheldon and Katsuhito in the main room.

"He's getting attached to you," Nobuyuki said without preamble. "More than I expected."

"Is that a problem?"

"I don't know yet." The architect accepted the tea Katsuhito offered, wrapping both hands around the cup. "It's not what I would have predicted. Tenchi has been... careful, since his mother died. He doesn't let people in easily."

"But he let you in," Katsuhito added, looking at Sheldon. "Almost immediately. As if he recognized something in you."

The words echoed things the old priest had said before—about children seeing clearly, about trust being given rather than earned. Sheldon didn't know how to respond, so he simply waited.

"I've been thinking about it all week," Nobuyuki continued. "Trying to understand what makes you different. You're kind, yes. Patient. Good with children. But other people have been kind to him, patient with him, and he's kept them at arm's length."

"Maybe it's timing," Sheldon offered. "Maybe he was ready to let someone in, and I happened to be the one who arrived."

"Maybe." Nobuyuki didn't sound convinced. "Or maybe there's something about you specifically. Something I can't see but he can."

The conversation brushed against territory Sheldon couldn't enter—the truth of his origin, the mystery of his gifts, the cosmic intervention that had placed him here. He felt the weight of secrets pressing against his tongue and kept them locked away.

"Whatever the reason," he said carefully, "I'm glad to be here. I'll do my best not to let him down."

"That's all anyone can ask." Nobuyuki finished his tea and set down the cup. "But if you hurt him—if you leave without warning, if you break his trust—"

"I won't."

"You can't know that."

"No," Sheldon admitted. "But I can know my intention. And my intention is to stay."

The two men regarded each other across the table—the father protecting his son, the stranger who had become something more. Something passed between them that wasn't quite acceptance but was adjacent to it. Recognition, maybe. Or the beginning of trust.

"Good night," Nobuyuki said finally. "And thank you. For the dinner, and for... everything else."

"Good night."


The house settled into sleep.

Sheldon sat alone on the engawa, watching the stars—normal stars, the right number of them—wheel slowly overhead. The night was cool but not cold, carrying the smells of spring and growing things.

*Thirteen days*, he thought. *And now I'm having conversations about intention and permanence with a man who barely knows me.*

But that was what family meant, wasn't it? Being known before you were understood. Being trusted before you'd proven yourself. Being given a place at the table and expected to earn it by staying.

He could do that. He would do that.

Tomorrow would bring Sunday, and then Monday, and then all the days after. The roof would be finished. The house would be ready. His life would continue, one day at a time, in this place he'd somehow been given.

*You are becoming what you need to be.*

He still didn't know what that meant. But he was starting to think it might not matter.

#### Scene 14.A: Fourteenth Morning

The fourteenth morning began with rain.

Sheldon woke to the sound of it drumming on the roof, steady and insistent, turning the world outside the screens into a gray wash of falling water. The kind of rain that wouldn't stop anytime soon—the kind that settled in and made itself comfortable.

*No tiles today*, he thought. *Yamada won't work in this.*

He rose anyway, following the routine that had become instinct over two weeks. Kitchen, pump, rice, soup. The rhythm of morning made manifest in small actions.


The household was slower to wake.

Sunday had its own pace, different from the weekday urgency of kindergarten buses and work schedules. When Nobuyuki finally appeared in the kitchen doorway, it was past seven—later than usual, the kind of indulgence that weekend visits allowed.

"Good morning." The architect's hair was still sleep-mussed, his borrowed yukata slightly askew. "Is that coffee?"

"Tea. But I can make coffee if you prefer."

"Tea is fine." Nobuyuki settled at the table, accepting the cup Sheldon poured. "Tenchi is still sleeping. He wore himself out yesterday."

"He was excited to see you."

"He's always excited." A small smile, carrying equal parts love and guilt. "I wish I could be here more often."

"He knows you work hard."

"Does knowing make it easier?"

The question hung in the air, weighted with the particular burden of a parent who felt he wasn't enough. Sheldon didn't have an answer—not a good one, not one that would help.

"I think," he said carefully, "that children understand more than we give them credit for. Tenchi knows you love him. He knows you come when you can. That's what matters."

"Is it?"

"It's all any of us can do. Love them and show up when we're able."

Nobuyuki studied him over the rim of his cup. "You sound like you've thought about this."

"I've had time to think. These past two weeks." Sheldon gestured toward the rain-gray window. "Days like this, when work isn't possible. The mind wanders."

"Where does it wander to?"

*White flowers. Too many stars. A voice like bells.* But that wasn't what Nobuyuki was asking about, and it wasn't what Sheldon could share.

"To what matters," he said instead. "To family. To building something that will last."


The morning passed in the particular rhythm of rainy Sundays.

Tenchi woke around eight, immediately seeking out his father, immediately demanding to know what they would do today. The rain meant no outdoor adventures, no trips to the village, no explorations of the forest path.

"We could tell stories," Nobuyuki offered.

"Boring."

"We could draw pictures."

"Did that yesterday."

"We could help Sheldon-san in the kitchen."

Tenchi's face brightened. "Can we? Can we cook something?"

Sheldon found himself recruited into a project before he could object—not that he would have. The boy's enthusiasm was infectious, transforming a rainy morning into an opportunity.

"What should we make?" Tenchi asked, bouncing on his toes.

"Something that takes time," Nobuyuki suggested, "since we have plenty of it."

The internal voice offered an answer before Sheldon consciously reached for one: *Nikujaga. Meat and potatoes. Comfort food for a gray day.*

"Nikujaga," he said aloud. "Do you know what that is?"

Tenchi's face lit up. "Mother used to make that."

The words landed like a stone in still water, sending ripples through the room. Nobuyuki's expression flickered—grief, memory, something that never quite healed.

"She did," he said quietly. "She made it for me on our first anniversary."

Sheldon hadn't known. Couldn't have known. But the internal voice had guided him to this, to a dish that carried memory and meaning for this family.

"Then we should make it together," he said. "All three of us."


Cooking became ceremony.

Sheldon demonstrated the techniques—how to cut the meat, how to slice the onions so they wouldn't make you cry, how to layer the potatoes just right. Tenchi watched with fierce attention, his small hands helping where they could. Nobuyuki worked beside them, a little awkward in the kitchen but willing to learn.

"Kiyone always said I had no talent for cooking," the architect admitted, fumbling with a peeler. "She wasn't wrong."

"Talent can be learned. What you need is patience."

"She used to say that too."

The conversation turned to memories—small stories about Kiyone, about her kitchen disasters and her eventual triumphs, about the way she laughed when things went wrong and the pride she took when they went right. Tenchi listened with the attention of someone hearing legends about a figure more myth than memory.

"She made the best nikujaga," Nobuyuki said, stirring the pot as Sheldon had shown him. "I never found anyone who could match it."

"Maybe this one will be close."

"Maybe."

But when they sat down to eat, and Nobuyuki took his first bite, something changed in his face. Not exactly recognition—the recipes weren't identical, couldn't be—but something adjacent to it. Familiarity. The echo of a taste he thought he'd lost.

"This is..." He stopped, swallowed, tried again. "This is very good."

"Is it close?" Tenchi asked, bouncing with hope. "Is it like Mother's?"

Nobuyuki looked at Sheldon, then at his son, then at the simple bowl of meat and potatoes that somehow carried so much weight.

"Yes," he said finally. "Yes, I think it is."

- Ability guided him to meaningful dish

#### Scene 14.B: The Departure

The afternoon passed too quickly.

That was how it always was, Sheldon was learning—time moved differently depending on what was at stake. The slow hours of morning, filled with cooking and conversation, had given way to an afternoon that seemed to slip through fingers like water.

Nobuyuki played with Tenchi in the main room, the rain still drumming outside, their laughter echoing through the shrine. Card games, drawing contests, the kind of simple activities that required nothing but presence and attention. Sheldon watched from the kitchen, preparing dinner, feeling the weight of the approaching goodbye.

At four o'clock, Nobuyuki began to gather his things.

"Already?" Tenchi asked, his voice small.

"The train schedule doesn't care about our plans." Nobuyuki crouched to meet his son's eyes. "You know I have to go back."

"I know."

"But I'll be here next Saturday. Same as always."

"Saturday is forever away."

"It always feels that way." Nobuyuki pulled the boy into a hug, holding him with the particular intensity of a parent who knew he was leaving. "But the days go by faster than you think. And Sheldon-san will be here. And Grandfather. You won't be alone."

Tenchi's face was pressed against his father's chest, muffling whatever he said in response. But his arms tightened, and he didn't let go for a long time.


The goodbye was its own kind of ritual.

Katsuhito emerged from wherever he'd been—meditating, perhaps, or giving father and son privacy—to see Nobuyuki off. The four of them gathered on the engawa, the rain having finally eased to a drizzle, the late afternoon light gray and soft.

"Take care of them," Nobuyuki said to Sheldon, the words pitched low enough that Tenchi couldn't hear.

"I will."

"And take care of yourself. The house will be ready soon. You'll have your own life to build."

"I know."

Nobuyuki studied him for a moment, as if searching for something in Sheldon's face. Whatever he found seemed to satisfy him, because he nodded once and turned to his father.

"The shrine looks good," he said. "Better than it has in years."

"We've had help." Katsuhito's eyes found Sheldon. "Good help."

"I can see that."

The handshake between father and son was brief and formal, the kind of gesture that carried decades of relationship in its simplicity. Then Nobuyuki crouched down one more time to Tenchi's level.

"Be good. Listen to your grandfather. And to Sheldon-san."

"I will."

"I know you will." One more hug, shorter this time but no less fierce. "I love you."

"I love you too."


The car disappeared down the mountain road, swallowed by trees and distance and the gray of fading afternoon.

Tenchi stood at the top of the steps, watching long after there was nothing to see. Sheldon stood beside him, close enough to touch but not quite touching—present, available, waiting.

"I don't like Sundays," Tenchi said finally.

"I know."

"He always leaves on Sundays."

"I know that too."

The boy turned to look at him, eyes red-rimmed but dry. "Will you leave on a Sunday?"

The question hit harder than expected. Sheldon felt it land in his chest, in the place where promises lived and where breaking them would matter most.

"I don't know when I'll leave," he said honestly. "But I don't have anywhere else to go. And I don't want to be anywhere else."

"Promise?"

"I promise to stay as long as I can. As long as you need me."

It wasn't the forever Tenchi wanted. But it was the truth, and the boy seemed to recognize it as such. He nodded once, wiped his face with the back of his hand, and turned toward the shrine.

"Can we have hot chocolate? Sheldon-san?"

"I think we can manage that."


The evening was quiet.

Hot chocolate for Tenchi, tea for the adults, the four of them sitting in the main room as night settled over the mountain. The rain had stopped, leaving behind the particular clarity that came after storms—air washed clean, stars beginning to emerge through breaking clouds.

"The roof should be done tomorrow," Katsuhito said. "Weather permitting."

"That fast?"

"Yamada-san is efficient. And the east side was less damaged." The old priest sipped his tea. "You could be in the house by the end of the week."

*By the end of the week.* The words carried more weight now than they had before. Moving into the Tsukino house meant leaving the shrine—not permanently, not completely, but in a way that would change everything. Different meals, different mornings, different nights spent under a different roof.

"I'll still be here," Sheldon said, as much to himself as to anyone else. "Every day. That won't change."

"I know." Katsuhito's eyes held understanding. "But change is coming anyway. It's better to prepare for it than to pretend it isn't happening."

Tenchi had fallen asleep against Sheldon's side, the hot chocolate having worked its magic. The boy's breathing was slow and even, his small body warm and heavy with trust.

"He's attached," Katsuhito continued. "More than I expected. More than is perhaps healthy."

"Is that my fault?"

"No. It's not fault at all. It's simply what happened." The old priest set down his cup. "Children need anchors. You became his. That's not wrong—it's necessary. But it means you carry a responsibility now that you didn't ask for."

"I know."

"Do you?" The question wasn't accusatory, just curious. "Do you understand what it means to be needed by a child who has already lost too much?"

Sheldon looked down at Tenchi, at the small face peaceful in sleep, at the hand still clutching his sleeve even in unconsciousness.

"I'm beginning to," he said. "Day by day."

"Good." Katsuhito rose. "I'll take him to bed. You should rest. Tomorrow will be busy."


Sleep came slowly.

Sheldon lay in the guest room—his room, Nobuyuki's room, the room that had become familiar over two weeks—and listened to the shrine settle into nighttime silence. The rain had truly stopped now, leaving only the drip of water from eaves and leaves, the gradual return of insect sounds.

*Two weeks*, he thought. *Fourteen days since I woke up in that field.*

It felt like longer. It felt like years. The person who had opened his eyes in the rice paddy seemed impossibly distant now—a stranger wearing his face, lost and confused and not yet knowing what he would become.

*You are becoming what you need to be.*

The dream-voice's words rose again, as they did more often now. Sheldon let them settle, finding in them a comfort that surprised him.

Whatever he was becoming, it involved this place. These people. This child who had chosen to trust him, this old priest who had chosen to accept him, this absent father who had chosen to allow him into his family's life.

*It's enough*, he thought. *For now, it's enough.*

He slept, and dreamed of nothing, and woke to the fifteenth morning.

#### Scene 15.A: Fifteenth Morning

The fifteenth morning came clear and bright.

Sheldon woke to sunlight streaming through the paper screens, the rain of yesterday a memory dissolved by a sky scrubbed clean of clouds. The kind of day that invited work, that demanded progress, that would brook no further delays.

*The roof*, he thought. *Today the roof is finished.*

He rose with purpose, the routine of morning now so deeply ingrained that his body moved without consulting his mind. Pump, rice, soup. The kettle heating. The first sounds of the household stirring.

Two weeks. Fifteen days since he'd opened his eyes in that field. And today, a milestone.


Breakfast was quick and efficient.

Tenchi was subdued in the way of Monday mornings—the weekend's joy faded, school waiting, the particular melancholy of five days stretching between now and Father's next visit. But he ate his rice without complaint, helped clear the dishes without being asked, and marched toward the waiting bus with only one backward glance at the top of the steps.

"Wave," Sheldon called, and waved himself.

Tenchi waved back, the gesture smaller than last week's, carrying the weight of accumulated goodbyes. Then he disappeared down the mountain, swallowed by the yellow bus and the ordinary machinery of weekday life.

"The house is waiting," Katsuhito said from behind him.

"I know."

"Yamada will be there already. He likes to start early after rain delays."

Sheldon nodded and went to gather his things.


The Tsukino house looked different in the morning sun.

The roof gleamed where new tiles had been laid—darker than the weathered originals, but matching in style and shape. The work Yamada had done over the past week was visible now, a patchwork of old and new that somehow cohered into a unified whole.

*Like me*, Sheldon thought. *Old and new. Different pieces, somehow fitting together.*

Yamada was on the scaffold already, laying the final section of tile on the east side. The roofer didn't pause as Sheldon approached, his attention focused on the work with the intensity of someone who knew they were close to completion.

"Last section," he said. "Another hour, maybe two."

"Anything you need from me?"

"Tea at ten. Otherwise, stay out of the way."

The familiar instruction, carrying no sting now. Sheldon had learned what Yamada meant by it—not dismissal, but respect for process. The work was the work. It didn't need company.


He went to the pond instead.

The surface was clear now, reflecting the sky in a mirror that rippled with each breath of wind. The work he'd done last week had held—no new debris, no returned murk. Just clean water over river-rock stones, waiting to be fully restored.

The edges still needed attention. Wild growth had claimed the banks, sending roots into the water, tangling with the careful stonework that had once defined the pond's shape. Sheldon found his tools and began the slow process of reclamation.

*Itsuki's place*, he thought, remembering what Katsuhito had told him. *The place she loved.*

He worked with that awareness in mind—not just clearing neglect, but honoring memory. Every cut of wild grass, every stone returned to its proper position, every inch of bank revealed beneath overgrowth felt like a small act of restoration. Not just of the pond, but of something larger.


Ten o'clock came and went.

Sheldon brought tea to Yamada, watched the roofer work for a few minutes, then returned to the pond. The pattern repeated at noon, when he brought lunch—onigiri from the morning's rice, pickled vegetables, a thermos of soup.

Yamada ate without stopping work, the tiles clicking into place with a rhythm that spoke of approaching completion.

"Almost done," the roofer said between bites.

"I can see that."

"Tomorrow I'll come back for the flashing. One more day after that for sealing." Yamada wiped his hands on his work cloth. "Then it's yours."

*Yours.* The word landed with unexpected weight. The house would be his—truly his, ready to live in, waiting for him to make it home.

"How much do I owe you?"

Yamada pulled a folded paper from his pocket—the estimate, marked up with adjustments. "Two hundred sixty thousand. The east side was easier than I expected."

Less than estimated. Sheldon felt something loosen in his chest—not quite relief, but the particular satisfaction of plans working out better than feared.

"I can pay you now. Full amount."

"Half now. Half when the sealing's done." The roofer's eyes held the particular honor of a man who kept his word and expected others to do the same. "That's how I work."

"Then half now."

Sheldon counted out the bills—cash from the envelope that had been waiting when he woke, the money that still felt borrowed from someone else's life. But it was his now, his to spend, his to use in building whatever came next.

Yamada accepted the payment without counting it. "The house will be good now. Another forty years, at least, before it needs major work."

"Forty years."

"Maybe fifty, if you take care of it." The roofer tucked the money away. "You seem like someone who takes care of things."


The afternoon stretched ahead, full of possibility.

Sheldon returned to the pond, working through the quiet hours while Yamada finished the last of the tiles. By mid-afternoon, the roof was done—complete, whole, ready to face whatever weather came.

Yamada descended from the scaffold for the final time, surveying his work with the critical eye of a craftsman.

"Good," he said finally. "It'll do."

High praise, from Yamada. Sheldon had learned to recognize it.

"Thank you. For everything."

"Thank me when the sealing's done." But the roofer extended his hand anyway, and Sheldon shook it. "You've been a good client, Tsukino-san. I meant what I said before. I'll tell people that."

"I appreciate it."

"Don't appreciate it. Earn it. Take care of the house. Take care of the people who matter to you." Yamada began packing his tools. "That's how you pay back trust."

*Take care of the people who matter.* The words echoed something Nobuyuki had said, something Katsuhito had implied. The same message, arriving from different directions.

Sheldon watched Yamada drive away, watched the truck disappear down the mountain road, watched the dust settle in its wake. Then he turned and looked at the house.

His house. Fifteen days since he'd arrived, and now he had a home.

*You are becoming what you need to be.*

For the first time, he thought he might understand what that meant.


- "Honoring memory" of Itsuki
- Surface clear; banks being reclaimed

**Yamada's Farewell:**

> "Take care of the house. Take care of the people who matter to you. That's how you pay back trust."

**15-Day Milestone:**

- Roof completion = home nearly ready
- "Fifteen days since he'd arrived, and now he had a home"
- Dream-voice understanding: "For the first time, he thought he might understand what that meant."

#### Scene 15.B: Fifteenth Evening

The walk back to the shrine felt different this evening.

Sheldon moved along the familiar path—three hundred meters of packed earth winding upward through the cedars—and found himself cataloguing changes. Two weeks ago, he'd stumbled up this same incline in the dark, disoriented and newly arrived in a body he didn't understand. Now his feet knew every root, every slight dip where rain pooled during storms, every spot where the late afternoon light broke through the canopy in golden shafts.

*Two weeks,* he thought. *Fifteen days. And I have a house now.*

Not quite his yet—the flashing work tomorrow, the sealing the day after—but close. Close enough that he could see it happening. Close enough that "temporary guest" was transforming into something more permanent.

The shrine emerged from the trees as it always did: first the stone torii gate, weathered red paint showing through grey, then the grounds opening before him like a clearing in a forest. The late sun caught the main building's roof, the old tiles glowing amber in the fading light.

And there, on the engawa of the residence, a small figure bounced to his feet.

"Sheldon-san!"

Tenchi launched himself across the courtyard before Sheldon had finished passing through the gate. The boy's momentum carried him into a collision-hug that hit Sheldon mid-thigh, small arms wrapping tight.

"You're back! Grandfather said the roof was finished today. Is it finished? Is it really? Can I see it? When can I see it?"

The questions tumbled out in a breathless rush, and Sheldon found himself smiling down at the crown of dark hair pressed against his hip.

"One at a time, small one." He rested a hand on Tenchi's head, feeling the silky warmth of the boy's hair. "Yes, it's finished. The tiles are all in place. Yamada-san and his team did excellent work."

"Can we go see? Right now?"

"Tomorrow," Sheldon said. "Yamada-san is coming back to do the flashing around the edges. You can meet him properly."

Tenchi's face scrunched in temporary disappointment, then smoothed as he found a new angle of attack. "What about dinner? Grandfather said you'd be tired from watching all day. Are you tired? I'm not tired. I had apple juice at school and sensei said I was very energetic."

"Did she now."

"She said it three times. That means it's important, right?"

Sheldon bit back a laugh. "I'm sure it does."


The kitchen warmth enveloped him as he stepped inside, the familiar scent of rice steam and something savory filling the space. Katsuhito stood at the counter, knife moving in patient strokes through what looked like daikon radish.

"Ah," the old priest said without turning. "The inspector returns. How does our new roof look?"

"Solid." Sheldon moved to wash his hands at the sink, shoulder-to-shoulder with Katsuhito at the counter. "Yamada-san knows his work. The tiles are seated properly, the batten strips are secured, the underlayment looked sound when I checked this morning."

"You checked the underlayment."

"I walked the ridgeline before the team arrived. The view is... educational."

Katsuhito's knife paused. "You walked the ridgeline of a roof."

"Someone should inspect the work properly." Sheldon dried his hands, reaching for an apron from the hook. "What are we making?"

"Oden." Katsuhito resumed his steady slicing. "Spring oden. Lighter than winter version. The daikon, some eggs, fishcake. Perhaps some tofu."

"I'll handle the broth."

They worked in comfortable silence for several minutes, Sheldon measuring dashi stock and adjusting seasonings while Katsuhito prepared the vegetables. Through the window, the courtyard was losing its golden light, the shadows of the cedars stretching long across the gravel.

"The boy has been counting hours since school ended," Katsuhito said quietly. "He asked me four times when you would return."

"Four times?"

"That I heard. Possibly more."

Sheldon stirred the broth, watching bubbles form at the edges of the pot. "He seems... settled. More than when I first arrived."

"He is." Katsuhito set down his knife, turning to face Sheldon directly. "You have given him something to look forward to each day. Someone who will be there. For a child who has lost that certainty once already, this is not a small thing."

The words landed somewhere in Sheldon's chest, warm and heavy. He thought of Tenchi's face at the breakfast table each morning—the way the boy's eyes lit up when Sheldon emerged from the guest room. The trust in that reaction. The expectation.

"I'm not going anywhere," Sheldon said.

"No." Katsuhito's eyes held that steady assessment Sheldon had come to recognize. "I don't believe you are. The house will be ready soon. Two days, Yamada said?"

"Two days for the work. I could move in by Thursday, if the weather holds."

"Thursday." The old priest nodded slowly. "Four more mornings here. Then three hundred meters of distance."

"Three hundred meters is nothing. Tenchi can—"

"Tenchi knows." Katsuhito's voice was gentle. "He has been preparing. Asking questions about how often he can visit, whether you'll still eat breakfast here, whether he can help with the garden."

Sheldon's throat tightened. "The garden?"

"The one you've been planning. He told me about it yesterday. Vegetables, he said. And herbs for cooking. He wants to help you plant them."

The image rose unbidden: Tenchi's small hands pressing seeds into fresh-turned earth, the boy's face bright with concentration as he helped bring something new to life. A garden. Their garden.

"I hadn't—" Sheldon paused, recalibrating. "I mentioned it once. Just thinking out loud. I didn't realize he was listening so closely."

"He listens to everything you say." Katsuhito turned back to his vegetables. "A child who has lost a parent watches the adults around him with great attention. He is learning how to be. Watching how you move through the world."

*How I move through the world.* 

Sheldon stirred the broth again, though it didn't need stirring. The weight of that responsibility settled on his shoulders—not unpleasantly, but present. Real.

"I'll try to be worthy of it," he said quietly.

"You already are." Katsuhito's knife resumed its rhythm. "That is why he chose you."


Dinner gathered them around the low table in the main room, the oden pot steaming at the center. Tenchi had claimed his usual spot at Sheldon's left, close enough that their elbows occasionally bumped when the boy reached for more fishcake.

"Grandfather said you walked on the roof," Tenchi announced between bites. "On the very top part."

"The ridgeline," Sheldon confirmed. "To check the tiles."

"Wasn't it scary? Being up so high?"

Sheldon considered the question. The answer that came surprised him—not because it was untrue, but because he hadn't consciously registered it until now.

"No," he said. "It wasn't scary. It felt... right. Like I belonged up there."

Tenchi's eyes went wide. "Like a ninja?"

"Like someone who needed to see if the work was done properly." Sheldon selected a piece of daikon from the pot. "Though I suppose ninjas might also inspect roofs. For different reasons."

"Secret reasons," Tenchi agreed solemnly. "Do you think Yamada-san is a ninja?"

"I think Yamada-san is a very skilled roofer who appreciates good craftsmanship."

"That's what a ninja would *want* you to think."

Katsuhito made a small sound that might have been a laugh suppressed. Sheldon glanced at the old priest, catching the gleam of amusement in those calm eyes.

"The boy makes a fair point," Katsuhito said mildly. "Hidden in plain sight. The classic approach."

"You're encouraging him."

"I am observing the conversation with scholarly interest."

Tenchi beamed, apparently delighted to have both adults engaged in his theory. "When I grow up, I want to be a ninja. Or maybe someone who fixes roofs. Or both. A ninja roofer."

"A worthy ambition," Katsuhito said. "The combination of stealth and practical trade skills would be formidable."

Sheldon shook his head, but he was smiling. This was what fifteen days had built: easy banter around a dinner table, a four-year-old's elaborate theories, an old priest's dry humor. Family, or something approaching it.


The evening routine unfolded in its now-familiar pattern. Tenchi's bath, supervised by Katsuhito while Sheldon cleaned the kitchen. Then the brief overlap in the main room where the boy's energy finally began to flag, his movements growing slower, his sentences stretching with yawns.

"Story?" Tenchi asked, already half-asleep against Sheldon's side on the cushions.

"One story."

Sheldon had started this three days ago—telling Tenchi stories from his own memory, adapted for a four-year-old audience. Not fairy tales exactly, but not quite history either. Stories about people who built things, who created solutions to problems, who persevered.

Tonight he chose one about a man who built a bridge across a river that everyone said couldn't be bridged. The details were vague—Sheldon couldn't remember where he'd first heard it, or whether it was even true—but the shape of the story was clear. Problem, attempt, failure, learning, attempt, success.

Tenchi was asleep before the bridge was half-finished, his breathing soft and even against Sheldon's arm.

Katsuhito appeared in the doorway. He took in the scene without comment, then moved silently to lift the sleeping boy from Sheldon's side. Tenchi murmured something unintelligible and nestled against his grandfather's shoulder.

"Goodnight," Katsuhito said softly.

"Goodnight."


The night had settled fully by the time Sheldon stepped onto the engawa. The stars were out in force, the Milky Way a pale river across the darkness. The air was cool but not cold, carrying the scent of cedar and the deeper green smell of spring growth.

Three hundred meters away, invisible in the darkness but present in his awareness, his house waited.

*My house.*

The thought still felt strange. Fifteen days ago, he'd owned nothing—not even a clear sense of who he was or how he'd arrived. Now he had a home. A community that knew his face. A child who counted the hours until he returned.

*What am I becoming?*

The question rose as it sometimes did, usually late at night when the day's activity had quieted. He wasn't the person he remembered being. That man had been older, more worn, his body a collection of accumulated damages and accommodations. This body—young, strong, impossibly restored—belonged to someone else. Someone he was still learning to be.

But perhaps that was the point. Perhaps becoming wasn't about understanding what you were, but about choosing what you would be.

He thought of the roof he'd walked that morning, steady and sure-footed on the ridgeline. He thought of Tenchi's face lighting up when he entered the courtyard. He thought of Katsuhito's words: *You are worthy of it.*

Not yet, perhaps. But working on it. Every day, a little more.

The stars wheeled overhead in their ancient patterns. Somewhere in the darkness, an owl called its hunting cry. Sheldon stood on the engawa of Masaki Shrine and let the night hold him.

Two more days of work. Then a home of his own.

The thought no longer frightened him. It felt, instead, like the next step in a journey he was only beginning to understand.


**[END SCENE 1.15.B]**
