#### Scene 16.A: Sixteenth Morning

The sixteenth morning arrived with rain.

Sheldon woke to the sound of it—a steady patter against the roof tiles, gentler than a storm but persistent enough to change the texture of the day. He lay still for a moment, listening to the rhythm of water on wood and stone, feeling the slight drop in temperature that rain always brought.

*Flashing day,* he thought. *Yamada-san won't be pleased.*

But even as the thought formed, he reconsidered. Yamada Kenji had struck him as the kind of craftsman who factored weather into his calculations, who knew when rain would interfere and when it could be worked around. Flashing work—the metal strips that sealed the edges where roof met wall—was precise work, but it wasn't necessarily rain-dependent.

He rose, dressed in the worn clothes he'd designated for work observation, and made his way to the kitchen.


Katsuhito was already there, as he often was now—Sheldon had stopped trying to determine whether the old priest woke before him or simply never seemed to sleep at all. The kitchen held the quiet warmth of early morning, the small stove already lit, water heating for tea.

"Rain," Katsuhito observed, not looking up from where he was slicing pickled vegetables. "The weatherman on NHK predicted it would clear by noon."

"You watched the weather report?"

"Tenchi wanted to see Minna no Uta before school. The weather came before it." A slight pause in the knife work. "He was very concerned about your roof."

Sheldon felt the familiar warmth that came with evidence of Tenchi's care. "The flashing should be fine in light rain. It's the sealing tomorrow that needs dry conditions."

"Ah. Then the weatherman's timing is convenient."

"Suspiciously so."

Katsuhito's knife resumed its steady rhythm. "Perhaps the spirits of the mountain are looking out for you."

"Or perhaps weather patterns in April are predictable enough that skilled roofers plan around them."

"That too."

They worked together in companionable silence—Sheldon preparing rice, Katsuhito finishing the pickles. By the time Tenchi emerged from his room, still rubbing sleep from his eyes, breakfast was ready.


"Is Yamada-san really a ninja?" Tenchi asked, chopsticks pausing midway to his mouth.

Sheldon exchanged a glance with Katsuhito over the boy's head. The ninja roofer theory, apparently, had survived overnight.

"I don't think ninja typically announce their arrival with trucks full of roofing materials," Sheldon said carefully.

"That's exactly what a ninja *would* do. To throw off suspicion."

"The boy has a point," Katsuhito murmured. "Misdirection is fundamental to the ninja arts."

"You're not helping."

"I am maintaining scholarly neutrality."

Tenchi beamed, clearly delighted by the ongoing engagement with his theory. "When you see him today, can you watch for ninja signs? Like if he disappears in smoke or walks on water?"

"The house isn't near water."

"What about smoke?"

Sheldon considered the metal flashing work, the small torch Yamada might use for certain fittings. "There may be some smoke involved in the process, actually."

Tenchi's eyes went wide with vindication. "I *knew* it."


The walk to the Tsukino house was different in the rain.

Sheldon had borrowed an umbrella from the shrine's collection—a traditional paper-and-bamboo affair that felt pleasantly archaic in his grip. The path was familiar now, every root and hollow known, but the rain transformed it. Water gathered in the low spots he'd learned to step over. The cedars seemed darker, their bark almost black with moisture. The air smelled of green growth and wet earth.

Three hundred meters. In two days, this would be his daily commute in reverse—from his house to the shrine, rather than shrine to house. The thought still carried a slight unreality, as if he were planning someone else's life.

*But it's not someone else's life anymore,* he reminded himself. *It's yours. You're making it yours.*

The Tsukino house emerged from the grey curtain of rain like a ship materializing from fog. And there, already present despite the early hour, was Yamada Kenji's battered white truck.


The roofer was on the covered engawa when Sheldon arrived, sipping tea from a thermos and studying the house with the patient attention of a doctor examining a patient.

"Tsukino-san." Yamada rose, offering a slight bow. "You're early."

"I wanted to see the process."

"Ah." Something shifted in Yamada's expression—approval, perhaps, or simply recognition. "The rain will stop before noon. NHK said so."

"So I heard."

"My wife listens every morning. She called me at five to confirm."

Sheldon found himself smiling. "Tenchi—the boy at the shrine—watches the same channel. He was also concerned about the weather."

"Children understand what matters." Yamada turned back to study the roofline. "The flashing work won't suffer from a little moisture. We'll start with the preparation—measuring, cutting. By the time we're ready to mount, the rain should be finished."

"Can I help?"

The question surprised them both, Sheldon thought. He hadn't planned to ask it. But something in him wanted to be more than an observer today. Wanted to learn the process, to understand how the work was done.

Yamada studied him for a long moment. "You climbed the ridgeline."

"To inspect."

"My apprentice told me. Said you moved like you'd done it before."

Sheldon had no response to that. The truth was, he didn't know why climbing had felt so natural, why his body had known exactly how to balance on the narrow ridge while his mind insisted he'd never done anything like it.

"I have an extra set of tin snips," Yamada said finally. "If you're willing to learn."


The morning passed in a rhythm Sheldon found unexpectedly satisfying.

Yamada was a patient teacher, explaining each step of the process without condescension. The flashing material—thin galvanized steel—had to be measured precisely, cut cleanly, shaped to fit the specific angles where roof met wall. Every house was different, Yamada explained. Every joint had its own personality.

"You read the building," the roofer said, demonstrating how to mark a measurement. "You don't impose your will on it. You find what it needs and provide that."

Sheldon's hands remembered something—not this specific skill, but the general shape of precision work. The careful measurement, the clean cut, the satisfaction of a piece that fit exactly as intended. His past life had involved different tools, different materials, but the fundamental approach was the same.

*Listen to what the problem is telling you,* he thought. *Don't assume. Observe.*

By mid-morning, the rain had eased to a drizzle. By eleven, it had stopped entirely, leaving the world washed clean and dripping. Steam began to rise from the cedar bark as weak sunlight broke through the clouds.

"Good timing," Yamada observed. "We can begin mounting the valley pieces."

"Valley pieces?"

"Where two roof sections meet." Yamada pointed to the corner where the main house connected to what had once been a small storage addition. "Water wants to gather there. The flashing needs to guide it safely down. Like building a river channel."

They climbed together—Yamada up a ladder, Sheldon following after the roofer confirmed the rungs were sound. The roof was still damp but not dangerously slick, and Sheldon found his footing with the same strange certainty he'd felt on the ridgeline.

"You've done this before," Yamada said, watching him.

"I don't think I have."

"Your body disagrees."

There was no accusation in the observation, just curiosity. Yamada, Sheldon was learning, approached people the same way he approached buildings—observing without imposing, noting what was there without demanding explanation.

"I don't understand it either," Sheldon admitted. "A lot of things about me don't make sense right now."

Yamada was quiet for a moment, fitting a piece of flashing into position. "My grandmother used to say that some skills come from before. From other lives, or from the ancestors, or from the spirits. She said a person's hands remember what their mind has forgotten."

"Do you believe that?"

"I believe—" Yamada paused, considering. "I believe that a good craftsman doesn't question useful skills. He accepts them, trains them, uses them well."

The flashing slid into place. Yamada secured it with practiced efficiency.

"You have steady hands," he said. "Good balance. A willingness to learn. These are not small things."


By noon, the valley flashing was complete and the edge work was well underway. Yamada declared a lunch break, and they sat together on the engawa, sharing rice balls Sheldon had brought from the shrine.

"Tomorrow we seal everything," Yamada said between bites. "If the weather holds, you could move in by Thursday evening."

"That's the plan."

"It's a good house." Yamada looked at the structure with something approaching affection. "Old, but well-made. The bones are sound. Whoever built it knew what they were doing."

"Do you know who that was?"

"Before my time. But I worked on this roof once before, maybe fifteen years ago. Small repair after a storm. The woman who lived here then—your mother, yes?—she made tea for the crew afterward. Very kind woman."

Sheldon felt the familiar ache that came with mentions of the family he was inhabiting. Sachiko Tsukino. The woman in the photograph. The mother he'd never met but whose name he now carried.

"I wish I'd known her better," he said, which was true in a way that went deeper than Yamada could know.

"Family is complicated." Yamada finished his rice ball, brushed his hands clean. "Sometimes we know people better after they're gone than we did when they were here. We find their marks on the world and understand them through those marks."

He gestured at the house—the solid construction, the careful details, the garden that had been allowed to grow wild but still showed the bones of intentional design.

"This place tells you who they were. What they valued. How they wanted to live." Yamada rose, stretching. "That's a kind of knowing too."


The afternoon work continued until the light began to fail. Yamada packed his tools with the same meticulous care he applied to everything else, loading the truck as the shadows lengthened.

"Tomorrow, eight o'clock," he said. "Sealing goes faster than flashing. We should be done by mid-afternoon."

"I'll be here."

Yamada paused at the truck door. "You're not what I expected, Tsukino-san."

"What did you expect?"

"Someone from the city. Someone who would hire the work done and wait inside while it happened." Yamada shook his head slowly. "You climbed the ridgeline. You learned to cut flashing. You asked questions about the craft, not just the timeline."

"I want to understand my house."

"Yes." Yamada nodded, something like satisfaction in his weathered face. "That's exactly it. You want to understand your house. Not just own it—understand it."

He climbed into the truck, engine coughing to life.

"Tomorrow," he called through the window. "Bring gloves. The sealant is unpleasant on bare hands."

The truck rattled down the narrow road, leaving Sheldon alone with his house and the fading light.

*Your house,* he thought. *Your home.*

The words were starting to feel true.


**[END SCENE 1.16.A]**

#### Scene 16.B: Sixteenth Evening

The walk back to the shrine felt earned tonight.

Sheldon's hands ached pleasantly from the work—the tin snips had required a grip strength he'd forgotten he possessed, and the repeated cuts had left faint red marks across his palms. Not blisters, not yet, but the promise of calluses to come. Evidence of labor.

*Yamada's grandmother was right,* he thought as he climbed the familiar path. *The hands remember.*

The evening air was clean after the rain, carrying the sharp green scent of wet cedar and the deeper earth-smell of the forest floor. Somewhere above, a bird sang its evening song—three notes descending, then silence, then the pattern repeated. Sheldon found himself counting the repetitions, matching his footsteps to the rhythm.

The shrine emerged from the trees, and with it came the sound he'd been anticipating: small feet on gravel, moving fast.

"Sheldon-san! Did you see any ninja signs?"

Tenchi materialized from the direction of the main hall, still in his kindergarten clothes but with the slightly disheveled look of a child who'd been playing hard since school ended. His eyes were bright with expectation.

"I saw smoke," Sheldon said gravely.

Tenchi's whole body vibrated with vindication. "I *told* you! I told Grandfather! What kind of smoke? Was it disappearing smoke?"

"It was... metalworking smoke. From the torch Yamada-san uses for certain fittings."

"That's probably how ninja make their special smoke bombs. They learned it from metalworking."

The logic was impeccable, in its way. Sheldon found himself nodding along despite his better judgment.

"Also," he added, "Yamada-san knew things about the house that he shouldn't have been able to see. Small details. Hidden things."

This was technically true—the roofer had an uncanny eye for structural issues that weren't visible from the surface. But Tenchi's interpretation was predictably different.

"Secret ninja knowledge," the boy breathed. "Did he teach you any?"

"He taught me to cut flashing."

"Is flashing a ninja technique?"

"It's a roofing technique. But—" Sheldon paused, considering how to phrase this. "He said something interesting. About how some skills come from before. From... other lives, or ancestors."

Tenchi's eyes went very wide. "Yamada-san knows about past lives?"

"His grandmother did. He was sharing her wisdom."

"That's definitely ninja wisdom." Tenchi nodded with absolute certainty. "Ninjas have to remember their past life training, or they'd have to start over every time."

Sheldon had no response to this that wouldn't encourage further elaboration. He settled for ruffling the boy's hair and steering them both toward the residence.

"Let's see what your grandfather is making for dinner."


Katsuhito had prepared nimono—simmered vegetables in a light broth, the kind of simple evening meal that felt restorative after a long day. They ate together at the low table, Tenchi providing a running commentary on his day at kindergarten between bites.

"—and then Yuki-chan said her father saw a tanuki on the mountain road, but I said tanuki don't come out in the daytime, and sensei said actually sometimes they do if they're hungry, but Yuki-chan's tanuki was probably a regular dog because tanuki have rounder faces—"

The words washed over Sheldon like warm water, requiring no response beyond occasional nods and sounds of interest. This was another thing he'd learned in sixteen days: Tenchi's monologues were not requests for engagement so much as shared processing. The boy thought out loud, working through his day by narrating it.

"—and then at lunch Kenji-kun traded me his pickled plum for my egg because he doesn't like eggs but I like everything so it was a good trade—"

Across the table, Katsuhito caught Sheldon's eye and offered the smallest of smiles. The old priest had clearly heard variations on these narratives countless times, but his attention never flagged. He listened to his grandson with the same patient focus he brought to everything else.

*This is family,* Sheldon thought. *This is what it looks like from the inside.*

The thought carried less strangeness than it once had. Sixteen days of shared meals, shared routines, shared silences and conversations. The bonds were real now, woven from small moments accumulated over time.

"The sealing work is tomorrow?" Katsuhito asked during a pause in Tenchi's narrative.

"Yes. Yamada-san expects to finish by mid-afternoon."

"And then?"

"Then the house is ready. I could move in Thursday, if the inspection goes well."

Tenchi's chopsticks paused. "Thursday is soon."

"It is."

"But you'll still come for breakfast? You said you would."

"Every morning," Sheldon confirmed. "And dinner most nights, if your grandfather doesn't get tired of feeding me."

"The kitchen has never been more interesting," Katsuhito said mildly. "It would be a shame to return to my own limited repertoire."

"You cook fine."

"I cook adequately. You cook as though the food is speaking to you and you are simply translating."

Sheldon had no response to that either. The observation was more accurate than comfortable.


The evening routine unfolded in its familiar pattern—Tenchi's bath, the brief period of post-bath energy followed by the gradual wind-down, the request for a story that Sheldon suspected was becoming as much ritual as entertainment.

Tonight he chose a story about a craftsman who built boats. A man who learned to read the grain of wood, to understand what each piece wanted to become. The parallels to Yamada's philosophy weren't subtle, but Tenchi seemed to appreciate them anyway.

"The boat builder listened to the wood," Tenchi repeated drowsily, curled against Sheldon's side. "Like Yamada-san listens to roofs."

"Something like that."

"And you're learning to listen too."

"I'm trying."

Tenchi's breathing slowed, deepened. Within minutes, he was asleep—the sudden transition of childhood exhaustion finally claiming its due.

Katsuhito appeared silently to carry the boy to bed. The ritual was smooth now, practiced, requiring no words.


Later, on the engawa, Sheldon sat with tea cooling in his hands and watched the stars emerge.

Tomorrow, the sealing. Thursday, the move. A new chapter beginning even as this first one approached its close.

*Sixteen days,* he thought. *And everything is different.*

Not just the external circumstances—the house, the routines, the relationships. Something inside had shifted too. He'd arrived in this place lost, confused, uncertain of everything including himself. Now... now he had purpose. Direction. People who expected him to be there, who would notice if he wasn't.

*You're learning to listen,* Tenchi had said.

Maybe that was the lesson of these sixteen days. Learning to listen—to the house, to the people around him, to the strange new body he inhabited and its unexpected capabilities. Accepting what came without demanding explanations. Finding his place in a story he didn't fully understand.

The stars wheeled overhead in their ancient patterns. The cedar trees whispered in the night breeze. And Sheldon Tsukino sat on the engawa of Masaki Shrine and let himself feel, for the first time in this new life, something very close to peace.

Tomorrow would bring new work, new learning, new steps forward.

For now, this moment was enough.


**[END SCENE 1.16.B]**

---

#### Scene 17.A: Hikaru Day

The seventeenth morning brought clear skies and the promise of completion.

Sheldon stood on the engawa of the shrine, watching the first light paint the mountain gold, and felt the peculiar weight of endings. Today, Yamada would finish the sealing. Tomorrow—or perhaps even tonight—he could move into his house. Seventeen days from waking in a field with cherry blossoms in his hair to standing on the threshold of his own home.

*Fast,* he thought. *And slow. Both at once.*

The paradox of time in this new life continued to surprise him. Each day felt full, unhurried, packed with small moments that accumulated into something larger. Yet somehow nearly three weeks had passed in what felt like a handful of breaths.

"You're thinking heavy thoughts for such an early hour."

Katsuhito's voice came from behind him, gentle and unsurprised. The old priest moved to stand beside Sheldon, his own cup of tea steaming in the cool morning air.

"Last day of preparation," Sheldon said. "Tomorrow I wake up in a different place."

"Three hundred meters away."

"It feels further."

Katsuhito nodded slowly. "Transitions often do. The first night in a new home is its own kind of journey."


Breakfast passed in the familiar rhythm—Tenchi's energy, the quiet comfort of shared food, the small rituals that had become Sheldon's anchor in this unfamiliar life. But today there was an undercurrent of anticipation that even the four-year-old seemed to feel.

"When can I see the finished roof?" Tenchi asked, picking at his rice. "You said today. You said when the sealing was done."

"After school," Sheldon confirmed. "Yamada-san should be finished by early afternoon. We can walk down together."

"And then you'll live there?"

"Soon. Maybe tomorrow."

Tenchi's face performed a complex series of expressions—excitement warring with something that looked almost like worry. "But you'll still come for breakfast?"

"Every morning. We talked about this."

"I know." The boy poked at his food again. "It's just... different. It'll be different."

Sheldon reached over, resting a hand on Tenchi's shoulder. "Different isn't bad. It means things are growing. Changing. That's what living things do."

"Like plants?"

"Exactly like plants. They start as seeds, then sprouts, then bigger and bigger. Each stage is different from the one before. But they're still the same plant."

Tenchi considered this with the gravity of a philosopher examining first principles. "So you moving is like... growing a new leaf?"

"Something like that."

"Okay." The boy's expression settled into acceptance. "But I still get to help with the garden."

"The garden won't work without you."


The walk to the Tsukino house carried a different quality today—a sense of culmination that changed the familiar path into something ceremonial. Sheldon wore the work gloves Yamada had recommended, tucked into his belt, and carried a small bundle of onigiri for the midday break.

Yamada's truck was already there when he arrived, the roofer's silhouette visible on the roof edge, applying sealant with methodical precision.

"Tsukino-san!" Yamada called down. "Good timing. I'm starting the ridge cap."

"Can I help?"

"Come up. I'll show you the technique."

The ladder was steady, the roof now as familiar as the path from the shrine. Sheldon climbed with the easy confidence his body had learned, though his mind still marveled at the absence of fear. Up here, the world opened into a panorama of forested mountains, the valley below, the distant glint of what might be the river.

"The sealant goes in the joints," Yamada explained, demonstrating with his caulking gun. "Not too much—you want it to fill the gap, not overflow. The roof needs to breathe."

"Breathe?"

"Moisture. Heat. Air moves through the structure. If you seal it too tight, you trap problems instead of preventing them." Yamada handed over the caulking gun. "Try the next section."

Sheldon's hands knew what to do before his mind caught up. The pressure, the angle, the steady movement along the joint—all of it felt natural, as if he'd done this work in some other life. The sealant flowed smoothly, filling the gap with precision.

"Good," Yamada said. "You have the touch."

"Your grandmother's theory?"

A quiet laugh. "Perhaps. Or perhaps you're simply a quick learner. The result is the same."


They worked through the morning, the sun climbing higher and the temperature warming. By noon, the ridge cap was complete and they'd moved to the edges where wall met roof. The house was beginning to look not just repaired but renewed—a structure that had been waiting for attention finally receiving it.

"Break?" Yamada suggested, descending the ladder with the ease of long practice.

They sat on the engawa, sharing Sheldon's onigiri and tea from Yamada's thermos. The comfortable silence of men who had worked together and found a rhythm.

"I worked on this house with my father," Yamada said, apropos of nothing. "Thirty years ago. I was your age, maybe younger. Learning the trade."

"You've known this house that long?"

"Known it. Watched it. A roofer learns to see buildings over time—how they age, how they settle, what they need." Yamada's eyes traced the roofline with something like affection. "This one was always well-made. Strong bones. The kind of house that wants to be lived in."

"And now it will be."

"Yes." Yamada finished his rice ball, brushed his hands clean. "My father would be pleased. He always said buildings have spirits—not ghosts, but the accumulated intention of everyone who built them, lived in them, cared for them. When a house sits empty too long, that spirit grows lonely."

Sheldon thought of Sachiko Tsukino, the woman he'd never met whose name he now carried. Her parents before her. The generations of intention accumulated in these walls.

"I'll try to be worthy of it," he said.

"You already are." Yamada rose, stretching. "Another few hours and we're done. Then the spirit can welcome you properly."


It happened in the mid-afternoon, when the sealing was nearly complete and Sheldon was cleaning up tools on the ground.

A flash of orange in his peripheral vision. Movement at the edge of the overgrown garden.

He turned, expecting a bird or perhaps a tanuki—the wildlife discussion from Tenchi's kindergarten stories still fresh in his mind. Instead, he found himself looking at a cat.

An orange tabby, to be precise. Male, from the size of him—a solid, muscular build that suggested survival experience. His coat was the color of autumn leaves, marked with the distinctive stripes of his kind and the characteristic 'M' on his forehead. His eyes were gold-green, watching Sheldon with an expression that managed to be both wary and curious.

"Hello," Sheldon said quietly, not moving.

The cat's ears swiveled toward the sound but he didn't flee. Instead, he sat down at the garden's edge, wrapping his tail around his paws with the dignity of a creature who had decided this spot belonged to him.

"Friend of yours?" Yamada's voice came from the roof, amused.

"First time seeing him. Stray, I think."

"Mountain has a few. They hunt the mice that come down from the fields." Yamada descended the ladder, moving slowly to avoid startling the animal. "This one looks healthy. Someone's been feeding him, or he's a good hunter."

The cat watched Yamada's approach with the same measured attention he'd given Sheldon. Not afraid, exactly, but alert. Assessing.

"We had a piece of fish from lunch," Sheldon remembered. "In my pack."

"Ah. That might explain his interest."

Sheldon retrieved the wrapped fish—a small piece of grilled mackerel he'd set aside for later. He knelt, setting it on the ground a few feet from where the cat sat.

The orange tabby considered the offering with what Sheldon could only describe as thoughtful suspicion. Then, moving with the careful grace of his kind, he approached. One sniff. A second. Then he began to eat, unhurried, as if accepting hospitality rather than scavenging a meal.

"He likes you," Yamada observed.

"He likes mackerel."

"Same thing, to a cat."


The sealing was complete by three o'clock.

Yamada performed a final inspection, walking the perimeter and checking each joint with professional thoroughness. Sheldon followed, learning to see what the roofer saw—the places where water might try to enter, the vulnerabilities of any structure exposed to weather.

"Done," Yamada announced, satisfied. "She'll hold through any storm now."

"Thank you." The words felt inadequate. "For everything. The work, the teaching, the... philosophy."

Yamada smiled, the expression transforming his weathered face. "The house taught you. I just translated."

They shook hands—a formal gesture that felt right for the moment. Two craftsmen acknowledging completed work.

"If you have problems, call. But you won't." Yamada gathered his tools, loading them into the truck with familiar efficiency. "The bones are good. You understand the house now. She'll tell you what she needs."

The truck rumbled to life, rattling down the narrow road and disappearing into the trees.

Sheldon stood alone in front of his house—truly his now, the work complete—and felt the weight of the moment settle around him.

And at his feet, the orange tabby sat cleaning his whiskers, apparently having decided that this was an acceptable place to be.


Tenchi's arrival came in the late afternoon, the boy running down the path from the shrine with Katsuhito following at a more measured pace. His kindergarten bag bounced against his back, and his face was bright with anticipation.

"Is it done? Is the roof done? Can I see?"

"It's done," Sheldon confirmed. "Want to walk around and look?"

Tenchi was already circling the house before the question finished, his small feet sure on the uneven ground. He examined the new flashing, the sealed edges, the tiles that gleamed in the afternoon light.

"It looks new," he said, awed. "Like a new house."

"Same house. Just taken care of properly."

"Grandfather says that's what makes things last." Tenchi completed his circuit, then stopped short as he noticed the orange cat still lounging near the garden edge. "A *cat*!"

The tabby's ears flicked at the exclamation but he didn't bolt. Instead, he regarded Tenchi with the same measured assessment he'd given the adults—curious, cautious, but not afraid.

"He showed up while we were working," Sheldon explained. "A stray, I think. From the mountain."

Tenchi approached slowly, with the instinctive care of a child who understood that some creatures required patience. He crouched a few feet away, making himself smaller, less threatening.

"Hello, cat. I'm Tenchi."

The orange tabby's tail swished once. Twice. Then, with the deliberation of a creature making an important decision, he rose and walked toward the boy.

Tenchi held his breath as the cat approached, coming close enough to sniff his outstretched hand. A moment of mutual assessment. Then the tabby butted his head against Tenchi's palm, a clear invitation.

The boy's face lit up like sunrise.

"He *likes* me!"

"Apparently so."

Tenchi stroked the cat's head with careful gentleness, his fingers tracing the 'M' marking on the tabby's forehead. "What's his name?"

"He's a stray. I don't think he has one."

"Then we should give him one." Tenchi's face went serious with the gravity of the responsibility. "He's orange. Like... like fire. Or the sun."

"Good observations."

"What about... Hikaru?" The boy looked up at Sheldon, seeking approval. "It means 'light.' Because he's bright like sunshine."

Sheldon looked at the orange cat, who had settled into being petted with the boneless contentment of his kind. Hikaru. Light. A name for a creature who had appeared on the day of completion, as if summoned by the turning of a page.

"I think that's perfect," he said.

Tenchi beamed. "Hikaru," he said to the cat, testing the name. "Your name is Hikaru now."

The cat purred.


Katsuhito had observed the naming ceremony from a respectful distance. Now he approached, studying the orange tabby with the same thoughtful attention he brought to everything.

"A good omen," he said quietly. "Cats choose their homes. If this one has chosen yours, it speaks well of the house's spirit."

"Yamada said something similar. About houses having spirits."

"Yamada is wiser than he appears. Most craftsmen are." Katsuhito knelt, extending a hand for the cat to investigate. Hikaru sniffed politely, then returned his attention to Tenchi's ministrations. "Will you keep him?"

Sheldon looked at the scene before him—his house, roof whole and strong; a four-year-old boy, face bright with joy; an orange cat who had wandered out of the mountain and decided to stay.

"I think he's decided to keep me," he said.

"That is usually how it works with cats."


The sun was beginning its descent when they finally headed back to the shrine, Tenchi chattering excitedly about Hikaru, about the roof, about how maybe the cat was also a ninja because he was so quiet and appeared without warning.

Hikaru followed them partway up the path, then stopped at an invisible boundary and sat down to watch them go. He would still be there tomorrow, Sheldon somehow knew. The cat had made his choice.

At the shrine gate, Tenchi looked back and waved. "Bye, Hikaru! See you tomorrow!"

The orange cat's tail swished once in acknowledgment.

"Tomorrow," Sheldon said quietly, more to himself than anyone else. "I move in tomorrow."

Katsuhito nodded. "And so a new chapter begins."


**[END SCENE 1.17.A]**

- Autumn leaf coloring, gold-green eyes
- Characteristic 'M' forehead marking
- Mountain stray, good hunter or been fed
- Chose Sheldon/house deliberately
- Accepted by Tenchi immediately

---

#### Scene 17.B: Seventeenth Evening

The boy had requested it specifically, citing the importance of the occasion. "It's your last night here," he'd explained with four-year-old gravity. "We should have something special."

Sheldon hadn't pointed out that he'd be back for breakfast in less than twelve hours. The sentiment mattered more than the logistics.

So he made curry—the real kind, with roux built from scratch the way his hands now knew how to do, the spices balanced by instinct rather than measurement. Tenchi helped wash vegetables, his small fingers careful with the carrots and potatoes, his commentary a constant stream of updates on Hikaru.

"Do you think he'll be there tomorrow? What if he leaves? What if he forgets us?"

"Cats have good memories," Sheldon said. "And he chose that spot. He'll be there."

"How do you know?"

"Because that's what cats do. They find a place that feels right and they stay."

Tenchi considered this while scrubbing a potato. "Like you did. You found the shrine and you stayed."

The observation landed with unexpected weight. Sheldon paused in his stirring, looking down at the boy beside him.

"Yes," he said quietly. "I suppose I did."


The curry was good—rich and warming, the kind of meal that settled into bones and made the world feel solid. They ate together at the low table, Katsuhito offering quiet appreciation while Tenchi worked through his second bowl with determined enthusiasm.

"Hikaru will like curry," the boy announced between bites. "Cats like meat. There's meat in curry."

"Cats shouldn't eat curry," Sheldon said. "The spices aren't good for them. But we can give him plain chicken sometimes."

"Plain chicken." Tenchi nodded seriously, filing this information away. "And fish. He liked the fish today."

"He did."

"I'm going to bring him fish from my lunch. Grandfather packs fish sometimes."

Katsuhito's eyebrows rose slightly. "Does he now."

"For Hikaru. He's my cat too, right, Sheldon-san? Even though he lives at your house?"

Sheldon met Katsuhito's eyes across the table. The old priest's expression held something that might have been amusement, or perhaps something deeper.

"He's our cat," Sheldon said. "The whole family's."

Tenchi beamed. "I've never had a cat before. Grandfather said cats find their own people and you can't make them stay if they don't want to. But Hikaru wants to stay. I could tell."

"How could you tell?"

"He purred." The boy's certainty was absolute. "Cats only purr when they're happy. He was happy with us."


The evening routine carried a different weight tonight—the last iteration of a pattern about to change. Tenchi's bath, the quiet hour of wind-down, the request for a story that Sheldon suspected was as much about delay as entertainment.

"Tell me about the house," Tenchi said, already curled against Sheldon's side. "About what it'll be like when you live there."

"What do you want to know?"

"Everything. What will you do in the morning? What will you eat? Where will Hikaru sleep?"

Sheldon considered the questions, painting a picture with words. "In the morning, I'll wake up and make tea. Then I'll walk up the path to the shrine for breakfast with you and your grandfather. After that, I'll work on the house—cleaning, repairing small things, getting the garden ready for planting."

"And I'll help with the garden."

"You'll help with the garden. We'll plant vegetables together. Tomatoes, maybe. Cucumbers. Whatever grows well here."

"Grandmother grew tomatoes." Tenchi's voice was soft. "Grandfather told me. She had a garden with tomatoes and herbs and flowers."

Sheldon felt the familiar ache that came with mentions of the family whose name he carried. "Then we'll grow tomatoes. In her memory."

"And Hikaru will watch us work. Cats like to watch."

"They do."

Tenchi yawned, the sound stretching into something that was almost a purr itself. "I'm glad you're staying, Sheldon-san. Even if you're staying in a different place."

"I'm glad too."

"And I'm glad Hikaru came. It's like... like the house was waiting for both of you. And now you're both here."

The words hung in the quiet room, simple and profound in the way only children's observations could be. Sheldon stroked the boy's hair, feeling the slow rhythm of Tenchi's breathing as sleep began to claim him.

"Maybe it was," he said softly. "Maybe we were all waiting for each other."


Later, after Katsuhito had carried the sleeping boy to bed, Sheldon stood on the engawa one last time as a guest.

The stars were out in force, the Milky Way a pale river across the darkness. The same stars he'd watched on his first night here, confused and lost and uncertain of everything. Now he knew their patterns, recognized the constellations that wheeled overhead, felt the rhythm of the mountain's nights in his bones.

*Seventeen days,* he thought. *Tomorrow, eighteen. And everything changes again.*

But change, he was learning, wasn't the same as loss. The shrine would still be here. The path would still connect his house to this place. The people who had welcomed him—the old priest with his quiet wisdom, the small boy with his enormous heart—they weren't going anywhere.

*Family isn't just the people who live under your roof,* he thought. *It's the people who hold space for you. Who expect you to come back.*

Katsuhito appeared silently, as he often did, settling onto the engawa beside Sheldon.

"Last night," the old priest observed.

"Last night as a guest. First of many nights as a neighbor."

"A good distinction." Katsuhito was quiet for a moment, watching the stars. "The boy will miss having you in the next room. But he understands. He's already planning his visits."

"I know. He told me about the fish."

A soft sound that might have been a laugh. "He's been planning that since we left your house. The logistics of cat-feeding are apparently quite complex."

"Apparently."

They sat in comfortable silence, two men watching the stars turn overhead. The night air was cool but not cold, carrying the scent of cedar and the first hints of spring warmth.

"Thank you," Sheldon said finally. "For everything. The room, the meals, the... acceptance. You didn't have to welcome a stranger into your home."

"You weren't a stranger." Katsuhito's voice was certain. "Not from the moment I saw you on that path. Some people belong in certain places. It's obvious, when you know how to look."

"Like cats choosing their homes?"

"Exactly like that." The old priest rose, moving toward the door. "Sleep well, Tsukino-san. Tomorrow you begin a new chapter. But the story continues."

"Goodnight, Katsuhito-san."

"Goodnight."


Sheldon stayed on the engawa a while longer, watching the stars and listening to the night sounds of the mountain. Somewhere down the slope, three hundred meters away, his house waited. And at its garden edge, an orange cat named Hikaru was probably curled in whatever shelter strays found for themselves, waiting for morning.

*Tomorrow,* he thought. *Tomorrow it all becomes real.*

He carried that thought with him into sleep—the last sleep in the guest room of Masaki Shrine, the final night of his first chapter in this new life.

Tomorrow, a new chapter would begin.


**[END SCENE 1.17.B]**

**[DAY 17 COMPLETE]**

**Threads Advanced:**
- Last night at shrine — ceremonial weight
- Hikaru planning (Tenchi wants to bring fish)
- Garden plans — tomatoes in grandmother's memory
- "Family isn't just people under your roof"

**Key Dialogue:**
- Tenchi: "The house was waiting for both of you"
- Katsuhito: "You weren't a stranger. Not from the moment I saw you."
- Katsuhito: "Tomorrow you begin a new chapter. But the story continues."

**Continuity:**
- Tomorrow = Day 18 = MOVE-IN DAY
- Tenchi already planning Hikaru visits and fish delivery
- Garden will include tomatoes (grandmother's memory)

---

#### Scene 18.A: Move In Day

The eighteenth morning began like all the others—and nothing like them at all.

Sheldon woke in the guest room of Masaki Shrine for the last time, watching the familiar play of early light on the ceiling. The same cedar beams. The same soft creak of old wood settling. The same distant birdsong filtering through the paper screens.

But today, everything was different. Today, he would wake up here and sleep somewhere else. The symmetry of that thought—arriving at dawn, departing for good—felt significant in a way he couldn't quite articulate.

*Eighteen days,* he thought. *Eighteen days from waking in a field to waking in... my life.*

He rose, folded the futon with care, and stood for a moment in the empty room. The space had been his sanctuary, his transition point, his introduction to this new existence. It had welcomed him when he had nothing—not even certainty about who he was. Now he was leaving it behind.

"Thank you," he said quietly, to the room or the house or perhaps to himself. Then he slid open the door and walked toward breakfast.


The meal was quieter than usual.

Tenchi ate with uncharacteristic focus, his eyes tracking Sheldon's movements as if memorizing them. Katsuhito maintained his usual calm, but there was something in the set of his shoulders that spoke of awareness—the recognition of a threshold being crossed.

"You'll come back for breakfast," Tenchi said, more statement than question.

"Every morning. We talked about this."

"I know." The boy pushed rice around his bowl. "It's just... I wanted to hear you say it again."

Sheldon reached across and rested a hand on Tenchi's shoulder. "Every morning. And you'll come visit after school. And we'll work on the garden together. Nothing important is changing, Tenchi. Just the place where I sleep."

"Promise?"

"Promise."

Tenchi's face relaxed slightly. He attacked his breakfast with renewed vigor, as if the verbal contract had settled something that had been worrying at him.

Katsuhito caught Sheldon's eye across the table. The old priest nodded once—acknowledgment, approval, something deeper than words could carry.


There wasn't much to move.

Sheldon had arrived in this life with nothing but the clothes on his back and the contents of his wallet. Over eighteen days, he'd accumulated a modest collection: a few changes of clothes purchased in the village, basic toiletries, a small stack of books Katsuhito had lent him, the work gloves that now bore the marks of roof work.

It all fit into a single canvas bag that Katsuhito produced from somewhere in the shrine's depths.

"It belonged to my son," the old priest said, handing it over. "He hasn't needed it in many years. I think he would want you to have it."

Sheldon took the bag carefully, feeling the weight of the offering. Another thread connecting him to the family whose periphery he now inhabited. Another piece of history woven into his present.

"Thank you. I'll take care of it."

"I know you will."


The walk down the mountain felt ceremonial.

Three hundred meters of familiar path, the same route he'd traveled dozens of times in the past eighteen days. But today each step carried weight. Today he wasn't visiting his house—he was going home.

The cedars whispered overhead in the morning breeze. Sunlight filtered through in golden shafts, dappling the packed earth of the path. Somewhere above, a bird sang its territorial claim to the day.

And there, at the edge of the overgrown garden, an orange shape waited.

Hikaru sat exactly where Sheldon had last seen him—as if he'd been stationed there overnight, keeping watch over the house until its owner returned. The cat's tail swished once in greeting, but he didn't rise. He simply observed as Sheldon approached, those gold-green eyes steady and unblinking.

"Good morning," Sheldon said. "I'm moving in today. I hope that meets with your approval."

Hikaru's tail swished again. He blinked slowly—the feline equivalent, Sheldon suspected, of a nod.

"I'll take that as a yes."


The house smelled of cedar and dust and something older—the accumulated scent of decades, of the family who had built these walls and lived within them. Sheldon set down his bag in the main room and stood for a moment, breathing it in.

This was his now. Not borrowed, not temporary, not contingent. His.

The thought was terrifying and wonderful in equal measure.

He spent the morning cleaning—sweeping floors, airing out rooms, clearing cobwebs from corners that had been neglected during the empty years. The work was satisfying in a way that transcended the physical results. Each stroke of the broom, each opened window, each layer of dust removed felt like a declaration. *This is my home. I am taking care of it.*

Hikaru followed him through the house, investigating each room with the thoroughness of a building inspector. The cat seemed to approve of the cleaning efforts, settling into various spots to observe before moving on to supervise the next area.

"You're very particular," Sheldon told him during a break, sitting on the engawa with tea he'd made on the small stove. "Is this up to your standards?"

Hikaru, stretched in a patch of sunlight, deigned to purr.

"I'll take that as a qualified yes."


The afternoon brought visitors.

Tenchi appeared on the path from the shrine at precisely the time kindergarten would have ended, running with the boundless energy of a four-year-old freed from obligation. His grandfather followed at a more measured pace, carrying a bundle wrapped in cloth.

"I came to visit!" Tenchi announced, slightly out of breath. "Just like you said! Is Hikaru here? Did you clean? Can I see your room? Where's Hikaru?"

"Slow down." Sheldon laughed, catching the boy as he careened into his legs. "Hikaru is in the garden. I've been cleaning. You can see everything."

The tour that followed was perhaps the most thorough inspection the house had ever received. Tenchi examined every room with solemn attention, offering commentary that ranged from the practical ("This room is smaller than your room at the shrine") to the philosophical ("The wood here sounds different when you walk on it—can wood have different voices?").

Hikaru, apparently sensing that his presence was requested, emerged from whatever garden hiding spot he'd claimed and submitted to Tenchi's enthusiastic greeting with the patience of a creature who had decided this small human was acceptable.

"I brought him fish," Tenchi said, producing a small wrapped packet from his pocket with the triumph of a magician revealing his best trick. "From my lunch. I saved it special."

The fish was slightly warm and smelled strongly of the schoolday it had survived, but Hikaru approached with evident interest. He sniffed, considered, then accepted the offering with the gravity of a lord receiving tribute.

"He likes it!" Tenchi's face glowed. "Grandfather, did you see? He liked my fish!"

"I saw." Katsuhito had settled onto the engawa, the wrapped bundle beside him. "You've made a friend for life. Cats never forget who feeds them."


The bundle, when unwrapped, proved to be a collection of essential items that Katsuhito had apparently decided Sheldon would need: a set of dishes, a teapot with two cups, a small knife of obvious quality, and a hand-sized wooden carving of a seated cat that looked remarkably like Hikaru.

"Housewarming gifts," Katsuhito explained. "The dishes are simple, but serviceable. The teapot belonged to my wife—she would have wanted someone to use it. The knife is for cooking. And the cat..."

"It's Hikaru!" Tenchi grabbed the carving, examining it with wonder. "Grandfather, you made Hikaru!"

"I carved it this morning, while you were at school. Every house needs a guardian spirit. This one can watch over Sheldon's home until the real one decides to come inside."

Sheldon turned the carving over in his hands. The work was exquisite—far beyond simple craft. Every detail of Hikaru's pose had been captured: the alert ears, the tucked paws, the somehow-smug expression of a cat who knew his worth.

"This is... you made this in a few hours?"

"I've had practice." Katsuhito's eyes held that gleam of hidden amusement. "Seven hundred years gives a man time to develop his hobbies."

Sheldon blinked. "Seven hundred...?"

"A joke." The old priest's smile was serene. "I'm told my sense of humor takes some getting used to."


They stayed for dinner.

Sheldon cooked—the first meal prepared in his own kitchen, in his own home. Nothing elaborate: miso soup, rice, grilled fish, pickled vegetables. Simple food, made with care, served to the people who had made this new life possible.

The small table in the main room barely fit three, but the crowding felt right. Tenchi pressed close to Sheldon's side, chattering about his day, about Hikaru, about the garden they would plant. Katsuhito ate with quiet appreciation, offering occasional observations that ranged from the mundane to the unexpectedly profound.

"The food tastes different here," Tenchi observed. "Even though it's the same food."

"The cook's state of mind affects the meal," Katsuhito said. "Your mother used to say that. Food made with intention carries that intention to the eater."

Sheldon paused with his chopsticks halfway to his mouth. "That's... surprisingly specific."

"Kiyone was a wise woman. She understood many things that I'm still learning."

The name hung in the air—Tenchi's mother, gone but not forgotten, present in the small rituals and remembered wisdom that shaped this family. Sheldon felt the weight of it, the responsibility of stepping into spaces that grief had carved.

"I'll try to cook with good intentions," he said quietly.

"You already do." Katsuhito's eyes met his. "That's why the food tastes the way it does."


The evening came too quickly.

Katsuhito collected Tenchi when the boy's energy finally flagged, lifting the drowsy child with practiced ease. Tenchi murmured protests that faded into silence as sleep claimed him.

"Thank you," Sheldon said, standing at the door. "For everything. The gifts, the help, the... everything."

"We're neighbors now." Katsuhito shifted Tenchi's weight against his shoulder. "Neighbors help each other. It's not charity—it's community."

"Still."

"Still." The old priest nodded. "Sleep well, Tsukino-san. Your first night in your own home. Try not to let the significance keep you awake."

"I'll do my best."

He watched them climb the path until the darkness swallowed them, Katsuhito's white robes the last thing visible before they disappeared among the cedars. Then he turned back to his house—his home—and closed the door.


The first night was quieter than Sheldon had expected.

The house settled around him with small sounds—creaks and sighs that might have been alarming in a different context but felt almost welcoming now. This building had stood for decades, sheltering generations. It knew how to hold someone safely through the darkness.

He spread his futon in the main room—the smaller bedroom would need more work before it was truly habitable—and lay watching the moonlight filter through the windows. The moon was nearly full tonight, bright enough to cast shadows on the tatami.

Hikaru appeared from somewhere, padding silently across the floor to investigate the sleeping arrangements. After a thorough sniffing inspection, the cat apparently decided that Sheldon's feet were an acceptable proximity. He curled into a tight orange spiral and began to purr.

"First night," Sheldon said quietly. "We made it."

The purring continued, steady and somehow reassuring. Outside, an owl called its hunting cry. The cedar trees whispered secrets to each other. And somewhere up the mountain, three hundred meters away, a small boy slept in the room next to his grandfather's, probably dreaming of cats and fish and gardens.

*Eighteen days,* Sheldon thought. *From waking in a field to sleeping in my own home. From having nothing to having... this.*

Family. Community. Purpose. A house that wanted to be lived in and a cat who had chosen to stay.

It wasn't the life he'd had before—whatever that life had been, however it had ended. It was something new. Something he was building, day by day, choice by choice, meal by meal.

The moon moved across the sky. Hikaru's purring slowed into sleep-breathing. And Sheldon Tsukino, in his own home for the first time, finally closed his eyes and let the darkness hold him.

He slept without dreams.


**[END SCENE 1.18.A]**

**[MOVE-IN DAY COMPLETE]**


## SCENE NOTES

**Tools Used (Law 25):**
- Web search: April 20, 1989 day of week (Thursday), moon phase (nearly full)

**Time at Scene End:** ~9:00 PM, April 20, 1989 — First night in Tsukino house

**CRITICAL EVENT COMPLETE:**
- **MOVE-IN DAY** — Sheldon now lives at Tsukino House
- First meal cooked in own kitchen
- First night sleeping in own home
- Hikaru has entered the house (sleeps at Sheldon's feet)

**Housewarming Gifts from Katsuhito:**
- Dishes (simple, serviceable)
- Teapot with two cups (belonged to Itsuki, his wife)
- Cooking knife (quality tool)
- Hand-carved Hikaru statue (guardian spirit)

**Katsuhito's Joke:**
- "Seven hundred years gives a man time to develop his hobbies"
- Claimed it was a joke; Sheldon uncertain

**Threads Advanced:**
- Kiyone mentioned: "Food made with intention carries intention to eater"
- First breakfast at shrine as neighbor (tomorrow)
- Garden project still planned
- Tenchi brought fish for Hikaru (successful)

**Continuity:**
- Canvas bag belonged to Katsuhito's son (Nobuyuki, unspoken)
- Teapot belonged to Itsuki (Katsuhito's wife)
- Hikaru now indoor/outdoor cat
- Day 20 (April 22) = Nobuyuki visit
- Day 22 (April 24) = Tenchi's birthday

---

#### Scene 19.A: Nineteenth Day

The nineteenth morning began with birdsong and the smell of cedar.

Sheldon opened his eyes to unfamiliar ceiling beams—darker than the ones at the shrine, older somehow, with the patina of decades. For a moment, disorientation fluttered through him. Then memory caught up: his house, his first night, his new beginning.

Hikaru was still curled at his feet, a warm orange weight that hadn't moved since settling in the night before. The cat's eyes opened as Sheldon stirred, gold-green and knowing.

"Good morning," Sheldon said quietly.

Hikaru stretched with the elaborate slowness of his kind, extending each leg in turn, yawning to display an impressive array of teeth. Then he padded across the futon and butted his head against Sheldon's hand—demanding acknowledgment, or perhaps simply saying hello in the way cats did.

"I'll take that as 'good morning' back."


The walk to the shrine felt different today.

Not the path itself—that remained unchanged, the same packed earth and towering cedars, the same play of morning light through the canopy. But Sheldon's relationship to it had shifted. Yesterday, he'd walked this path as someone leaving. Today, he walked it as someone who would return.

*Home,* he thought, testing the word. *I'm walking from home.*

The concept still felt new, fragile, like a seedling just broken through soil. But it was real now in a way it hadn't been before. He had a place. A foundation. Somewhere to come back to.

At the shrine gate, Tenchi was waiting.

"Sheldon-san! You came!"

"I promised I would."

"I know, but—" The boy caught himself, grinning sheepishly. "I wanted to see if it felt different. You coming from your house instead of from the guest room."

"Does it?"

Tenchi considered the question with gravity. "A little. You smell different."

"I... smell different?"

"Like wood. Different wood. The shrine wood smells one way, and your house wood smells another way. Now you smell like your house."

Sheldon found himself laughing—a genuine sound that surprised him with its ease. "I suppose I do. Houses do have their own scents."

"Grandfather says houses remember everyone who lives in them. The smells get into the wood and stay forever."

"Your grandfather is very wise about houses."

"He's wise about everything." Tenchi grabbed Sheldon's hand, tugging him toward the residence. "Come on, breakfast is almost ready. I helped make the rice."


Breakfast at the shrine carried a new quality—subtle but unmistakable.

The food was the same: rice, miso soup, grilled fish, pickled vegetables. The table was the same, the cushions, the morning light slanting through the screens. But Sheldon's position had changed. He was a guest now in a way he hadn't been before—welcomed, expected, but no longer a resident.

Katsuhito seemed to sense the shift. His manner was as warm as ever, but there was a formality underneath it now, a recognition of boundaries that hadn't existed before.

"How was your first night?" the old priest asked.

"Quiet. Peaceful. Hikaru kept watch."

"Cats are good at that. They see things we miss."

Tenchi looked up from his rice. "What kind of things?"

"Small things. Movement in shadows. The settling of a house." Katsuhito's eyes held that familiar glint of hidden knowledge. "Cats notice what others overlook."

"Is that why they stare at walls sometimes? Are they seeing secret things?"

"Perhaps. Or perhaps they simply like staring at walls. Cats are mysterious creatures."


After breakfast, Tenchi departed for school with the reluctance of a child who had better things to do than learn. Sheldon walked him to the point where the path split—one branch leading to the village, the other down to the Tsukino house.

"Tomorrow is Saturday," Tenchi said. "No school. I can come help with the garden."

"That would be good. We should start planning what to plant."

"Tomatoes. You promised tomatoes."

"I did. And cucumbers, and whatever else grows well here."

"And a space for Hikaru to sit and watch. Cats like watching gardens."

"A cat-watching space. I'll make sure to include that in the plans."

Tenchi grinned, satisfied with the concession, and continued down toward the village with the bounce of a child anticipating weekend freedom.


The day passed in quiet productivity.

Sheldon returned to his house and set about the work of making it truly his. The main room was livable now, but the smaller bedroom needed attention—dust and cobwebs had accumulated during the years of emptiness, and the tatami showed signs of age that might require eventual replacement.

He cleaned methodically, finding satisfaction in the physical labor. Each swept corner, each wiped surface, each opened window felt like a small victory. The house was waking up, shaking off the lethargy of abandonment, remembering what it meant to shelter someone.

Hikaru supervised from various vantage points, offering silent judgment on the quality of the work. Occasionally he would investigate a newly cleaned area, sniffing corners and checking behind furniture with the thoroughness of an inspector. His apparent approval—expressed through a lack of visible disapproval—felt oddly validating.

"You're very particular," Sheldon told him during a mid-morning break. "Were you always this demanding, or is it something about this house?"

Hikaru's tail swished once. He began grooming his paw with studied disinterest.

"I'll take that as 'I've always been this way.'"


In the afternoon, Sheldon turned his attention to the garden.

The plot behind the house had clearly been tended once—the bones of raised beds were still visible beneath the overgrowth, and here and there, hardy plants had survived the years of neglect. Wild mint grew in one corner, its sharp scent rising when Sheldon brushed past. Something that might have been oregano occupied another patch, gone leggy and sprawling but still alive.

*Sachiko's garden,* he thought. *Or her parents' before her. Generations of intention, layered in the soil.*

He began clearing the worst of the weeds, working by hand without tools. The earth was rich and dark, well-composted by years of fallen leaves. Good soil. Ready soil. Waiting for someone to remember what it could become.

The work was meditative—bend, pull, shake loose the dirt, set aside. His hands knew the rhythm even if his mind didn't remember learning it. Another of those strange competencies that seemed to live in his body without explanation.

*Don't question it,* he reminded himself. *Accept it. Use it well.*

By late afternoon, he'd cleared one full bed and started on a second. His back ached pleasantly, his hands were filthy, and something in his chest felt lighter than it had in days.


Tenchi appeared on the path just as Sheldon was finishing for the day, the boy's school bag bouncing against his back and his face bright with Friday energy.

"You started without me!"

"Just the clearing. The planting we'll do together."

Tenchi surveyed the cleared bed with the critical eye of a four-year-old expert. "It looks good. The dirt is very brown."

"That's a good sign. Brown dirt has lots of nutrients."

"What are nutrients?"

"Food for plants. Like how we eat rice and fish, plants eat things from the soil. Good brown dirt means lots of plant food."

"Oh." Tenchi nodded, filing this information away. "So we're making dinner for the tomatoes."

"In a way, yes."

The boy circled the cleared bed, pausing to examine the wild mint Sheldon had spared. "What's this? It smells... sharp."

"Mint. It was already growing here, so I left it. Someone planted it a long time ago."

"Grandmother?"

"Maybe. Or her parents. Gardens remember the people who tend them, just like houses do."

Tenchi was quiet for a moment, processing this. Then: "So when we plant tomatoes, the garden will remember us?"

"It will."

"Forever?"

"For as long as the garden is here."

The boy smiled—a soft, thoughtful expression that made him look older than his four years. "I like that. Being remembered by a garden."


Dinner that evening was simple: rice, soup, and vegetables from the shrine's stores that Katsuhito had sent down with Tenchi. They ate together in Sheldon's main room, the small space warm with shared presence.

"Father is coming tomorrow," Tenchi announced between bites.

Sheldon paused. "Your father?"

"Yes. He visits on Saturdays sometimes. Grandfather said he's coming tomorrow to meet you."

The information settled with unexpected weight. Nobuyuki—Tenchi's father, Katsuhito's son. The owner of the canvas bag that now held Sheldon's belongings. A thread in the family web that Sheldon hadn't yet encountered.

"What's he like?" Sheldon asked. "Your father?"

Tenchi considered the question carefully. "He's tall. And he works in the city, so he wears suits. He laughs a lot, but sometimes his eyes look sad when he thinks I'm not watching." The boy poked at his rice. "He misses Mother. I can tell."

"That's understandable. Losing someone you love leaves marks."

"Grandfather says time helps, but the marks never go away completely. They just become part of you."

Sheldon thought of his own marks—the loss he couldn't quite name, the life he'd had before this one that existed only as echoes and absences. "Your grandfather is right."

"He usually is." Tenchi brightened slightly. "But Father will like you. I told him about you in my letters. About how you cook and help with the shrine and found Hikaru. He said he wants to thank you."

"Thank me for what?"

"For being here." Tenchi's voice was matter-of-fact, as if this were obvious. "Grandfather needed someone. And so did I."


Later, after Tenchi had returned to the shrine and the evening settled into darkness, Sheldon sat on his engawa and watched the stars emerge.

Hikaru had claimed the spot beside him, stretched along the wooden boards with the boneless relaxation of a creature who had decided this was home. The cat's purr was a low rumble, constant and somehow comforting.

*Tomorrow I meet Nobuyuki,* Sheldon thought. *Another piece of this family. Another relationship to navigate.*

He wasn't nervous, exactly. But there was awareness—the recognition that fathers were protective creatures, that strangers appearing in their children's lives warranted scrutiny. Whatever Tenchi had written in his letters, Nobuyuki would need to form his own judgments.

*I'll just be myself,* Sheldon decided. *Whatever that means now.*

The stars wheeled overhead. The cedar trees whispered their ancient secrets. And in his own home, on his own engawa, with his own cat purring beside him, Sheldon Tsukino waited for tomorrow to arrive.


**[END SCENE 1.19.A]**

**[DAY 19 COMPLETE]**


## SCENE NOTES

**Time at Scene End:** ~9:00 PM, April 21, 1989 — Day 19 Complete

**Key Events:**
- First full day as resident of Tsukino house
- First breakfast at shrine as neighbor (not guest)
- Garden clearing begun — one bed complete
- Wild mint and possible oregano discovered (survivors from previous garden)
- Tenchi announces Nobuyuki visiting tomorrow

**Threads Advanced:**
- Garden project in progress
- "House smell" concept (Tenchi's observation)
- Katsuhito's wisdom about cats and gardens
- Setup for Nobuyuki introduction (Day 20)

**Character Notes:**
- Tenchi described Nobuyuki: tall, wears suits, laughs a lot, "eyes look sad sometimes"
- Tenchi writes letters to his father about shrine life
- Tenchi: "Grandfather needed someone. And so did I."

**Foreshadowing:**
- Cats "see things we miss" — Katsuhito's hint
- Garden remembering people — layered history
- Nobuyuki's protective father instincts anticipated

---

#### Scene 20.A: Nobuyuki Visit

The twentieth morning brought anticipation.

Sheldon woke with awareness humming beneath his skin—not anxiety, exactly, but heightened attention. Today he would meet Nobuyuki Masaki. Tenchi's father. Katsuhito's son. The man whose canvas bag now held Sheldon's belongings, whose wife's wisdom about food and intention had already become part of Sheldon's understanding of this family.

*First impressions matter,* he thought, rising from his futon. *But so does authenticity. Be yourself. Whoever that is.*

Hikaru stretched from his spot at the foot of the bedding, offering the morning's first purr. The cat had settled into the rhythm of the house with remarkable ease, as if he'd always lived here and was simply allowing Sheldon to share the space.

"Big day," Sheldon told him. "We're being evaluated."

Hikaru's expression suggested that he, personally, had already passed any evaluation that mattered.


Breakfast at the shrine carried an undercurrent of excitement that radiated primarily from Tenchi.

"Father's train arrives at ten-thirty," the boy reported, practically vibrating. "Grandfather and I will walk to the station to meet him, and then we'll all come back together, and then we can show him the house and the garden and Hikaru and—"

"Breathe," Katsuhito suggested mildly.

Tenchi paused, took an exaggerated breath, and continued at slightly reduced velocity. "—and you can cook something because Father said he's heard about your cooking and wants to try it."

Sheldon's eyebrows rose. "He's heard about my cooking?"

"I told him in my letters. I tell him everything important." Tenchi's tone suggested this should have been obvious. "Your cooking is very important. It's the best cooking."

"That's... high praise."

"It's true." The boy attacked his rice with renewed enthusiasm, the matter apparently settled.

Across the table, Katsuhito's eyes held that familiar glint of amusement. "My son has been curious about the stranger who appeared on our mountain. Tenchi's letters have made him more curious, not less."

"Should I be worried?"

"Nobuyuki is protective of his son. As any father should be." Katsuhito paused, considering. "But he is also fair. He judges people by their actions, not their origins."

*That's good,* Sheldon thought, *since my origins would be rather difficult to explain.*


The morning passed in preparation.

Sheldon returned to his house after breakfast to clean, organize, and ensure everything was presentable for inspection. The main room was tidy, the smaller bedroom still in progress but at least swept. The garden showed the first signs of intentional work—cleared beds, spared herbs, the promise of future growth.

He considered what to cook. Something impressive but not ostentatious. Something that honored the ingredients rather than showing off technique. Something that said *I belong here* without saying *I'm trying to prove I belong here*.

In the end, he settled on simplicity: a proper lunch spread of onigiri, grilled fish, pickled vegetables, and clear soup. Food that any Japanese household might serve. Food that would taste better than it looked because that was how his hands worked now—intention translating into flavor without conscious effort.

*Kiyone's philosophy,* he thought as he shaped the rice. *Food made with intention carries that intention to the eater.*

He wondered what she would have thought of him, this woman he'd never met whose wisdom had become his guide. What would she have made of a stranger appearing from nowhere to become part of her family's story?


The knock came just after noon.

Sheldon opened the door to find them waiting: Katsuhito standing with his usual serene composure, Tenchi bouncing with barely contained energy, and between them—

Nobuyuki Masaki was tall, as Tenchi had described. But the word didn't capture him. He had the lean build of a man who worked with his hands as well as his mind, the slightly rumpled look of someone who cared more about substance than appearance, and eyes that were sharp with intelligence despite the warm smile on his face.

*He has Tenchi's smile,* Sheldon realized. *Or rather, Tenchi has his.*

"Tsukino-san." Nobuyuki bowed, formal but not stiff. "I've heard a great deal about you. Thank you for welcoming us."

"Thank you for coming." Sheldon returned the bow, matching depth and duration. "Please, come in. I've prepared lunch."


The meal served as introduction.

They gathered around the small table in Sheldon's main room, knees almost touching in the cramped space. Tenchi sat pressed against his father's side, one hand occasionally reaching up to touch Nobuyuki's arm as if confirming his presence. The gesture was unconscious, automatic—a child reassuring himself that the parent was real and here and not about to disappear.

*He doesn't see his father often enough,* Sheldon thought. *Weekend visits aren't the same as daily presence.*

The food, when served, drew appreciative silence.

"Tenchi wasn't exaggerating," Nobuyuki said after his first bite of rice. "This is exceptional."

"Thank you. It's simple food."

"Simple food well-made is harder than complex food adequately made. Anyone can hide mediocrity behind technique. You can't hide it behind rice and fish."

Sheldon recognized the observation for what it was: an architect's understanding of fundamentals. A man who built things knew that foundations mattered more than ornamentation.

"Your son tells me you work in design."

"Architecture. Commercial and residential, though residential is where my heart lies." Nobuyuki's eyes moved around the room, professional assessment evident in his gaze. "This house has good bones. Traditional construction, solid craftsmanship. The roof work is recent?"

"Yamada-san finished it a few days ago."

"Kenji Yamada? I know his work. My father hired him years ago for some restoration at the shrine." Nobuyuki nodded appreciatively. "You chose well. Or rather, you were fortunate that he was available."

"Fortunate seems to be a theme in my life recently."

The words came out before Sheldon could consider them. Nobuyuki's eyes sharpened slightly, but his expression remained friendly.

"Tenchi mentioned you arrived... unexpectedly."

*Carefully now.* "I inherited this house from a distant relative. The circumstances of my arrival were unusual, but I've been grateful for your father's welcome."

"My father rarely does anything without reason." Nobuyuki's tone was light, but his eyes held weight. "If he's welcomed you, it means he sees something in you that merits welcoming."

"I hope to prove worthy of his judgment."

"From what I've seen today, you're already doing that."


After lunch, Tenchi demanded a tour of the garden.

The boy led his father around the property with proprietary pride, pointing out the cleared beds, the surviving herbs, the spot designated for tomatoes. Nobuyuki followed with the patient attention of a parent accustomed to these enthusiastic presentations, asking questions that showed genuine interest.

"And this is where Hikaru sits when he watches us work," Tenchi explained, gesturing to a patch of sun-warmed earth. "Cats like to watch gardens. Sheldon-san says so."

"Does he?" Nobuyuki glanced at Sheldon. "You know cats well?"

"I'm learning. Hikaru has been an excellent teacher."

As if summoned, the orange tabby emerged from wherever he'd been lurking, approaching the group with studied casualness. He sniffed Nobuyuki's offered hand, considered, and apparently decided the newcomer was acceptable. A head-butt against Nobuyuki's palm sealed the verdict.

"He likes you!" Tenchi beamed. "That means you're good. Hikaru only likes good people."

"High praise from a discerning judge," Nobuyuki said, scratching behind the cat's ears. "Where did he come from?"

"He appeared the day the roof was finished," Sheldon explained. "A stray from the mountain. He decided to stay."

"Cats don't stay where they're not wanted. If he's claimed this place, it means the house has a welcoming spirit." Nobuyuki's voice was matter-of-fact, as if he were discussing building codes rather than metaphysics. "My wife used to say that. She believed houses had personalities."

*Kiyone.* The name hung unspoken in the air.

"Your father mentioned her wisdom about food," Sheldon said carefully. "About intention carrying through to the eater."

Nobuyuki's expression flickered—grief surfacing, then controlled. "She had many wisdoms. I'm still discovering them, even now. Finding pieces of her in unexpected places."

Tenchi had wandered toward the garden, chasing Hikaru toward the mint patch. His laughter floated back, bright and uncomplicated.

"He seems happy here," Nobuyuki said quietly, watching his son. "The letters have been different these past weeks. More alive. He writes about you constantly."

"He's a remarkable child."

"He is." Nobuyuki turned to face Sheldon directly. "My son lost his mother when he was not quite two. He's grown up loved—my father ensures that—but he's grown up without... without certain things. Younger energy. Someone to keep up with his questions. Someone who sees the world with wonder instead of having already catalogued it."

Sheldon understood what was being said. "You think I provide that."

"I think you might. I think my father thinks so too, or he wouldn't have let you become part of Tenchi's life so quickly." Nobuyuki's eyes were steady, evaluating but not hostile. "I'm not here to challenge that judgment. I'm here to understand it. To see for myself what kind of man has entered my son's world."

"And what have you concluded?"

A long pause. The garden rustled in a light breeze. Tenchi's laughter mixed with Hikaru's indignant meow—some game had apparently involved an unwilling feline participant.

"I've concluded," Nobuyuki said finally, "that Tenchi was right. Your cooking is exceptional. And that my father's judgment, as always, is sound."

The tension Sheldon hadn't realized he was holding released. "Thank you."

"Don't thank me yet. I'll be visiting regularly. Watching. Making sure this continues to be good for my son." The words were firm but not threatening. "That's what fathers do."

"I would expect nothing less."


The afternoon continued with easier energy.

Katsuhito had remained on the engawa during the garden tour, observing without interfering. Now he joined the conversation as they gathered for tea, contributing observations and gentle humor in equal measure. The dynamics became clearer as Sheldon watched: Nobuyuki deferring to his father on matters of tradition while asserting himself on practical concerns, Katsuhito allowing his son independence while maintaining quiet guidance.

*A family learning to function around absence,* Sheldon thought. *Kiyone's ghost in every interaction, shaping things by not being there.*

Tenchi, oblivious to the adult undercurrents, provided continuous entertainment. He demanded attention, demanded participation, demanded that his father see everything he'd discovered and learned and accumulated over the past three weeks. The constant reporting was exhausting to witness but clearly nourishing to Nobuyuki, who absorbed each detail like a man drinking after long thirst.

*He misses his son as much as Tenchi misses him. Weekend visits aren't enough for either of them.*

The thought carried weight. Geography and profession had separated this family, with Katsuhito serving as anchor while the others circled in widening orbits. Sheldon's arrival had added a new element—not replacing anything, but filling spaces that had gone unfilled.

*Is that what I am? A space-filler? Or something more?*

He didn't know. But watching Nobuyuki's careful attention and Katsuhito's quiet approval and Tenchi's unguarded joy, he suspected the answer mattered less than the living of it.


Dinner was a collaboration.

Sheldon cooked the main dishes while Nobuyuki handled rice—not trusting anyone else with the task, he admitted, because his late wife had taught him exactly how she liked it and he couldn't bear to eat it any other way. The confession was offered casually, but Sheldon heard the love underneath it. A man holding onto his wife through ritual, keeping her alive in the small acts she'd once performed.

"Kiyone was particular about rice," Katsuhito confirmed from his spot at the table. "She said it was the foundation of the meal. Get the foundation wrong, nothing else matters."

"An architect's daughter," Nobuyuki said, smiling. "She applied design principles to everything. Even cooking."

"Is that where you learned?" Sheldon asked. "Architecture, I mean. From her family?"

"From her father, yes. He saw something in me—potential, or maybe just enthusiasm. Trained me from barely out of school. I owe him my career." Nobuyuki's hands moved automatically through the rice-washing rhythm. "I owe him everything, really. Including Kiyone."

The admission hung in the air, intimate and exposed. Sheldon let it settle without comment, understanding that some words needed space to breathe.


After dinner, as darkness gathered outside, Nobuyuki prepared to catch the late train back to the city.

"Tenchi's birthday is Monday," he said, shrugging into his coat. "I'll return Friday evening and stay through the celebration. There will be cake."

"I want a big cake," Tenchi interjected. "With five candles. Because I'll be five."

"Five candles," Nobuyuki confirmed. "And presents. Several presents."

"What kind of presents?"

"The kind that five-year-olds like. You'll have to wait and see."

Tenchi's face suggested that waiting was an unreasonable expectation but one he would tolerate under duress.

At the door, Nobuyuki paused. He looked at Sheldon with an expression that had evolved over the course of the day—from cautious evaluation to something approaching genuine warmth.

"Thank you," he said. "For the meals. For your patience with my questions. For..." He glanced at Tenchi, who was attempting to attach himself to his father's leg. "For being here. For whatever reason you came, wherever you came from. Thank you for being here."
