"Thank you for trusting me with him."

"Trust is earned. You're in the process of earning it." Nobuyuki smiled—Tenchi's smile, aged and weathered but still bright. "Keep cooking. Keep being present. Keep being whoever it is you're becoming."

He bent to hug his son, a long embrace that Tenchi clung to with desperate affection. Then he straightened, nodded to his father, and walked into the darkness toward the village and the train that would carry him back to his other life.

Tenchi watched until the sound of footsteps faded entirely.

"He'll be back," Sheldon said quietly.

"I know." The boy's voice was small. "He always comes back. But the going-away part is hard."

"It is."

Sheldon didn't offer false comfort—no claims that it would get easier, no promises about time healing all wounds. Instead, he simply stood beside Tenchi in the doorway, two people watching darkness swallow someone they cared about, and waited for the moment to pass.


Later, after Tenchi had been escorted back to the shrine and the night had fully fallen, Sheldon sat on his engawa with Hikaru and considered the day.

*Twenty days,* he thought. *And now I've met the full family. The old priest. The young child. The working father. A constellation of grief and love and duty, orbiting around an absence.*

He understood his place now, or at least understood it better. He was the unexpected variable—the stranger who'd arrived and stayed, who filled gaps without trying to replace what had been lost. A support structure rather than a foundation. A branch added to a tree that had been growing long before he appeared.

*Is that enough?* he wondered. *Being a branch? Being useful without being essential?*

The stars offered no answers. But Hikaru's purr was steady, and somewhere up the mountain, a four-year-old boy was probably dreaming of birthday cake and presents, and in two days that boy would turn five.

Two days. Then a birthday. Then whatever came after.

The story continued. And Sheldon Tsukino was part of it now, for better or worse, for as long as he was allowed to stay.


**[END SCENE 1.20.A]**

**[DAY 20 COMPLETE]**


## SCENE NOTES

**Time at Scene End:** ~9:00 PM, April 22, 1989 — Day 20 Complete

**CRITICAL CHARACTER INTRODUCTION:**

**NOBUYUKI MASAKI** — First appearance
- Tenchi's father, Katsuhito's son
- Architect (commercial and residential; heart in residential)
- Trained by Kiyone's father
- Widower — Kiyone died when Tenchi was ~2 (~1986)
- Lives/works in city; weekend visits
- Tall, lean, intelligent eyes, warm smile
- "Tenchi's smile, aged and weathered"

**Key Character Moments:**
- Rice ritual: Makes rice exactly as Kiyone taught him
- Assessment: Watched Sheldon carefully, evaluated fairly
- Verdict: "My father's judgment, as always, is sound"
- Promise: Will visit regularly, watching, ensuring good for Tenchi

**Threads Advanced:**
- Kiyone's philosophy continues to spread
- Nobuyuki will return Friday for birthday
- Birthday Monday (Day 22) — Tenchi turns 5

**Foreshadowing:**
- "I owe him everything, including Kiyone" — debt to father-in-law
- Houses have personalities (Kiyone's belief via Nobuyuki)
- "Keep being whoever it is you're becoming" — transformation theme

---

#### Scene 21.A: Birthday Eve

The twenty-first morning arrived with the particular energy of a day before something important.

Sheldon felt it the moment he woke—an anticipatory hum that seemed to fill the house, as if even the old wooden beams understood that tomorrow mattered. Tenchi's fifth birthday. A milestone in any child's life, but especially significant in this household where milestones had been marked by absence as often as celebration.

Hikaru, immune to human anticipation, yawned and stretched with feline indifference. The cat had opinions about many things, but calendars were not among them.

"You're right," Sheldon told him. "One day at a time. But today, we prepare."


Breakfast at the shrine confirmed the prevailing mood.

Tenchi was vibrating at a frequency that seemed barely compatible with sitting still. He'd woken early, according to Katsuhito—before dawn, unable to contain the excitement of being *almost* five. The distinction between four and five apparently represented a transformation of cosmic significance.

"When I'm five, I'll be big," the boy explained between bites that were more gesture than consumption. "Bigger than now. Maybe taller. Do you get taller on your birthday?"

"Not usually overnight," Sheldon said. "Growth happens gradually."

"But I'll be *officially* big. That's different from just growing."

"That's true. Official bigness is its own category."

Tenchi nodded, satisfied that someone understood. "Father is bringing the cake. He said it would be big too. With five candles."

"Five candles for five years."

"And I get to blow them all out at once. Grandfather says if you blow them all out, your wish comes true. But you can't tell anyone the wish or it won't work."

"That's the traditional rule," Katsuhito confirmed from across the table. "Birthday wishes require discretion."

"I already know what I'm going to wish for." Tenchi's face went serious with the weight of the secret. "But I can't tell you."

"We wouldn't dream of asking."


After breakfast, Sheldon considered the problem of gifts.

He had money—more than enough to purchase something from the village shops. But money felt impersonal, and the shops themselves were limited in what they might offer a four-year-old about to become five. Toys, perhaps. Books. Practical things that any adult might choose for any child.

*What does Tenchi actually want?* he wondered. *What would mean something to him specifically?*

The answer came as he stood in his garden, surveying the cleared beds and the work yet to be done. Tenchi wanted to help with this. Tenchi wanted to be part of building something that would grow and change and produce visible results. Tenchi wanted—

*His own section.*

The idea crystallized with satisfying clarity. Not just helping with Sheldon's garden, but having a piece of it that was entirely his. A child-sized plot where he could plant whatever he chose, tend it himself, watch things grow under his own care.

*A garden of his own. That's a gift that keeps giving.*


The trip to the village took most of the morning.

Sheldon walked down the mountain path with a mental list: seeds appropriate for a child's first garden, small tools sized for small hands, perhaps a marker or sign to designate the plot as Tenchi's own. The village shops might not have everything, but they would have enough.

The agricultural supply store—the same one where he'd been directed weeks ago when asking about the Tsukino house—proved surprisingly well-stocked. The elderly proprietor remembered him, greeting him with the measured warmth of rural shopkeepers who observed everything and commented on little.

"Seeds for a child's garden?" The old man considered. "Radishes grow fast—good for impatient young hands. Sunflowers are dramatic. Cherry tomatoes are forgiving."

"All of those," Sheldon decided. "And whatever else you'd recommend."

The proprietor assembled a collection with the care of a curator: packets of seeds, a small trowel and fork sized for a child, a watering can that wouldn't be too heavy when full, and a hand-painted wooden stake that could serve as a garden marker.

"For the Masaki boy?" the old man asked while wrapping the purchases.

"For his birthday tomorrow."

"Ah." Something shifted in the proprietor's expression—not quite a smile, but something warmer than neutrality. "His mother used to shop here. Before." The word carried weight. "She had good instincts for growing things. The boy will too, I expect. It runs in families."

Sheldon absorbed this new piece of information—another thread connecting him to the woman whose absence shaped everything. "Thank you. For the seeds and the... insight."

"Growing things is good for children. Teaches patience. Responsibility. The satisfaction of results that take time." The old man handed over the wrapped bundle. "Tell him the sunflowers are from me. A birthday gift from an old man who remembers his mother."


The afternoon was spent in preparation of a different kind.

Back at his house, Sheldon marked out a section of garden bed near the corner where morning sun would be strongest. Child-sized—perhaps two meters by one—but positioned prominently where Tenchi would see it immediately upon arriving. He turned the soil one final time, removing any remaining stones or roots that might frustrate small hands.

The gift assembled itself: seeds arranged in their packets, tools laid out with care, the wooden stake waiting to be inscribed with Tenchi's name. Sheldon considered adding his own carving—something to match Katsuhito's skill with the Hikaru statue—but dismissed the idea. His talents, whatever they were, apparently did not extend to woodworking.

Instead, he wrote on the stake in careful characters: *Tenchi's Garden*. The calligraphy wasn't elegant, but it was legible. It would serve.

Hikaru supervised from the engawa, offering the occasional meow of what Sheldon chose to interpret as approval.

"You could help, you know."

The cat's expression suggested that helping was beneath his dignity, but watching was a vital contribution.


Evening brought the final element of preparation: food.

Tomorrow's celebration would center on Nobuyuki's cake, but Sheldon wanted to contribute something as well. A birthday breakfast that exceeded the ordinary. Something memorable.

He spent the hours before dinner planning: sweet rolled omelet, special rice with red beans, grilled fish arranged in festive shapes, fruits cut into flowers. Nothing beyond his capabilities, but everything elevated by intention and care.

*Kiyone's philosophy again,* he thought as he made notes. *Food made with intention. A birthday meal made with love.*

The word startled him. *Love.* Was that what this was? This warmth he felt for the boy who had attached himself so completely, so trustingly? This desire to see him happy, to contribute to his joy, to be part of his life?

*Yes,* he decided. *Whatever else I am or was or might become—yes. This is love. This is what it feels like from the inside.*


Dinner at the shrine that evening was quieter than breakfast had been.

Tenchi's morning excitement had modulated into something deeper—anticipation giving way to the solemn awareness that something significant was about to happen. He ate with unusual focus, occasionally glancing toward the door as if expecting tomorrow to arrive early through sheer force of will.

"One more sleep," he said finally, pushing away his empty bowl. "Just one more sleep and then I'll be five."

"Just one more sleep," Sheldon confirmed.

"Will you come early? For breakfast? You have to be there when I wake up."

"I'll be there."

"Promise?"

"Promise."

Tenchi nodded, satisfied. Then, with the sudden physicality of children, he lunged across the table to wrap his arms around Sheldon's neck.

"Thank you for being here," he said into Sheldon's shoulder. "For my birthday. For everything."

Sheldon held him carefully, feeling the small body's warmth, the absolute trust in the embrace.

"Thank you for letting me be here," he said quietly. "It's the best gift I could have received."


Later, walking back to his house under the stars, Sheldon carried the warmth of that moment with him.

*Twenty-one days,* he thought. *Three weeks since I woke up in that field. And somehow I've ended up here—with a house, a garden, a cat, and a family that isn't mine by blood but is mine by choice.*

The stars overhead were the same stars that had witnessed his first confused night in this world. But everything else had changed. He had changed. Was still changing, day by day, meal by meal, conversation by conversation.

*Tomorrow Tenchi turns five. And I get to be there. I get to be part of it.*

The thought filled him with something he could only call gratitude—deep and simple and entirely unexpected.

Hikaru was waiting on the engawa when he arrived home, tail swishing in greeting. The cat had learned the rhythms of this new life as quickly as Sheldon had, adapting with feline pragmatism to the reality of regular meals and reliable shelter.

"Big day tomorrow," Sheldon told him. "Birthday. Celebrations. Probably fish."

Hikaru's ears perked at the last word.

"I thought that might interest you."

They went inside together—man and cat, resident and guardian, two creatures who had found each other in an unexpected place and decided to stay.

Tomorrow would bring celebration. Tonight brought peace.

It was enough. It was more than enough.


**[END SCENE 1.21.A]**

**[DAY 21 COMPLETE]**


## SCENE NOTES

**Time at Scene End:** ~9:00 PM, April 23, 1989 — Day 21 Complete

**Key Events:**
- Birthday preparation day
- Gift assembled: Tenchi's own garden plot
- Village visit — seeds, child-sized tools, garden marker
- Agricultural store proprietor knew Kiyone ("good instincts for growing things")
- Sunflowers as gift from proprietor who remembers Kiyone
- Birthday breakfast planned
- Tenchi's hug: "Thank you for being here"

**Gift Components:**
- Garden plot (2m x 1m, corner with morning sun)
- Seed packets: radishes, sunflowers, cherry tomatoes, others
- Child-sized tools: trowel, fork, watering can
- Wooden stake marked "Tenchi's Garden"

**New Character Detail:**
- Agricultural store proprietor: elderly, remembers Kiyone
- Kiyone shopped there, "had good instincts for growing things"
- Gifted sunflower seeds for Tenchi in her memory

**Foreshadowing:**
- "Growing things runs in families" — potential inherited abilities
- Sheldon acknowledges feeling love
- "Whatever else I am or was or might become"

**Continuity:**
- Tomorrow = Day 22 = TENCHI'S 5TH BIRTHDAY
- Nobuyuki bringing cake (returns Friday, but cake delivered?)
- Birthday breakfast at shrine

---

#### Scene 22.A: Tenchi Birthday

The twenty-second morning began before dawn.

Sheldon woke in darkness, guided by intention rather than alarm. He had breakfast to prepare—not ordinary breakfast, but a birthday breakfast for a boy who was about to become officially big. The meal needed to be ready before Tenchi woke, waiting for him like a gift unwrapped by morning light.

Hikaru observed from his usual spot as Sheldon gathered the ingredients he'd prepared the night before. The cat's expression suggested mild curiosity about these pre-dawn activities, but no inclination to participate.

"Birthday," Sheldon explained quietly. "Special occasion."

Hikaru's tail swished once—acknowledgment, or perhaps dismissal of human sentimentality.


The walk to the shrine in the gray pre-dawn carried the quality of a pilgrimage.

Sheldon moved quietly, the canvas bag over his shoulder holding the wrapped gift—seeds, tools, the marker with Tenchi's name. The larger gift, the garden plot itself, waited at his house for later. But this smaller package would be opened at breakfast, a promise of what was to come.

The shrine emerged from the darkness as the first hints of light touched the eastern sky. Katsuhito was already awake—a lamp glowing in the kitchen window, the soft sounds of morning preparation filtering through the screens.

"You're early," the old priest said as Sheldon entered through the kitchen door.

"I have a breakfast to make. For a certain five-year-old."

"Ah." Katsuhito's eyes warmed. "He's been officially five for approximately—" he glanced at the clock, "—four hours now. Born just after midnight."

"Has he woken yet?"

"Three times. Each time to confirm that he was, in fact, still having a birthday. I finally convinced him that birthdays are more enjoyable when celebrated well-rested." Katsuhito stepped aside, making room at the counter. "The kitchen is yours."


The birthday breakfast came together with the focused intensity that cooking always brought.

Sweet rolled omelet, golden and slightly sweet, cut into careful segments. Red bean rice—sekihan—the traditional celebratory dish, its color promising good fortune. Grilled fish arranged in the shape of a leaping carp, symbol of strength and perseverance. Fruits transformed into flowers through careful knife work: strawberry roses, apple blossoms, orange chrysanthemums.

Sheldon worked in silence, hands moving with the assurance that had become familiar over these three weeks. Each dish was intention made visible, love translated into flavor and form.

By the time Tenchi emerged from his room—unable to contain himself any longer—the table was set and waiting.

"*Whoa.*"

The boy stopped in the doorway, eyes wide. He was still in his sleeping clothes, hair standing in multiple directions, but his face held the pure wonder of a child encountering the extraordinary.

"Happy birthday, Tenchi." Sheldon stepped back from the table, allowing the full effect to register. "You're five now."

"I'm *five*." The word emerged as a whisper, heavy with significance. Then, louder: "I'm five! Grandfather! I'm five!"

"So I've been informed," Katsuhito said dryly, appearing behind his grandson. "Several times. Throughout the night."

But his eyes were soft as he watched Tenchi approach the table, taking in each element of the breakfast with reverent attention.

"The fish is a carp," the boy observed. "Carps are strong. They swim up waterfalls."

"They do. I thought it was appropriate for someone entering their fifth year."

"And the flowers are made of fruit. Real fruit. How did you make flowers out of fruit?"

"Practice. And sharp knives."

Tenchi's gaze moved to the red-tinged rice. "Sekihan. That's for celebrations. Grandmother used to make it for special days."

The mention of Kiyone—his mother, not his grandmother, but child-language sometimes compressed generations—carried unexpected weight. Katsuhito's expression flickered, then steadied.

"Your mother did make excellent sekihan," the old priest said quietly. "She would be pleased to see it on your birthday table."

Tenchi nodded, solemn for a moment. Then the solemnity cracked, replaced by the irrepressible energy of a five-year-old facing a feast.

"Can we eat now? Please? I'm officially five and officially hungry."


The meal was consumed with enthusiasm and appropriate ceremony.

Tenchi narrated each bite, providing running commentary on flavors and textures and the specific qualities that made birthday food superior to ordinary food. Katsuhito ate with quiet appreciation, occasionally meeting Sheldon's eyes with looks that communicated more than words could carry.

"This is the best birthday," Tenchi announced between mouthfuls. "Better than four. Four was good, but five is *better*."

"What made four good?" Sheldon asked.

"Grandfather made special breakfast. And Father came. And I got a toy car that I still have." Tenchi paused, considering. "But five has fruit flowers. And carp fish. And *you*."

The last word landed with weight that the boy didn't seem to notice. *And you.* Present. Included. Part of the celebration in a way that apparently elevated it above previous years.

"I'm glad to be here," Sheldon said, meaning it more than the simple words could convey.


The gift-giving came after the meal.

Sheldon retrieved the wrapped package from where he'd left it, presenting it to Tenchi with the formality the moment seemed to require.

"This is part of your gift," he explained. "The rest is waiting at my house. But this part you can open now."

Tenchi's hands trembled slightly as he worked at the wrapping—not impatience, but the awareness of significance. When the contents were revealed, his eyes went wide.

"Seeds," he breathed. "And tools. And—" He held up the wooden stake with its careful calligraphy. "It says my name. *Tenchi's Garden.*"

"You'll have your own section. A plot that's entirely yours to plant and tend. These seeds are what you'll put in the ground."

"My own garden?" The concept seemed to require processing. "Like... my own garden that belongs to me?"

"Exactly like that."

Tenchi looked at the seeds, at the child-sized tools, at the stake with his name. Then he looked at Sheldon with an expression that made the early rising and careful preparation worth every moment.

"This is the best present," he said. "Not just for five. For *ever*."

"The sunflowers are a special addition," Sheldon added. "From the man at the seed shop in the village. He remembered your mother, and wanted to send them for your birthday."

The information settled slowly. "The shop man knew my mother?"

"She used to buy seeds from him. He said she was good at growing things."

Tenchi looked at the sunflower packet with new reverence. "Then I'll grow the best sunflowers. For her."


Kindergarten could not be avoided, despite Tenchi's pointed suggestions that birthdays should probably be holidays.

"You can tell your friends it's your birthday," Katsuhito offered as consolation. "And this afternoon, we'll go to Sheldon's house and you can see your garden."

"And plant seeds?"

"If there's time before dinner. But we want to do it properly, not rush."

"Properly is good." Tenchi clutched his gift package, clearly planning to show it to everyone at school. "Can I bring these?"

"The tools should stay here. But the seed packets might travel well in your bag."

The compromise was acceptable. Tenchi prepared for school with uncharacteristic speed, eager to begin the day so that the afternoon could arrive sooner.

"I'll be here when you get back," Sheldon promised at the door. "We'll walk down to the house together."

"And plant?"

"And plant."

Tenchi's smile could have illuminated the mountain.


The day passed in preparation and anticipation.

Sheldon returned to his house to ensure the garden plot was perfect—one final turning of the soil, one last check for stones or debris that might frustrate small hands. Hikaru supervised from his preferred spot, offering occasional commentary in the form of meows that might have been encouragement or criticism.

"He's going to love it," Sheldon told the cat. "I hope."

Hikaru's expression suggested that the quality of the garden was less important than the continued provision of fish, but Sheldon chose to interpret this as agreement.

By early afternoon, when Tenchi would be returning from school, everything was ready. The plot waited in its corner of sun, turned earth dark and promising. The marker stood at one end, *Tenchi's Garden* facing outward like a declaration.

The sound of running feet announced the boy's arrival before he appeared—that familiar rapid patter on the path from the shrine, barely slowed by the descent.

"Where is it? Where's my garden?"

Tenchi burst through the garden gate with Katsuhito following at a more measured pace. His eyes found the prepared plot immediately, drawn by the stake with its calligraphy.

"There." Sheldon pointed, unnecessarily.

The boy approached his garden with the reverence of a pilgrim approaching a shrine. He knelt at the edge, touching the turned earth with careful fingers.

"It's real," he whispered. "My garden. My actual garden."

"Ready for planting. When you are."

Tenchi looked up, face bright with determination. "I'm ready. I've been ready all day. I told everyone at school about my garden and they were jealous but in a nice way and sensei said it was a wonderful present and I couldn't wait to come home and now I'm home and it's *real*."

The words tumbled out in a single breathless stream. Sheldon found himself smiling.

"Then let's plant."


The planting became ceremony.

Sheldon showed Tenchi how to create small furrows in the soil, spacing them properly for each type of seed. The boy absorbed the instruction with intense focus, asking questions about depth and distance and which seeds needed more sun.

"Radishes first," Sheldon suggested. "They grow fast. You'll see results in a few weeks."

"A few weeks is fast?"

"For gardens, yes. Some things take months. Some take years."

"That's a long time."

"It is. But the waiting is part of it. Watching something grow, day by day. Seeing the changes."

Tenchi considered this as he carefully placed radish seeds in their furrow. "Like growing up?"

"Exactly like that."

The sunflowers came next—the gift seeds from the shop owner who remembered Kiyone. Tenchi handled these with particular care, creating a row at the back of the plot where they would grow tall without shading the smaller plants.

"For Mother," he said quietly as he covered the seeds with soil. "So she knows I'm growing things too."

Katsuhito, watching from the engawa, made a soft sound that might have been acknowledgment or emotion or both.

Cherry tomatoes, radishes, sunflowers. The packets from the shop had included a few others—green onions, herbs, lettuce. Each found its place in Tenchi's garden, planted by small hands under careful guidance.

When the last seed was covered, Tenchi stood back to survey his work.

"Now what?"

"Now we water. Gently—we don't want to wash the seeds away."

Sheldon demonstrated with the small watering can, creating a soft rain that darkened the soil without disturbing it. Then he handed the can to Tenchi.

"Your garden. Your water."

The boy completed the task with solemn attention, ensuring every inch received its share. When the can was empty, he set it down and looked at the plot with satisfaction.

"Now they grow?"

"Now they grow. But you have to keep watering them. Every day, or every other day, depending on the weather. And watch for weeds."

"I will. I promise." Tenchi's voice carried the weight of sacred oath. "This is my garden. I'll take care of it."


Dinner that evening was at the shrine, a continuation of the birthday celebration.

The sekihan had been saved for the evening meal, joined by other celebratory dishes Katsuhito had prepared. The atmosphere was warm with accomplishment and anticipation—seeds planted, a new year of life begun, promises made for the growing season ahead.

"Father will bring cake on Friday," Tenchi reported, mouth full of rice. "He said it would be a big cake with five candles. Five candles for five years."

"The official birthday celebration," Katsuhito confirmed. "When family can gather properly."

"But today is the real birthday," Tenchi said with certainty. "Today I turned five. Friday is just... extra birthday."

"Extra birthday is a useful concept," Sheldon agreed. "One can never have too much celebration."

"Exactly." Tenchi nodded with the satisfaction of being understood. "Today is birthday. Friday is extra birthday. And now I have a garden."

"The best gift," Katsuhito said, echoing his grandson's earlier words. "Something that grows with you."


The evening wound down in the familiar rhythm of bath, stories, and gradual surrender to sleep.

But tonight Tenchi requested something specific: a story about gardens.

Sheldon obliged, weaving a tale about a boy who planted a magic seed—one that grew not into a plant, but into a door. A door that opened to a garden where everything the boy had ever loved was waiting: lost toys, forgotten friends, memories of happy days.

"The garden remembers everything," Sheldon explained as Tenchi's eyes grew heavy. "Because gardens are patient. They hold on to things. They keep them safe until we're ready to find them again."

"Like my garden will remember me?"

"Exactly like that. Even when you're grown up and have gardens of your own, this first one will remember. It will always be yours."

"That's good." Tenchi's voice was fading, sleep claiming him despite his best efforts. "I want to be remembered. By gardens. And by people."

"You will be," Sheldon promised. "You absolutely will be."

The boy's breathing slowed, steadied, deepened into dreams. Katsuhito appeared silently to carry him to bed, nodding to Sheldon in the doorway.

"Thank you," the old priest said quietly. "For all of it. The breakfast, the garden, the story. The being here."

"Thank you for letting me be here to give it."


The walk home under the stars felt like closing a chapter.

*Twenty-two days,* Sheldon thought. *And now he's five. Growing, changing, becoming whoever he's going to be. And somehow I'm part of that.*

The thought filled him with something he was learning to recognize as purpose. Not the abstract, cosmic purpose he sometimes sensed lurking at the edges of his awareness, but something simpler and more immediate. Day-to-day purpose. The purpose of showing up, of being present, of planting seeds—literal and metaphorical—and watching them grow.

Hikaru waited on the engawa, tail swishing in greeting. The cat had no interest in birthdays or gardens or cosmic significance, but he was consistent in his welcome.

"Good day," Sheldon told him. "One of the best."

Together, they went inside. Tomorrow would bring new routines, new growth, new steps forward. But tonight had been a celebration—of five years lived, of gardens planted, of family found in unexpected places.

It was enough. It was more than enough.


**[END SCENE 1.22.A]**

**[TENCHI'S 5TH BIRTHDAY COMPLETE]**


## SCENE NOTES

**Time at Scene End:** ~9:00 PM, April 24, 1989 — Day 22 Complete

**CRITICAL EVENT COMPLETE:**
- **TENCHI'S 5TH BIRTHDAY** ✓
- Born just after midnight (per Katsuhito)
- Now officially 5 years old

**Birthday Breakfast:**
- Sweet rolled omelet
- Sekihan (red bean rice)
- Carp-shaped grilled fish
- Fruit flowers (strawberry roses, apple blossoms, orange chrysanthemums)

**Gift Components Given:**
- Seed packets (radishes, sunflowers, cherry tomatoes, green onions, herbs, lettuce)
- Child-sized tools (trowel, fork, watering can)
- Wooden stake marked "Tenchi's Garden"
- Garden plot (2m × 1m, morning sun)

**Planting Complete:**
- All seeds planted in proper rows
- First watering done by Tenchi
- Care instructions given

**Key Emotional Beats:**
- "This is the best present. Not just for five. For *ever*."
- Sunflowers "for Mother" — planted in her memory
- Magic garden story — gardens remember everything
- Tenchi: "I want to be remembered. By gardens. And by people."

**Schedule:**
- Friday (Day 26): Nobuyuki returns with cake for "extra birthday" celebration

---

#### Scene 23.A: Twenty Third Morning

Sheldon woke to the weight of Hikaru on his feet.

The cat had migrated during the night from his customary spot at the foot of the futon to directly atop Sheldon's ankles, a warm and surprisingly heavy presence for an animal that weighed perhaps four kilograms. The orange tabby's purr vibrated through the blanket like a small motor.

"You're not subtle," Sheldon murmured.

Hikaru's response was to knead his paws twice against the futon, claws catching slightly in the fabric, before settling deeper into his position. The message was clear: *I'm comfortable. You figure it out.*

Sheldon lay still for a moment, cataloging the room in the gray pre-dawn light. Five days now. Five days since he'd carried his bag through that door and set it down in the corner. Five days of waking in this room, on this futon, in this house that bore his name even though it had belonged to people he'd never met.

The Tsukino house. His house, now.

The thought still didn't quite fit, like a shirt cut for broader shoulders. But it fit better today than it had on Day 18, when he'd stood in the empty main room and wondered what he was supposed to do with all this space. It fit better than Day 19, when he'd spent most of the day clearing the overgrown garden plot and feeling like an imposter playing house. Better than Day 20, when Nobuyuki Masaki had appeared on the shrine path and Sheldon had felt himself being measured by eyes that held no warmth until the end.

Better, but not perfect. Maybe perfect wasn't the goal.

Hikaru stretched, yawned, and finally deigned to relocate to the patch of futon Sheldon's leg had warmed. The cat curled into a tight ball, tucked his nose under his tail, and was immediately asleep again.

"Some help you are," Sheldon said, but he was smiling as he pushed back the blanket and rose.

The morning routine had taken shape over the past week. He folded the futon with economical movements—muscle memory now, hands knowing where the folds went—and stored it in the oshiire. He washed his face in the bathroom, the cold water sharp enough to banish the last of sleep. He dressed in work clothes: worn trousers, a simple shirt, the boots that had walked him from Okayama Station to Masaki Shrine on that first impossible day.

Twenty-three days. Had it only been twenty-three days?

In the kitchen—his kitchen, strange thought—Sheldon filled Hikaru's water bowl and measured out a portion of the dry food he'd picked up at the general store in the village. The sound of kibble rattling into the ceramic dish brought padding footsteps from the other room. Hikaru appeared in the doorway, stretched elaborately, and approached the food bowl with the studied casualness of a cat pretending he hadn't just sprinted from the bedroom.

"Your dignity is showing," Sheldon observed.

Hikaru ignored him and began eating.

Sheldon didn't prepare his own breakfast here—that happened at the shrine, where Katsuhito and Tenchi would be waiting. Instead, he tidied what little needed tidying, checked that the windows were secure, and stepped out into the morning.

The air held that particular quality of late April: cool enough to need movement, warm enough to promise summer's approach. The cherry blossoms were nearly gone now, the last petals clinging to branches or scattered across the ground in fading pink drifts. In another week, maybe two, the trees would be fully green.

Sheldon took the path toward the shrine at an easy pace. He'd walked this route enough times now that his feet knew the way without conscious thought—the slight dip where roots crossed the path, the stone that always seemed to catch the morning light, the bend where the trees opened and the torii gate became visible ahead.

The shrine compound was quiet this early. Smoke rose from the residence's chimney—Katsuhito already had the fire going. And there, on the engawa facing east, a small figure sat with his knees drawn up to his chest, watching the path.

Tenchi spotted him immediately.

"Sheldon-san!" The boy launched himself off the engawa and came running, bare feet slapping against the wooden platform and then the packed earth of the yard. "You're here! Come see if the seeds sprouted!"

"Good morning to you too," Sheldon said, but he was already being tugged by the hand toward the Tsukino house—his house—and the small garden plot at its rear.

"I wanted to check when I woke up," Tenchi said, words tumbling over each other in his eagerness, "but Grandfather said I had to wait until you were here because it's your garden too even though it's mostly my garden because I planted all the seeds and named all the rows—"

"You named the rows?"

"The radish row is Usagi and the carrot row is Kuma and the sunflower row is—" Tenchi stopped at the edge of the garden plot, his grip on Sheldon's hand tightening. His whole body went still.

Sheldon looked.

The garden was exactly as they'd left it yesterday afternoon: dark soil, carefully raked, with small markers indicating where each type of seed had been planted. The surface was undisturbed. No green shoots pushing through. No signs of life at all.

Just dirt.

"Oh," Tenchi said.

The disappointment in that single syllable was profound. Five years old, and already learning that wanting something didn't make it appear.

Sheldon crouched down beside him, bringing their eyes level. "What did we put in the ground yesterday?"

"Seeds," Tenchi said, his voice small.

"And what are seeds?"

Tenchi's brow furrowed. "They... grow into plants?"

"Eventually, yes. But first, they need to wake up." Sheldon touched the soil gently—cool and slightly damp from overnight dew. "Right now, the seeds are sleeping in there. They're feeling the water we gave them, feeling the warmth of the soil, starting to stretch inside their shells. It's like how you need time to wake up in the morning."

"I wake up fast," Tenchi said, though some of the devastation had left his voice.

"You do. Seeds are slower. The radishes—Usagi—will probably show their heads in about a week. Maybe a little less if the weather stays warm."

"A whole week?" The concept seemed to stagger Tenchi. A week was forever when you were five.

"Gardening teaches patience," Sheldon said. "Every day, we water. Every day, we look. And one morning—maybe when we're not even paying attention—we'll see a tiny green leaf poking through the soil."

Tenchi stared at the garden as if he could will the seeds to sprout through sheer intensity. Then his shoulders dropped slightly, accepting. "Can we still water today?"

"We should water today. The seeds need us to keep our promise."

That seemed to help. Tenchi retrieved the watering can from where they'd left it by the house's corner—already filled from yesterday's evening routine, topped off by dew—and began the careful process of giving each row a gentle soaking. He moved with the exaggerated care of someone entrusted with important work, tongue poking out slightly from the corner of his mouth.

Sheldon watched, and something in his chest ached sweetly.

"The sunflowers are for Mama," Tenchi said suddenly, not looking up from his task. "When they get big, they'll be tall enough to see from far away."

"They will," Sheldon agreed. His voice came out rougher than he'd intended.

"Grandfather says she can see us from everywhere now, but I think she'd like having something tall to look at. Something yellow, like her favorite color."

Sheldon had no response to that. None that would be adequate. Instead, he said, "I think she'd like that very much."

Tenchi finished watering, examined the garden one more time for any sign of change—there was none—and finally nodded with the gravity of a farmer surveying his fields. "Okay. Tomorrow we'll check again."

"Tomorrow," Sheldon agreed.

They walked back to the shrine together, Tenchi's earlier urgency replaced by a more contemplative mood. Katsuhito met them at the residence entrance, a knowing glint in his eyes.

"No sprouts yet, I take it."

"Sheldon-san says they need to wake up first," Tenchi reported. "Like sleeping, but for a whole week."

"Ah. Seeds are indeed slower than certain young boys I know." Katsuhito stepped aside to let them enter. "Breakfast is ready. Tenchi, you have school in an hour and a half, and you haven't dressed yet."

"But—"

"Miso soup grows cold faster than seeds grow tall. Go."

Tenchi went, padding toward his room with only one backward glance at Sheldon—a look that said *we'll check again tomorrow* as clearly as if he'd spoken it.

"His mother's garden was vegetables too," Katsuhito said quietly once Tenchi was out of earshot. "Tomatoes, eggplant, cucumbers. Kiyone always said flowers were pretty but food was useful. The sunflowers were Tenchi's addition."

Sheldon absorbed that. Another piece of a woman he'd never meet, preserved in the habits of those she'd left behind.

Breakfast was miso soup, rice, pickled vegetables, and grilled fish—the standard morning meal that Sheldon had learned to prepare under Katsuhito's patient guidance. They ate together, the three of them, while spring sunlight crept across the tatami and Tenchi chattered about what he might do at school today.

At eight-thirty, Tenchi was dressed in his kindergarten uniform, backpack strapped on, standing at the entrance of the residence with his outdoor shoes in hand.

"Extra birthday in three days," he said to Sheldon. "Three days is like... this many." He held up three fingers.

"I can count, Tenchi."

"But do you *remember*?"

"Your father is bringing a cake on Friday. I remember."

Tenchi nodded seriously, satisfied that the information had been properly recorded. Then he was off, running down the shrine steps—three hundred twenty of them, Sheldon had counted—with the boundless energy of a child who had never learned to walk when running was an option.

Sheldon watched until the small figure disappeared around the first bend, then turned to find Katsuhito beside him.

"You handled the garden disappointment well," the old priest said.

"It wasn't disappointing, not really. Just... educating." Sheldon paused. "He didn't expect them to sprout overnight. He hoped."

"There's a difference?"

"Hope survives disappointment. Expectation breeds resentment."

Katsuhito made a thoughtful sound. "That's an old soul's observation."

Sheldon didn't know how to respond to that, so he said nothing.

The morning spread out before them, empty of obligations for the moment. Tenchi wouldn't return until mid-afternoon. The shrine needed its daily maintenance, but that was Katsuhito's rhythm, not his. The house—his house—had no urgent projects waiting.

"I was thinking," Sheldon said, "of starting on the front garden today. The Tsukino house's front garden," he clarified. "It's overgrown, but there's structure underneath. Someone planted it deliberately, years ago."

"Setsuko Tsukino," Katsuhito said. "Himari's mother. She loved azaleas."

Another name, another piece of history preserved in soil and stems. This village was layered with the memories of people Sheldon had never known, each generation planting something the next would tend.

"I'll see what can be salvaged," Sheldon said.

Katsuhito nodded slowly. "There may be tools in the shrine's storage shed. Old, but serviceable. I'll show you after I've made my morning rounds."

"Thank you."

They stood together for a moment longer, watching the empty path where Tenchi had vanished, listening to the birds beginning their morning chorus in the trees around the shrine.

Twenty-three days. Twenty-three days of learning this place, these people, these rhythms.

Sheldon had no idea how many more days stretched ahead. Thousands, probably. Tens of thousands. A lifetime, if he was lucky.

He'd better get started.

---

#### Scene 23.B: Twenty Third Afternoon

The shrine's storage shed stood at the edge of the compound, half-hidden behind a stand of bamboo that had clearly been planted to screen it from the main buildings. It was old—older than the residence, Sheldon suspected, built from timber that had weathered to a silver-gray that seemed to absorb light rather than reflect it.

Katsuhito produced a key from somewhere in his robes and worked it into a lock that looked like it predated the Meiji Restoration.

"This shed has been here longer than I have," the old priest said, which was either a joke or a confession, depending on how one interpreted his earlier comment about seven hundred years. "The tools inside are... eclectic."

The door swung open with a creak that suggested the hinges hadn't seen oil in decades. Sheldon peered inside.

Eclectic was generous.

The shed was perhaps three meters deep and four wide, and every centimeter of wall space had been pressed into service. Tools hung from pegs driven into the timber: saws of various sizes, their teeth dark with age; hammers with handles worn smooth by generations of hands; chisels, planes, files, rasps. A workbench ran along one wall, its surface scarred and stained but solid. Shelves held boxes and jars whose labels had faded to illegibility.

And in the back, propped against the wall like soldiers awaiting orders, stood an array of garden implements.

"The Tsukino family stored their tools here after Himari passed," Katsuhito said. "Her father was the last to use most of these. That was... twenty years ago, perhaps more."

Sheldon stepped inside carefully, mindful of the packed earth floor and the dust that hung in the air like suspended time. His eyes adjusted to the dimness, and details emerged: a hand scythe with a curved blade that still held an edge; a set of pruning shears in a leather case; a hori-hori—a Japanese garden knife—with a blade that showed the marks of frequent sharpening; several trowels of different sizes; a rake with bamboo tines.

He reached for the hori-hori first. The handle was wood, darkened by decades of contact with hands and soil. The blade was steel, slightly rusted near the base but sound. He tested the edge against his thumb—still sharp enough to matter.

"This belonged to Setsuko," Katsuhito said, watching him. "Himari's mother. She was particular about her tools. Cleaned and oiled them after every use."

"She took care of them," Sheldon said. The hori-hori felt right in his hand, balanced and purposeful. "They took care of her garden."

"A philosophy."

"A relationship."

Katsuhito made that thoughtful sound again—the one that seemed to contain entire conversations he chose not to voice.

Sheldon selected a few more tools: the pruning shears, a small hand rake, the trowels. He found a canvas bag hanging from a peg—also old, also well-made—and loaded his selections into it. The weight settled against his hip as he slung the strap over his shoulder.

"I'll bring them back when I'm done," he said.

"Keep them at the house." Katsuhito's voice was mild. "Setsuko's tools should return to Setsuko's garden. They've waited long enough."

He had no words for that. He simply nodded and stepped back out into the spring sunlight.


The front garden of the Tsukino house had been invisible to him for the first two weeks—not literally, but functionally. He'd walked past it every day on his way to and from the shrine, registered it as "overgrown vegetation," and moved on. There had been more pressing concerns: the roof, the interior cleaning, the rear garden plot for Tenchi's seeds.

Now, standing at the edge of the property with Setsuko's tools on his hip, he actually looked.

The garden occupied the space between the house's front wall and the low fence that marked the boundary with the path. Perhaps six meters wide and three deep, it was a tangle of growth that had clearly once been something else. Weeds had claimed the open spaces. Vines had climbed what might have been trellises. Grass had invaded from the edges, creeping inward year after year.

But underneath—yes, there was structure. He could see it now that he was looking. The bones of paths, buried under leaf litter. The shapes of beds, their edges softened but still present. And rising above the chaos, the woody stems of something that had survived despite years of neglect.

Azaleas. They had to be.

Sheldon set down the tool bag, rolled up his sleeves, and began.


The work was meditative in its simplicity.

He started at one corner, clearing a small section completely before moving to the next. The hand rake pulled away the accumulated debris—dead leaves, fallen twigs, the detritus of seasons piled upon seasons. Beneath it, he found soil: dark, rich, alive with the movement of insects that fled the sudden light.

The weeds came next. He used the hori-hori for the deep-rooted ones, sliding the blade into the soil beside them and levering them out with their root systems intact. The shallow-rooted invaders he simply pulled by hand, tossing them into a growing pile at the garden's edge.

His hands knew this work.

That was the strange thing—the thing he'd noticed before but still couldn't explain. His body remembered movements his mind had never learned. The angle of the blade for maximum leverage. The way to grip a weed's base to extract the entire root. The rhythm of clear, examine, move that turned chaos into order one handspan at a time.

He'd never gardened seriously in his previous life. A few houseplants, maybe. A failed attempt at tomatoes on an apartment balcony. Nothing like this deliberate, patient restoration of someone else's abandoned creation.

Yet his hands moved with confidence, and the garden responded.

By mid-morning, he'd cleared perhaps a quarter of the space. The bones of Setsuko's design were emerging: a curved path of flat stones, nearly invisible under the debris; a raised bed edged with larger rocks, arranged with obvious intention; and the azaleas—three of them, their woody stems twisted and overgrown but unmistakably alive.

Sheldon sat back on his heels and studied them.

They'd grown wild without pruning, sending out branches in all directions, competing with each other and with the weeds for light and space. But the root systems were established, decades old, drawing from soil that Setsuko had prepared and planted them in. They'd survived her absence. They'd survived Himari's absence. They'd survived twenty years of neglect.

They were still here.

"You're tougher than you look," Sheldon said to the nearest azalea. Its response was a slight movement of leaves in the breeze, which he chose to interpret as agreement.

He reached for the pruning shears.


Pruning was different from clearing. Clearing was removal—taking away what didn't belong. Pruning was sculpture—deciding what to keep and what to sacrifice, shaping the plant toward some future version of itself.

Sheldon had never pruned azaleas before. He was certain of that. The theoretical physicist from his memories had possessed no knowledge of ornamental shrub maintenance whatsoever.

But his hands knew where to cut.

He started with the dead wood—branches that had died and dried, serving no purpose except to crowd the living growth. The shears snipped through them cleanly, and he tossed the debris onto the pile. Then the crossing branches, the ones that rubbed against each other and would eventually damage the bark. Then the interior growth that blocked airflow and light.

With each cut, the azalea's shape emerged more clearly. It had been planted to be rounded—a soft dome of foliage that would have been covered in flowers every spring. Twenty years of neglect had turned it into a wild tangle, but the underlying form was still there, waiting to be revealed.

He worked slowly, stepping back frequently to assess, making adjustments, following an aesthetic logic he couldn't have articulated but somehow understood.

By the time his stomach informed him it was past noon, the first azalea looked like a plant again rather than a thicket. The second and third still needed attention, but the clearing around them was complete. The stone path was fully visible now, its curves leading the eye from the entrance to the house's front step. The raised bed awaited replanting—he'd need to think about what might go there.

Sheldon set down the shears, stretched his back, and surveyed his work.

Not bad for a morning. Not bad at all.


Lunch was a simple affair: rice from the shrine's morning pot, reheated in a small pan over the kitchen fire; pickled vegetables from the jar Katsuhito had given him as a housewarming gift; a single egg, scrambled with green onions he'd found growing wild at the garden's edge.

The egg was perfect.

He hadn't tried for perfect—hadn't consciously aimed for the exact moment when the curds were set but still glossy, when the onions had softened just enough to sweeten but not enough to lose their bite. He'd simply cooked, and the result had been exactly what the ingredients wanted to become.

Supreme Chef. That was what he'd started calling it in his head, though the name felt grandiose for what amounted to an inability to make bad food. Every meal he prepared came out precisely as it should. Every cut, every temperature, every timing—all of it aligned without conscious effort.

It was useful. It was also deeply, persistently strange.

Sheldon ate his perfect scrambled eggs at the low table in the main room, Hikaru watching from a patch of sunlight near the window. The cat had emerged from wherever he'd spent the morning—probably the bedroom, probably asleep—and was now performing his supervisory duties with appropriate gravitas.

"Don't give me that look," Sheldon said. "You've already been fed."

Hikaru's expression suggested that previous feedings were historical events with no bearing on present circumstances.

"No."

The cat yawned, clearly unimpressed by this show of resolve, and began grooming his paw.

Sheldon finished his meal, washed the dishes, and returned to the garden.


The afternoon passed in the same rhythm as the morning: clear, examine, prune, move. The second azalea took shape under his hands, then the third. He found other survivors buried in the overgrowth—a small camellia he hadn't noticed, its glossy leaves nearly hidden by vines; what might have been a dwarf maple, badly in need of attention but alive; a ground cover plant he couldn't identify, spreading beneath the chaos with quiet determination.

Setsuko had planted a garden meant to last. She'd chosen plants that could endure, rooted them deep, given them the foundation to survive whatever came. And they had. Through neglect, through abandonment, through years of accumulated debris, they had held on.

Sheldon thought about that as he worked. About things that endured. About foundations laid by people who couldn't know what would come after them. About the strange responsibility of inheriting someone else's creation and deciding what to do with it.

He was still thinking about it when he heard footsteps on the path and looked up to see Tenchi running toward him.

"Sheldon-san! Sheldon-san, I'm back from school!"

The boy skidded to a stop at the garden's edge, eyes wide as he took in the transformation. The tangle of that morning was gone, replaced by something that actually looked like a garden—rough, still recovering, but recognizable.

"You found plants," Tenchi said, wonder in his voice. "There were plants under there the whole time?"

"Azaleas," Sheldon said, pointing. "And a camellia, and maybe a maple. They were planted a long time ago by a woman named Setsuko. She was your great-great-grandmother's mother."

Tenchi's brow furrowed as he tried to work out that genealogical chain. "That's... a lot of greats."

"It is. She planted this garden many years before you were born. And the plants she chose were strong enough to survive until someone came to take care of them again."

Tenchi considered this with the gravity it apparently deserved. Then: "Like the seeds in my garden?"

"Yes. Like seeds. They wait for what they need, and when they get it, they grow."

Tenchi nodded slowly. Then his eyes lit up. "Can I help?"

Sheldon looked at the remaining work—one more section to clear, debris to dispose of, tools to clean. All of it manageable alone, but none of it requiring solitude.

"Get the hand rake from the bag," he said. "I'll show you how to clear without damaging the roots."

Tenchi practically dove for the tool bag, emerging with the bamboo rake held like a trophy. Sheldon positioned him at a cleared section, demonstrated the gentle scraping motion that removed debris without disturbing the soil beneath, and watched as the boy began to work with the same focused intensity he'd brought to watering his own garden that morning.

They worked together as the afternoon light softened toward evening, the old garden slowly remembering what it had been, the new gardener learning what it could become.

---

#### Scene 23.C: Twenty Third Evening

The light had turned golden by the time they stopped.

Tenchi sat back on his heels, the bamboo rake still clutched in both hands, surveying the cleared section with the satisfaction of someone who had done real work. His face was streaked with dirt where he'd wiped sweat away, and bits of leaf debris clung to his hair.

"We did a lot," he said.

"We did." Sheldon stretched, feeling the pleasant ache in his shoulders and lower back—the kind of tiredness that came from honest labor rather than strain. "The garden looks better than it has in twenty years."

"Is that a long time?"

"Longer than you've been alive. Four times longer."

Tenchi's eyes went wide as he tried to process that math. "That's... really long."

"It is." Sheldon stood, brushing soil from his knees. "Time to clean up. The tools need care before we put them away."

He showed Tenchi how to knock the loose dirt from the rake's tines, how to wipe down the metal parts with an oiled rag to prevent rust, how to hang each tool in its proper place. The boy watched with serious attention, mimicking each movement with the deliberate care of someone learning something important.

"Setsuko-san did this too?" Tenchi asked.

"Every time she used these tools. That's why they still work after all these years."

Tenchi nodded slowly, absorbing this. Then: "Will my garden tools last that long?"

"If you take care of them the same way."

The tool bag went into the small storage space beneath the engawa—not the shrine's shed anymore, but here, where Setsuko had kept them. Where they belonged.

"We should tell Grandfather about the azaleas," Tenchi said as they walked toward the shrine path. His energy had shifted from the focused intensity of work to the bubbling excitement of news to share. "And the camellia. And the maybe-maple."

"Maybe-maple?"

"You said 'might be a maple.' So it's a maybe-maple until we know for sure."

Sheldon couldn't argue with that logic.


Katsuhito was on the engawa when they arrived, a cup of tea cooling beside him, watching the evening light filter through the trees. His eyes tracked their approach—noted the dirt, the tired satisfaction, the way Tenchi immediately launched into a breathless report of the afternoon's discoveries.

"—and there were three azaleas the whole time, just hiding, and Setsuko-san planted them before I was even born, before *anyone* was born practically, and Sheldon-san let me use the rake and I cleared a whole section by myself—"

"I see," Katsuhito said, his voice mild but his eyes amused. "And did the garden appreciate being found?"

Tenchi paused, considering this with unexpected seriousness. "I think so. The plants looked happier after we cleared all the stuff away. They could breathe."

"Plants do appreciate breathing room." Katsuhito's gaze shifted to Sheldon. "You've been productive."

"The front garden was waiting for someone to remember it." Sheldon settled onto the engawa, accepting the unspoken invitation. "Setsuko built something meant to last. It just needed tending."

"Most worthwhile things do."

Tenchi had already disappeared inside, presumably to wash up before dinner. The sound of running water and splashing carried through the open shoji.

"He worked well," Sheldon said. "Focused. Patient, mostly."

"He wanted to impress you."

The observation landed with quiet weight. Sheldon turned it over, examining it from different angles. "I hope I gave him reason to feel proud instead."

Katsuhito made that thoughtful sound—the one Sheldon was beginning to recognize as approval, or at least acknowledgment that something worth thinking about had been said.


Dinner was a collaborative effort.

Katsuhito had rice cooking and miso soup simmering, but the fish—three small mackerel from the village market—sat waiting on the cutting board. Sheldon was at the counter before conscious decision caught up, knife in hand, preparing the fish for grilling with movements that felt as natural as breathing.

Supreme Chef. He'd named the ability in his own head, but moments like this made the name feel inadequate. It wasn't just skill—it was *rightness*, a sense of exactly what the ingredients needed and how to give it to them. The knife found the perfect angle. The seasoning measured itself. The grill reached temperature at precisely the right moment.

Tenchi watched from the table, freshly scrubbed and wearing clean clothes, as Sheldon laid the fish over the coals.

"How do you know when they're done?"

"The skin tells you." Sheldon pointed to where the edges were beginning to crisp. "See how it's pulling back slightly? And the flesh is turning opaque from the bottom up. When the color change reaches about three-quarters of the way—"

"You flip it?"

"Exactly."

"Can I flip one?"

Sheldon glanced at Katsuhito, who gave an almost imperceptible nod.

"Come here. Use this." He handed Tenchi a flat spatula, positioning the boy's hands correctly. "Slide it under—gently—and turn it toward you, not away. Ready?"

Tenchi's tongue poked out in concentration. The flip was slightly clumsy but successful, the fish landing skin-side up without breaking apart.

"I did it!"

"You did."

The meal that followed was simple—grilled mackerel, rice, miso soup, pickled vegetables—but the fish had achieved that precise balance of crispy skin and tender flesh that elevated simple into satisfying. Tenchi ate with the unselfconscious enthusiasm of a child who had worked hard and was now properly hungry.

"Extra birthday in two days," he announced between bites. "That's... this many." He held up two fingers.

"I remember," Sheldon said.

"Dad's bringing cake."

"You mentioned."

"Chocolate cake. With strawberries." Tenchi's expression suggested this was the most important information in the universe. "And candles. Five candles because I'm five, plus one extra for good luck."

"Six candles for five years. The math works out."

Katsuhito smiled into his soup.


After dinner, they walked to the rear garden together—Tenchi's garden, the one they'd planted together—for the evening watering ritual.

The soil was still dark from the morning's watering, but the late afternoon sun had dried the surface. Tenchi filled the watering can from the rain barrel, carried it carefully to the edge of the plot, and began the same methodical process he'd performed that morning.

Still no sprouts. The soil remained undisturbed, giving no sign of the seeds sleeping beneath.

"Tomorrow?" Tenchi asked, though his tone suggested he already knew the answer.

"Tomorrow we check again. And the day after, and the day after that."

"Until Usagi wakes up."

"Until Usagi wakes up."

Tenchi finished watering, set down the can, and stood for a moment looking at the garden with an expression too complex for his four years—hope and patience and a kind of tender protectiveness for things not yet visible.

"Goodnight, seeds," he said quietly. Then, seeming to remember he had an audience, he turned to Sheldon with slightly pink cheeks. "I say goodnight. So they know someone's watching."

"That's a good practice."

They walked back to the shrine together, the evening settling around them like a comfortable garment. Katsuhito was waiting on the engawa, ready for the bedtime routine that had become familiar over the past weeks.

"Goodnight, Sheldon-san," Tenchi said at the entrance, already half-asleep on his feet. "Thank you for the garden."

"Thank you for the help."

Tenchi smiled—a small, tired smile that held more warmth than seemed possible from such a compact source—and disappeared inside.


The walk back to the Tsukino house was quiet, the path familiar now even in the dimming light. Sheldon's feet found the roots and stones without conscious attention, his body navigating while his mind drifted.

Twenty-three days. The number kept returning, a kind of milestone marker. Twenty-three days since he'd woken in this body, in this place, with no explanation and no clear purpose. Twenty-three days of building something he couldn't name—routines, relationships, the slow accumulation of trust and familiarity.

The house emerged from the trees, darker than the shrine residence but somehow welcoming. A shadow moved in the window—Hikaru, keeping watch.

Sheldon let himself in, set his boots by the genkan, and padded across the tatami to where the cat waited.

"You've been supervising my absence?"

Hikaru's response was to wind between his ankles, a brief acknowledgment before resuming his post on the windowsill.

The evening routine unfolded: water for the cat, a brief check of the house, preparation of the futon. Sheldon's muscles announced their fatigue as he moved through each task—not pain, just the honest accounting of a body that had done good work.

He lay down on the futon, Hikaru settling at his feet, and stared at the ceiling in the gathering darkness.

The garden would be waiting tomorrow. Setsuko's azaleas, slowly remembering what they were meant to be. Tenchi's vegetables, still dreaming beneath the soil. The camellia, the maybe-maple, the ground cover plant whose name he still didn't know.

All of them waiting. All of them patient. All of them growing in ways that couldn't be seen but were happening nonetheless.

Sheldon closed his eyes.

Tomorrow was Day 24. Two more days until Tenchi's extra birthday. Four more days until the end of April. Beyond that—unknowable distances, countless tomorrows, a lifetime of days not yet lived.

But tonight, this day was complete.

He let sleep take him.

---

#### Scene 24.A: Twenty Fourth Morning

Rain.

Sheldon woke to the sound of it—a steady patter against the roof tiles, softer than he'd expected, almost musical. The room was darker than usual, the gray morning light filtered through clouds that must have rolled in overnight.

Hikaru had abandoned his post at the foot of the futon for the windowsill, where he sat watching the water streak down the glass with the focused intensity of a scientist observing an experiment.

"Fascinated or offended?" Sheldon asked.

The cat's ear twitched in acknowledgment, but his attention remained fixed on the rain.

The morning routine adjusted itself to the weather. Sheldon dressed in indoor clothes rather than work gear—no garden restoration today, not with the soil turning to mud. He fed Hikaru, checked the windows for leaks (none; the repairs from Day 16 were holding), and stood for a moment at the door, watching the rain transform the familiar landscape into something softer, edges blurred, colors deepened.

The path to the shrine would be slick. He pulled on his boots, took the umbrella he'd acquired from the village store, and stepped out into the wet morning.


The shrine compound wore the rain well.

Water dripped from the eaves of the haiden in silver curtains. The gravel paths had darkened to pewter. The trees seemed to lean into the moisture, leaves catching drops and releasing them in small cascades. Everything smelled of wet earth and green things growing.

Tenchi was not on the engawa this morning—too wet for his usual perch. Instead, Sheldon found him pressed against the shoji inside, nose nearly touching the paper, watching the rain through the translucent screen.

"It's raining," Tenchi announced, as if this might not be obvious.

"I noticed."

"We can't check the garden."

"We can check it. We just can't work in it."

Tenchi considered this distinction with visible skepticism. "But it's *raining*."

"Rain is good for gardens. The seeds are getting water without us having to carry it."

This seemed to mollify him slightly. "So Usagi is still waking up?"

"Usagi is getting a bath. Seeds like baths."

Katsuhito appeared from the kitchen, tea already prepared. "The garden will survive one day without inspection, Tenchi. As will you."

"But tomorrow is—"

"Tomorrow is tomorrow. Today is today." Katsuhito handed Sheldon a cup. "The rain should clear by afternoon, according to the radio. Light showers, nothing sustained."

Breakfast was quieter than usual, the rain providing a backdrop that seemed to encourage contemplation rather than chatter. Even Tenchi ate with less commentary, though his eyes kept drifting toward the windows.

"Tomorrow," he said finally, unable to contain it any longer, "is my extra birthday."

"It is," Sheldon agreed.

"Dad is coming."

"He is."

"With cake."

"Chocolate, with strawberries. Six candles."

Tenchi nodded, satisfied that the essential facts remained in order. Then, with the abrupt topic shifts characteristic of five-year-olds: "Will it rain tomorrow?"

"I don't know. Would rain ruin your birthday?"

Tenchi thought about this with unexpected seriousness. "No," he decided. "Because cake is inside. And candles work inside. And Dad will come even if it rains because he's Dad."

"Sound logic."

"But I want to show him the garden." Tenchi's voice dropped slightly. "Both gardens. The one with Usagi and the one with the azaleas."

"If it rains, we can still show him. Rain doesn't make gardens invisible."

"But they look better when it's sunny."

Sheldon set down his chopsticks. "They look different when it's sunny. Not necessarily better. Gardens in rain have their own kind of beauty—everything washed clean, colors deeper, the plants drinking. Your father might like seeing them that way."

Tenchi absorbed this, his expression shifting from worry to curiosity. "Different beautiful?"

"Different beautiful."

Katsuhito made that thoughtful sound—the one Sheldon had learned to recognize as silent approval. The conversation moved on to school, to what Tenchi might do in class today, to the small logistics of a rainy Wednesday morning.

At eight-thirty, Tenchi stood at the entrance in his rain boots and yellow slicker, hood pulled up, backpack protected under a waterproof cover Katsuhito had produced from somewhere.

"One more sleep," he said, holding up a single finger.

"One more sleep," Sheldon confirmed.

Tenchi grinned—bright and unguarded—and splashed down the shrine steps into the rain.


With Tenchi gone and the garden work suspended, the morning opened into unexpected space.

Sheldon helped Katsuhito with indoor shrine maintenance—polishing the offering vessels, sweeping the haiden's wooden floor, organizing the storage closet where ritual implements were kept. The work was meditative, requiring attention but not strain, leaving room for the mind to drift.

"You've settled well," Katsuhito observed during a pause in the work.

"I'm still figuring out what 'settled' means."

"Most people are." Katsuhito ran a cloth along a brass vessel, bringing out its dull shine. "The difference is whether you're fighting the uncertainty or building within it."

"Which am I doing?"

"That's not a question I can answer for you."

They worked in companionable silence. The rain continued its gentle percussion against the roof. Somewhere in the village below, a dog barked once and fell quiet.

"Tenchi talks about you constantly," Katsuhito said. "To me. To Nobuyuki on the phone. Yesterday he told his teacher about the maybe-maple."

Sheldon's hands stilled on the vessel he was polishing. "He's easy to talk to."

"He's easy to love. Whether you allow yourself to do so is a different matter."

The observation landed with precision—not accusation, just acknowledgment of something Sheldon had been carefully not examining. He resumed polishing, the rhythm of the motion providing space to think.

"I'm not sure what I'm allowed," he said finally. "Or what's fair to him."

"Fair to him would be honesty about your presence. Which you've given—you're here, you're participating, you're not pretending to be something you're not." Katsuhito set down the vessel and looked at Sheldon directly. "What you're uncertain about is permission. Whether you have the right to matter to him."

"Do I?"

"You don't get to decide that. He does. And he already has."

The rain filled the silence that followed—not uncomfortable, just present. Sheldon turned Katsuhito's words over, examining them from different angles.

"I'm not going anywhere," he said. "Not by choice."

"Then the question becomes irrelevant. You're here. He's chosen you. The rest is just fear dressed up as philosophy."

Katsuhito returned to his polishing, the conversation apparently complete. But something had shifted—some knot of uncertainty loosened, if not untied entirely.

Sheldon finished the vessel in his hands and reached for the next one. Outside, the rain began to lighten.

---

#### Scene 24.B: Twenty Fourth Evening

The rain stopped shortly after noon, leaving the world washed and gleaming.

By the time Tenchi came running up the shrine steps—boots splashing through the puddles rather than avoiding them—patches of blue had broken through the clouds. The afternoon light had that peculiar clarity that follows rain, making colors sharper and distances seem closer.

"Did you check the garden?" was his first question, breathless from the climb.

"I waited for you."

Tenchi's face split into a grin that suggested this was exactly the right answer.

They walked to the rear garden together, boots squelching in the wet grass. The soil was dark and saturated, small pools collecting in the lower spots. Tenchi crouched at the edge, peering at the surface with the intensity of someone expecting revelation.

Still nothing. Just earth, undisturbed, giving no sign of what stirred beneath.

"The rain helped," Sheldon said. "Even if we can't see it yet."

"Usagi is drinking." Tenchi nodded, accepting this. "Getting stronger."

"Exactly."

They checked the front garden next—a quick survey rather than work. The rain had been gentle enough not to damage the pruned azaleas, and the cleared paths were already draining. The maybe-maple's leaves held droplets that caught the late sunlight like small crystals.

"Tomorrow Dad can see everything," Tenchi said, satisfied. "Even if it's still wet."

"Different beautiful?"

"Different beautiful."


Dinner was Sheldon's turn to cook—a rotation that had developed naturally over the past weeks. He made curry rice, adjusting the spice level for Tenchi's palate, letting the familiar rhythms of kitchen work settle his thoughts.

Katsuhito's words from the morning lingered. *The rest is just fear dressed up as philosophy.* The observation was uncomfortably accurate. Sheldon had been holding something back—not affection, exactly, but the acknowledgment of it. As if naming the bond would somehow make it more vulnerable.

But Tenchi had already named it, in his own way. Every "Sheldon-san," every garden check, every breathless report of discoveries real and imagined. The boy wasn't waiting for permission.

The curry came out perfectly—Supreme Chef never missed—and Tenchi pronounced it "the best curry in the world," which was almost certainly hyperbole but felt earned nonetheless.

"Tomorrow," Tenchi said around a mouthful of rice, "no more sleeps. Just wake up and it's my birthday."

"That's how it works."

"And Dad will be here."

"In the afternoon, after work."

"And there will be cake."

"Chocolate with strawberries. Six candles."

Tenchi held up five fingers, counting them to make sure the number was right. It was.

After dinner, they performed the evening watering—unnecessary after the rain, but Tenchi insisted. "So the seeds know we didn't forget them." He walked the rows carefully, watering can held like a ritual object, giving each section a ceremonial sprinkle.

"Goodnight, Usagi. Goodnight, Kuma. Goodnight, sunflowers." He paused, looking at Sheldon. "Do you want to say goodnight?"

Sheldon crouched beside him. "Goodnight, seeds. Keep growing."

Tenchi nodded approvingly, satisfied that the ritual had been properly observed.


The walk back to the Tsukino house felt different tonight.

Not the path—the path was the same, familiar now in a way that felt permanent rather than temporary. But something in Sheldon had shifted, some resistance loosened. Katsuhito's words, maybe. Or Tenchi's unselfconscious invitation to say goodnight to seeds. Or just the accumulation of days, each one building on the last, creating something that looked increasingly like a life.

Hikaru met him at the door, tail high, performing his usual inspection of Sheldon's boots before allowing entry.

"I'm home," Sheldon said, and for the first time, the words didn't feel strange.

The evening routine: water for the cat, check the windows, prepare the futon. Outside, the clouds had fully cleared, revealing stars that seemed closer than they had in Tokyo—less light pollution here, more sky.

Tomorrow was Day 25. Tenchi's extra birthday. Nobuyuki would come with cake and questions. The gardens would be inspected and praised. Six candles would be lit and blown out.

And Sheldon would be there for all of it, not as a temporary presence but as whatever he was becoming—family, maybe, or something close enough that the word stopped mattering.

He lay down on the futon. Hikaru claimed his usual spot. The stars wheeled slowly overhead, invisible through the roof but present nonetheless.

Tomorrow would be a good day.

Sheldon let himself believe it.

---

#### Scene 25.A: Extra Birthday Morning

Sheldon woke to silence—no rain, no cat on his feet. Just the pale light of early morning and the distant sound of birds beginning their chorus.

He found Hikaru at the window again, but this time the cat was watching something outside rather than water on glass. Sheldon rose and looked: the sky had cleared completely overnight, leaving behind the kind of crystalline blue that only appeared after storms.

"Good birthday weather," he said.

Hikaru's tail twitched in what might have been agreement.

The walk to the shrine felt different today—charged with anticipation that wasn't entirely his own. When he reached the compound, he understood why: Tenchi was already outside, still in his pajamas, standing at the edge of the rear garden with a stuffed rabbit clutched under one arm.

"Sheldon-san!" Tenchi spotted him and came running, the rabbit bouncing against his side. "It's my birthday! Well, my extra birthday, but it still counts!"

"Happy birthday." Sheldon crouched to meet him at eye level. "You're up early."

"I couldn't sleep anymore. I tried, but my eyes kept opening." Tenchi held up the rabbit—worn, well-loved, one ear slightly lopsided from years of handling. "Usagi wanted to see the garden too. I told her I named a row after her."

Sheldon looked at the rabbit with appropriate gravity. "I'm sure she's honored."

"She is. She said so."

They walked to the garden together, Tenchi's bare feet leaving prints in the dew-wet grass. The soil was still damp from yesterday's rain, dark and rich, and Tenchi crouched at the edge with Usagi propped beside him, both of them peering at the undisturbed surface.

"Still sleeping," Tenchi said, but there was no disappointment in his voice this time. "That's okay. Usagi—the row Usagi, not bunny Usagi—is getting stronger. Maybe she'll wake up for my real birthday."

"When's your real birthday?"

"April 24th. But that was a Monday, and Dad couldn't come because of work." Tenchi picked up Usagi the bunny and brushed a bit of grass from her fur. "So we have an extra birthday when Dad can be here. Because birthdays are for family."

"You are," Sheldon agreed, and meant it.


Breakfast was festive by Katsuhito's understated standards—special rice with red beans, grilled fish, pickled plum. Tenchi ate with the vibrating energy of a child counting down hours rather than days.

"Dad will be here at four," he announced, as if this information might have changed overnight. "That's after school, after lunch, after... a lot of things."

"It is."

"But then there's cake. And candles. And I get to blow them out and make a wish." Tenchi's expression grew serious. "I already know what I'm going to wish for."

"Don't tell us," Katsuhito said mildly. "Wishes told don't come true."

"I know." Tenchi nodded solemnly. "I won't tell. But it's a good wish."

At eight-thirty, he was dressed in his kindergarten uniform, backpack on, Usagi tucked carefully inside where she could watch but wouldn't get dirty. He paused at the entrance, looking back at Sheldon.

"You'll be here when I get back? And when Dad comes?"

"I'll be here."

"Promise?"

"Promise."

Tenchi held up his pinky finger—the Japanese gesture for a binding oath. Sheldon matched it, their fingers linking briefly.

"Yubikiri genman," Tenchi recited, the traditional promise rhyme, then grinned and ran down the steps, leaving the adults in his wake.


The morning passed quietly. Sheldon helped Katsuhito with shrine preparations—nothing elaborate, but the haiden received extra attention, the grounds were swept clean of storm debris, the offering vessels polished to their brightest shine.

"You're preparing for Nobuyuki," Sheldon observed.
