"I'm preparing for family." Katsuhito set down the cloth he'd been using. "There's a difference."

"Is there?"

"Nobuyuki is Tenchi's father. But family includes whoever shows up to celebrate a child's birthday." The old priest's gaze was direct. "You're expected. You know this."

Sheldon did know it. That was the strange part—not the expectation, but how natural it felt to accept it.

"What should I know about him? About Nobuyuki?"

Katsuhito considered the question. "He loved my granddaughter absolutely. He lost her too soon. He's raising their son while working long hours in the city, and every weekend he drives three hours each way to spend two days here before driving back." A pause. "He's tired, and grieving, and doing his best. Judge him by that, not by first impressions."

Sheldon remembered their first meeting—Day 20, the wary eyes, the questions that felt like tests. "He was protecting Tenchi."

"He still is. But protection can become suspicion if you let it." Katsuhito returned to his polishing. "Show him who you are. Let him decide what to do with it."

---

#### Scene 25.B: Extra Birthday Celebration

Tenchi came up the shrine steps at a run, school bag bouncing, voice carrying ahead of him.

"I'm back! Is Dad here yet? Is he here?"

"Not yet." Sheldon caught him at the top of the steps, steadying him before he could trip over his own momentum. "He said four o'clock. It's barely two."

"That's forever."

"It's two hours."

"Forever hours." But Tenchi was grinning, too excited to maintain the complaint. "Can we check the gardens while we wait? Both of them? So I know what to show Dad?"

They made the rounds together—rear garden first, where the soil remained stubbornly silent, then the front garden, where the azaleas were beginning to show the first hints of new growth at their pruned tips. Tenchi catalogued everything with the focus of a tour guide preparing his material.

"The azaleas are here, and the camellia is there, and the maybe-maple is over there, and the stone path goes like this—" He traced the curve with his hand. "And Sheldon-san found all of it under the weeds. Like treasure."

"The plants did the hard work," Sheldon said. "I just cleared away what was hiding them."

"That's still important." Tenchi's voice was matter-of-fact. "Hiding something doesn't mean it's not there. Someone has to look."

Sheldon had no response to that. Sometimes children said things that landed with unexpected weight.


The sound of a car engine reached them at ten minutes to four.

Tenchi's head snapped toward the road, his whole body going taut with anticipation. Then he was running—down the shrine path, through the gate, toward the parking area where a familiar sedan was pulling in.

"DAD!"

Sheldon stayed where he was, giving them the moment. He could see Nobuyuki emerging from the car, a large box carefully balanced in his arms, then setting it on the roof to catch his son as Tenchi launched into a full-body hug.

They stayed like that for a long moment—father and son, holding on. Whatever else Sheldon might wonder about Nobuyuki Masaki, this much was clear: the man loved his child absolutely.

Katsuhito appeared beside Sheldon, silent as always. "He drove straight from work. Three hours."

"For a birthday party."

"For his son." Katsuhito's voice was mild. "The party is incidental."

Nobuyuki approached with Tenchi attached to his side like a barnacle, the cake box now held in both hands. He looked tired—the kind of deep tiredness that sleep alone couldn't fix—but his smile when he looked at Tenchi was genuine.

"Tsukino-san." The greeting was polite, neutral. Testing.

"Masaki-san." Sheldon gave a small bow. "Thank you for making the drive."

"Thank you for being here." The words came slowly, as if Nobuyuki was weighing each one. "Tenchi has told me about the gardens. Both of them. Repeatedly."

"He's been working hard."

"So I hear." Nobuyuki's gaze held Sheldon's for a moment—assessing, but not hostile. Whatever he saw seemed to satisfy something, because the tension in his shoulders eased slightly. "I understand there's a tour scheduled."

"DAD." Tenchi tugged at his father's sleeve. "The cake first or the tour first?"

"Tour first," Katsuhito said, emerging from the residence. "The cake needs time in the cold box. And your father needs tea before anything else."

Nobuyuki looked at his father-in-law with something that might have been gratitude. "Tea would be welcome."


The garden tour was Tenchi's show.

He led his father through the rear garden first, explaining each row by name ("This is Usagi, like my bunny, and this is Kuma, and the sunflowers are for Mama"), pointing out where the seeds were sleeping, demonstrating the proper watering technique with ceremonial gravity.

Nobuyuki listened with the focused attention of a parent who understood that the content mattered less than the telling. He asked questions—"How deep did you plant them? When will they sprout?"—and received answers that mixed genuine knowledge with five-year-old confidence.

"Sheldon-san showed me everything," Tenchi said, leading them toward the front garden. "He knows about seeds because his hands know things his head didn't learn."

Nobuyuki's eyebrows rose slightly. He glanced at Sheldon.

"He's paraphrasing," Sheldon said. "I have some gardening skill I can't fully explain. It's useful."

"Apparently." Nobuyuki's tone was dry but not unfriendly. "Tenchi says you found plants buried under twenty years of neglect."

"Setsuko Tsukino planted them well. They survived."

They reached the front garden, and Nobuyuki stopped at the edge, his expression shifting as he took in the transformation. The cleared paths. The shaped azaleas. The small camellia, the maybe-maple, the ground cover spreading between the stones.

"This was jungle," he said quietly. "I remember. Kiyone always wanted to restore it, but there was never time."

The name landed softly in the spring air. Kiyone. Tenchi's mother. Nobuyuki's wife.

"The plants she would have found are still here," Sheldon said. "Waiting."

Nobuyuki was silent for a long moment. Then: "Thank you. For finding them."

There was nothing more to say. They stood together in the garden that had waited twenty years to be remembered, while Tenchi pointed out features his father had already seen, filling the silence with the easy chatter of a child who didn't know he was bridging grief.


The cake was chocolate with strawberries, exactly as promised. Six candles stood in a careful circle on top—five for age, one for luck.

Katsuhito lit them with a long match, and the small flames danced in the evening light filtering through the shoji. Tenchi sat before the cake with the reverent attention of someone approaching a sacred ritual.

"Make a wish," Nobuyuki said. "But don't tell us."

"I know." Tenchi closed his eyes, his face screwing up with the intensity of concentrated wishing. Then he took a deep breath and blew.

All six candles went out on the first try.

"I did it!" Tenchi's eyes flew open, checking to make sure. "They all went out! That means my wish comes true!"

"That's the rule," Katsuhito agreed solemnly.

The cake was cut, slices distributed. Sheldon's piece was perfect—of course it was, someone had made it with care—but Nobuyuki's eyes tracked him as he ate, still assessing, still deciding.

"Tenchi says you eat breakfast here every day," Nobuyuki said, his voice casual but his eyes sharp.

"Most days. Katsuhito-san has been kind enough to include me."

"And you've been teaching Tenchi about gardens."

"We've been learning together. He teaches me as much as I teach him."

Something in Nobuyuki's expression shifted—not quite a smile, but something adjacent to it. "He talks about you constantly. On the phone, every evening. 'Sheldon-san said this, Sheldon-san showed me that.' I wasn't sure what to expect."

"What did you expect?"

Nobuyuki set down his fork. "I expected someone who wanted something. People don't appear from nowhere and attach themselves to grieving families without reason."

The observation was fair. Sheldon had thought the same thing, in Nobuyuki's position.

"I didn't appear from nowhere," he said. "I walked from Okayama Station. I was tired, and lost, and Katsuhito-san offered me shelter. The rest happened because your son is impossible not to care about."

Nobuyuki was quiet for a moment. Then: "That's not an explanation."

"No. It's not." Sheldon met his eyes directly. "I don't have a complete explanation. I'm here. I'm not going anywhere. I'm not a threat to your family. Beyond that, I can only show you who I am, day by day."

The silence stretched. Katsuhito drank his tea. Tenchi, oblivious to the undercurrents, was attempting to eat his second slice of cake with more enthusiasm than technique.

"Kiyone would have liked you," Nobuyuki said finally. "She had no patience for people who wouldn't answer questions directly."

"Is that approval?"

"It's... acknowledgment." Nobuyuki picked up his fork again. "We'll see about approval."

It wasn't trust. Not yet. But it was a door left open, and that was more than Sheldon had expected.


The evening wound down gently.

Nobuyuki helped Tenchi through his bath and into pajamas while Sheldon cleaned up from dinner. Katsuhito disappeared to perform evening rites at the haiden, leaving the household to its domestic rhythms.

When Sheldon finished in the kitchen, he found Nobuyuki on the engawa, watching the stars emerge over the shrine compound. The man looked less tired now—still weary, but the sharp edges had softened.

"He's asleep," Nobuyuki said. "Crashed about thirty seconds after his head hit the pillow. Birthday exhaustion."

"It was a big day for him."

"Yes." Nobuyuki was quiet for a moment. "I can't be here as much as I should be. Work, the commute, the apartment in the city—it doesn't leave room for daily presence. Katsuhito does what he can, but he's not young, and Tenchi needs..."

He trailed off. Sheldon waited.

"Tenchi needs people," Nobuyuki finished. "People who see him. Who listen to him. Who take his gardens and his stuffed rabbit and his endless questions seriously." A pause. "You do that."

"He makes it easy."

"He makes it necessary. Not the same thing." Nobuyuki turned to face Sheldon directly. "I don't know who you are or where you came from. I don't know why you're here. But my son is happier than he's been in months, and that matters more than answers."

Sheldon said nothing. There was nothing adequate to say.

"I'll be back next weekend," Nobuyuki continued. "And the weekend after that. If you're still here, still... whatever this is... we'll talk more. But for now—" He extended his hand. "Thank you. For the gardens. For the time. For taking him seriously."

Sheldon shook it. "Thank you for trusting him to show me."

Nobuyuki almost smiled. "I didn't have much choice. He decided you were worth knowing. Five-year-olds are very certain about these things."

He went inside to check on Tenchi one more time. Sheldon stayed on the engawa, watching the stars wheel overhead, thinking about trust and family and the strange ways they intersected.

Tomorrow Nobuyuki would drive back to the city. The weekend visits would continue, the gradual building of something that might someday be trust. And Sheldon would be here, day by day, showing who he was.

It was enough. For now, it was enough.

---

#### Scene 26: Twenty Sixth Day

Nobuyuki left before breakfast.

Sheldon came up the shrine path to find him loading his bag into the sedan, Tenchi clinging to his leg with the determined grip of someone who understood that letting go meant waiting.

"I'll call tonight," Nobuyuki was saying, one hand resting on his son's head. "And I'll be back next weekend. Same as always."

"Promise?"

"Promise."

Tenchi accepted this with the gravity of a diplomat receiving a treaty. He released his father's leg and stepped back, allowing the door to close.

Nobuyuki's eyes found Sheldon across the courtyard. A nod—brief, acknowledging—and then the engine turned over and the car disappeared down the mountain road.

"He always has to go," Tenchi said, his voice matter-of-fact rather than sad. "But he always comes back."

"That's how it works with family that lives far away."

"Is your family far away?"

The question landed unexpectedly. Sheldon considered it—his grandmother, decades gone; his parents, never close; his previous life, impossibly distant in ways that had nothing to do with miles.

"Yes," he said finally. "Very far away."

Tenchi absorbed this, then reached up and took Sheldon's hand. "Then we can be your close-by family. So you don't have to wait too long."

There was nothing to say to that. Sheldon let his hand be held and walked with Tenchi to check the garden.


Day 5 of the garden watch. Still no sprouts.

"Soon," Tenchi said, with the confidence of someone who had decided the universe would cooperate. "Usagi is almost ready. I can tell."

"How can you tell?"

"The soil looks different. Darker in the middle."

Sheldon looked. The soil looked exactly the same as it had yesterday, and the day before. But he nodded anyway.

"You might be right."

The day unfolded quietly after that. Tenchi went to kindergarten with Usagi the bunny in his backpack. Sheldon worked on the front garden—pruning the maybe-maple, which was definitely a maple now that he could see its distinctive leaf shape emerging. Katsuhito appeared occasionally, observed, offered the occasional comment, and disappeared again.

By evening, the routine had reasserted itself: dinner at the shrine, garden watering, goodnight to the seeds, the walk home in the gathering dark.

Hikaru met him at the door with reproachful meowing—the food bowl was empty and this was apparently an unforgivable oversight.

"I was busy," Sheldon told him.

The cat was not interested in excuses.

---

#### Scene 27: Twenty Seventh Day Showa

The village was quiet for the holiday.

Showa Day—the first Showa Day, technically, since Emperor Hirohito had died in January and this was the first observance of what had been his birthday. Sheldon remembered reading about the transition in a newspaper that felt like it belonged to another life. The Heisei era was only four months old.

No school for Tenchi, which meant he appeared at the Tsukino house shortly after dawn, still in his pajamas, announcing that they should check the garden immediately because holidays were lucky and maybe the seeds knew it was special.

They checked. Day 6. Still nothing visible.

"Tomorrow," Tenchi said. "Tomorrow for sure."

"What makes you sure about tomorrow?"

"Because tomorrow is the last day of April. And Usagi wants to see the sun before May."

The logic was impeccable in its own five-year-old way.

They spent the morning exploring the village—a rare excursion that Katsuhito encouraged with a wave and a comment about boys needing to see more than shrines and gardens. Tenchi served as tour guide, pointing out the important landmarks: the convenience store where his father bought him candy, the bridge where you could see fish if you looked at the right time, the house where an old woman had a dog that barked at everything.

Sheldon let himself be led, storing details, building a map of the place that was becoming home.

Lunch was at the shrine—cold soba noodles, appropriate for the warming weather. Tenchi ate with the distracted energy of someone already thinking about the next thing.

"What if they never wake up?" he asked suddenly. "The seeds. What if they stay asleep forever?"

"They won't."

"But what if?"

Sheldon set down his chopsticks. "Then we'd plant new ones. And wait again. And keep waiting until something grew."

"That sounds hard."

"It is hard. That's why it matters when it works."

Tenchi considered this, noodles forgotten. "Like Dad coming back every weekend? That's hard but it matters?"

"Exactly like that."

The afternoon passed in the gentle rhythm of a holiday: no obligations, no urgency, just time and the people who filled it. They watered the garden at sunset, said goodnight to the seeds, and Sheldon walked home under a sky that was beginning to fill with stars.

One more day. Then May.

---

#### Scene 28: Last Day April FINALE

The morning was cool and clear, the kind of spring day that felt like a held breath before summer's exhale.

Sheldon woke early—earlier than usual—with a sense of something impending that he couldn't name. Hikaru was already at the window, watching the light strengthen, and when Sheldon rose, the cat gave him a look that seemed to say *about time*.

He dressed, fed the cat, and walked to the shrine.

Tenchi was waiting. Not at the garden this time, but at the top of the shrine steps, bouncing on his heels with barely contained energy.

"I didn't look yet," he said as Sheldon approached. "I waited. So we could see together."

"That was very patient of you."

"It was VERY hard." Tenchi grabbed his hand. "Come on. Come *on*."

They walked to the rear garden together, Tenchi's grip tight, his whole body vibrating with anticipation. The soil was dark from the morning dew, the rows still clearly marked, everything exactly as it had been yesterday and the day before and the day before that.

Except.

"Sheldon-san." Tenchi's voice had dropped to a whisper. "Sheldon-san, *look*."

In the first row—Usagi's row, the radishes—a tiny curve of green had broken the surface. Then another. And another. A dozen pale stems, barely visible, pushing up through the dark earth toward the light.

"They woke up," Tenchi breathed. "They *woke up*."

He dropped to his knees at the edge of the garden, face inches from the sprouts, as if closer observation might reveal the actual moment of emergence. His expression held wonder and triumph and something deeper—the particular joy of patience rewarded.

"You grew," he told the sprouts. "You finally grew. I knew you would. I *knew* it."

Sheldon crouched beside him, looking at the tiny green threads that had pushed through darkness into light. Seven days. Seven days of watching and waiting and saying goodnight, and here was the proof that waiting worked.

"The carrots will be next," he said. "Then the sunflowers."

"Kuma is slower," Tenchi agreed. "But she's trying. I can tell."

They stayed there for a long time, watching the sprouts that weren't doing anything visible but somehow demanded watching anyway. Eventually Katsuhito appeared on the engawa, observed the scene, and made that thoughtful sound that Sheldon had learned to recognize.

"The first sprouts," he said. "A good omen for May."

"They woke up for the last day of April," Tenchi said. "Just like I said they would."

"You did say that."

Tenchi stood, brushing dirt from his knees, his face split by a grin that seemed too large for his small frame. "I'm going to tell Dad. Can I use the phone? He has to know about Usagi right away."

"It's early—"

"He won't mind. Not for this."

He ran toward the shrine residence, leaving Sheldon and Katsuhito alone at the garden's edge. The sprouts swayed slightly in the morning breeze, impossibly fragile, impossibly present.

"One month," Katsuhito said quietly.

"One month."

"You've become part of this place. You know that."

Sheldon didn't answer. The sprouts were answer enough—things planted, things tended, things that grew whether you understood how or not.

Tomorrow would be May. New days, new growth, new complications he couldn't foresee. But today was the last day of April, and the seeds had woken up, and a five-year-old boy was calling his father to share the news.

It was enough.

It was more than enough.

---


**END OF CHAPTER 1**


*Total Words: ~98,884*
